Divine Transcendence

God's Transcendence: Five Negations
Sanusi enumerates negations asserting God's transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five negations that establish God's absolute difference from created things and why each negation is necessary.

Core Idea
God must be declared free from all attributes of contingent things.

Sanusi now moves from affirming God's power and will to establishing His transcendence. He lists five specific negations that safeguard God's uniqueness and prevent any similarity to created beings. This passage is a cornerstone of tanzih, showing that any hint of corporeality or spatial limitation would undermine God's divinity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the five negations Sanusi mentions and the logical consequence he draws.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the five negations
List the five things Sanusi says God must be exalted above: being a body, subsisting in a body, being adjacent to a body, being in a direction relative to a body, and being depicted in imagination.
These are all attributes of contingent things.
3
Understand the argument
Sanusi argues that any of these would imply similarity to contingent things, leading to the absurd consequence that what applies to them (origination) would apply to God, undermining His pre-eternity and divinity.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هنا تعلم أيضاً وجوب تنزيهه تعالى أن يكون جزماً ، أو قائماً بهِ ، أو محاذياً له ، أو في جهةٍ له ، أو مرتسماً في خيالِهِ ؛ لأنَّ ذلكَ كلَّه يُوجِبُ مماثلتَهُ للحوادثِ ، فيجب له ما وجبَ لها ! وذلك يقدح في وجوب قدمِهِ وبقائِهِ ، بل وفي كل وصفِ مِنْ أوصاف أُلوهِيَّتِهِ .
From this you also learn the necessity of declaring Him exalted above being a body (jism), or subsisting in a body (qā'im bihi), or being adjacent to it (muḥādhiyan lahu), or being in a direction (jiha) relative to it, or being depicted in one's imagination (murtasiman fī khayālihi). Because all of that necessitates His similarity to contingent things, so whatever is necessary for them would be necessary for Him! And that would detract from the necessity of His pre-eternity and persistence, nay, from every attribute of His divinity.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a brief enumeration of five negations that establish God's transcendence (tanzīh). Sanusi states that we must declare God exalted above five things: being a body, subsisting in a body (as an accident), being adjacent, being in a direction, and being mentally pictured. He argues that any of these would imply similarity to contingent things (ḥawādith). If God were similar to them, then the same necessities that apply to contingent things (like origination) would apply to Him, undermining His pre-eternity, persistence, and all divine attributes.

Key Terms
تنزيه
جسم
جهة
مماثلة
حوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God being 'above' means a literal direction or location.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly negates being in any direction, including 'above' as a spatial direction.

Checkpoint
Which of the following does Sanusi affirm about God in this passage?
A) He is a body
B) He subsists in a body
C) He is not in any direction
D) He can be imagined

Key Takeaway
God must be declared free from any attribute of contingent things to preserve His pre-eternity and divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you unconsciously imagine God in a particular direction or form? How does this lesson convict you, and what specific step will you take to purify your worship from such mental images?
?
Honestly, what mental image of God do you find hardest to let go of? Confess it now, and commit to repeating these five negations daily until your heart is free.
?
The passage warns that any similarity would make God subject to the limitations of created things. In your daily actions, where do you treat God as though He shares human weaknesses? What practical change will you make this week to honor His absolute otherness?

The Attribute of Being Unlike Created Things
Sanusi proves God must be transcendent above all created attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that God is unlike all created things and how any form of similarity would contradict His divinity.

Core Idea
God's transcendence is proven by the impossibility of any similarity to creation.

Sanusi has already shown that God must be eternal and not originated. Now he extends that reasoning to show God is completely unlike everything He created. This passage lists five specific forms of similarity that are impossible for God and explains why denying similarity is essential to His Lordship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very concise style here.
2
Identify the five negations
The passage lists five things that God is not: a part, an accident, adjacent, in a direction, or picturable. Find each one and understand what it means.
These correspond to five ways created things are characterized.
3
Understand the logical proof
Sanusi argues by contradiction: if any of these applied to God, He would be similar to created things. Then whatever applies to creation would apply to Him, which is impossible for the Eternal.
This is a 'reductio ad absurdum' argument.
4
See the consequence for divinity
Similarity would negate God's pre-eternity (qidam) and permanence (baqā'), and ultimately every divine attribute.
Thus mukhālafah is not just a negative attribute but a positive necessity.
5
Reflect on the attribute of mukhalafa
Consider why even being picturable in imagination is considered a form of similarity. This shows how thoroughly God transcends all categories of creation.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفة المخالفة للحوادثِ ومن هنا تعلمُ أيضاً وجوبَ تنزُّههِ تعالى أنْ يكونَ جزماً ، أو قائماً بهِ ، أو محاذياً له ، أو في جهةٍ له ، أو مرتسماً في خيالِهِ ؛ لأنَّ ذلكَ كلَّه يُوجِبُ مماثلتَهُ للحوادثِ ، فيجب له ما وجبَ لها ! وذلك يقدح في وجوب قدمِهِ وبقائِهِ ، بل وفي كل وصف مِنْ أوصاف ألوهيته .
The attribute of being unlike originated things (mukhālafat al-ḥawādith). And from here you also learn the necessity of declaring Him exalted above being a part (juz'), or something subsisting in Him (qā'im bihi), or adjacent to Him (muḥādhiyan lahu), or in a direction (jihat) for Him, or being depicted in His imagination (murtasiman fī khayālihi); because all that necessitates His similarity (mumāthala) to originated things, and then what is necessary for them would be necessary for Him! And that detracts from the necessity of His pre-eternity and permanence, nay, from every attribute of His divinity.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a proof. Sanusi builds on the previous argument to establish the attribute of being unlike creation (mukhālafat al-ḥawādith). He lists five things that God must be free from: being a part (juz'), being an accident subsisting in something (qā'im bihi), being adjacent (muḥādhi), being in a direction (jihat), and being picturable in imagination (murtasim). All these would imply similarity (mumāthala) to created beings. If similarity were true, then whatever applies to created beings would apply to God, which contradicts His necessary pre-eternity (qidam) and permanence (baqā'), and ultimately all divine attributes.

Key Terms
تنزيه (tanzīh)
مماثلة (mumāthala)
الحوادث (al-ḥawādith)
جزء (juz')
قائم به (qā'im bihi)
محاذ (muḥādhi)
جهة (jihat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'unlike creation' is merely a negative attribute with no positive content.
Correction: Sanusi shows that mukhalafa is positively proven by the impossibility of similarity; it is a necessary consequence of God's eternal nature.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for God to be in a direction?
A) Directions are relative and change.
B) Being in a direction would make Him a body.
C) It would necessitate similarity to created things.
D) The Qur'an does not mention directions.

Key Takeaway
God's transcendence is not just an absence but a necessary consequence of His eternal nature; any similarity would destroy His divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God, do you ever unconsciously picture a form or location in your mind? How does Sanusi's proof expose that as a dangerous similarity? Today, consciously replace every mental image with the affirmation that He is unlike anything you can imagine.
?
You affirm that God is not like His creation. But do your actions show that you truly believe He is incomparable? Do you treat His commands as if He is limited or petty? Repent and resolve to obey Him as the Absolute Transcendent Lord.
?
Sanusi warns that any similarity between God and creation would destroy His divinity. Have you ever described God in a way that reduces Him to something worldly? Write down one such statement you've made or heard, and replace it with a proper declaration of His transcendence.

Proof that the Creator Cannot Be a Nature or Necessary Cause
Sanusi argues that the Creator cannot be a nature or necessary cause and refutes the objection of impediment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that attributing a delay to an impediment leads to impossibility, thereby proving that the Creator cannot be a nature or necessitating cause.

Core Idea
A nature that acts by necessity cannot delay its effect without an infinite regress.

Sanusi has already established that the Creator cannot be subject to temporal attributes. Now he extends this reasoning to show that the Creator cannot be a 'nature' or a 'necessitating cause' that acts by blind necessity. Would a delay in the effect of such a nature be possible? Sanusi anticipates an objection and refutes it with a powerful argument that leads to either the non-existence of the Eternal or an infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Understand the previous conclusion
Sanusi begins by stating a conclusion from earlier: that something cannot be characterized by a temporal attribute or an accident in place of its permissible opposites, because that would imply either the attribute is eternal or the non-existence of the Creator continues—both impossible.
This sets the foundation for the new argument.
2
Follow the deduction about the Creator
From that impossibility, Sanusi directly concludes that the Creator cannot be a nature or a necessitating cause. Notice the logical link: a nature or necessitating cause would require temporal attributes, which have been ruled out.
This is a crucial step in Islamic theology regarding divine causality.
3
Analyze the objection and refutation
Sanusi presents an objection: perhaps the delay in the effect of a nature is due to an impediment or missing condition. Then he refutes it by showing that the same argument would apply to that impediment or condition, leading to either the non-existence of the Eternal (God would not be always the Creator) or an infinite regress, both of which are impossible.
This refutation strengthens the original conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا صفة ولا زمن بدلاً عن نقائضها الجائزة ، فيلزم إمَّا قدمُكَ ، أو استمرار عدمِكَ ، وهما محالانِ . ومِنْ هنا تعلم استحالة كون الصانع طبيعة أو عِلَّةً مُوجِبةً . فإن أُجيب عن التأخُرِ في الطبيعة بالمانع أو فواتِ الشرط .. لزم عدم القديم أو التسلسل ؛ لنقل الكلام إلى ذلك المانع وذلك الشرط .
And neither attribute nor time instead of its permissible opposites, so either your eternity [is necessitated] or the continuation of your non-existence, both of which are impossible. And from this you learn the impossibility of the Maker being a nature or a necessitating cause. For if it is answered concerning the delay in nature by an impediment or the absence of a condition, the non-existence of the eternal or infinite regress would follow, because the discussion would be transferred to that impediment and that condition.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a proof and an objection-and-answer. Sanusi first concludes from a previous argument that the Creator cannot be characterized by a temporal attribute or an accident in place of their permissible opposites, otherwise either the attribute would be eternal or the non-existence of the Creator would continue—both impossible. From this, he deduces the impossibility of the Creator being a nature or a necessitating cause (ʿilla mūjiba). Then he presents an objection: perhaps the delay in the effect of a nature is due to an impediment or lack of a condition. He refutes this by showing that if one posits an impediment or condition for the delay, the same argument applies to that impediment or condition, leading to either the non-existence of the eternal (i.e., God would not have always been the Creator) or an infinite regress of impediments and conditions. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
طبيعة
علة موجبة
المانع
التسلسل
القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a delay caused by an impediment would preserve the possibility of the Creator being a nature.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the impediment itself leads to an infinite regress or the non-existence of the Eternal, making the nature explanation impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what follows if one attributes the delay in the effect of a nature to an impediment?
A) The Creator must be eternal.
B) The impediment itself is impossible.
C) It leads to non-existence of the eternal or infinite regress.
D) The nature becomes self-sufficient.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi proves that the Creator cannot be a nature or necessitating cause because any purported hindrance leads to an infinite regress or the non-existence of the Eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on cause and effect every day – how does this argument challenge the assumption that God's action is like a natural cause?
?
When you think of God's will, are you tempted to see it as a blind necessity? How does Sanusi's argument expose that error?
?
If God were a necessary cause, you would be nothing more than a necessary effect – how does that feel, and why is it impossible?

Divine Transcendence

Why God Has No Obligations, Injustice, or Purposes
Sanusi proves impossibility of obligation, injustice, and purpose for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments proving that obligation, injustice, and purpose in action are impossible for Allah, affirming His absolute sovereignty and perfection.

Core Idea
God is above obligation, injustice, and purpose—He acts from absolute freedom.

In this passage, Sanusi makes a crucial move in establishing divine transcendence: he shows that Allah cannot be subject to obligation (wujub) or injustice (zulm), and therefore His actions cannot be for any purpose (gharad). These claims are not arbitrary; they are grounded in rational proofs that protect tawhid from anthropomorphic limitations. Let’s explore how he builds his case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style; each clause carries a proof.
2
Identify the two impossibilities
The passage first states that obligation and injustice are impossible for God. Find the reasons given for each impossibility.
Obligation would require disobedience; injustice would require acting improperly.
3
Understand the purpose argument
Sanusi deduces that God's actions cannot be for a purpose. Follow his reasoning: if there were a purpose, it would either oblige God or make Him subjugated—both impossible.
The Quranic verse (28:68) is cited to reinforce the point.
4
Examine the additional argument
The passage ends with a separate argument about the eternality of purpose and action. Understand why this leads to a contradiction.
This refers to an earlier discussion (referenced as 'it has already passed').
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why these concepts are essential for a proper understanding of God's attributes and actions.
They safeguard God's transcendence and absolute independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالوجوب إذا والظلمُ عليهِ محالانِ ؛ إذ الوجوب يستدعي تعاصي بعض الممكناتِ ، والظلم يستدعي التصرُّفَ على خلاف ما ينبغي . ومِنْ هنا استحالَ أنْ يكونَ فعلهُ تعالى لغرض ؛ لأنَّهُ لو كانَ لهُ غرضُ في الفعل لأوجبَهُ عليهِ ، وإلا لم يكنْ علَّةٌ لهُ ، فيكونُ مقهوراً ، كيف وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيَختار الله [ القصص : ٦٨] ؟! وأيضاً : فالغرضُ إِمَّا قديمٌ فيلزم قدم الفعل ، وقد مرّ
Thus obligation and injustice are impossible for Him. For obligation necessitates the disobedience of some possible things, and injustice necessitates acting contrary to what is fitting. Hence it is impossible for His action to be for a purpose. For if He had a purpose in the action, it would necessitate it upon Him, otherwise it would not be a cause for Him, and He would be subjugated. How (can that be) when 'your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' (28:68)? Also, the purpose is either eternal, necessitating the eternalness of the action, and it has already passed.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a proof that obligation (wujub) and injustice (zulm) are impossible for God. It first argues: obligation would require that some possible things disobey God, which is impossible since nothing disobeys Him; injustice would mean acting improperly, which is impossible due to divine perfection. From this it deduces that God's actions cannot be for a purpose (gharad). Reasoning: if there were a purpose, it would either oblige God to act (so He would be under obligation) or He would not be the true cause, making Him subjugated. Both are impossible. Then cites Quran 28:68 to affirm God's absolute freedom in creation and choice. Finally, it adds another argument: if the purpose were eternal, the action would be eternal, which is false (and the discussion of that point is said to have already passed).

Key Terms
وجوب
ظلم
غرض
محال
مقهور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's actions are for a purpose like human actions.
Correction: God's actions are not for a purpose because that would impose obligation or subjugation, contradicting His absolute sovereignty.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for God's action to be for a purpose according to this passage?
A) Because purposes are temporal
B) Because it would either necessitate an obligation on God or make Him subjugated
C) Because God has no will
D) Because actions are eternal

Key Takeaway
God's absolute perfection and sovereignty mean He is free from obligation, injustice, and purpose—all of which would imply limitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is free from all obligation toward you, does your prayer sometimes feel like you are trying to 'obligate' Him to answer? What does this lesson teach you about the need to approach Him with pure gratitude and dependence, not expectations?
?
When life feels unfair, have you ever accused God of injustice in your heart? How does the truth that injustice is impossible for God demand that you surrender your limited perspective and trust His perfect wisdom?
?
You act for purposes every day—to earn, to please, to achieve. But God acts without any purpose outside Himself. What does this say about your need to align your purposes with His will, rather than demanding that He serve your purposes?

God's Transcendence and the Nature of Reward and Punishment
Sanusi describes God's transcendence and the non-purposeful nature of reward and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's attributes are perfect and unchanged, and that reward and punishment are solely by His will without any obligation or need.

Core Idea
God's actions are perfect and do not affect His essence; reward is pure favor, punishment is pure justice.

Sanusi here summarizes key principles of Islamic theology regarding God's transcendence. He affirms that God's power, knowledge, and will are perfect and unchangeable, and that He existed alone before creation. Then he explains the nature of reward and punishment, emphasizing that they are purely by God's will without any external cause. This passage helps the student understand that God is in no need of creation and acts freely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Notice the flow from divine attributes to reward and punishment.
The passage is dense but each sentence builds on the previous.
2
Identify the three attributes
The passage starts by mentioning God's power, knowledge, and will. Notice that these are described as 'bāhir' (manifest) and that no perfection or deficiency attaches to His essence from them.
3
Grasp the concept of immutability
The phrase 'God existed with nothing, and He is now as He was' affirms God's eternal existence and absolute changelessness. This is a core Ash‘arī belief.
4
Analyze reward and punishment
Reward is given purely by God's favor, not due to any inclination or obligation. Punishment is just, not vengeful. Both are signs of His sovereignty and the submission of all contingents to His will.
Note the negations: 'lā limaylin ilayhi' and 'lā lishifā'i ghayz'.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Each event occurs as it should, according to God's knowledge and will, without adding any perfection or deficiency to Him. This reinforces His absolute self-sufficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على باهر قدرته جل وعز وسعة علمه ونفوذ إرادته. لا يتطرق لذاته العلية من ذلك كمال ولا نقص. كان الله ولا شيء معه، وهو الآن على ما كان عليه، فأكرم سبحانه من شاء بما لا يكيف من أنواع النعيم بمجرد فضله، لا لميل إليه، أو قضاء حق وجب له عليه، وعدل فيمن شاء بما لا يطاق وصفه من أصناف الجحيم، لا لإشفاء غيظ ولا لضرر ناله من قبله. وكلا النوعين دال على سعة ملكه، وانقياد جميع الممكنات لإرادته، وعدم تعاصيها على باهر قدرته، كل منها واقع على ما ينبغي من جريه على وفق علمه وإرادته، من غير أن يتجدد له بذلك كمال أو نقص حالاً ولا مالاً
Upon His manifest power, glory and majesty, and the vastness of His knowledge and the execution of His will. No perfection or deficiency from that applies to His lofty essence. God existed and nothing with Him, and He is now as He was. So He honored, glory to Him, whom He wills with types of bliss beyond description, sheerly by His favor, not from inclination towards it, nor fulfilling a right that was due upon Him; and He is just towards whom He wills with unbearable varieties of torment, not from venting anger nor from harm He received from them. And both types indicate the vastness of His kingdom, the submission of all contingents to His will, and their inability to resist His manifest power. Each occurs as it should, running in accordance with His knowledge and will, without any perfection or deficiency accruing to Him thereby, in state or property.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a description of God's transcendence and the nature of reward and punishment. It begins by affirming God's attributes of power, knowledge, and will. Then it states that these actions do not affect His essence. Then it affirms the eternal existence of God and His immutability. Then it explains that reward is purely by His favor, not due to any inclination or obligation, and punishment is just, not due to revenge. Then it notes that both reward and punishment demonstrate His sovereignty and the obedience of all possible things to His will. Finally, it emphasizes that these acts occur according to His knowledge and will without adding perfection or deficiency to Him.

Key Terms
قدرة
علم
إرادة
نعيم
جحيم
فضل
عدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think reward is a right due to the creature or that punishment is vengeful.
Correction: The passage clarifies reward is sheer favor without obligation, and punishment is just without revenge.

Checkpoint
According to the text, reward from God is?
A) A right due to the creature
B) Based on God's inclination towards the creature
C) Sheerly by God's favor without any obligation
D) A result of God's need

Key Takeaway
God's actions are free from any need or deficiency; reward is pure favor, punishment is pure justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself feeling entitled to God's reward, as if He owes you? How does this passage's statement that reward is 'sheerly by His favor, not ... fulfilling a right' cut against that sense of entitlement? What would it look like to live in constant awareness that every blessing is an undeserved gift?
?
When you face hardship or see others suffering, do you secretly wonder if God is angry or vengeful? This passage says punishment is not from 'venturing anger' or 'harm He received.' Can you truly accept that God's justice is untouched by emotion or reaction? How might that change how you respond to trials?
?
If God is exactly as He was before creation—unchanged and unaffected—then your obedience or sin does not alter His essence. Does that thought free you or unsettle you? Are you willing to worship a God who is not made more perfect by your devotion, or do you secretly hope your good deeds add something to Him?

Corrupt Beliefs of the Common People
Sanusi enumerates false beliefs of the laity and their juristic status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the false beliefs commonly held by the laity and the scholarly consensus regarding their status.

Core Idea
Common people often hold beliefs that contradict sound Islamic creed.

In this passage, the author warns about the spread of corrupt beliefs among those who neglect the company of scholars. He lists several erroneous doctrines that are widespread among the common folk. Some of these are deemed kufr by consensus, while others are disputed. This lesson examines those beliefs and their gravity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage describing the common people's beliefs once without analyzing. Notice the list of beliefs mentioned.
2
Identify each false belief
Go through each item listed: corporealism, direction, natural causality, God acting for a purpose, His speech being letters/sound, and Him speaking/silence like humans.
These are all deviations from orthodox Ash'ari theology.
3
Understand the consensus and disagreement
Note that some of these beliefs are unanimously considered kufr, while others are subject to difference. Think about why that might be.
The passage does not specify which are which, but the commentary can help.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما العامَّةُ : فأكثرهم ممَّنْ لا يعتني بحضور مجالس العلماء ومخالطة أهل الخير ، نتحقَّقُ منهم اعتقاد التجسيم والجهة ، وتأثير الطبيعة ، وكون فعل الله تعالى لغرض ، وكون كلامه جلَّ وعلا حرفاً وصوتاً ، ومَرَّةً يتكلَّمُ ومَرَّةً يسكت كسائر البشر ، ونحوَ ذلكَ اعتقادات أهل الباطل ، وبعضُ اعتقاداتهم أجمع العلماء على كفْرِ معتقدها ، وبعضُها اختلفوا فيه . وكثيرٌ مِنْ أهل البادية ينكرُ البعثَ ، ولقد أخبرني بعضُ مَنْ أَثْقُ بهِ أنَّه سمع ذلك صريحاً منهم ، قال : وبعضُهم ممَّنْ يحفظ لفظ القرآن : ولقد حكى لي بعضُ أصحابنا مثلَ ذلكَ عمَّنْ لا يُظَنُّ به ذلكَ ممَّنْ يتعاطى العلمَ بتِلِمْسانَ ، وله أصل في رئاسة العلم ، قال : وصَرَّحَ لي بأنَّ رأيه وعقيدته ـ والعياذ بالله منهُ ومِنْ عقيدته - نفي المعادِ البدني ، كرأي الفلاسفة أبعدهم الله تعالى وأخلى منهمُ الأَرضَ . قالَ : وجادلتُهُ في ذلكَ مِراراً ، فطبع على قلبه ولم يقبل ! وأظنُّ أنَّ المصيبة جاءَتِ الرجلَ مِنْ مطالعتِهِ بعض كتب الفلاسفة قبل إتقانِ علم التوحيدِ على شيخ عارف
As for the common people: most of them are among those who do not attend the gatherings of scholars or mix with the people of goodness. We ascertain from them the belief in corporealism (tajsīm) and direction (jiha), the influence of nature (ṭabīʿa), that the action of Allah Most High is for a purpose (gharaḍ), that His speech (kalām) is letter and sound, that He speaks at times and is silent at times like other humans, and similar beliefs of the people of falsehood. Some of their beliefs are such that the scholars are unanimous on the disbelief (kufr) of their holder, and some they differ about. And many of the Bedouins deny the Resurrection (al-baʿth). Indeed, someone I trust informed me that he heard that explicitly from them. He said: Some of them are among those who memorize the wording of the Quran. And one of our companions told me something similar about someone whom one would not suspect of that, among those who practice learning in Tlemcen, and he has a foundation in the leadership of learning. He said: He declared to me that his opinion and belief — and may Allah protect us from him and his belief — is the denial of bodily resurrection (maʿād badanī), like the opinion of the philosophers, may Allah distance them and empty the earth of them. He said: I debated him repeatedly on that, but his heart was sealed and he did not accept. And I think that the calamity came to the man from his reading some books of the philosophers before mastering the science of tawḥīd under a knowledgeable shaykh.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage describes the corrupt beliefs of the common people and recounts a specific case of a scholar who denied bodily resurrection. The author first lists the false beliefs common among the laity: corporealism, direction, natural causality, that God acts for a purpose, that His speech consists of letters and sounds, and that He speaks and is silent like humans. Some of these beliefs are considered kufr by consensus, while others are disputed. Then he mentions that many Bedouins deny the Resurrection. He then narrates an account from a trusted source about a scholar in Tlemcen who openly denied bodily resurrection, adopting the view of the philosophers. The author attributes this deviation to the scholar's premature study of philosophy before properly learning tawḥīd from a qualified teacher.

Key Terms
العامَّة
التجسيم
الجهة
تأثير الطبيعة
كلام الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That common people's corrupt beliefs are all equally condemned as disbelief.
Correction: The passage indicates that some beliefs are unanimously kufr, while others are differed upon, so not all are at the same level.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT mentioned as a belief of the common people?
A) Corporealism
B) Direction
C) Predestination
D) Influence of nature

Key Takeaway
Common people often hold erroneous beliefs about Allah and His attributes; some of these beliefs are unanimously kufr, others are disputed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself believing that Allah is in a particular direction or that He has a body, even unconsciously? What will you do to purify your creed after this lesson?
?
If you neglect attending lessons of theology, aren't you risking falling into the very errors Sanusi warns about? Commit today to a consistent study of ʿaqīda.
?
When you hear someone claim that Allah's speech is like human speech, will you have the courage to correct them? How will you use the knowledge of this passage to protect your faith and others?

A Scholar Denies Bodily Resurrection
Sanusi narrates an incident of a scholar denying bodily resurrection due to philosophical influence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the danger of studying philosophy before mastering Islamic creed, as illustrated by a scholar who denied bodily resurrection.

Core Idea
Philosophy without sound creed leads to heresy.

The author shares a cautionary tale about a scholar in Tlemcen who openly denied bodily resurrection, adopting the view of the philosophers. The author attributes this deviation to his reading philosophical works before properly learning tawḥīd from a qualified teacher. This highlights the importance of grounding oneself in Islamic theology before engaging with other disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote

2
Identify the scholar's error
The scholar denied bodily resurrection, a core Islamic belief, and was known for his learning. Reflect on why someone knowledgeable could fall into such error.
The author blames his reading of philosophy before mastering tawheed.
3
Extract the lesson
The key takeaway is the importance of proper prioritization in religious education. Philosophy is not inherently evil, but it must be approached after firm grounding in creed.

Sanusi's Refutation of Nature as Cause of the Self
Sanusi refutes the claim that nature (tab') is present in the existence of the self.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses a reductio ad absurdum to refute the claim that nature (ṭabʿ) is the cause of the self's existence, based on the opponents' own principle that a uniform nature produces a uniform spherical shape.

Core Idea
If nature caused your self, you would be a sphere.

This passage presents a crisp logical refutation. Sanusi quotes a statement: 'there is no nature in the existence of your self, otherwise you would be spherical.' He then explains that this is a necessary consequence (ilzām) based on the opponents' own belief that a nature equal from all aspects necessitates a spherical shape in composite things. The argument shows that since the self is not spherical, it cannot be caused by nature — and by extension, not by any cause (ʿilla). We will walk through the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the passage is a refutation (ilzām). The author quotes a statement and then unpacks the reasoning.
The key is to see how the opponents' own principle is turned against them.
2
Understand the opponents' principle
The opponents believe that a nature that is uniform in all respects produces a shape that is uniform in all respects, which is spherical. They use this to explain the celestial sphere.
This principle is the foundation of the reductio.
3
Apply the principle to the self
If the self were caused by a uniform nature, it would be spherical. But it is not. Therefore, nature is not the cause. The argument concludes that if nature is negated, cause (ʿilla) is even more soundly negated.
The term 'cause' here refers to a more deterministic cause, nature being a weaker cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأيضاً لا طبعَ لها في وجودِ ذاتِكَ، وإلا لكنتَ على شكل الكرةِ ) هذا الزام على مذهب الخصوم؛ فإنَّهم يقولون : إنَّ الطبيعة المتساوية مِنْ كلّ وجه تقتضي شكلاً متساوياً مِنْ كل وجه، وهو الكُرِيُّ في المركَّباتِ، ولذلك زعموا أنَّ جوهرَ الفَلكِ لمَّا كانَ طبيعةً واحدةً كان كُرِيَّاً، وإذا انتفى الطبع لها فأحرى العِلَّةُ .
His saying: 'And also there is no nature (ṭabʿ) for it in the existence of your self, otherwise you would be on the shape of the sphere.' This is a necessary consequence (ilzām) according to the doctrine of the opponents; for they say: nature that is equal from every aspect necessitates a shape equal from every aspect, which is the spherical in composite things. Therefore they claimed that the substance of the celestial sphere, since it was a single nature, was spherical. And if nature is negated for it, then even more so cause (ʿilla).
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (ilzām) of the opponents' view that the self's existence is due to nature. The author quotes a statement: 'there is no nature in the existence of your self, otherwise you would be spherical.' The argument is based on the opponents' own principle: a nature that is uniform in all respects would produce a uniform shape, i.e., spherical in composite bodies. They use this to explain why the celestial sphere is spherical—because its substance has a single nature. Hence, if the human self were due to a nature, it would also be spherical, which it is not. Therefore, nature is not the cause. If nature is denied, then cause (ʿilla) is even more so denied, since cause is even more deterministic.

Key Terms
طبع (ṭabʿ)
كرة (kura)
الزام (ilzām)
جوهر الفلك (jawhar al-falak)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that humans have any spherical aspect at all.
Correction: The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if nature were the cause, the entire self would have to be perfectly spherical like a sphere. It does not deny that some parts of the body are round; it's about the self's form as a whole.

Checkpoint
Why would the self be spherical if nature were its cause, according to this argument?
A) Because nature is always spherical
B) Because a uniform nature necessitates a uniform shape, which is spherical
C) Because the celestial sphere is spherical
D) Because the self is composite

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes nature as a cause for the self by showing it would absurdly make the self spherical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly believed that your existence or your personality is simply 'the way you are'—a fixed nature? This passage denies that nature is the cause of your self. If your self is not caused by its own nature, then what is the source of your being? Will you today attribute every breath not to your own nature but to the One who creates you anew each moment?
?
Look at one persistent flaw in your character—anger, jealousy, or laziness. If that flaw were simply part of your nature, wouldn't it be uniform and predictable, like a sphere? But it is not uniform; it varies with your choices and circumstances. What does that inconsistency tell you about the real cause of your self? Are you willing to take responsibility for changing that flaw, knowing that your self is not determined by a fixed nature?
?
The argument ends with 'if nature is negated, then cause is even more negated.' This means nothing in your self—not your talents, not your weaknesses—is self-caused. Everything points to a Cause beyond you. Does that realization humble you? Can you commit to spending one minute today thanking the true Cause for even the smallest aspect of your existence?

The Contingency of the World Necessitates Its Glorification of God
Sanusi explains that the world's contingency necessitates the glorification of God by all things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the contingency and incapability of the world require that everything glorifies God, and how this relates to the necessity that the Creator is not a part of creation.

Core Idea
The world's very existence proclaims its need for Allah.

This passage from Sanusi connects two theological truths: the world’s contingency (hadath) and its incapability (ʿajz). From these, he argues that everything in creation—whether animate or inanimate—glorifies its Creator. The passage also clarifies that the Maker must be utterly different from His creation, for otherwise He would share its incapability. We will explore how these ideas fit together.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and its Translation
Begin by reading the Arabic original and its literal English translation. Notice how the author unfolds the argument step by step.
2
Identify the Key Premises
The passage relies on two premises: (a) the world is originated (huduth) and (b) all parts of the world are incapable (ʿajz). Underline these in the text.
3
Understand the Two Conclusions
These premises lead to two conclusions: (i) the Maker is not a body or accident, and (ii) everything glorifies God by its state or speech. See how the second conclusion flows from the first.
4
Reflect on 'Tongue of State' vs. 'Tongue of Speech'
Consider how inanimate objects glorify God without speech—through their very contingency and neediness. This deepens our appreciation of divine transcendence.
5
Connect to Broader Theology
This passage underlies the Islamic concept that all creation bears witness to its Creator. Think of how this relates to the verse 'There is not a thing except it glorifies His praise.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولُهُ : ( وأنَّ جميعَهُ عاجز ) يعني : ومِنْ هذا المعنى وجبَ أنْ يكون صانع العالم ليسَ شيئاً منهُ ؛ لوجوب عموم العجز لجميعِه ، فلا يكون فاعلُهُ جِرْماً ولا قائماً بهِ ، وإلا لعجز كعجزه ، وسيأتي لذلك مزيد بيان إن شاء الله تعالى (۱) أيَّ قوله : ( وإنْ مِنْ شيءٍ إلا يُسبِّحُ بحمدِهِ ) يعني : لمَّا وجبَ الحدوث للعالم ؛ وهو كلُّ ما سوى الله جل وعلا ، ووجبَ عجزُ عموماً عنِ التأثيرِ في شيءٍ ، أي شيءٍ كانَ ، وكانَتِ الدلالةُ جميعه على ذلكَ مِنْ جهةِ فطرتِهِ .. صارَ كلُّ جزءٍ مِنْ أجزائِهِ وكلُّ صفةٍ مِنْ صفاته ينبئ بعظيم افتقاره إلى مبدع له غاية الكمال ، ويُثني على ذاتِهِ العليَّةِ وصفاتِهِ الكاملة بلسان الحال أو بلسان المقال ، ويعترفُ بالعجزِ عن الإدراك والشكرِ لِمَنْ تَحيَّرَتِ العقولُ في كُنْهِ جَلالِهِ ، وتَنَزَّهَ أَنْ يكونَ لهُ مِنْ جميعِ ما يُتخيَّلُ مثال ، تباركَ اللهُ ربُّ العالمينَ
And for Him: (And that all of it is incapable) meaning: And from this meaning it is necessary that the Maker of the world is not something from it; due to the necessity of the generality of incapability for all of it, so its agent is not a body nor subsistent in it, otherwise it would be incapable like its incapability, and more clarification will come for that if God wills. That is: His saying: (And there is not a thing except it glorifies [Him with] His praise) means: When the origination (huduth) of the world was necessary—and it is everything other than God Almighty—and the generality of incapability from affecting anything, whatever it may be, was necessary, and the indication of all of it to that from the aspect of its innate disposition... every part of its parts and every attribute of its attributes became proclaiming its great need for an Originator of utmost perfection, and praising His lofty Essence and His perfect attributes by the tongue of state or by the tongue of speech, and acknowledging incapability of perceiving and thanking Him whose intellects are bewildered in the depth of His majesty, and He is exalted from having any likeness from all that can be imagined, blessed be God, the Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage explains the comment 'And that all of it is incapable' from the earlier text. It states that from the meaning of the world's contingency (huduth) it is necessary that the Maker of the world is not a part of it, because all parts of the world are incapable, and a body or an accident is incapable. Then it moves to explain the verse 'There is not a thing except it glorifies Him with His praise.' The author explains that since the world is contingent and every part is incapable, everything in the world indicates its need for the Creator. Therefore, everything glorifies God either by its state (bi-lisan al-hal) or by speech (bi-lisan al-maqal), acknowledging its incapability. The passage concludes with praising God.

Key Terms
عجز
حدوث
تسبيح
لسان الحال
لسان المقال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only living beings can glorify God.
Correction: The author clarifies that inanimate things also glorify God by their very state of contingency and need, which is called the 'tongue of state'.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does everything in the world glorify God?
A) Because it is created with the ability to speak.
B) Because its contingency and incapability point to its need for the Creator.
C) Because it is commanded to do so in the Qur'an.
D) Because God is present in everything.

Key Takeaway
Everything in creation, by its very contingency and need, proclaims the perfection of its Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe your own weaknesses, needs, and limits, do you resent them or do they drive you to proclaim your utter dependence on Allah? How would your daily attitude change if you saw every limitation as a silent 'subhanAllah'?
?
If everything in the universe, from atoms to galaxies, glorifies Allah by its very existence, what does your own existence say? When no one is watching, does your life—your choices, your priorities—declare the same glorification that your tongue claims on Friday?
?
The passage says that even the tongue of state acknowledges the inability to truly perceive or thank Allah. Are you content with that inability, or do you try to reduce Allah to something you can grasp? What would it take for you to stand in awe before His majesty and simply say, 'I cannot comprehend You, but I affirm Your perfection'?

The Inapplicability of Time to God
Sanusi defines time as a property of contingent things and proves it cannot apply to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why time, as commonly understood, is a property of contingent things and cannot be attributed to God.

Core Idea
Time is tied to motion and change; God is beyond both.

Sanusi now addresses a critical question: does time apply to God? Many people casually speak of 'God before time' or 'God in eternity', but this passage clarifies that the time we experience is intrinsically linked to celestial motion and change. Since God is transcendent and unchanging, He cannot be in time. The following passage lays out the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi begins by referring to time in customary usage.
This is a single continuous argument, so read it as a whole.
2
Identify the two considerations of time
The passage distinguishes between time as commonly understood and the absence of time in pre-eternity (al-azal). Follow how Sanusi proves time requires spheres and motion.
3
Understand the conclusion
Sanusi concludes that time is exclusively a property of contingent things (hawadith). Ask yourself: what does this imply for God's relationship to time?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو الموجود كثيراً في تعارفِ أهل العاداتِ ، ولا شك في انعدام الزمانِ بهذا المعنى أيضاً في الأزلِ ؛ إذْ لا فَلَكَ فيهِ ولا حركة ؛ لما عرفت مِنْ برهان حدوثِ كلٌّ ما سوى اللهِ جَلَّ وعلا
ويستحيلُ أنْ يمرَّ عليهِ جلَّ وعلا الزمانُ بهذا المعنى ؛ لأنَّهُ إِنَّما يمرُّ على الأفلاكِ وما أحاطَتْ بهِ ممَّا سُجِنَ في جوفها ، حتى تمر عليه الأزمنةُ مِنَ الساعاتِ والليل والنهار وفصول السنة وأشهرها بحسَبِ تحرُّكِ الأفلاكِ فوقه وتحته ، وظهور الشمس وارتفاعها فوق الأُفُقِ وغَيبتها وانخفاضها تحت الأفقِ ؛ لتتقيّد بذلك أعراضُهُ المتجدّدةُ ؛ مِنْ يقظة ونوم، وصحة وسقم ، وحياةٍ وموتٍ ، ونحو ذلك ، وتتقيّد معايشُهُ المقدَّرة خريفاً وصيفاً وربيعاً وشتاءً بتدبيرِ مَنْ ليس كمثلِهِ شيء ، لا إله إلا هو ربُّ كلِّ شيءٍ تبارك وتعالى ، ومَنْ تنزَّهَ أَنْ تحيط به الأمكنة ، أو تتجدد أو تتغيَّرَ له صفةٌ .. كيف يتصوَّرُ أنْ يكونَ لهُ معَ شيءٍ مِنَ العالم اتصال أو انفصال ؟!
فقد اتضحَ لكَ : أنَّ الزمانَ على كلا الاعتبارينِ إِنَّما هو مِنْ صفاتِ الحوادثِ ، ولا يتقيَّدُ به إلا ما هو حادث ، فالقدم إذاً باعتباره خاص بالحوادث
It is that which exists frequently in the customary usage of people, and there is no doubt about the non-existence of time (az-zaman) in this sense in pre-eternity (al-azal), because there is no sphere and no motion in it, as you have known from the proof of the contingency (huduth) of everything other than Allah, Glorified and Exalted. And it is impossible for time in this sense to pass over Him, Glorified and Exalted, because it only passes over the spheres and what surrounds them of what is enclosed in their interiors, such that times pass over them from hours, night and day, seasons of the year and months according to the movement of the spheres above and below them, and the rising of the sun and its elevation above the horizon, and its setting and its descent below the horizon, so that its changing attributes become determined by that: from wakefulness and sleep, health and sickness, life and death, and the like, and its apportioned livelihoods become determined as autumn, summer, spring, and winter, by the management of Him Whose like is nothing, there is no god but He, the Lord of everything, Blessed and Exalted, and Who is transcendent beyond being encompassed by places, or having a attribute that is renewed or changed... How could it be conceived that He has connection or disconnection with anything of the world?! Thus it has become clear to you: that time, according to both considerations, is only from the attributes of contingent things, and nothing is determined by it except what is contingent. So al-qidam (the past) in that respect is specific to contingent things.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of time (zaman) and demonstrates that it cannot apply to God. It begins by referring to time as commonly understood in customary usage, but immediately points out that time in this sense did not exist in pre-eternity (al-azal) because there were no celestial spheres (aflak) and no motion—since everything other than God is proven contingent. This establishes that time is tied to physical motion. The author then argues that it is impossible for time to pass over God, because time only passes over spheres and their contents, which undergo continuous change (waking, sleeping, seasons, etc.). Such changes are determined by the sun's rising and setting, etc. God, however, is transcendent beyond being encompassed by places or undergoing any change. The passage concludes that time is exclusively a property of contingent things (hawadith), and the 'past' (al-qidam) in that sense is also specific to contingencies—implying that God cannot be described by any temporal attribute. This prepares the ground for the next discussion of the other meaning of qidam as divine eternity.

Key Terms
الزمان (az-zaman)
الأزل (al-azal)
الأفلاك (al-aflak)
الحوادث (al-hawadith)
القدم (al-qidam as past)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since God is eternal, time must apply to Him.
Correction: The passage clarifies that time only applies to changing, contingent things; God's eternity is not temporal.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does time not exist in pre-eternity (al-azal)?
A) Because pre-eternity is infinite and time is finite.
B) Because there were no spheres and no motion in pre-eternity.
C) Because time only exists for created beings, not for God.
D) Because God created time after the world.

Key Takeaway
Time, as defined by celestial motion, is exclusive to contingent beings and cannot describe God.

?
You measure your life by hours, seasons, and years. If you truly believed that Allah is beyond all this, would you still cling to your own timelines and plans? What would it look like to surrender your schedule to the One who transcends time?
?
This passage argues that only changing, contingent things are subject to time. Your soul is contingent—it is passing through time. How does that urgency affect your relationship with your Creator today? What specific change can you make before the time runs out?

Divine Transcendence

God's Necessary Transcendence from Resembling Creation
Sanusi argues for God's necessary transcendence from body, place, direction, and imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that Allah is absolutely unlike contingent beings, and why attributes like being a body, occupying space, or being confined to a direction are impossible for Him.

Core Idea
If God resembled creation, He would be contingent — so He must be absolutely transcendent.

Sanusi now builds on the foundation of God's necessary pre-existence and everlastingness to derive a core theological truth: Allah is absolutely unlike His creation. This passage shows how any attribute that would imply similarity to originated things — like being a body, occupying space, or being confined to a direction — is rationally impossible for God. Let's follow the logical chain that leads to this conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the list of attributes that Sanusi negates of God.
The passage begins with 'From this you also learn...' — the 'this' refers to the previous lesson on God's pre-existence and everlastingness.
2
Identify the five impossible attributes
Sanusi lists five things that God is necessarily transcendent from: being a body (jirm), having something subsisting in Him, facing Him, being in a direction, or being depicted in imagination. Find each in the text.
3
Understand the argument
The argument links these attributes to similarity with contingent beings. If God had any of these, He would be like originated things, and thus would be contingent — but He is necessarily existent.
The key phrase is 'because all of that necessitates His similarity to contingent beings'.
4
Explore the definition of jirm
Sanusi defines jirm as a magnitude occupying void, which includes the indivisible atom and the body. He notes that jirm is inseparable from motion or rest (accidents), which are originated.
This shows why being a body is particularly problematic — it would make God composed of originated atoms and accidents.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why any similarity to contingent beings would destroy the necessity of God's existence and His attributes of divinity. How does this reinforce Tawheed?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هنا تعلم أيضاً وجوب تنزُّهِهِ تعالى أنْ يكونَ جزماً ، أو قائماً بهِ ، أو محاذياً لهُ ، أو في جهةٍ له ، أو مرتسماً في خيالِهِ ؛ لأنَّ ذلكَ كلَّه يُوجِبُ مماثلتَهُ للحوادثِ ، فيجب له ما وجب لها ! وذلك يقدح في وجوب قدمِهِ وبقائِهِ ، بل وفي كلِّ وصفِ مِنْ أوصاف ألوهيته . يعني : أنَّكَ إذا علمْتَ وجوبَ وجودِهِ جل وعلا ، وأَنَّهُ لا يقبلُ العدم السابق لوجوبِ قدمِهِ ، ولا العدمَ اللاحق لوجوب بقائه.. علمت استحالة هذه الأمور كلها في حقّهِ تعالى ؛ لاستلزامها مماثلتَهُ لما قامَ البرهان على وجوب حدوثِهِ ، وهو الجواهر والأعراض . استحالة الجزمية على الله تعالى ، ويدخل فيهِ الجسم والجوهر ، والصغر والكبرُ . فقوله : ( ومن هنا ) إشارة إلى وجوبِ قدمِهِ وبقائِهِ . وقوله : ( جِرْماً ) أي : مقداراً يشغل فراغاً ، فيتناول الجوهر الفردَ ، والمركَّبَ منه وهو الجسم ؛ وذلك لأنَّ الجِرْمَ ملازم للحركة أو
From this you also learn the necessity of His transcendence (جل وعلا) from being a body, or something subsisting in Him, or facing Him, or being in a direction relative to Him, or being depicted in His imagination; because all of that necessitates His similarity to contingent beings, and then what is necessary for them would be necessary for Him! And that would impugn the necessity of His pre-existence and everlastingness, indeed every attribute of His divinity. Meaning: that when you know the necessity of His existence (جل وعلا), and that He does not accept prior non-existence due to the necessity of His pre-existence, nor posterior non-existence due to the necessity of His everlastingness... you know the impossibility of all these things with respect to Him (تعالى); because they entail His similarity to what has been proven by demonstration to be originated, namely substances and accidents. The impossibility of bodily characteristics for Allah (تعالى), and within that enters the body and the atom, smallness and bigness. So His statement: 'From this' is an indication of the necessity of His pre-existence and everlastingness. And His statement: 'jirm' is a magnitude that occupies a void, thus it includes the indivisible atom and what is composed from it, namely the body; and that is because the jirm is inseparable from motion or...
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is an argument for God's necessary transcendence (تنزيه) from any resemblance to contingent beings (الحوادث). It begins by stating that from the fact of God's necessary pre-existence (قدم) and everlastingness (بقاء), one also learns the obligation of declaring God transcendent from being a jirm (جرم – body or magnitude), from having something subsisting in Him (قائم به), from facing Him (محاذي له), from being in a direction relative to Him (في جهة له), or from being depicted in someone's imagination (مرتسم في خيال). The reason is that all these would necessitate similarity (مماثلة) to contingent things; and if He were similar to them, then what is necessary for them (i.e., origination and dependence) would also be necessary for Him, which would impugn His necessary pre-existence and everlastingness, and indeed every attribute of divinity. The passage then explains: when you know that God's existence is necessary and He accepts neither prior non-existence (because of necessary pre-existence) nor posterior non-existence (because of necessary everlastingness), you know the impossibility of all these attributes for Him, because they entail similarity to what demonstration has proven to be originated – namely substances (جواهر) and accidents (أعراض). The text then provides a note: 'Impossibility of bodily characteristics for Allah' (استحالة الجزمية على الله تعالى), which includes the body (الجسم), the atom (الجوهر), smallness, and bigness. Finally, it explains the phrase 'From this' as referring to the necessity of pre-existence and everlastingness, and defines 'jirm' as a magnitude occupying a void, which includes the indivisible atom and its composite (the body), because the jirm is inseparable from motion or rest (which are accidents, thus originated).

محاذياً له
في جهة له
مرتسماً في خياله
مماثلة للحوادث
الجوهر
الجسم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could have a body or be in a place in a metaphorical sense without compromising His divinity.
Correction: This passage shows that any attribute implying similarity to contingent beings — even metaphorically — is impossible for God because it would entail resemblance to originated things, contradicting His necessary pre-existence and everlastingness.

Checkpoint

A) Because a body is limited and Allah is unlimited.
B) Because being a body would entail similarity to contingent beings, which would contradict His necessary pre-existence and everlastingness.
C) Because bodies cannot exist without accidents, and Allah cannot have accidents.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
God's absolute transcendence from all forms of resemblance to contingent beings is rationally necessary because any similarity would compromise His necessary pre-existence and everlastingness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah, do you unconsciously visualize a form, light, or direction? This passage declares that any such mental image is a form of resemblance to creation. Are you willing to abandon every mental picture you have of Allah, even if it feels comforting, in obedience to His transcendence? What concrete step will you take to purify your imagination?
?
You have just learned that if God resembled any created thing, He would be contingent like them. Reflect: In your daily life, do you sometimes reduce Allah to a manageable concept—like a 'cosmic helper' or 'kind father'—that fits your expectations? How does the rational necessity of His absolute otherness challenge the way you think about your relationship with Him? Will you let this truth remake your worship?
?
The argument here depends on God's necessary pre-existence and everlastingness. If God is truly pre-eternal and everlasting, then nothing—not your sins, your doubts, or your trials—can alter His nature. Are you living as if God's transcendence makes Him distant, or as if His sovereignty is unaffected by your circumstances? Which of your fears or anxieties would dissolve if you truly internalized that He is not like anything you have ever known or suffered?

Proof That God Cannot Be a Body Based on Motion and Rest
Sanusi proves God's transcendence over corporeality using the temporality of motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God, being eternal, cannot be a body because bodies are necessarily characterized by temporal motion or rest.

Core Idea
God's eternity precludes the temporality of motion and rest inherent in every body.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a foundational argument of Islamic theology: God's necessary existence and permanence cannot coexist with the properties of bodies. Since every body must be either moving or at rest, and both are temporal, a body cannot be eternal. This lesson unpacks that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic carefully, noticing the flow from the general statement to the detailed proof.
Pay attention to the concise formal proof at the end.
2
Understand the key terms
Grasp the technical meanings of jirm, tahayyuz, haraka, sukun, hadith, azali.
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Follow the proof structure
Trace the argument: if a body existed eternally, it would be either moving or at rest; both are impossible; therefore body is temporal.
Observe the conditional reasoning used.
4
Apply to God
Conclude that God, who is eternal, cannot be a body or possess any bodily attributes.
This affirms His transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المخالفة للحوادث
ومن هنا تعلمُ أيضاً وجوبَ تنزُّهِهِ تعالى أنْ يكونَ جرماً ، أو قائماً بهِ ، أو محاذياً لهُ ، أو في جهةٍ له ، أو مرتسماً في خيالِهِ ؛ لأنَّ ذلكَ كلَّه يُوجِبُ مماثلتَهُ للحوادثِ ، فيجب له ما وجب لها ! وذلك يقدح في وجوب قدمِهِ وبقائِهِ ، بل وفي كلِّ وصفِ مِنْ أوصاف ألوهيته.
يعني : أنَّكَ إذا علمْتَ وجوبَ وجودِهِ جل وعلا ، وأَنَّهُ لا يقبلُ العدم السابق لوجوبِ قدمِهِ ، ولا العدمَ اللاحق لوجوب بقائه.. علمت استحالة هذه الأمور كلها في حقِّهِ تعالى ؛ لاستلزامها مماثلتَهُ لما قامَ البرهان على وجوب حدوثِهِ ، وهو الجواهر والأعراض .
وقوله : ( ومن هنا ) إشارة إلى وجوبِ قدمِهِ وبقائِهِ.
وقوله : ( جرماً ) أي : مقداراً يشغل فراغاً ، فيتناول الجوهر الفردَ ، والمركَّبَ منه وهو الجسم ؛ وذلك لأنَّ الجرمَ ملازم للحركة أو السكون ؛ لأنَّ التحيز صفةٌ نفسيَّةٌ لهُ ، فإن بقي في حيزِهِ فهو ساكن ، وإن انتقلَ عنه فهو مُتحرِّكُ ، والحركة والسكونُ حادثانِ ، وقد سبق برهانه.
وأخصر شيء في ذلكَ أنْ تقول : الحركة لا تكون أزليَّة ؛ لعدم إمكان بقائها ، ولملزوميتها سبقَ السكون في الحيز المنتقل عنه ، والأزلي لا يكون مسبوقاً بغيرِه ، والأزلي أيضاً يلزمُ بقاؤُهُ ، وأمَّا السكونُ أيضاً فلا يكون أزليّاً ، وإلا لاستحال عدمه ، فيستحيلُ أنْ يتحرَّكَ الجرم دائماً ، والعقل والمشاهدة يُكذِّبَانِهِ ، فتقول على هذا في نظم الدليل على حدوثِ الأجرام : لو وُجِدَ جِرْمٌ فِي الأَزْلِ لم يخلُ أَنْ يكونَ فيهِ مُتحرِّكاً أو ساكناً، لكنَّ التالي باطل بقسميه ، فالمقدَّمُ مثلُهُ. وبالجملة : فالحركة والسكون لا يكونان إلا حادثين ضرورةً ، فما لازمَهُما ـ وهو الجرم - يجب حدوثه ، ويتعالى مَنْ وجَبَ له القدمُ والبقاء أنْ يكونَ حادثاً.
Incompatibility with accidents. And from this you also know the necessity of His transcendence, exalted is He, from being a body (jirm), or an accident subsisting in Him, or adjacent to Him, or in a direction relative to Him, or being depicted in someone's imagination; because all of that entails His similarity to accidents, so what is necessary for them would become necessary for Him! And that impugns the necessity of His pre-eternity and permanence, rather impugns every attribute of His divinity. Meaning: that when you know the necessity of His existence, may He be glorified and exalted, and that He does not accept prior non-existence due to the necessity of His pre-eternity, nor later non-existence due to the necessity of His permanence... you know the impossibility of all these things with respect to Him, exalted is He; because they entail His similarity to that which proof has established the necessity of its temporality, namely substances and accidents. And His saying: 'from this' refers to the necessity of His pre-eternity and permanence. And His saying: 'a body' means: a quantity occupying space, thus it includes the indivisible atom and its composite, which is the body; and that is because the body is inseparable from motion or rest; because occupying space (al-tahayyuz) is a psychical attribute of it, and if it remains in its space it is at rest, and if it departs from it it is in motion, and motion and rest are temporal, and its proof has preceded. And the briefest thing in that is to say: motion cannot be eternal, because of the impossibility of its persistence, and because it necessitates the precedence of rest in the space from which it departs, and the eternal cannot be preceded by something else, and the eternal also requires its own permanence. As for rest, it also cannot be eternal, otherwise its non-existence would be impossible, so it would be impossible for the body to move eternally, but reason and observation falsify that. So you say in this way, in arranging the proof for the temporality of bodies: if a body existed in eternity, it would inevitably be either moving or at rest within it, but the consequent is false in both divisions, so the antecedent is likewise false. In sum: motion and rest are only temporal of necessity, so what is inseparable from them — namely the body — must be temporal, and exalted is He for whom pre-eternity and permanence are necessary from being temporal.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a proof for God's transcendence over corporeality. It begins by stating the general principle: God must be free from being a body, or having any property of bodies, because that would make Him similar to contingent things (hawadith). The author then explains that if you know God's necessary existence and His inability to cease existing, you realize that any attribute of contingency is impossible for Him, as it would entail likeness to substances and accidents which are proven temporal. The key point is that a body (jirm) is defined as a quantity occupying space, which necessarily entails either motion or rest (since occupying space is its characteristic property; if it stays in its space it is at rest, if it leaves it is in motion). Both motion and rest are temporal — they come into being after not existing — so whatever is necessarily characterized by them must also be temporal. The author then gives a concise formal proof: motion cannot be eternal because it requires prior rest in the space of departure; rest also cannot be eternal because then it could never cease, making motion impossible, contradicting observation. Therefore any body existing in eternity would have to be either moving or at rest eternally, but both are impossible, so bodies cannot exist eternally. The conclusion: God, who is necessarily eternal and permanent, cannot be a body. This argument relies on the premise that bodies are necessarily characterized by motion or rest, a standard Ash'ari position.

Key Terms
جرم
تحيز
حركة
سكون
حادث
أزلي
قدم
بقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'being in a direction' or 'being an accident' are not necessarily comparable to being a body.
Correction: Any attribute that entails similarity to contingent things (like occupying space, being in a direction) is impossible for God, as it would make Him temporal.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that God cannot be a body (jirm)?
A) Because a body must be composed of parts, which implies contingency.
B) Because a body must be characterized by motion or rest, both of which are temporal.
C) Because a body occupies space, which is impossible for God.
D) Because a body can be larger or smaller, requiring a specific size.

Key Takeaway
God's necessary eternity and permanence are incompatible with the temporality of motion and rest, so He cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own body, which is constantly in motion or rest, do you ever reflect that it is a sign of temporality and dependence on the Eternal? How does that awareness affect your priorities today? Commit to one action that acknowledges your body's transience.
?
Sanusi proves that God is absolutely free from spatial limitations. In your worship, do you sometimes imagine God as being 'up' or in a certain direction? How can you train your heart to worship without spatial concepts, knowing He is beyond all location?
?
If God cannot be a body, then He cannot be perceived by physical senses. Does this challenge your desire to 'see' or 'feel' God in tangible ways? What does this demand from your faith in the unseen? Resolve to strengthen your reliance on revelation over imagination.

Divine Transcendence

Proof That God Cannot Be a Body (Size Variation Argument)
Sanusi argues that if God were a body, He would be capable of being larger or smaller, requiring a specific size and a determiner, indicating contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of being larger or smaller implies that a body cannot be eternal, and so God cannot be a body.

Core Idea
A body's specific size requires a cause, so God cannot be a body.

This lesson presents Sanusi's second argument against the idea that God has a body. By examining the nature of size and the impossibility of infinite bodies, we see that any corporeal being would need a determiner for its specific dimensions, making it contingent and temporal. Since God is eternal and necessary, He cannot be a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the logical flow. Notice the conditional statement and the conclusion.
The argument is concise: if God were a body, then X, Y, Z follow, leading to impossibility.
2
Analyze the premises
Identify the two key premises: (1) a body could be larger or smaller, and (2) an infinite body is impossible. Understand why these premises are considered self-evident.
The impossibility of an infinite body is taken as axiomatic in kalam.
3
Draw the conclusion
Follow the reasoning: because a specific size must be chosen from possibilities, a determiner (mukhaṣṣiṣ) is needed. This implies contingency (ḥādith), which is incompatible with God's necessity.
The conclusion is that God being a body is impossible (muḥāl).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فلو كان جرماً لجازَ أنْ يكونَ أكبر مما هو عليهِ أو أصغرَ ؛ ولاستحالة وجودِ جرمٍ لا نهاية له ، فيحتاجُ إلى مُخصِّص يخصِّصُهُ بما عليهِ مِنَ المقدارِ دونَ غيرِهِ مِنَ المقادير الجائزة ، فيكون حادثاً . وهو محال
And also: if He were a body, it would be possible for Him to be larger than He is or smaller; and because the existence of a body with no limit is impossible, He would need a determiner (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to specify Him with the quantity He has, to the exclusion of other possible quantities; then He would be temporal. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof that God cannot be a body. The argument runs: if God were a body, He would have a specific size (e.g., this large, not larger or smaller). But it is conceivable for a body to be larger or smaller than it actually is. Since an infinite body is impossible (no body can be unlimited in size), there must be a reason why this particular size was chosen over other possible sizes. That reason is a 'determiner' (mukhaṣṣiṣ), which must be an external agent that selects one possibility. The need for a determiner shows that the body's size is contingent — it could have been otherwise — and therefore the body itself is temporal (hadith), because an eternal being cannot depend on something else for its specific attributes. Since God is eternal and necessary, He cannot be a body. This argument relies on the principle of sufficient reason: any contingent attribute requires a cause that specifies it.

Key Terms
جرم
مقدار
مخصِّص
حادث
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal being could have a determinate size without any cause.
Correction: The Ash'ari position holds that any possibility of being otherwise (like a different size) entails contingency and thus a need for a cause. An eternal being cannot depend on external causes for its attributes.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that if God were a body, He would need a determiner (mukhaṣṣiṣ)?
A) Because bodies are always created by a determiner.
B) Because a body could be larger or smaller, and an infinite body is impossible, so a specific size requires a cause.
C) Because God cannot be a body, so a determiner is needed to make Him a body.
D) Because the body would have motion and rest.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of being larger or smaller shows that any body's specific size requires a cause, proving that God cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own body and its specific shape and size, do you ever think about how it could have been different? How does this lesson challenge you to see that every detail of your existence is contingent on God's will and determination?
?
If God had a body, He would need a cause for His specific attributes. Do you sometimes act as if God is limited by your expectations or that He should meet your standards? How can you cultivate worship of the God who is absolutely independent and beyond any limitation?
?
The proof shows that needing a determiner means being temporal and dependent. In what areas of your life do you still rely on your own strength or planning rather than recognizing your total dependence on God every moment? What would it look like to truly live with that awareness?

Proof That God Cannot Be Composed of Parts
Sanusi provides a proof from absurdity against divine composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic theologians argue that God cannot be a composite body and why this argument reinforces tawhid.

Core Idea
God's oneness is contradicted by any composition, because parts would each need His attributes.

Sanusi presents a concise yet powerful argument against those who claim God has a body. If God were made of parts, each part would have to possess all divine attributes — which is rationally impossible. This short passage thus reinforces the absolute unity and simplicity of God, a cornerstone of Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
The passage is a single logical statement.
2
Understand the implication
Identify what would be necessary if God were composite: each part would have to possess all divine attributes like knowledge, power, and life.
Think about why this is impossible.
3
Reflect on the absurdity
Consider the consequences: each part would be a god (polytheism), or parts lacking attributes would make the whole deficient (imperfection).
This proves God cannot be composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فلو كان جسماً مُركَّباً مِن جزأين فأكثر .. لَلَزِمَ أنْ يقوم بكل جزء منه صفة العلم والقدرة والحياة وسائر صفاتِ الإله
And also: if He were a body composed of two or more parts... it would be necessary that in each part of Him subsist the attributes of knowledge, power, life, and all the other attributes of divinity.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents a third proof: if God were a composite body (made of parts), then each part would have to possess the divine attributes — knowledge, power, life, etc. This is impossible for several reasons: (1) Each part would itself be a god, implying multiple gods; (2) The divine attributes are indivisible and cannot be shared among parts; (3) A part that lacks any of these attributes would be deficient, and the whole would depend on parts, contradicting divine perfection. The argument is cut off at the page end, but the intended conclusion is that God cannot be composite, because that would entail absurd consequences. This is a standard kalam refutation of anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
جسم مركب
جزء
صفات الإله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think composition is not problematic if the parts are not separate or if they share attributes harmoniously.
Correction: Any composition implies dependence on parts, which contradicts divine self-sufficiency and unity. The argument shows that even sharing attributes among parts leads to polytheism or imperfection.

Checkpoint
What would necessarily follow if God were a body composed of parts?
A) Each part would have all divine attributes, leading to absurdity.
B) The parts would be separate from God.
C) God would have a specific size.
D) God would be subject to motion and rest.

Key Takeaway
God's absolute unity forbids any composition, which would entail absurdities like multiple gods or divine imperfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This argument shows that any dependence on parts would make God imperfect. Where in your life are you depending on something or someone other than God as if they were an independent source? What practical step can you take this week to redirect that reliance solely to Allah?
?
If you truly internalize that God is absolutely one and not composed, how would that change your response to your own desires that compete with His commands? Will you let this truth dismantle any 'parts' in your heart that rival Him?
