The Impossibility of God Being a Body
Sanusi argues against divine corporeality using the need for a specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the possibility of different sizes for a body leads to the need for a specifier, proving that God cannot be a body.

Core Idea
A body's possible sizes demand a cause; God has no cause, so He is not a body.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a crisp argument from the nature of bodies: any body could logically be larger or smaller, so its actual size must be chosen by something. Since God is eternal and uncaused, He cannot depend on such a choice—therefore, He is not a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical flow from assumption to conclusion.
The passage is a single, compact proof.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Sanusi starts with 'if He were a body' and draws implications. Spot the words 'لجاز' (it would be possible) and 'فيكون' (so He would be).
3
Understand the key moves
Break the argument into its components: possibility of different sizes, impossibility of infinite body, need for a specifier, conclusion of temporal origination.
Each step builds on the previous.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Sanusi ends with 'وهو محال' (and that is impossible). Ask yourself why the conclusion is impossible for God.
This connects to God's eternal nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فلو كان جِرْماً لجازَ أنْ يكونَ أكبر مما هو عليهِ أو أصغرَ ؛ ولاستحالة وجودِ جِرْمِ لا نهاية له ، فيحتاجُ إلى مُخصِّص يخصِّهُ بما عليهِ مِنَ المقدارِ دونَ غيرِهِ مِنَ المقادير الجائزة ، فيكون حادثاً . وهو محال
Also: if [God] were a body, then it would be possible for Him to be larger or smaller than He is; and because of the impossibility of the existence of a body without end, He would need a specifier to specify Him to the quantity He has rather than other possible quantities, so He would be temporally originated. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against God being a body (جرم). If God were a body, He would have a specific size. However, it would be rationally possible for Him to be larger or smaller than He actually is. Moreover, the existence of an infinite body is impossible (every body must have finite limits). Therefore, God's actual size among the possible sizes requires a specifier (مخصِّص) to determine it—something that chooses one possibility over others. The need for a specifier is a sign of contingency and temporality (حدوث). So if God were a body, He would be temporally originated, which is impossible (محال) for God, who is eternal. Therefore, God cannot be a body.

Key Terms
جرم
مخصِّص
مقدار
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God could have a necessary size that does not require a cause.
Correction: The argument shows any specific size is one among rational possibilities, so it must be caused. God cannot be caused, so He cannot have a size.

Checkpoint
Why does the possibility of being larger or smaller lead to the need for a specifier?
A) Because God cannot change
B) Because any possible size requires a cause to actualize one possibility
C) Because size is an accident
D) Because infinite bodies are impossible

Key Takeaway
If God were a body, He would be subject to possibility and thus need a specifier, making Him temporal—impossible for the eternal God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The argument shows that any body needs a cause. If you are a body, then you are in need of a cause. Do you truly thank the Cause of your existence each day, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient? Resolve to recite a short supplication acknowledging your dependence on Him.

God is Neither a Body Nor an Accident
Sanusi proves that God cannot be a body or an accident, giving arguments from divine necessity and perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be a body or an accident, and how His self-subsistence and necessity preclude all dependence and change.

Core Idea
God is neither a body nor an accident; He is self-subsistent and free from all dependence.

This passage builds on earlier proofs to establish God's absolute transcendence. Sanusi argues that if God cannot be a body, then bodily attributes are also impossible. More importantly, he demonstrates that God cannot be an accident, because accidents require a substrate and are subject to change, while God is necessary and perfect. The argument culminates in showing that God must be self-subsistent, possessing all perfect attributes without needing anything else.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style; the proof is built in stages.
2
Identify the two main claims
The passage asserts two impossibilities: God being a body, and God being an accident. Find where each is stated and note how Sanusi connects them.
The first claim is given as a consequence of an earlier proof.
3
Understand the argument against accidentality
Sanusi argues that if God were an accident, He would be dependent on a substrate and subject to change – both impossible for the Unchanging Lord. Trace this reasoning.
He recalls a prior proof that accidents are temporal because they change.
4
Grasp the two aspects of self-subsistence
Sanusi explains that God must be self-subsistent (qā'im bil-nafs), meaning He depends on neither a specificier (due to pre-eternality) nor a substrate (due to possessing perfect attributes). Work through each reason.
These reasons correspond to God’s necessity and perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا استحالَ أنْ يكونَ تعالى جِرْماً استحالَ وصفه بالصغرِ والكبرِ اللذين هما مِنْ أوصاف الأجرام.

استحالة العرضية على الله تعالى ]

قوله : ( أو قائماً بهِ ( يعني : بالجِرْمِ ؛ لأنَّهُ يُوجِبُ أَنْ يكونَ عَرَضاً مفتقراً إلى محلَّ يقومُ بهِ ، وقد سبق برهان حدوثِ الأعراض بتغيُّرها قبولاً وحصولاً (۲) ، والرب جل وعلا يستحيل عليه التغير مطلقاً

ويجب له القيام بنفسِهِ ؛ أي: لا يفتقر إلى محل ولا مخصص . أمَّا عدمُ افتقارِهِ إِلى مُخصِّص : فلوجوب القدم والبقاء لذاته ولصفاته .

وأما عدمُ افتقارِهِ إلى محلَّ : فلوجوب اتصافه بالصفاتِ العليَّةِ الوجوديَّةِ ؛ مِنَ العلم والقدرة والإرادة والحياة والسمع والبصر والكلام ، ولو كان مفتقراً إلى محلَّ لكانَ صفةَ معنى مِنَ المعاني والصفة لا تتصفُ بشيءٍ مما سبق ، وأيضاً فلو كان مفتقراً إلى محل لم
And if it is impossible for the Exalted to be a body, then describing Him with smallness and largeness – which are attributes of bodies – is impossible. [Section: The impossibility of accidentality for Allah, Exalted.] His statement: 'or subsisting in something' – meaning: in the body – because it necessitates that He be an accident dependent on a substrate in which He subsists, and the proof of the temporality of accidents through their change in acceptance and occurrence has preceded. And the Lord, Majestic and Exalted, it is impossible for change to occur to Him absolutely. And self-subsistence is necessary for Him; i.e., He does not depend on a substrate nor a specificier. As for His not depending on a specificier: because of the necessity of pre-eternality and everlastingness for His essence and His attributes. And as for His not depending on a substrate: because of the necessity of His being described with the supreme existential attributes: knowledge, power, will, life, hearing, sight, and speech. And if He were dependent on a substrate, He would be a property of meaning among meanings, and a property is not described with anything of what preceded. Also, if He were dependent on a substrate, [He would not be described with those attributes]...
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God cannot be a body or an accident. It begins by stating that if it is impossible for God to be a body (as argued earlier), then attributes of bodies like smallness and largeness are also impossible. Then it moves to the impossibility of God being an accident (something inhering in a body). The author explains that if God were an accident, He would be dependent on a substrate, which is impossible. He recalls an earlier proof that accidents are temporal because they change. God cannot change. Therefore God must be self-subsistent (qā'im bil-nafs), meaning He does not depend on a substrate nor does He need a specificier. The reason He does not need a specificier is because He is pre-eternal and everlasting by His essence and attributes. The reason He does not need a substrate is because He must be described with the perfect existential attributes (knowledge, power, will, etc.). If He depended on a substrate, He would be an accident (a property of something else), and such an accident cannot be described with these attributes. The argument is cut off but the conclusion is that God cannot be an accident because that would negate His attributes and necessity.

Key Terms
جرم
عرض
محل
القيام بالنفس
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God can be described as having a body or being an accident, like created things.
Correction: This passage shows that such descriptions would entail dependence, change, and limitation, which contradict God's necessary and perfect existence. God is absolutely transcendent and self-subsistent.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi argue that God cannot be an accident?
A) Because accidents do not have attributes.
B) Because accidents are temporal and change, but God does not change.
C) Because accidents are not created by God.
D) Because accidents are not real.

Key Takeaway
God's necessity and perfection require that He be self-subsistent, not a body or accident, and free from all dependence and change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage argues that if God were dependent on a substrate, He would be a mere property, incapable of possessing perfect attributes. How does this realization impact the way you conceive of God in your worship? Are there times you subconsciously limit God by thinking of Him in human terms? What practical step can you take today to purify your tawhid?
?
Sanusi says God cannot change. How does this truth confront the way you react to life's uncertainties? When you face trials, do you doubt God's unchangeable power and wisdom? What concrete action will you take to anchor your heart in His unchanging nature?

Divine Transcendence
beginner
6 min
God Cannot Be a Body or an Accident
Sanusi proves God's utter transcendence of body and accident, establishing self-subsistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must be self-subsisting and cannot be described as a body, an accident, or dependent on a substrate or determiner.

Core Idea
God is not a body, not an accident, and depends on nothing.

Previously, the impossibility of God being a body was established. Now, Sanusi builds on that to show that if God is not a body, then He cannot have size attributes, and moreover He cannot be an accident subsisting in a body. He must be self-subsisting, independent of any substrate or determiner. This passage provides two independent proofs for God's self-subsistence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the logical flow from body impossibility to self-subsistence.
2
Identify the two conclusions
The passage reaches two clear conclusions: (1) God cannot have size attributes, (2) God must be self-subsisting. Locate these in the text.
Size attributes are smallness and bigness.
3
Understand the proof of no determiner
The first sub-proof for self-subsistence: God does not need a determiner (mukhassis) because His essence and attributes are necessarily pre-eternal and permanent. No external cause is needed.
Pre-eternity (qidam) and permanence (baqa') are essential.
4
Understand the proof of no substrate
The second sub-proof: God possesses supreme existential attributes (knowledge, power, etc.). If He depended on a substrate, He would be an accident, and accidents cannot be described with such attributes. Also, the substrate would share divinity, leading to multiple gods.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the two proofs together establish God's transcendence beyond body, accident, and any dependency. This is foundational for tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا استحالَ أنْ يكونَ تعالى جِرْماً استحالَ وصفه بالصغرِ والكبرِ اللذين هما مِنْ أوصاف الأجرام . استحالة العرضية على الله تعالى ] قوله : ( أو قائماً بهِ ( يعني : بالجِرْمِ ؛ لأنَّهُ يُوجِبُ أَنْ يكونَ عَرَضاً مفتقراً إلى محلّ يقومُ بهِ ، وقد سبق برهان حدوثِ الأعراض بتغيُّرها قبولاً وحصولاً ، والرب جل وعلا يستحيل عليه التغير مطلقاً ويجب له القيام بنفسِهِ ؛ أي: لا يفتقر إلى محل ولا مُخصص . أمَّا عدمُ افتقارِهِ إِلى مُخصِّص : فلوجوب القدم والبقاء لذاته ولصفاته . وأما عدمُ افتقارِهِ إلى محلَّ : فلوجوب اتصافه بالصفاتِ العليَّةِ الوجوديَّةِ ؛ مِنَ العلم والقدرة والإرادة والحياة والسمع والبصر والكلام ، ولو كان مفتقراً إلى محلَّ لكانَ صفةَ معنى مِنَ المعاني والصفة لا تتصفُ بشيءٍ مما سبق ، وأيضاً فلو كان مفتقراً إلى محل لم يكن بالألوهية أولى مِنَ المحل الذي افتقر إليه ، فإنْ فُرِضَ أَنَّهما النهانِ لزم تعدد الآلهة
And if it is impossible for the Most High to be a body, it is impossible to describe Him with smallness and bigness, which are attributes of bodies. [Section: The impossibility of accidentality for Allah] His saying: 'or subsisting in it' means: in the body; because it necessitates that He be an accident dependent on a substrate in which it subsists, and the proof of the temporality of accidents by their change in acceptance and occurrence has preceded, and the Lord, might and majesty, change is impossible for Him absolutely, and self-subsistence is necessary for Him; meaning: He does not depend on a substrate nor a determiner. As for His not depending on a determiner: due to the necessity of pre-eternity and permanence for His essence and His attributes. And as for His not depending on a substrate: due to the necessity of His being described with the supreme existential attributes: knowledge, power, will, life, hearing, sight, and speech. And if He were dependent on a substrate, He would be an attribute of some meaning, and the attribute is not described with any of the above. Also, if He were dependent on a substrate, He would not be more deserving of divinity than the substrate upon which He depends. If it is assumed that they are both gods, then multiplicity of gods would follow.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God cannot be a body nor a substrate for accidents, and that He is self-subsisting. It begins with a conditional: if it is impossible for God to be a body, then it is impossible to describe Him with size (smallness or bigness), because these are attributes of bodies. Then the author discusses the phrase 'or subsisting in it' from an earlier text, explaining that it refers to subsisting in a body, because that would require God to be an accident dependent on a substrate. The earlier proof of the temporality of accidents is recalled: accidents change (by acceptance and occurrence), and change is impossible for God. Therefore, God must be self-subsisting (qa'im bi nafsihi), meaning He does not depend on a substrate (mahall) or a determiner (mukhassis). The author then provides two separate proofs: (1) The proof that He does not need a determiner: because He is necessarily eternal and permanent in His essence and attributes, so no external cause determines Him. (2) The proof that He does not need a substrate: because He necessarily possesses existential attributes (knowledge, power, will, life, hearing, sight, speech). If He were dependent on a substrate, He would be an accident (a 'meaning' inhering in a substance), and an accident cannot itself be described with such perfect attributes. Furthermore, if He were dependent on a substrate, that substrate would share in divinity, leading to multiplicity of gods. Thus the argument establishes God's utter transcendence above body and accident.

Key Terms
جرم (body)
عرض (accident)
محل (substrate)
قيام بالنفس (self-subsistence)
قدم (pre-eternity)
بقاء (permanence)
مخصص (determiner)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'self-subsisting' means God is like a substance (jawhar) that exists independently, similar to atoms.
Correction: Self-subsistence (qiyam bi nafsihi) in this context means God does not depend on any substrate or determiner at all. He is not a substance or an accident; He is unique and beyond all categories.

Checkpoint
Why must God be self-subsisting (qa'im bi nafsihi) according to this passage?
A) Because He is described with size and bigness
B) Because otherwise He would be an accident, and accidents are temporal and changing, but God does not change
C) Because He needs a determiner to exist
D) Because He is a body

Key Takeaway
God cannot be a body, an accident, or dependent on anything; He is self-subsisting, proven by His eternal attributes and perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson proves that God depends on nothing. Do you live as if God is the only One you truly depend on, or do you secretly rely on people, positions, or possessions? What is one attachment you need to break today to affirm tawhid in your heart?
?
If you truly believed that God is not a body and not confined to any place, how would that change the way you pray? Would you sense His nearness even without imagining Him in a direction? What would it take for you to feel that?
?
The lesson ends by warning that if God depended on a substrate, that substrate would rival Him. Examine your life: is there anything you have allowed to become a 'substrate'—something you feel you cannot live without, something that competes with God for your ultimate trust? What step will you take to remove that from its throne?

The Impossibility of Union (Ittihad) for God
Sanusi defines ittihad and proves its impossibility via a trilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of ittihad (union) and why it is logically impossible for God and for any two things, based on Sanusi's trilemma proof.

Core Idea
True union means two things becoming one — but that's impossible for any two things.

This passage from Sanusi's al-Muqaddimat tackles a crucial aspect of divine transcendence: God's absolute oneness cannot be compromised by any notion of union with creation. Sanusi first defines what 'union' (ittihad) actually means in theological discourse — a literal becoming one — and then demolishes it with a clear logical argument that applies to all beings, eternal or temporal. As you read, notice how the proof leaves no logical possibility for ittihad.

Guided Path
1
Read and define
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify how Sanusi defines ittihad: 'the becoming of two things into one thing.'
The definition is explicit and literal — no figurative nuance.
2
Analyze the trilemma
The proof presents three possible scenarios when two things allegedly become one. List each scenario and see why each fails to result in a genuine union.
The three scenarios are exhaustive: both remain, both cease, or one ceases.
3
Apply the conclusion
Understand that the impossibility applies to both eternal and temporal beings, so God cannot unite with anything. Reflect on how this safeguards God's transcendence and oneness.
This refutes doctrines like incarnation or pantheistic union.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا استحال افتقارُهُ إلى محلَّ استحال اتحاده به ، ومعنى الاتحاد : صيرورة الشيئين شيئاً واحداً ، وهو محال مطلقاً في القديم والحادثِ وبرهانُهُ : أنَّ أحدَ الشيئين إذا اتَّحد بالآخرِ : فإن بقيا على حالهما فهما اثنان لا واحدٌ ، فلا اتحاد ، وإِنْ عُدِما كانَ الموجود غيرهما ، وإِنْ عُدِمَ أحدهما دونَ الآخرِ امتنع الاتحاد ؛ لأنَّ المعدومَ لا يكون عينَ الموجود .
And if His dependence on a substrate is impossible, then His union with it is impossible. And the meaning of union (al-ittihad) is: the becoming of two things into one thing. And it is absolutely impossible in the eternal and the temporal. And its proof: that when one of the two things unites with the other, if they both remain in their state, then they are two, not one, so no union. If both cease to exist, then the existent is other than them. If one of them ceases without the other, union is impossible, because the non-existent cannot be identical to the existent.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of ittihad (union) and provides a logical proof of its impossibility for all beings, whether eternal or temporal. The definition: ittihad means two things becoming one thing. The proof is a trilemma: if two things unite, either (1) both remain as they were, in which case they are still two, not one — no union occurred; or (2) both cease to exist, so something else comes into existence, which is not the original two — again no union; or (3) one ceases and the other remains, then the one that ceased is non-existent and cannot be identical to the existent one — so no union. Therefore, ittihad is logically impossible in all cases. This argument is foundational for refuting doctrines that claim God unites with creation.

Key Terms
اتحاد (union)
قديم (eternal)
حادث (temporal)
عين (identical)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'union' (ittihad) between God and creation can mean a close spiritual connection or unity of purpose.
Correction: In classical kalam, ittihad means literal ontological oneness — two distinct entities becoming one substance. This is impossible for any two things, as Sanusi's proof demonstrates.

Checkpoint
According to the proof given, what is the outcome if both things remain unchanged after an alleged union?
A) They become one thing
B) They are still two, so no union
C) They both cease to exist
D) One becomes the other

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's trilemma proves that ittihad (two things becoming one) is logically impossible, thereby preserving God's absolute oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel close to God in worship, does your heart ever slip into thinking of Him as 'inside' you or 'one' with your spirit? How does Sanusi's trilemma challenge that feeling and call you to purify your understanding of closeness?
?
If ittihad is impossible, then what does it truly mean to 'seek nearness' to God? Does your longing for intimacy with Him sometimes lean toward a desire for union that this proof forbids? How will you redirect that desire toward something He has actually promised?
?
Have you ever heard someone say 'God is everything' or 'we are all part of God'? How does this passage equip you to respond with clarity and compassion? Will you now guard your tongue and your heart from even implying such a thing?

Refuting the Union of the Word with Jesus
Refutes the Christian doctrine of union by applying proof of divine transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of God depending on a substrate or uniting with anything entails the invalidity of the Christian doctrine of the union of the Word with Jesus, and recognize the internal contradiction in one interpretation of that union.

Core Idea
God's transcendence leaves no room for union with creation.

This passage applies the ironclad proof of divine transcendence to dismantle the Christian claim that the Word (an attribute of God) united with Jesus. By showing that God cannot depend on a substrate or unite with anything, the argument forces the Christian position into logical contradiction. The author also highlights the disagreement among Christians themselves, exposing the incoherence of their doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Let the argument's flow come through before interpretation.
Notice the compressed style and the logical connectors.
2
Identify the general principle
The passage begins with a principle: God cannot depend on a substrate or unite with anything. Based on previous proofs, this is established.
This principle is key to the refutation.
3
Apply the principle to the Christian claim
The same impossibility applies to God's attribute (the Word): it cannot subsist in another or unite with it. Thus the Christian claim of union is false.
4
Examine the internal disagreement and its consequence
Christians disagree on the meaning of union. The passage examines one view: union as accident subsisting in substance. This interpretation would separate the Word from the divine essence, a contradiction.
This shows the doctrine is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكذا وإذا عرفت استحالة افتقاره إلى محل ، واتحادهِ بهِ. يستحيل قيامُ صفتِهِ بذاتِ غيرِهِ واتحادهها بهِ : فبطل ما قالَتِ النصارى أهلكَهمُ الله (۱) : أنَّ أُقنوم الكلمةِ اتَّحد بناسوتِ عيسى عليهِ السلام ) ، واختلفوا في معنى الاتحادِ ؛ فمنهم مَنْ قالَ : إِنَّ الاتحاد يرجع إلى قيامها بهِ كما يقومُ العرضُ بالجوهر ، وهذا يُوجِبُ مفارقته لذات الجوهر الذي هو مجموع الأقانيم الثلاثةِ عندهم ؛ ضرورةً أنَّ
Likewise, when you know the impossibility of His dependence on a substrate and His union with it, it is impossible for His attribute to subsist in the essence of another and for it to unite with it. Thus what the Christians—may Allah destroy them—said is invalid: that the hypostasis of the Word united with the humanity of Jesus, peace be upon him. And they differed regarding the meaning of union; among them are those who said that union reduces to its [the Word's] subsisting in it [the humanity] as the accident subsists in the substance. And this necessitates its separation from the essence of the substance which is the sum of the three hypostases according to them, necessarily because...
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (tafri') of the Christian doctrine of the union of the Word (kalima) with Jesus. It builds on the previous proofs: since it is impossible for God to depend on a substrate or to unite with anything, it is also impossible for an attribute of God (i.e., the Word) to subsist in the essence of another or to unite with it. Therefore, the Christian claim is false. The author then notes that Christians themselves disagreed on the meaning of union: some said it means the Word subsists in Jesus's humanity as an accident subsists in a substance. The author points out that this would necessitate the separation of the Word from the divine essence (the sum of three hypostases), because an accident is distinct from its substance. The passage cuts off mid-argument, but the conclusion is clear: the Christian position is self-contradictory.

كلمة (Word)
ناسوت (humanity)
اتحاد (union)
قيام (subsistence)
عرض (accident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Christian doctrine of union means a moral or spiritual closeness, not a literal ontological union.
Correction: The text refutes a literal ontological union, which some Christian groups held, and shows it leads to contradiction.

Checkpoint

A) The Word becomes part of Jesus's body
B) The Word would be separated from the divine essence (the three hypostases)
C) Jesus becomes divine
D) The Word ceases to exist

Key Takeaway
The Christian doctrine of the union of the Word with Jesus is impossible because God's transcendence prevents any literal union with creation, and the specific interpretation as accident-substance leads to self-contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Christians' claim of union collapsed into self-contradiction. In what areas of your understanding of God might you be holding onto ideas that 'fit' your human logic but actually contradict His revealed nature? Will you examine your beliefs honestly?
?
This passage refutes a specific heresy, but the principle of divine transcendence guards against subtle idolatries. In your daily life, do you ever 'contain' God within your own plans, expectations, or desires? What would it look like to let God be wholly other, even when it challenges your comfort?

Refutation of Union as Mixture
Refutation of union as literal mixture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the interpretation of union as mixture is invalid for the divine Word.

Core Idea
Mixture belongs to bodies, not the eternal Word.

In this passage, the author refutes those who interpret the union (ittihad) between God and creation as a literal mixture, like blending wine and water. He argues that mixture is a property of physical bodies, whereas the divine Word is an attribute of the eternal Essence, which is immaterial. Therefore, such an interpretation is inconceivable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the rhetorical question at the end.
The passage uses a rhetorical question to emphasize the absurdity of the interpretation.
2
Identify the two arguments
The author presents two reasons: (1) all objections to the first interpretation apply here, and (2) mixture is a property of bodies, but the Word is not a body.
The first reason refers back to earlier refutations; the second is unique to this interpretation.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the immaterial nature of the divine Word precludes mixture. How does this safeguard divine transcendence?
This connects to the broader theme of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم مَنْ فسَّرَ هذا الاتحاد بالاختلاط والمزج ؛ كاختلاط الخمرِ والماء ونحوهما مِنَ المائعاتِ ، وجميع ما وَرَدَ على الأَوَّلِ يرد على هذا ، ويزيدُ بأنَّ الاختلاط مِنْ أحكام الأجسامِ ، فكيفَ يُعقَلُ في الكلمة التي هي خاصيَّةُ الذاتِ الأزليَّةِ ؟!
And among them are those who interpreted this union as mixing and blending, like the mixing of wine and water and similar liquids. And everything that was brought against the first applies to this, and adds that mixing is a property of bodies, so how can it be conceived in the Word, which is a characteristic of the eternal Essence?!
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage refutes the interpretation of union as literal mixture or blending (imtizaj). The author states that all objections raised against the previous interpretation (likely the incarnation view) apply equally here. Furthermore, mixture is only a property of physical bodies, but the divine Word (al-Kalima) is an attribute of the eternal Essence, not a body. Therefore, it is inconceivable to speak of mixture in relation to the Word.

Key Terms
اختلاط
مزج
مائعات
أجسام
خاصية
الكلمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Union as mixture is plausible because mixing is common in the physical world.
Correction: The author points out that mixture is a property of bodies, but the divine Word is not a body, so mixture cannot apply to it.

Checkpoint

A) Because mixture is a property of bodies, but the Word is not a body.
B) Because wine and water do not actually mix.
C) Because the first interpretation was already refuted.
D) Because the Word is a body and cannot mix.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of union as mixture is invalid because mixture is a property of bodies, but the divine Word is a non-physical attribute of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

Refutation of the Crucifixion Claim
Refutation of the crucifixion claim through logical and scriptural arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Christian claim of Jesus' crucifixion by exposing the contradiction between the union of natures and the separation in crucifixion, and by citing Jesus' own words from the Gospel.

Core Idea
If divine and human natures are united, one cannot be crucified without the other.

The author tackles a central Christian claim about Jesus' crucifixion. He presents the Christian defense that only the human nature suffered, then dismantles it by pointing out the logical inconsistency given the union of natures. He then reinforces his argument with a direct quote from the Gospel where Jesus refers to God as 'my God,' demonstrating his servitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the overall flow. Notice the dialogue-like structure: claim and counter-claim.
The passage uses rhetorical questions to expose contradiction.
2
Identify the Christian claim
Find where the Christians state that Jesus was crucified and how they qualify it (only the human nature).
3
Understand the first refutation
The author asks: if the two natures are united, how can one be crucified alone? This is the core logical argument.
This is based on the doctrine of ittihād (union).
4
Analyze the scriptural evidence
The author cites Jesus' words from the Gospel, highlighting phrases like 'my God' and 'your Father' to show Jesus' worship and non-divinity.
The verse is from John 17:20.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإدراك الحقائقِ على مثلهم عسير، وقد قالوا: إنَّ صُلِبَ عيسى، فقيل لهم: كيف يُصْلَبُ الإلهُ؟ فقالوا: المصلوب الناسوت، فقيل لهم: كيفَ ينفرد بالصَّلْبِ الناسوتُ دونَ اللاهوتِ وقد اتحدا؟! ثم قد ورد في إنجيلهم ما يشير إلى تعبد المسيح وخضوعه وخشوعِهِ للرب سبحانَهُ، والتزامِهِ أحكام العبيدِ؛ مِنَ التَذَلُّلِ وطلب الجزاء مِنَ اللهِ تعالى، حتى قال: ( أنا ماض إلى أبي وأبيكم، وإلهي وإلهكم ) انظر ( إنجيل يوحنا » ( ١٧/٢٠ ) )، فإن كانوا يتمسكون بلفظ ( أبي ) فقد قال: ( وأبيكم )، فبالمعنى الذي أثبت الأبوَّةَ لهم مِنَ التربية واللطفِ يثبتُ له، وقوله: ( وإلهي ) تصريح بإثباتِ الألوهية لغيرِهِ.
And perceiving truths is difficult for the likes of them. They said that Jesus was crucified, so it was said to them: How can the god be crucified? They said: The crucified is the human nature (nāsūt). So it was said to them: How can the human nature alone be crucified without the divine nature (lāhūt) when they are united? Then it is found in their Gospel what indicates the worship, humility, and reverence of Christ to the Lord, glorified be He, and his adherence to the rules of servants: from abasement and seeking reward from God Almighty, until he said: 'I am going to my Father and your Father, and my God and your God' — see (Gospel of John 17:20). If they cling to the word 'my Father', he also said 'and your Father'; so by the meaning by which he affirmed fatherhood to them from nurture and kindness, it is affirmed for him. And his saying 'and my God' is an explicit affirmation of divinity (ulūhiyya) to other than Him.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Christian claim that Jesus was crucified, based on the doctrine of incarnation (ittihād). The author argues that the claim is self-contradictory. He presents the Christian argument: the divine nature (lāhūt) did not suffer, only the human nature (nāsūt) was crucified. The author responds: if the two natures are united (ittahadā), how can one be separated from the other in crucifixion? Then he adduces evidence from the Gospel: Jesus' words show he worshipped God and called God 'my God' (Ilāhī), proving he was not divine. The term 'father' (ab) is used metaphorically for nurture and kindness, not literal fatherhood. Thus, the Christian claim fails.

الناسوت
اللاهوت
الأبوّة
الألوهية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Christians claim that only the human nature of Jesus suffered crucifixion, so the divine nature was unaffected.
Correction: The author argues that if the two natures are united, it is impossible for one to suffer without the other, making the incarnation doctrine incoherent.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Christian claim of crucifixion is refuted by the logical inseparability of the united natures and by Jesus' own words affirming God as his God.

Impossibility of God Being Parallel (Muḥādhāh)
Sanusi defines proximity and proves its impossibility for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be parallel or physically proximate to any body.

Core Idea
Parallelism is a property of bodies, not of God.

This lesson begins by examining the claim that God could be parallel to a body. The author defines parallelism as proximity, either through contact or separation, and demonstrates why both are impossible for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition of muḥādhāh
Start by reading the Arabic passage that defines 'parallelism' (muḥādhāh). Notice that the author immediately explains it as proximity (qurb).
2
Understand the two types of proximity
The author distinguishes between proximity of contact and proximity of separation. Reflect on why both would involve a body in some way.
3
Grasp the proof that these are body-properties
The author states that both types of proximity are impossible because they are properties of bodies (ajrām). Connect this to the core idea: what applies to bodies cannot apply to God.
4
Apply the principle: God is not a body
Recall that earlier in the text it was established that God is not a body. Then ask yourself: if parallelism requires a body, and God is not a body, then parallelism is impossible for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استحالة المحاذاة ] قوله : ( أو محاذياً لهُ ) أي : قريباً منه؛ إِمَّا قُرْبَ اتصال حتى يكونَ الجِرْمُ مكاناً لهُ يتمكَّنُ عليه، أو قُرْبَ انفصال حتى يكونَ - أي : الجرم - في جهةٍ له، وكلاهما محال؛ لأنَّهما مِنْ خواص الأجرام.

[ استحالة الجهة ] قوله : ( أو في جهةٍ لهُ ) أي : للجرْمِ، فليسَ فوقَ شيءٍ مِنَ العالم ولا تحته ولا أمامَهُ ولا خلفَهُ ولا عن يمينه ولا عن شمالِهِ؛ لأنَّ الجهة تستلزم التحيّز، وكلُّ متحيّز فهو جِرْمٌ، والله جلَّ وعلا ليس بجرم، وقد سبق بيانه (3). ولم يقل بالجهة إلا طائفتانِ مِنَ المبتدعة؛ وهم الكرَّامِيَّةُ

(3) تقدم ( ص ٢٤٨ )
[Impossibility of parallelism (muḥādhāh)] His saying: 'or parallel to it' means: near to it; either proximity of contact such that the body becomes a place for it upon which it settles, or proximity of separation such that it — i.e., the body — is in a direction relative to it. Both are impossible because they are properties of bodies.

[Impossibility of direction (jiha)] His saying: 'or in a direction relative to it' means: relative to the body. So He is not above anything in the universe, nor below it, nor before it, nor behind it, nor on its right, nor on its left; because direction entails occupying space (taḥayyuz), and everything that occupies space is a body, and God, majestic and exalted, is not a body. This has been explained previously (3). And no one has affirmed direction except two sects of innovators: the Karrāmiyya.

(3) Preceded (p. 248).
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a proof of God's transcendence over spatial relations. The author explains two impossible attributes: being parallel (muḥādhāh) to a body, and being in a direction (jiha) relative to a body. Parallelism means proximity, either by contact (making the body a place for God) or by separation (placing God in a direction). Both are impossible because they are characteristics of bodies (ajrām). Similarly, direction (jiha) entails occupying space (taḥayyuz), which is a property of bodies. Since God is not a body, He cannot be above, below, or in any direction relative to the universe. The author notes that only two heretical sects, the Karrāmiyya, affirm direction for God.

Key Terms
المحاذاة
القرب
الجرم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say parallelism (muḥādhāh) is impossible for God?
A) Because it would imply God is a body.
B) Because God is everywhere.
C) Because parallelism does not exist in the universe.
D) Because God cannot be near anything.

Key Takeaway
God cannot be parallel or physically proximate to any body because these are properties of bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is near to me' in prayer, do you ever unconsciously imagine a physical closeness? How can you train your heart to know that God's nearness is not spatial but spiritual, and that He transcends all physical categories? Bow your head now and silently resolve to correct any physical imagery in your worship.

?
If God cannot be physically near, then what kind of relationship can you have with Him? Read the source again: 'Both are impossible because they are properties of bodies.' Let this truth shake you: your Lord is not bound by space. Will you now seek closeness to Him through correct belief and sincere action, rather than false imaginations? Commit to one action today that draws you nearer to the Transcendent One.

Impossibility of God Having Direction (Jiha)
Sanusi denies direction for God and refutes the Karrāmiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be in any direction relative to the universe.

Core Idea
Direction implies occupying space, which is only possible for bodies.

Having established that God cannot be parallel to bodies, the author now addresses a related claim: that God is in a direction. He explains that direction entails occupying space (taḥayyuz), a property of bodies, and refutes those who affirm direction for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic denial of direction
Begin by reading the Arabic passage that explicitly denies that God is in any direction—above, below, etc.
2
Understand the six directions enumerated
The author lists all spatial directions: above, below, front, back, right, left. Notice that these are relative to a body.
3
Grasp the logical argument
The author presents a syllogism: direction requires taḥayyuz (occupying space), and anything that occupies space is a body. Since God is not a body, He cannot have direction.
4
Note the refutation of heretics
The author mentions that only two sects (Karrāmiyya) affirm direction for God, marking them as innovators. This highlights the gravity of the error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استحالة المحاذاة ] قوله : ( أو محاذياً لهُ ) أي : قريباً منه؛ إِمَّا قُرْبَ اتصال حتى يكونَ الجِرْمُ مكاناً لهُ يتمكَّنُ عليه، أو قُرْبَ انفصال حتى يكونَ - أي : الجرم - في جهةٍ له، وكلاهما محال؛ لأنَّهما مِنْ خواص الأجرام.

[ استحالة الجهة ] قوله : ( أو في جهةٍ لهُ ) أي : للجرْمِ، فليسَ فوقَ شيءٍ مِنَ العالم ولا تحته ولا أمامَهُ ولا خلفَهُ ولا عن يمينه ولا عن شمالِهِ؛ لأنَّ الجهة تستلزم التحيّز، وكلُّ متحيّز فهو جِرْمٌ، والله جلَّ وعلا ليس بجرم، وقد سبق بيانه (3). ولم يقل بالجهة إلا طائفتانِ مِنَ المبتدعة؛ وهم الكرَّامِيَّةُ

(3) تقدم ( ص ٢٤٨ )
[Impossibility of parallelism (muḥādhāh)] His saying: 'or parallel to it' means: near to it; either proximity of contact such that the body becomes a place for it upon which it settles, or proximity of separation such that it — i.e., the body — is in a direction relative to it. Both are impossible because they are properties of bodies.

[Impossibility of direction (jiha)] His saying: 'or in a direction relative to it' means: relative to the body. So He is not above anything in the universe, nor below it, nor before it, nor behind it, nor on its right, nor on its left; because direction entails occupying space (taḥayyuz), and everything that occupies space is a body, and God, majestic and exalted, is not a body. This has been explained previously (3). And no one has affirmed direction except two sects of innovators: the Karrāmiyya.

(3) Preceded (p. 248).
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a proof of God's transcendence over spatial relations. The author explains two impossible attributes: being parallel (muḥādhāh) to a body, and being in a direction (jiha) relative to a body. Parallelism means proximity, either by contact (making the body a place for God) or by separation (placing God in a direction). Both are impossible because they are characteristics of bodies (ajrām). Similarly, direction (jiha) entails occupying space (taḥayyuz), which is a property of bodies. Since God is not a body, He cannot be above, below, or in any direction relative to the universe. The author notes that only two heretical sects, the Karrāmiyya, affirm direction for God.

التحيّز
الكرّامية

A) Because direction entails occupying space, and God is not a body.
B) Because the universe has no direction.
C) Because the Quran says God is everywhere.
D) Because only the Karrāmiyya believe in direction.

Key Takeaway
God is free from all spatial directions; only bodies occupy space.

The Impossibility of Adjacency to Allah
Sanusi defines and disproves two types of adjacency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why physical adjacency—whether by contact or separation—is impossible for Allah.

Core Idea
Adjacency is a property of bodies; Allah is not a body.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a common but mistaken notion: that Allah could be 'close' to creation in a physical sense. He defines two possible forms of adjacency and then demonstrates why both are impossible. As you read, note how the argument relies on a simple but powerful principle: properties of bodies cannot apply to the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice that the passage begins by stating the impossibility, then defines the two types of adjacency.
The Arabic term 'muhadhah' is translated as 'adjacency'.
2
Identify the two types of adjacency
Find where Sanusi splits adjacency into 'qurb ittisal' (closeness of contact) and 'qurb infisal' (closeness of separation). Understand what each entails.
3
Understand the proof
Note the conclusion: both types are impossible because they are from the properties of bodies (khawass al-ajram). Ask yourself: why does being a property of bodies make it impossible for Allah?
The proof is implicit: since Allah is not a body, properties specific to bodies cannot apply to Him.
4
Connect to divine transcendence
Reflect on how this principle guards against anthropomorphism. Realizing that Allah is beyond physical attributes strengthens tawhid.
This is a key point in Aqeedah: Allah is not like His creation.

استحالة المحاذاة

قوله : ( أو محاذياً لهُ ) أي : قريباً منه ؛ إِمَّا قُرْبَ اتصال حتى يكونَ الجِرْمُ مكاناً لهُ يتمكَّنُ عليه ، أو قُرْبَ انفصال حتى يكونَ - أي : الجرم - في جهةٍ له ، وكلاهما محال ؛ لأنَّهما مِنْ خواص الأجرام .
The impossibility of adjacency. His statement: 'or adjacent to him' means close to him; either closeness of contact so that the body becomes a place upon which he rests, or closeness of separation so that the body is in a direction relative to him, and both are impossible because they are among the properties of bodies.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the impossibility of adjacency (muhadhah) between Allah and any body. The author explains that adjacency is of two types: either contact (ittisal) such that the body becomes a place (makan) for Allah, or separation (infisal) such that the body is in a direction (jiha) relative to Allah. Both are impossible because they are specific properties of bodies (khawass al-ajram). Since Allah is not a body, these properties cannot apply to Him. The proof is straightforward: adjacency is a property of bodies, Allah is not a body, therefore adjacency is impossible for Allah.

Correction: This passage clarifies that any physical adjacency—whether contact or mere directional closeness—is impossible for Allah because it is a property of bodies.

Impossibility of Allah Being in a Direction
Sanusi proves the impossibility of direction for Allah and refutes the sects that affirm a direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why direction is impossible for Allah based on the necessity that direction implies being a body, and you will learn the views of the Karramiyya and Hashwiyya sects.

Core Idea
Direction implies being a body; Allah is not a body, so direction is impossible.

In this passage, Sanusi establishes a fundamental principle of Islamic theology: Allah is free from direction. He argues that direction necessitates occupying space, which implies being a body. Since Allah is not a body, direction cannot be attributed to Him. He then addresses the innovated sects that insisted on a direction and their internal disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview.
Note the logical flow.
2
Understand the proof
Focus on the argument: direction requires tahayyuz, tahayyuz implies being a body, Allah is not a body, therefore no direction.
This is the core rational proof.
3
Identify the sects
Sanusi names two sects: Karramiyya and Hashwiyya. Notice their positions and internal divisions.
The Karramiyya are subdivided into three groups.
4
Reflect on istiwa
Consider the Hashwiyya's literal interpretation of istiwa and why Sanusi postpones discussion.
This connects to broader issues of anthropomorphism.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استحالة الجهة

قوله : ( أو في جهةٍ لهُ ) أي : للجرْمِ ، فليسَ فوقَ شيءٍ مِنَ العالم ولا تحته ولا أمامَهُ ولا خلفَهُ ولا عن يمينه ولا عن شمالِهِ ؛ لأنَّ الجهة تستلزم التحير ، وكلُّ متحيّز فهو جِرْمٌ ، والله جلَّ وعلا ليس بجرم ، وقد سبق بيانه

ولم يقل بالجهة إلا طائفتانِ مِنَ المبتدعة ؛ وهم الكرَّامِيَّةُ والحشوية ، وعيّنوا مِنَ الجهاتِ جهة فوق .

ثم اختلفَتِ الكراميَّةُ بعد ذلكَ ؛ فمنهم مَنْ قالَ : إِنَّهُ مماس للعرشِ تعالى الله عن ذلك ومنهم مَنْ زعمَ أَنَّهُ مباين له ، ثم اختلف هؤلاء ؛ فمنهم مَنْ زعمَ أَنَّهُ مباين بمسافة متناهية ، ومنهم مَنْ زعمَ أَنَّهُ مباين بمسافة غير متناهية

والحشوية حملَتِ الاستواء في الآية على ظاهرِهِ ، وامتنعَتْ مِنَ التأويل ، وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى الكلام على بعض ما أشكلَ مِنْ ظاهر القرآن والحديث في موضع أليقَ بهِ مِنْ هذا .
The impossibility of direction. His statement: 'or in a direction relative to him' means relative to the body. So He is not above anything of the world, nor below it, nor in front of it, nor behind it, nor to its right, nor to its left, because direction necessitates occupying space (tahayyuz), and everything that occupies space is a body, and Allah, exalted and high, is not a body, and its explanation has preceded. And only two sects of the innovators affirm direction: the Karramiyya and the Hashwiyya, and they specified from among the directions the direction of above. Then the Karramiyya differed after that: some of them said that He is in contact with the Throne – exalted is Allah above that – and some claimed that He is separate from it. Then these differed: some claimed that He is separate by a finite distance, and some claimed that He is separate by an infinite distance. And the Hashwiyya interpreted the word 'istiwa' in the verse according to its literal meaning and refused to interpret it metaphorically, and there will come, Allah willing, discussion of some of what is problematic from the apparent meanings of the Qur'an and hadith in a more appropriate place than this.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage begins by proving the impossibility of Allah being in any direction (jiha). The author explains that direction implies occupying space (tahayyuz), and everything that occupies space is a body. Since Allah is not a body, direction is impossible. Then he mentions that only two innovated sects affirm direction: the Karramiyya and the Hashwiyya. They specify the direction of 'above'. Then he details the internal disagreements among the Karramiyya: some say Allah is in contact with the Throne (mumass li-l-arsh), others say He is separate (mubayin). Those who say separate disagree on whether the separation is by a finite or infinite distance. Then he mentions that the Hashwiyya take the literal meaning of 'istiwa' (mounting/established) in the verse and reject metaphorical interpretation. The author promises to address problematic literal readings later in a more appropriate place.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that affirming that Allah is 'above' is a literal spatial direction.
Correction: This passage shows that direction implies being a body, which is impossible for Allah. The 'above' in the Qur'an is not spatial but denotes rank or honor.

Impossibility of Allah Being Imagined in the Mind
Sanusi proves that Allah cannot be imagined in the mind since imagination only apprehends bodies and their accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah cannot be depicted in the imagination and how definitive proofs establish His existence beyond mental imagery.

Core Idea
Imagination only captures bodies; Allah is beyond all limitation.

Sanusi addresses a subtle misconception: that Allah might be pictured in the mind. He explains that imagination is inherently limited to bodies and their properties, so it cannot represent the Creator. This lesson clarifies the boundaries of human cognition and reinforces that Allah is known through proofs, not images.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation, to grasp Sanusi's concise argument.
Notice the compact style; the passage combines a negative claim with a positive proof.
2
Identify the key claim
Pinpoint the statement 'nothing is depicted in the imagination except bodies and their accidents'. This is the logical basis for the impossibility.
Understand that this is a general epistemological principle.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by affirming that definitive proofs exist for Allah's existence. Consider why this conclusion is necessary after denying mental imagery.
This shows that faith is based on proof, not imagination.

مولانا لا يرتسم في خيالِ جِرْمِ

قوله : ( أو مُرتسماً في خيالِهِ ) الضمير يعود على الجزم ؛ أي : إِنْ كانَ لهُ خيال ؛ لأنَّهُ لا يَرتسِمُ في الخيالِ إلا الأجرام وأعراضُها وبالجملة : فقد قامَتِ البراهينُ القطعية على وجودِ الذاتِ العليَّةِ
Our Lord is not depicted in the imagination of a body. His statement: 'or depicted in his imagination' – the pronoun refers to the body; i.e., if he had an imagination; because nothing is depicted in the imagination except bodies and their accidents. In sum: definitive proofs have been established for the existence of the Supreme Essence.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage states that Allah cannot be imagined (yartasimu) in any mind. The author explains that the pronoun in 'his imagination' refers to the body (possibly the person). He clarifies that only bodies (ajram) and their accidents (a'rad) can be depicted in the imagination. Since Allah is neither a body nor an accident, He cannot be imagined. The passage concludes by affirming that definitive proofs (barahin qat'iyya) have been established for the existence of the Supreme Essence (al-dhat al-aliyya).

Correction: The passage clarifies that any mental image is a representation of a body, which cannot be Allah. Imagination is limited to bodies and accidents, so it cannot depict the Creator.

Why Similarity Necessitates Origination: The Proof
Sanusi explains the logical proof that similarity to contingents forces origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that any attribute implying similarity to contingent things would force God to be originated, contradicting His necessary pre-eternity.

Core Idea
If God were similar to contingent beings in any essential attribute, He would be contingent.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary presents a rigorous logical argument for why God cannot share any attribute with contingent beings. By analyzing the relations between any two existents, he shows that similarity in essential attributes forces equality in all necessary properties, including origination. This argument is crucial for establishing divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical terms used.
2
Identify the three types of relations
The passage classifies relations between any two existents based on equality in essential attributes. Find the three categories: similars, opposites, and different things.
Similars are equal in essential attributes.
3
Understand the key implication for similars
The passage states that if two are similars, they share all necessary, possible, and impossible predicates. This is the core of the proof.
4
Apply the argument to God
If God had any attribute making Him similar to bodies or accidents, He would share their necessity of origination. But He is necessarily pre-eternal. Therefore, such attributes are impossible.
This shows why divine attributes must be unique.
5
Reflect on the logical form
The author notes this argument can be expressed as a categorical syllogism in the first figure. Consider how this strengthens the proof.
First figure syllogisms are the most direct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ( لأن ذلك كله يوجب مماثلته للحوادث ) أي: مساواته لها في صفاتها النفسية؛ لأن كل موجودين: إما أن يتساويا في صفات النفس أو لا، فإن تساويا فهما مثلان، وإن لم يتساويا في صفات النفس فلا يخلو: إما أن يصح اجتماعهما أو لا، فإن لم يصح فضدان، وإن صح فخلافان، وكل مثلين فإنه يلزم استواؤهما في كل ما يجب لأحدهما وفي كل ما يجوز عليه أو يستحيل؛ فلهذا لو اتصف جل وعلا بشيء مما سبق.. للزم مماثلته للأجرام أو أعراضها، وذلك يستلزم أن يساويها فيما يجب لها من الحدوث، وقد سبق وجوب قدمه وبقائه، وهذا معنى قوله: ( فيجب له ما وجب لها! وذلك يقدح . . . ) إلى آخره. والاستدلال على هذا المطلب بالقياس الاقتراني ينتظم من الشكل الأول.
His statement: 'Because all that necessitates His similarity to contingent things' — meaning: His equality with them in their essential attributes. Because any two existents are either equal in essential attributes or not. If they are equal, they are two similars (mithlān). If they are not equal in essential attributes, it is not free from: either their coexistence is possible or not. If not possible, they are two opposites (ḍiddān). If possible, they are two different things (khilāfān). And every two similars, it necessarily follows that they are equal in everything that is necessary for one of them and in everything that is possible or impossible for it. Hence, were the Exalted and Majestic to be described with anything from what preceded, it would necessitate His similarity to bodies (ajrām) or their accidents (a'rāḍ), and that would necessitate that He equal them in what is necessary for them, namely origination (ḥudūth). And the necessity of His pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqā') has preceded. This is the meaning of his statement: 'So what is necessary for them becomes necessary for Him! And that is detrimental...' etc. And the proof of this point by conjunctive syllogism (qiyās iqtirānī) is organized in the first figure (shakl awwal).
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a lemma from the main text, providing a logical proof that if God were described with any attribute that implies similarity to contingent things (ḥawādith), then He would share their necessary attribute of origination (ḥudūth), contradicting His necessary pre-eternity. The author begins by clarifying the meaning of 'similarity' (mumāthala) as equality in essential attributes (ṣifāt nafsiyya). He then presents a logical classification of the relation between any two existents based on equality in essential attributes: if they are equal, they are 'similars' (mithlān); if not equal, either they cannot coexist (then they are 'opposites' (ḍiddān)) or they can coexist (then they are 'different' (khilāfān)). The crucial point is that if two are similars, they necessarily share all necessary, possible, and impossible predicates. Therefore, if God had any attribute that makes Him similar to bodies or their accidents, He would also share their necessity of origination. But it has already been established that God is necessarily pre-eternal (qadīm) and everlasting (bāqī). Thus, such attributes are impossible. Finally, the author notes that this argument can be formulated as a categorical syllogism in the first figure, which is the most direct logical form.

Key Terms
المماثلة
الصفات النفسية
المثلان
الضدان
الخلافان
الحدوث
القدم
القياس الاقتراني
الشكل الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that similarity in some attributes (like existence) is harmless and does not imply contingency.
Correction: The proof shows that similarity in essential attributes forces equality in all necessary attributes, including origination. Even one essential attribute shared with contingents would entail contingency.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, if two existents are equal in essential attributes, what is their relation?
A) They are opposites (ḍiddān)
B) They are different (khilāfān)
C) They are similars (mithlān)
D) They are incomparable

Key Takeaway
Any essential attribute shared with contingent beings would force God to be originated, which contradicts His necessary pre-eternity.

?
If you truly internalize that God shares no essential attribute with anything, how should that change your fear of Him and your hope in Him? Are you placing any creature's qualities on par with God's, even in your heart? What specific step will you take today to purify your tawhid from subtle similarities?

Classifying Beings: Like, Opposite, Different
Commentator defines 'likeness' and classifies relationships between beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of relationships between beings based on essential attributes and how they establish that Allah cannot be like creation.

Core Idea
To avoid likening Allah to creation, we must first understand the possible relations between beings.

The commentator begins by explaining the key term 'likeness' (mumathala) and defines it as equality in essential attributes. To show that Allah cannot be described with temporal attributes, he introduces a classification of all possible relationships between two existing entities. This classification is the foundation for the logical proof that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the commentator uses a very structured definition and classification.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage presents three relationships: two likes (mithlan), two opposites (diddan), and two differents (khilafan). Find how each is defined based on equality of essential attributes and possibility of coexistence.
3
Understand the implication for Allah
The passage argues that if Allah were described with any temporal attribute, He would be equal to temporal beings in their essential attributes, making Him like them. This would force Him to share their necessary property of temporal origination (huduth), contradicting His pre-eternity (qidam).
This shows why Allah cannot have any attribute that would make Him similar to creation.
4
Reflect on the trichotomy
Consider how this three-way classification covers all possibilities, leaving no room for a middle ground. This is a logical tool used in kalam to establish tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موصوفة بصفاتٍ كاملةٍ لا يُحاط بها ، وعلى قيامِهِ جل وعلا بنفسِهِ ، واستحالةِ مماثلتِهِ تعالى لكلِّ ما يخطر بالبال ، واستحالةِ اتصافِهِ بكلِّ ما يستلزم مماثلته للحوادثِ ، والعجز بعد هذا عن الإدراك واجب ولا يَعرِفُ اللَّهَ إلا الله جل وعلا .
•
لَعَمْرِي لَقَدْ طُفْتُ الْمَعَاهِدَ كُلَّهَا وَسَرَّحْتُ طَرْفِي بَيْنَ تِلْكَ الْمَعَالِمِ فَلَمْ أَرَ إِلَّا وَاضِعاً كَفَّ حَائِرٍ عَلَى ذَقَنٍ أَوْ قَارِعاً سِنَّ نَادِمِ(١)
قولُه : ( لأنَّ ذلكَ كلَّه يُوجِبُ مماثلته للحوادث ( أي : مساواته لها في صفاتها النفسيَّةِ ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ موجودين : إما أن يتساويا في صفاتِ النفس أو لا ، فإن تساويا فهما مِثْلانِ ، وإن لم يتساويا في صفاتِ النفس فلا يخلو : إمَّا أنْ يصح اجتماعُهما أو لا ، فإن لم يصح فضدَّانِ ، وإن صح فخلافانِ ، وكلُّ مِثْلينِ فَإِنَّهُ يلزم استواؤُهما في كلِّ ما يجب لأحدهما وفي كلّ ما يجوز عليهِ أو يستحيل ؛ فلهذا لو اتَّصف جلَّ وعلا بشيء مما سبق .. لَلَزِمَ مماثلته للأجرام أو أعراضها ، وذلك يستلزم أن يساويها فيما يجب لها مِنَ الحدوثِ ، وقد سبقَ وجوبُ قدمِهِ وبقائِهِ ، وهذا معنى قوله : ( فيجب له ما وجبَ لها ! وذلك يقدح . . . ) إلى آخره .
والاستدلال على هذا المطلب بالقياس الاقتراني ينتظمُ مِنَ الشكل الثاني ، فنقولُ : الله جل وعلا ليسَ بحادث ، وكلُّ متصف بواحدٍ مِنْ تلك الأمور المذكورة فهو حادث ؛ فينتجُ : الله جل وعلا ليس بمتصفٍ بواحدٍ مِنْ تلك الأمور المذكورة .
قلت
هذا إن أتيت بالدليل مُجملاً لجميعِها ، وإنْ فصَّلْتَهُ لكلّ واحدٍ في الأول ـ وهو استحالةُ أنْ يكونَ حِرْماً ـ : الله جلَّ وعلا ليسَ بحادث ، وكلُّ جرم فهو حادث ، فينتج : الله جل وعلا ليس بجرم ، ثم امض على ذلك إلى آخرها .
قوله : ( بل وفي كلِّ وصفٍ مِنْ أوصافِ أُلوهِيَّتِهِ ) يعني : كوجوبِ الوحدانية له جلَّ وعلا ، ووجوب نفوذ قدرتِهِ وإرادتِهِ في كلِّ ممكن ، ووجوب إحاطة علمهِ بكل معلوم ، ونحو ذلك ؛ لأنَّ هذه الأوصاف لا تجب للحوادثِ ، فكذا لا تجب لما ماثلها
Described with complete attributes that cannot be encompassed, and upon His self-subsistence, and the impossibility of His likeness to everything that occurs to the mind, and the impossibility of His being described with everything that entails His likeness to temporal things, and incapacity after this for comprehension is obligatory, and none knows Allah except Allah, the Mighty and Majestic.
•
By my life, I have toured all the institutes and roamed my eye among those landmarks; I saw only one placing his palm on his chin in bewilderment, or one gnashing his teeth in regret. (1)
His statement: (Because all of that necessitates His likeness to temporal things) means: His equality with them in their essential attributes; because every two existing things: either they are equal in essential attributes or not. If they are equal, then they are two likes; and if they are not equal in essential attributes, then it is not free from: either their coexistence is possible or not. If it is not possible, then they are two opposites; and if it is possible, then they are two differents. And every two likes, it is necessary that they be equal in everything that is necessary for one of them and in everything that is possible for it or impossible; therefore, if He, the Mighty and Majestic, were described with something of what preceded, it would necessitate His likeness to bodies or their accidents, and that necessitates His equality with them in what is necessary for them of temporal origination, and the necessity of His pre-eternity and everlastingness has preceded. And this is the meaning of His statement: (Then what is necessary for them would be necessary for Him! And that impugns...) etc.
And the inference on this matter by means of a conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) is composed of the second figure, so we say: Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, is not temporal; and everything described with one of those mentioned matters is temporal; hence the conclusion: Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, is not described with one of those mentioned matters.
I say:
This is if you bring the proof in summary for all of them; but if you detail it for each one regarding the first—which is the impossibility of being a body—: Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, is not temporal; and every body is temporal; hence the conclusion: Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, is not a body. Then proceed like that to the rest.
His statement: (Rather, in every attribute of His divinity) means: like the necessity of oneness for Him, the Mighty and Majestic, and the necessity of the penetration of His power and will in every possible thing, and the necessity of the encompassment of His knowledge of everything known, and so on; because these attributes are not necessary for temporal things, so likewise they are not necessary for what is like them.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a proof for divine transcendence (tanzih). It begins by restating the impossibility of Allah being described with attributes that would entail likeness to temporal beings. Then it quotes a line of poetry expressing bewilderment in understanding. The commentary then explains the author's statement: 'Because all of that necessitates His likeness to temporal things.' The explanation defines 'likeness' as equality in essential attributes (sifat al-nafs). It establishes a classification: any two existing beings are either equal in essential attributes (then they are 'likes' – mithlan), or not equal. If not equal, either they cannot coexist (then they are 'opposites' – diddan) or they can coexist (then they are 'differents' – khilafan). For any pair of likes, they must share all the same necessary, possible, and impossible attributes. Hence, if Allah were described with anything temporal, He would become like bodies or accidents, implying temporal origination (huduth). This contradicts His necessary pre-eternity and everlastingness. The argument is then formalized as a second-figure conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani min al-shakl al-thani): major premise – everything described with temporal attributes is temporal; minor premise – Allah is not temporal; conclusion – Allah is not described with temporal attributes. The commentator adds that one can also detail the proof for each attribute individually, e.g., Allah is not a body because bodies are temporal. Finally, the passage extends the argument to attributes of divinity (e.g., oneness, omnipotence, omniscience): since these are not necessary for temporal beings, they cannot be necessary for what is like them, i.e., any being resembling temporal beings would not necessarily possess these divine attributes. This refutes any notion that Allah could have attributes that would make Him similar to creation.

Key Terms
مماثلة
مثلان
ضدان
خلافان
صفات النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'different' (khilafan) means no relationship at all, so Allah could have some attributes in common with creation without being fully 'like'.
Correction: The classification is exhaustive: if two beings are not equal in essential attributes, they are either opposites or differents. In either case, they are not 'likes', so the rule for likes does not apply. However, the argument here is different: if Allah had any attribute that entails likeness (i.e., equality in essential attributes), then He would be a 'like' to temporal beings and would share their necessary attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what are the three possible relationships between two existing beings with respect to essential attributes?
A) Similar, different, opposite
B) Like, similar, equivalent
C) Like, opposite, different
D) Identical, contrary, distinct

Key Takeaway
Allah cannot be described with any attribute that would make Him equal to temporal beings in essential attributes, because that would force Him to share their necessary property of temporal origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you picture Allah in ways borrowed from your experience with created things? What is one specific mental image or assumption you hold about Allah that you need to repent of because it reduces His transcendence? Will you actively replace it with what the Quran teaches today?
?
If Allah were like us, He would be limited, changeable, and needy. In what area of your life are you living as if Allah is limited—as if He cannot reach you, understand you, or help you? What step will you take this week to affirm His absolute perfection and power over that situation?
?
The passage shows that any likeness to creation would make Allah temporal. Do you truly believe that Allah is completely unlike anything you know? Or do your actions and hopes reveal a hidden assumption that He operates like a powerful human? What would it look like to trust His transcendence in a specific difficulty you face today?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
6 min
The Logical Syllogism for Transcendence
Commentator presents a formal logical syllogism to prove Allah's transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second-figure conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) is used to formally prove that Allah cannot have any temporal attributes.

Core Idea
Logic formalizes the proof: Allah is not temporal, but everything temporal is not Allah.

Having established the classification of beings and the implication of likeness, the commentator now presents a formal logical proof. He uses a conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) in the second figure to demonstrate that Allah cannot be described with any attribute that entails temporal origination. This syllogism is a powerful tool in Islamic theology to express arguments concisely and conclusively.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the syllogism
Read the Arabic passage and locate the major premise, minor premise, and conclusion of the conjunctive syllogism. The commentator states the general form first, then gives a detailed example.
The second figure syllogism has the structure: major premise (Allah is not temporal), minor premise (everything described with temporal attributes is temporal), conclusion (Allah is not described with temporal attributes).
2
Understand the detailed example
The commentator illustrates the syllogism for each attribute individually, starting with the impossibility of Allah being a body. See how the same pattern applies: Allah is not temporal, every body is temporal, so Allah is not a body.
This shows the argument can be applied to any proposed attribute.
3
Connect to the broader point about divine attributes
The passage ends by extending the argument to attributes of divinity (like oneness, omnipotence, omniscience). Since these are not necessary for temporal beings, they cannot be necessary for what is like temporal beings—thus reinforcing that Allah's attributes are unique.
This final point refutes any attempt to claim Allah has attributes that would make Him similar to creation.

Commentary
This passage is a proof for divine transcendence (tanzih). It begins by restating the impossibility of Allah being described with attributes that would entail likeness to temporal beings. Then it quotes a line of poetry expressing bewilderment in understanding. The commentary then explains the author's statement: 'Because all of that necessitates His likeness to temporal things.' Hence, if Allah were described with anything temporal, He would become like bodies or accidents, implying temporal origination (huduth). This contradicts His necessary pre-eternity and everlastingness. The argument is then formalized as a second-figure conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani min al-shakl al-thani): major premise – everything described with temporal attributes is temporal; minor premise – Allah is not temporal; conclusion – Allah is not described with temporal attributes.
