Divine Transcendence
intermediate
8 min
Proof that God Transcends All Temporal Attributes
Sanusi gives a compact and detailed syllogistic proof that God has no temporal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses a categorical syllogism to demonstrate that God possesses none of the temporal attributes that define contingent beings, and that even attributes of divinity are unique to God.

Core Idea
God's perfection excludes every temporal attribute.

Sanusi now presents a crisp logical argument to establish divine transcendence. He offers both a compact and a detailed version of a syllogism that shows God cannot have any attribute that belongs to contingent beings. The argument extends even to attributes of divinity, which are unique to God and not necessary for temporal beings.

Guided Path
1
Understand the compact syllogism
Read the compact syllogism: God is not temporal; everything characterized by those attributes is temporal; therefore God is not characterized by them. Notice the logical structure.
2
See how to detail the proof for each attribute
Sanusi explains that you can apply the same syllogism individually to each attribute, starting with 'being a body' (jurm). Follow the pattern: God not temporal, every body is temporal, so God is not a body.
3
Grasp the extension to attributes of divinity
The passage explains that attributes like necessary unity, effective power, and encompassing knowledge are not necessary for temporal beings, so they cannot be attributed to God in the same way. Understand why this safeguards divine uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني ، فنقولُ : الله جل وعلا ليسَ بحادث ، وكلُّ متصف بواحدٍ مِنْ تلك الأمور المذكورة فهو حادث ؛ فينتجُ : الله جل وعلا ليس بمتصفٍ بواحدٍ مِنْ تلك الأمور المذكورة .
قلت
هذا إن أتيت بالدليل مُجملاً لجميعِها ، وإنْ فصَّلْتَهُ لكلّ واحدٍ في الأول ـ وهو استحالةُ أنْ يكونَ حِرْماً ـ : الله جلَّ وعلا ليسَ بحادث ، وكلُّ جرم فهو حادث ، فينتج : الله جل وعلا ليس بجرم ، ثم امض على ذلك إلى آخرها .
قوله : ( بل وفي كلِّ وصفٍ مِنْ أوصافِ أُلوهِيَّتِهِ ) يعني : كوجوبِ الوحدانية له جلَّ وعلا ، ووجوب نفوذ قدرتِهِ وإرادتِهِ في كلِّ ممكن ، ووجوب إحاطة علمهِ بكل معلوم ، ونحو ذلك ؛ لأنَّ هذه الأوصاف لا تجب للحوادثِ ، فكذا لا تجب لما ماثلها
Second: We say: God, may He be glorified and exalted, is not temporal. And everything characterized by one of those mentioned attributes is temporal; so it follows: God, may He be glorified and exalted, is not characterized by any of those mentioned attributes. I said: This is if you present the proof summarily for all of them. But if you detail it for each one, in the first — which is the impossibility that He be a body — [you say]: God, may He be glorified and exalted, is not temporal, and every body is temporal, so it follows: God, may He be glorified and exalted, is not a body. Then proceed in that manner to the end of them. His statement: 'Rather, in every attribute of His divinity' means: like the necessity of oneness for Him, may He be glorified and exalted, and the necessity of the penetration of His power and will in every possible, and the necessity of His knowledge encompassing everything known, and the like. Because these attributes are not necessary for temporal beings, so also they are not necessary for what resembles them.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a proof establishing divine transcendence from all temporal attributes. It begins with the second point (الثاني) of a larger argument. The author presents a categorical syllogism: God is not temporal; everything characterized by any of the mentioned attributes (which define contingent beings) is temporal; therefore, God is not characterized by any of them. Then he notes that this is the compact version; if one wants to detail each attribute individually, the first is being a body (جرم). For each, the same syllogism applies: God is not temporal; every body is temporal; so God is not a body. The same pattern continues for other attributes. Next, he explains a statement from the original matn: 'Rather, in every attribute of His divinity.' This includes attributes like the necessity of unity, the necessity of the effectiveness of His power and will over all possibles, and the necessity of His knowledge encompassing all things. The reasoning is that these attributes are not necessary for temporal beings (الحوادث), so they cannot be necessary for what resembles them—meaning God's attributes are not like those of contingents. Thus the passage covers both negative attributes (e.g., not being a body) and positive attributes of divinity, arguing that both types are not found in created things, thereby safeguarding divine uniqueness.

Key Terms
حادث
جرم
ألوهية
وجوب الوحدانية
نفوذ القدرة
إحاطة العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that attributes like 'necessary unity' could apply to God and to contingent beings in the same way.
Correction: The passage shows that these attributes are not necessary for temporal beings, so they cannot be necessary for what resembles them—meaning they are unique to God and not shared with creation.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the compact syllogism in this passage?
A) God is not temporal.
B) Everything characterized by those attributes is temporal.
C) God is not characterized by any of those attributes.
D) Every body is temporal.

Key Takeaway
God is not characterized by any attribute that belongs to temporal beings, including both negative attributes like being a body and positive attributes like necessary unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God to yourself or others, do you unconsciously compare Him to created beings? In what specific ways have you imagined God's attributes as 'like ours but bigger'? How can you uproot that assumption starting today?
?
This lesson shows that even positive attributes like 'power' and 'knowledge' as applied to God are not the same as human power and knowledge. Does your worship reflect that difference, or do you pray to a God you've recreated in your own image? What concrete step can you take to make your awe of God more real?
?
If you truly internalized that God is not characterized by any temporal attribute—not body, not location, not change—how would your daily thoughts about God change? What habit of thinking about God do you need to break right now?

The Mu'tazila Argument Against Seeing God
Sanusi relates the Mu'tazila position on the impossibility of seeing God and their argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila position that God cannot be seen and their argument based on the physical conditions of vision.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila denied vision of God because they believed sight requires physical conditions that do not apply to God.

Sanusi presents the view of some Mu'tazila who argued that God cannot be seen, just as He does not see. Their reasoning draws on the physical requirements for vision: rays, a bodily structure, and facing. This lesson unpacks their argument and sets the stage for the later refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Get a sense of the Mu'tazila's concise argument.
Notice that Sanusi mentions the later refutation.
2
Identify the conditions for vision
The Mu'tazila list three physical conditions necessary for vision. Find each one in the text.
These conditions are: connection of rays, emission from a structure, and facing.
3
Connect conditions to the conclusion
Understand why these conditions lead to the denial of seeing God. Since God is not a physical body, none of these conditions can apply.
4
Note the promised refutation
Sanusi says a fuller refutation will come later. This shows the passage is introductory.
This refutation is found later in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصارَ بعضُ المعتزلة إلى أنَّ البارىء جلَّ وعلا عما يقولُ الظالمونَ علوّاً كبيراً لا يرى ، كما أنَّهُ لا يُرى ، وهو قياس مذهبهم في اشتراط اتصال الأشعة ، وانبعاثها مِنْ بِنْيَةٍ مخصوصة ، والمقابلة أو ما في حكمها في الرؤية ، وسيأتي إنْ شاءَ الله تعالى في فضل الرؤية إبطال مذهبهم في . ذلك بأشبع قول(۱) سيأتي ( ص ٤٨٢ ) .
And some of the Mu'tazila came to hold that the Creator, exalted and high above what the wrongdoers say, is not seen, just as He does not see—and that is the analogy of their doctrine in requiring the connection of rays, their emission from a specific structure, and facing (muqabala) or what is in its ruling for vision. And it will come, God willing, in the chapter on the excellence of vision, the refutation of their doctrine in that, with the fullest statement. (1) It will come (p. 482).
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila view on the vision of God (ru'ya). They deny that God can be seen, arguing that vision requires certain physical conditions: the connection of rays (ittisal al-ash'а'), their emission from a specific bodily structure (binyah makhsusa), and facing (muqabala) or what is equivalent. Since God is not a physical body, these conditions cannot apply. The author notes that a fuller refutation of this doctrine will come later in the section on the excellence of vision. The footnote indicates that promised section is on page 482.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila denied vision of God solely on rational grounds without specific physical reasoning.
Correction: The Mu'tazila specifically argued from the physical conditions necessary for vision: rays, structure, and facing. They held that since God lacks these, He cannot be seen.

Checkpoint

A) God's transcendence makes Him invisible by essence.
B) Vision requires physical conditions like rays, structure, and facing.
C) Revelation explicitly denies it.
D) They believed God has no attributes.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila denied the possibility of seeing God because they held that vision requires physical conditions incompatible with God's non-corporeal nature.

Definition of Negative Attributes
Sanusi defines negative attributes and distinguishes wajib from permissible negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of negative attributes (al-ṣifāt al-salbiyya) and the distinction between necessary and permissible negations.

Core Idea
Negative attributes are negations of what is impossible for Allah.

In this passage, Sanusi presents two definitions of negative attributes, clarifying what they are and how they relate to divine transcendence. He distinguishes between the common understanding and the verified view (al-taḥqīq), and explains that some negations are permissible rather than obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's definitions sink in.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the common definition (كل ما يمتنع أن يوصف به البارى) and the verified definition (نفي كل ما يمتنع). Notice the shift in phrasing.
The verified view is more precise: it is about negation of what is impossible, not just what cannot be described.
3
Distinguish necessary and permissible negations
The passage notes that some negations are permissible (جائزة) rather than necessary, like His forgiveness after transgression. Understand why this is a negation of punishment.
Permissible negations are not required but are valid descriptions of Allah's actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الصفات السلبية فقالوا : إنّها عبارةٌ عن كل ما يمتنع أن يُوصف به البارى جل وعلا والتحقيق : أنَّها عبارةٌ عن نفي كل ما يمتنع ... إلى آخرِهِ ؛ وذلكَ كسلب الشريك والجسميَّةِ والعرضيَّةِ ونحو ذلك ، وقد تكون بعضُ السلوبِ جائزةً في حقَّهِ تعالى ، ومنهم مَنْ يُعبر عنها بالحدوثِ (۲) ؛ وذلك كعفوه تعالى وحلمِهِ بعدَ الجنايةِ ؛ فإنَّهُ عبارةٌ عن إسقاط العقوبة معَ تحقُّقِ الجنايةِ
As for the negative attributes, they said: they are expressions for everything that is impossible to be described of the Exalted and Glorified Creator. The verified view: they are expressions for the negation of everything that is impossible... etc.; such as negating a partner, corporeality, accidentality, and the like. Some negations may be permissible in His right, and among them are those who express them by temporality (al-ḥudūth) (2); such as His pardon and forbearance after transgression; for it is an expression for dropping punishment while the transgression is actualized.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage defines negative attributes (al-ṣifāt al-salbiyya). First, it gives the common definition: they are whatever it is impossible to describe God with. Then it gives the verified view (al-taḥqīq): they are the negation of everything impossible for Him, such as negating a partner, body, accident, etc. It notes that some negations are permissible, not necessary, and some express them by temporality (al-ḥudūth), like His forgiveness after sin, which is the dropping of punishment despite the sin being actual.

Key Terms

السلوب
الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negative attributes are only negations of imperfection and do not include permissible actions.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that some negations are permissible (jāʾiza) in Allah's right, such as His forgiveness.

Checkpoint

A) Everything that cannot be described of God
B) The negation of everything impossible for God
C) Attributes that negate each other
D) Permissible descriptions of God

Key Takeaway
Negative attributes are essentially negations of whatever is impossible for Allah, including both necessary and permissible negations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The verified view teaches that negative attributes are active negations, not mere impossibility. Will you commit today to uprooting even one such notion from your heart?

The Syllogism of Divine Essence
Sanusi presents a second-figure syllogism proving God's essence is unknowable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses a second-figure syllogism to argue that God's essence is a true particular (juz'i haqiqi) and therefore unknowable in its particularity, and how he responds to an objection about distinction by attributes.

Core Idea
God's essence is a true particular, so we cannot know it directly; we only know His attributes.

Sanusi presents a tightly reasoned syllogism to establish a key point: while we can know God's attributes, His essence remains beyond direct knowledge. He then defends this position against an objection that distinction might come from attributes rather than essence. This lesson walks through the argument and its defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the translation. Note the logical structure: premise, conclusion, objection, answer.
The argument uses second-figure syllogism, which we will examine.
2
Understand the syllogism components
Identify the major premise (nothing we know prevents participation), minor premise (His essence prevents participation), and the conclusion (so we don't know His essence).
The conclusion is that we do not know God's essence as a true particular.
3
Analyze the objection
The objection argues that God is distinguished by attributes, not necessarily by essence. Understand the nuance: the dispute is about whether distinction is by essence or attributes.
The objection also notes that we know the essence in a general sense.
4
Examine the response
Sanusi answers that the objection is merely terminological; the rational impossibility of participation is conceded by the opponent.
This clarifies that the argument is not about linguistic conventions but about real distinction in existence.
5
Apply the reasoning to your understanding of tawhid
Reflect on how this argument reinforces the uniqueness of God's essence (tawhid al-dhat) and the limits of human knowledge.
This is a foundational argument in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أيضاً : ( كلُّ ما عرفناهُ مِنْ صفاتِ اللهِ تعالى فإنَّ مفهومَهُ غيرُ مانع مِنْ وقوع الشَّرْكَةِ - يعني : لأنَّا بعد معرفة تلك الأوصاف نحتاج إلى إقامة الدليل على وحدانيته تعالى - ، ومعرفة حقيقتِهِ تعالى مانعة مِنْ وقوع الشِّرْكَةِ - يعني : لأنَّ ذاتَهُ جزئي حقيقي - ، فالمعلوم لنا منه حقیقتِهِ ) ، قال : ( وهذا قياس جليٍّ مِنَ الشكل الثاني ) . قلتُ : ووجه نظمِهِ أَنْ يُقالَ : لا شيء مما عرفناه في حقه تعالى بمانع مِنْ وقوعِ الشَّرْكةِ ، وحقيقتُه تعالى مانعةٌ مِنْ وقوع الشِّرْكَةِ ؛ ينتج : لا شيء ممَّا عرفناه بحقيقتِهِ تعالى ، وهو المطلوب . واعترض عليهِ : بأَنَّهُ لا نزاعَ أَنَّهُ تعالى مُميَّز في وجودِهِ بهذهِ الأوصاف عن سائر الموجوداتِ ، وإنَّما النزاع في أنَّ هذا التمييز تمييز بالحقيقة ، أو بأمور لازمة للحقيقة ؟ مع أنَّ الحقيقة غير معلومة لنا مِنْ حيثُ هي هي ، وإنْ كانَتْ معلومة في الجملة . فإن قالَ : إِنَّ لفظ الإله غيرُ مانع مِنَ الشَّرْكَةِ مِنْ حيثُ الوضْعُ ، وإِنْ قامَ القاطع على امتناع الشَّرْكة فيهِ عقلاً ، فهو كلي . قلنا : هذا راجع إلى اصطلاح في التسمية وتلقيبِ ، وإلا فالعلم بالتمييز في الوجودِ يستدعي امتناعَ الشِّرْكَةِ العقليَّةِ ، وقد سلَّمَ ذلك .
He also said: 'Everything we know of the attributes of Allah Most High, its concept does not prevent the occurrence of participation (shirkah) – meaning: because after knowing those descriptions we need to establish the proof of His unity; and knowledge of His essence (haqiqah) prevents the occurrence of participation (shirkah) – meaning: because His essence is a true particular (juz'i haqiqi). So what is known to us of His essence.' He said: 'This is a clear syllogism (qiyas jali) of the second figure (al-shakl al-thani).' I said: The way of its arrangement is to say: Nothing that we know concerning Him Most High prevents the occurrence of participation, and His essence prevents the occurrence of participation; the conclusion: nothing that we know is His essence, which is the desired outcome. And objection was raised against it: That there is no dispute that He Most High is distinguished (mumayyaz) in His existence by these attributes from other existents, but the dispute is whether this distinction is a distinction by the essence (al-haqiqah) or by matters necessary to the essence? While the essence is unknown to us as it is in itself, even if it is known in a general way. If he says: 'The word ilah does not prevent participation from the perspective of linguistic convention (wad'), even if a decisive proof (qati') has been established of the impossibility of participation (shirkah) in it intellectually ('aqlan), it is a universal (kulli).' We say: This reverts to a convention in naming and titling; otherwise, knowledge of distinction in existence necessitates the impossibility of intellectual participation (shirkah 'aqliyyah), and he has conceded that.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument about knowledge of God's essence and attributes, followed by an objection and its answer. The author begins by quoting an argument: everything we know of God's attributes does not prevent the possibility of multiple beings sharing that attribute (participating in it), because after knowing those attributes we still need proof of His uniqueness. However, knowledge of His very essence would prevent such participation because His essence is a true particular (unique individual). This is then cast as a clear second-figure syllogism: Major premise: Nothing we know of Him prevents participation. Minor premise: His essence prevents participation. Conclusion: Therefore, nothing we know is His essence. This proves that we do not know God's essence directly. An objection is raised: The opponent concedes that God is distinguished from other existents by His attributes, but the dispute is whether that distinction comes from the essence itself or from necessary attributes. Moreover, the essence is unknown to us in its true nature, even if known in a general sense. Then a counter-objection: Perhaps the word 'ilah' (god) is a universal term that by convention does not prevent participation, even if reason shows it is impossible for another to share divinity. The author answers: This is merely a terminological issue; in reality, the intellectual impossibility of participation is proven, and the opponent has already conceded that distinction exists in actual existence.

Key Terms
شركة (shirkah)
حقيقة (haqiqah)
جزئي حقيقي (juz'i haqiqi)
شكل ثان (second figure)
تمييز (tamyiz)
كلي (kulli)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves we cannot know God's essence at all.
Correction: The argument proves we do not know His essence as a true particular (juz'i haqiqi), but we know it in a general sense (bi al-jumlah). The passage itself mentions this: 'even if it is known in a general way'.

Checkpoint
According to the syllogism presented, what is the relationship between what we know of God's attributes and the possibility of participation (shirkah) in His essence?
A) What we know of His attributes prevents participation.
B) What we know of His attributes does not prevent participation.
C) His attributes are identical to His essence.
D) Participation is possible in His essence.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's syllogism establishes that God's essence is a true particular, making it rationally impossible for another to share in it, thus we only know His essence in a general sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly assume you can fully comprehend God's essence, or have you accepted the humbling truth that you only know Him through attributes? How would your worship change if you truly internalized that His real 'what-ness' is beyond your reach?
?
When you proclaim 'La ilaha illa Allah,' do you rely on the fact that you understand the concept, or does this syllogism force you to admit that your tawhid rests on a rational proof that you cannot fully know the One you worship? What assurance do you have that your shahada is genuine?
?
The objection in the text suggests that perhaps we only distinguish God by attributes, not essence. If that were true, your monotheism might be shaky. After reading this lesson, can you confidently say why your tawhid is secure? What would you say to someone who argues that the word 'God' is just a universal label?

Proof from the Cessation of an Attribute's Opposite
Commentator explains the proof that a new attribute's opposite must have ceased, proving the attribute is created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the cessation of an attribute's opposite proves that the attribute is created, and why this argument cannot apply to God.

Core Idea
A new attribute's opposite must cease, proving the attribute is created.

In this passage, the commentator explains a key proof used in kalam to demonstrate that newly originating attributes must be created. The argument hinges on the fact that an eternal being cannot cease to exist, so any opposite that ceases must itself be originated. By extension, any attribute that appears after its opposite has ceased is also originated. The commentator then connects this to the proof for the createdness of the world, warning that the same logic would apply to God if He had originated attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the commentator's argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: condition, implication, and cross-reference.
2
Identify the logical steps
The argument proceeds: (1) A new attribute appears; (2) its opposite must have ceased; (3) the opposite cannot be eternal because the eternal cannot cease; (4) therefore the opposite is originated; (5) hence the new attribute is originated. Find each step in the text.
Pay attention to the phrase 'طَراً عَدَمُهُ' and how it relates to the impossibility of the eternal's non-existence.
3
Understand the cross-reference
The commentator references a previous proof for the world's creation. He argues that the same logic would apply to God's attributes, but since God is eternal, He cannot have originated attributes. This shows the consistency of the proof.
This step reinforces the principle of 'طرد الدليل' (consistency of the proof).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طَراً عَدَمُهُ؛ لِمَا عَرَفْتَ مِن اسْتِحَالَةِ عَدَمِ الْقَدِيمِ، فَإِذاً لَا يُمْكِنُ الْاتِّصَافُ بِصِفَةٍ حَادِثَةٍ إِلَّا وَضِدُّهَا أَوْ مِثْلُهَا اللّذَانِ سَبَقَ الِاتِّصَافُ بِهِمَا ثُمَّ طَرَا عَدَمُهُمَا.. حَادِثَانِ؛ ضَرُورَةَ أَنَّ مَا ثُبِتَ قِدَمُهُ اسْتَحَالَ عَدَمُهُ. هَذَا مَعْنَى قَوْلِي: (وَدَلِيلُ حُدُوثِهِ) أَيْ: حُدُوثِ ضِدِّ الْوَصْفِ الْحَادِثِ (طَرَيَانُ عَدَمِهِ) يَعْنِي: بِدَلِيلِ الْاتِّصَافِ بِهَذَا الْوَصْفِ الْحَادِثِ؛ إِذْ يَسْتَحِيلُ أَنْ يُتَّصَفَ بِهِ مَعَ بَقَاءِ ضِدِّهِ الَّذِي اتَّصَفَ بِهِ قَبْلُ، وَإِلَّا اجْتَمَعَ الضِّدَّانِ. وَقَوْلُهُ: (لِمَا عَلِمْتَ مِنِ اسْتِحَالَةِ عَدَمِ الْقَدِيمِ) بَيَانٌ لِكَوْنِ طَرَيَانِ الْعَدَمِ عَلَى الضِّدِّ دَلِيلًا عَلَى وُجُوبِ حُدُوثِهِ وَاسْتِحَالَةِ قِدَمِهِ. وَقَوْلُهُ: (وَقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ مِثْلَ ذَلِكَ فِي الِاسْتِدْلَالِ عَلَى حُدُوثِ الْعَالَمِ) يَعْنِي: تَقَدَّمَ لَهُ فِي الدَّلِيلِ الثَّانِي لِحُدُوثِ الْعَالَمِ (۹)؛ حَيْثُ اسْتَدَلَّ عَلَى حُدُوثِهِ بِحُدُوثِ صِفَاتِهِ؛ أَيْ: فَلَوْ كَانَ شَيْءٌ مِنْ صِفَاتِهِ تَعَالَى حَادِثًا.. لَدَلَّ عَلَى حُدُوثِهِ كَمَا دَلَّ حُدُوثُ صِفَاتِ الْعَالَمِ عَلَى حُدُوثِهِ؛ إِذْ وَجْهُ الدَّلَالَةِ وَاحِدٌ (۲)، وَالدَّلِيلُ يَجِبُ طَرْدُهُ، فَيَسْتَحِيلُ أَنْ يُوجَدَ فِي مَوْضِعٍ وَلَا يَدُلَّ عَلَى مَدْلُولِهِ.
The coming into existence of its non-existence, because of what you know from the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal. So then it is not possible to be described with a newly originated attribute unless its opposite [or its like] which were previously described and then their non-existence occurs, are both newly originated; necessarily because what is proven eternal is impossible to cease. This is the meaning of my saying: 'And the proof of its occurring' i.e., the occurrence of the opposite of the new attribute (is the occurrence of its non-existence) meaning: by the proof of being described with this new attribute; for it is impossible to be described with it while its opposite which was previously described remains, otherwise the two opposites would coexist. And his saying: 'because of what you know from the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal' is an explanation that the occurrence of non-existence of the opposite is a proof for the necessity of its being originated and the impossibility of its eternity. And his saying: 'and there has preceded the like of that in the proof of the world's occurrence' means: there preceded him in the second proof for the world's occurrence (9) where he proved its occurrence by the occurrence of its attributes; i.e., if something from His attributes – exalted is He – were originated, it would prove His occurrence just as the occurrence of the attributes of the world proved its occurrence; since the aspect of the proof is one (2), and the proof must be consistent, so it is impossible that it exists in one place and does not indicate its conclusion.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a proof concerning contingent attributes. The commentator clarifies that when a new attribute appears in something, its opposite must have ceased to exist, and because the cessation of an eternal being is impossible, that opposite must itself be originated. This shows that the new attribute is also originated, as it depends on the opposite's non-existence. The commentator then relates this to a previous argument for the world's creation, where the occurrence of attributes in the world proved its createdness. Similarly, if any attribute of God were originated, it would imply His createdness, which is impossible. The argument relies on the principle that the same proof must apply consistently across cases.

Key Terms
طريان العدم
استحالة عدم القديم
الضدان
حدوث الصفة
طرد الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only applies to physical objects and cannot be extended to God.
Correction: The commentator explicitly extends the argument to show that if God had originated attributes, the same proof would imply His createdness, which is impossible. Therefore, God cannot have originated attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does the appearance of a new attribute in an eternal being prove that the attribute is originated?
A) Because the new attribute must be eternal itself.
B) Because the opposite of the new attribute must have ceased, and the cessation of an eternal being is impossible, so the opposite must be originated.
C) Because two opposites can coexist in an eternal being.
D) Because the new attribute is identical to the opposite.

Key Takeaway
The cessation of an attribute's opposite proves that the attribute is created, and this argument must be applied consistently, thus God cannot have originated attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof emphasizes that the eternal cannot cease. In what area of your life have you been acting as if something temporal (your status, relationships, or achievements) is eternal? What would it cost you to detach from it and anchor your security in God alone?
?
God cannot have originated attributes. How does this truth confront your tendency to imagine God as changing based on your actions or circumstances? Where do you need to repent of thinking God’s love or anger fluctuates like human emotions?
?
The proof must be applied consistently (طرد الدليل). Where in your faith have you accepted a logical argument for one doctrine but rejected the same logic for another? How does this lesson call you to intellectual integrity before God?

Refuting the Objection of Possible Freedom from Both Opposites
Commentator presents and refutes the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that a subject could be free of both an attribute and its opposite is invalid in kalam reasoning about attributes and originatedness.

Core Idea
If a subject could be free of both opposites, it could be free of all attributes—an impossibility.

This lesson tackles a sophisticated objection to a key kalam principle. The objector suggests that a subject might initially lack both an attribute and its opposite, only later acquiring one. The commentator refutes this by showing it would allow a subject to be free of all attributes it can receive, which is impossible for both contingent and necessary beings. Understanding this refutation deepens your grasp of how attributes are necessarily tied to their subjects.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: the objector challenges the necessity that a subject of an attribute cannot be free of both the attribute and its opposite. They propose it could be free of both initially, then acquire one, avoiding the conclusion that the attribute must be originated.
Notice the logical structure: if freedom from both is possible, the argument for originatedness fails.
2
Analyze the first part of the answer
The commentator argues that if freedom from both is allowed, the same capacity would permit freedom from all attributes the subject can receive, because capacity is a single essential quality. This would lead to absurdity for contingent beings, which must have spatial attributes.
Key concept: capacity (القبول) is essential and does not differ across attributes.
3
Examine the second part for the Eternal
For God, emptying Him of all attributes contradicts what His actions indicate (knowledge, power, will). Additionally, if His attributes were originated, they would require something already possessing them to cause them, leading to circularity or infinite regress.
This reinforces the necessity of eternal attributes for God.
4
Connect to the original argument
Trace how this refutation upholds the original claim that whatever is inseparable from the originated is itself originated. The objection fails because it cannot be consistently applied.
The lesson ties back to the broader kalam proof for the originatedness of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تأكيدُ أنَّ ما لازم الحادث فهو حادث ]
فإن قلت : إنَّما يتمُّ ذلكَ إذا وجبَ أَنَّ القابل للشيء لا يخلو عنه أو عن ضدّهِ ، ولِمَ لا يُقالُ بجوازِ خلوّه عنهما معاً ، ثم يطرأ الاتصافُ بهما (۱) ، فتُتحقق ذاته دونهما (٢) فلا يلزم الحدوث ؟
فالجواب : أنَّهُ لو خلا عنهما معَ قَبولِهِ لهما.. لجازَ أَنْ يخلو عن جميع ما يقبلُهُ مِنَ الصفاتِ ؛ إِذِ القَبولُ لا يختلفُ ؛ لأنَّهُ نَفسي ، وإلا لزم الدور أو التسلسل. ما يقبلُهُ مِنَ الصفاتِ محال مطلقاً في [؟] وخلو القابل عن جميع الحادث [؟] لوجوب اتصافه بالأكوان ضرورةً ، وفي القديم لوجوب اتصافه بما دلّ عليه فعله ؛ كالعلم والقدرة والإرادة ، ولو فُرِضَتْ حادثة لَلزِمَ الدورُ أَوِ التسلسل ؛ لتوقف إحداثها عليها.
Confirmation that whatever is inseparable from the originated is itself originated.
If you say: That only holds if it is necessary that the subject of a thing cannot be free of it or its opposite. Why is it not said that it is permissible for it to be free of both together, then the description with them occurs (1), so its essence is realized without them (2), and thus origination does not follow?
The answer: That if it were free of both while having capacity for them.. it would be permissible for it to be free of all the attributes it can receive; because capacity does not differ, being essential. Otherwise circularity or infinite regress would follow. What it can receive of attributes is absolutely impossible in [؟] and the subject being free of all originated things [؟] because of the necessity of its being described with primary attributes necessarily, and in the Eternal because of the necessity of its being described with what His action indicates—like knowledge, power, and will. And if they were assumed to be originated, circularity or infinite regress would follow, because their bringing-into-existence depends on them.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer within the kalam argument. The objector questions the premise that a subject cannot be free of both an attribute and its opposite; if it could be free initially and then acquire one, the attribute would not need to be originated. The answer explains that if the subject can be free of both while having the capacity for them, it could be free of all attributes it can receive, because capacity is a single, essential quality. This would lead to absurd results. For contingent beings, absolute emptiness from all accidents is impossible because they must have spatial attributes. For the Eternal God, emptying Him of all attributes would contradict what His actions imply (like knowledge and power). Moreover, if God's attributes were assumed originated, they would require for their creation something already possessing those attributes, causing circularity or infinite regress. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
الخلو عن الضدين
القبول
الدور
التسلسل
الأكوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is logically possible for a subject to be free of both opposites (attribute and its opposite) at the same time, thus avoiding the necessity of the attribute being originated.
Correction: If such freedom were possible, the same capacity would allow freedom from all attributes, which is impossible for contingent beings (they must have spatial attributes) and for God (His attributes are necessitated by His actions and cannot be originated without circularity).

Checkpoint
What is the main problem with the objection that a subject could be free of both an attribute and its opposite?
A) The subject would then have no essence.
B) The capacity for attributes is not essential.
C) It would imply the subject could be free of all attributes, which is impossible for both contingent and necessary beings.
D) The opposite would still lead to the attribute being eternal.

Key Takeaway
The objection that a subject could be free of both opposites is refuted because it would imply universal freedom from all attributes, which is impossible for contingent beings and leads to circularity for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's attributes, do you ever wish you could set some aside to make Him fit your limited understanding? What does this passage teach you about the cost of that wish?
?
The objector wanted to avoid affirming that the world is originated. Are there truths about God you resist accepting because they demand a change in how you live?
?
If God's attributes are necessary and cannot be separated from His essence, how does that challenge the way you pray or speak about Him? Will you commit today to letting His revealed attributes shape your worship, rather than your preferences?

God's Power is Not Through Intermediaries
Sanusi argues against causal intermediaries in God's actions, refuting tasalsul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming causal intermediaries in God's actions leads to infinite regress and why observed correlations do not imply real causation.

Core Idea
God's act is direct; no created means are needed for His power.

Sanusi presents a powerful argument against attributing causal efficacy to created things. If intermediaries were necessary, they would themselves need intermediaries, leading to infinite regress. Moreover, common examples like food causing satiety are mere accompaniments, not causes. This lesson unpacks the proof and its implications for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the central claim: God's power does not operate through intermediaries.
The passage begins by stating that intermediaries would lead to infinite regress.
2
Understand the infinite regress argument
Sanusi argues that supposed intermediaries are themselves contingent and originated, so they would require other intermediaries, and so on. This is an impossibility (tasalsul). Ask yourself why an infinite regress is problematic in theology.
This argument is a cornerstone of Islamic theology.
3
Examine the examples and their refutation
The passage lists examples like eating and satiety, drinking and quenching, etc. Sanusi refutes any causal link, stating these are mere accompaniments. Compare the examples to see the pattern.
The key point: existence and non-existence of the accompaniment are equal regarding the effect.
4
Reflect on the Quranic verses
The passage ends with two verses: 'Be, and it is' (Ya-Sin 82) and 'no weariness touched Us' (Qaf 38). Notice how they confirm God's direct, effortless creation. Consider the implication for divine transcendence.
The phrase 'without kaf or nun' emphasizes the immediacy of God's command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قدرته جل وعزَّ ، وذلك يؤدّي إلى التسلسل ؛ لأنَّ تلك الوسائط المقدرة هي مِنْ جملة الممكناتِ الحادثة ؛ إذْ لا يجب الوجود إلا لذاتِهِ العليَّةِ وصفاتِهِ ، فيجب أن يتوقَّفَ إيجادها أيضاً على وسائط حادثة أخرى ، ثم كذلك
وبهذا تعلمُ : أَنَّ اختيارَهُ سبحانه وتعالى لإيجادِه ممكناً معَ ممكن آخر ؛ كاختيارِهِ جلَّ وعلا إيجادَ الشَّبع معَ الأكل ، والرِّي مع الشرب ، والإحراق معَ مس النارِ ، وتفريقِ الأجزاء مثلاً معَ حدَّ السيفِ وجز والمقدور معَ القدرةِ الحادثة، ونحو ذلك مما لا يدلُّ جميعُ ذلك على أنَّ لتلك الأمور المقارنة تأثيراً فيما اقترنَتْ بهِ ، لا استقلالاً ولا معاونةً ، بل وجودها وعدمها بالنسبة إلى التأثير سواء ، وإيجاده جلَّ وعلا الممكنَ معَ ممكن يقارنه كإيجادِهِ لهُ تعالى منفرداً بدونِ مقارنة ممكن آخر ، فتعالى أنْ يكونَ فعله بواسطة أو علاج ، ﴿ إِنَّمَا أَمْرُهُ إِذَا أَرَادَ شَيْئًا أَن يَقُولَ لَهُ كُن فَيَكُونُ ﴾ [يسَ : ۸۲] ، بلا كافٍ ولا نون ، وقال جلَّ مِنْ قائل : ﴿ وَلَقَدْ خَلَقْنَا السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ وَمَا بَيْنَهُمَا فِي سِتَّةِ أَيَّامٍ وَمَا مَسَّنَا مِن لُغُوب (ق : ۳۸) أي : ما مسنا في خلقهما مِنْ تعب ، فتباركَ اللهُ ربُّ العالمينَ
His power, may He be glorified and exalted, and that leads to an infinite regress; because those supposed intermediaries are among the contingent, originated things; for existence is necessary only for His exalted Essence and His attributes, so their existence must also depend on other originated intermediaries, and so on. And by this you know: that His choice, glorified and exalted, to bring into existence a possible thing along with another possible thing—like His choice, may He be glorified and exalted, to bring about satiety with eating, quenching thirst with drinking, burning with touching fire, the separation of parts for example with the edge of the sword and cutting, and the determined with the originated power, and the like of that—all of that does not indicate that those accompanying things have any influence on what they accompany, neither independently nor by assistance; rather their existence and non-existence are equal with respect to influence. And His bringing into existence, may He be glorified and exalted, a possible thing along with a possible thing that accompanies it is like His bringing it into existence, exalted, alone without the accompaniment of another possible thing. So exalted is He above His act being through an intermediary or a means. {His command, when He wills a thing, is only to say to it 'Be', and it is} [Ya-Sin 82], without kaf or nun. And He said, may He be glorified among speakers: {And We created the heavens and the earth and what is between them in six days, and no weariness touched Us} [Qaf 38], meaning: no fatigue touched Us in creating them. So blessed be Allah, Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a proof against causal intermediaries in God's actions, specifically refuting the idea that God needs created means to bring about effects. The author begins by stating that if God's power required intermediaries, it would lead to an infinite regress (tasalsul), because those intermediaries are themselves contingent and originated, thus needing other intermediaries, and so on. Then he clarifies that observed correlations—like eating with satiety, drinking with quenching thirst, fire with burning, a sword with cutting, and a created power with its action—do not prove that these things have any causal influence, whether independent or cooperative. Their existence or non-existence is equal regarding effect; God could create the effect alone without the accompanying thing. He cites the Quranic verse showing God's command is immediate, without letters (kaf and nun), and another verse showing no fatigue in creation, proving God's act is direct and without means.

Key Terms
التسلسل (tasalsul/infinite regress)
الوسائط (intermediaries)
الممكنات (contingent things)
القدرة الحادثة (originated power)
العلاج (means)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Observing that eating leads to satiety means the eating causes the satiety.
Correction: Sanusi explains that such correlations are mere accompaniment; God creates both simultaneously without causal link.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why would affirming intermediaries in God's action lead to an infinite regress?
A) Because intermediaries are eternal, so they multiply endlessly.
B) Because intermediaries are themselves contingent and would need other intermediaries.
C) Because God cannot act through means at all.
D) Because the Quran denies intermediaries explicitly.

Key Takeaway
God's power is direct and independent of any created means or intermediaries.

God's Will is Not for a Purpose
Sanusi refutes the possibility of God's will having any purpose via a two-branched argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is rationally impossible for God's will to be directed towards any purpose, whether returning to Him or to His creation.

Core Idea
God's will is absolutely free; purpose implies deficiency or necessitation.

This lesson examines a key argument from Sanusi's creed: that attributing a purpose to God's will leads to absurdities. The passage systematically refutes both kinds of purposes—those that would benefit God and those that would benefit creation—showing that both are impossible. By working through this argument, you will appreciate the Islamic emphasis on God's absolute self-sufficiency and freedom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation to get a sense of the full argument. Let the author's flow guide you before you analyze details.
Notice that Sanusi begins with a claim and then branches into two impossibilities.
2
Identify the two types of purpose
The passage divides purposes into two categories: purposes that pertain to God Himself and purposes that pertain to His creation. See how each is introduced.
The phrase 'الغرضِ الراجع إليهِ' and 'الغرضِ الراجع إلى خلقِهِ' are the key indicators.
3
Analyze the impossibility of a self-regarding purpose
For a purpose that would benefit God, two scenarios are considered: the purpose is pre-eternal or originated. Follow the consequences of each: pre-eternal leads to necessitation, originated leads to deficiency.
This part refutes the philosophers' view that the world is pre-eternal due to God's purpose.
4
Analyze the impossibility of an other-regarding purpose
For a purpose that benefits creation, Sanusi simply states that God is not obligated to consider benefit or the most beneficial. This refutes the Mu'tazilite view.
The brevity here indicates that the fuller proof is given elsewhere.
5
Connect to divine transcendence
Reflect on how this argument upholds God's transcendence: no need, no deficiency, no compulsion. God's will is free and self-sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وعلى إرادتِهِ أنْ تكونَ لغرض ) يعني : لغرض يبعثه على إيجاد الفعل ، وهو محال في حقه تعالى ، سواء كان الغرض راجعاً إليه ، أو إلى خلقه
أما وجه الاستحالة في الغرضِ الراجع إليهِ : فلأنَّهُ إِنْ كانَ ذلكَ الغرض قديماً وجبَ قدمُ العالم ، ولزمَ الفعلُ بالإيجاب ، وجاءَ مذهب الفلاسفة ، وذلكَ ممَّا قد فرغنا مِنْ إبطالِهِ ، وإنْ كانَ حادثاً يتّصفُ بهِ بعد الإيجادِ لزم نقصُهُ وحاجته قبل إيجادِهِ أفعالَهُ التي حصلَتْ له غرضَهُ ، ولزم اتصافه بالحوادثِ لتجدُّدِ الكمالاتِ له حينئذ بواسطة خلقهِ ، وذلكَ كلُّهُ مفض إلى حدوثِهِ ، ويتعالى عن ذلكَ مَنْ لا أَوَّلَ لوجودِهِ ، الغنيُّ الذي إليهِ يفتقرُ كلُّ شيءٍ ولا يفتقر هو إلى شيء . وأما وجه الاستحالة في الغرضِ الراجع إلى خلقِهِ : فلأنَّه لا يجب عليه تعالى مراعاة صلاح ولا أصلح ، وقد تكلمنا في العقيدة على برهان استحالة الأمرين في فصلِ خلْقِ الأفعال بأتمَّ مِنْ هذا ، في محله شرحاً يزيلُ عنهُ كلَّ غطاءٍ إِنْ شاءَ الله تعالى ذلك وسنشرحُ تعالى.
His saying: 'And that His will should be for a purpose' means: a purpose that impels Him to bring about the act, and that is impossible with respect to Him, exalted, whether the purpose pertains to Him or to His creation. As for the aspect of impossibility concerning the purpose pertaining to Him: for if that purpose is pre-eternal, then the pre-eternity of the world is necessitated, and the act comes about by compulsion, and the doctrine of the philosophers arises—and that is something we have already finished refuting. And if it is originated, such that He becomes characterized by it after bringing about [the act], then His deficiency and need before bringing about His acts that attain for Him His purpose is necessitated, and His being characterized by originated things is necessitated due to the renewal of perfections for Him then through His creation—and all of that leads to His being originated. And exalted above that is He who has no beginning to His existence, the Self-Sufficient, to whom everything is in need and He is in need of nothing. And as for the aspect of impossibility concerning the purpose pertaining to His creation: because it is not obligatory upon Him, exalted, to observe what is beneficial or most beneficial. And we have spoken in the Creed about the proof of the impossibility of both matters in the chapter on the creation of actions more completely than this, in its place an explanation that removes every cover, if Allah wills, and that we will explain, exalted.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God's will cannot be for any purpose, whether the purpose returns to Him or to His creation. The author first states the claim: having a purpose that impels Him to act is impossible for God. Then he gives the argument for the impossibility of a purpose returning to God: if the purpose is pre-eternal, then the world would also be pre-eternal and the act would be necessary (not free), which is the philosophers' view—already refuted. If the purpose is originated, God would be deficient before creating, needing the act to acquire a perfection, which implies change in God and leads to His being originated—impossible. For a purpose returning to creation, it is not obligatory for God to consider benefit or the most beneficial. The author refers to a fuller explanation elsewhere.

Key Terms
الغرض
الإرادة
القديم
الحادث
الإيجاب
الفلاسفة
الصَّلاح
الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God has purposes for His actions, just like humans do.
Correction: Any purpose would imply either pre-eternity of the world or deficiency in God, both of which are impossible. God's will is absolutely free and self-sufficient.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one consequence if God's purpose for acting is pre-eternal?
A) God would be deficient.
B) The world would be pre-eternal and the act would be necessary.
C) God would need creation.
D) The purpose would be impossible to attain.

Key Takeaway
God's will is free and purposeless; any attribution of purpose contradicts His transcendence and self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself thinking that God must do something for your benefit? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what does it demand of your trust in Him?
?
When you face hardship, do you demand a purpose from God? How does the reality of God's absolute self-sufficiency free you from needing to understand His reasons?
?
If you believed that God's will is not motivated by any need, would it change how you pray or what you ask for? Examine your expectations and submit to His freedom.

God's Attributes Are Not Through Limbs
Sanusi denies physical organs for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi denies that God's attributes of hearing, sight, speech, and perception are carried out through physical organs, and how this affirms divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God hears, sees, speaks, and perceives without any physical organs.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a common anthropomorphic misconception: that God's attributes must function like human faculties, requiring physical organs. He firmly denies this and refers to the prior proof of God's incorporeality. The lesson clarifies that divine attributes are not limited by physicality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation together. Note the key attributes mentioned: hearing, sight, speech, and perception.
2
Identify the denial
Sanusi explicitly denies that these attributes are 'through a limb' (بجارحة). Underline the phrase 'بغير أذن ولا صماخ' etc.
This is the core of the lesson.
3
Connect to the broader proof
Sanusi references a prior proof of the impossibility of corporeality for God. Recall that God is not a body (jism), so He cannot have limbs.
This links to earlier lessons on God's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وعلى سمعه وبصره وكلامه وإدراكه على القولِ بهِ أَنْ تكون بجارحةٍ ) هذا راجع إلى الجميع ، وقد قدمنا البرهان على استحالة الجرمية في حقه تعالى ، فهو يسمع بغيرِ أُذن ولا صماخ ، ويرى بغير حدقةٍ ، ويتكلم بغير فم ولا لسان ، ويدركُ على القولِ
His saying: 'And that His hearing, sight, speech, and perception—according to the view affirming it—should be through a limb'—this applies to all, and we have already presented the proof of the impossibility of corporeality with respect to Him, exalted. So He hears without an ear or auditory canal, sees without a pupil, speaks without a mouth or tongue, and perceives—according to the view.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is a denial that God's attributes of hearing, sight, speech, and perception are carried out through physical organs. The author states that the claim that these attributes are through limbs is impossible for God. He refers to a previous proof of the impossibility of God being a body (jism). He then affirms that God hears without an ear or auditory canal, sees without a pupil, speaks without a mouth or tongue, and perceives (on the view that affirms perception) without a limb. The passage is a concise affirmation of divine transcendence over bodily characteristics.

Key Terms
السمع (hearing)
البصر (sight)
الكلام (speech)
الإدراك (perception)
الجارحة (limb)
الجرمية (corporeality)
الصماخ (auditory canal)
الحدقة (pupil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's hearing, sight, speech, and perception are similar to humans and require physical organs.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that God hears, sees, speaks, and perceives without any physical organs, as He is not a body.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, how does God hear?
A) Through an auditory canal.
B) Through a limb.
C) Without an ear or auditory canal.
D) Perception is not affirmed.

Key Takeaway
God's attributes of hearing, sight, speech, and perception are not dependent on any physical organs; He is free from corporeality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God hearing your dua, do you ever subconsciously picture an ear? This passage explicitly denies that. How can you train your mind to truly think of God's hearing as perfect and without any physical representation? What practical step can you take this week to purify your concept of Allah?
?
You recite the Qur'an as God's speech. If His speech requires no mouth or tongue, what does that tell you about the majesty of the Qur'an? Does your reverence for the words match the reality of their source? Pause and reflect on one verse that you will recite with deeper awareness today.
?
Many people, including believers, sometimes limit God's sight to what is visible to physical eyes. This passage says God sees without a pupil. Have you ever acted as if Allah's sight were limited, perhaps sinning in private? How does knowing that His sight is unrestricted and without any weakness change your sense of accountability right now?

Moses' Transformation After Hearing Divine Speech
Describes Moses' aversion to created sounds after hearing God's eternal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the transformative effect of hearing God's eternal speech as exemplified by Prophet Moses, and why this illustrates divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Hearing God's speech transforms the soul such that created sounds become unbearable.

This passage from Sanusi's creedal text describes a remarkable narrative about Prophet Moses after he was honored with hearing God's eternal speech. It illustrates not only the overwhelming pleasure of divine communion but also the profound change it brings to a person's perception of the world. Let us read how Moses reacted and what this teaches us about the nature of God's speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation to grasp the overall narrative.
Notice the sequence: first God grants hearing, then Moses experiences aversion.
2
Understand the sequence
Identify the two main phases: (1) God granting Moses hearing of eternal speech, (2) the aftereffect where Moses cannot bear created sounds.
3
Reflect on the cause
Ask yourself why created sounds became unbearable. The answer lies in the incomparable pleasure of divine speech.
This connects to the doctrine of God's speech being eternal and unlike any created sound.
4
Consider the theological lesson
Understand that Moses' reaction is not contempt for creation but a consequence of experiencing God's speech. Also note the mercy of God in veiling that experience so Moses could function.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سمعاً وقواه حتى أدركَ به كلامَهُ القديم ، ثم منعَهُ بعد وردَّهُ إلى ما كانَ قبل سماع كلامِهِ ، وهكذا معنى كلامه لأهل الجنَّةِ . ورُوِيَ : أنَّ موسى عليهِ السلامُ عند قدومِهِ مِنَ المناجاة كانَ يسدُّ أذنيه لئلا يسمعَ كلامَ الخلْقِ) ؛ إذْ صارَ عندَهُ كأَشدَّ ما يكونُ مِنْ أصوات البهائم المنكرةِ ، حتى لم يكن يستطيع سماعه ؛ بحِدْثانِ ما ذاقَ مِنَ اللذَّاتِ التي لا يُحاط بها ولا تُكيَّفُ . . عند سماع كلامٍ مَنْ ليس كمثلِهِ شيءٌ جلَّ وعلا ، ولولا أنه سبحانَهُ يغيّبُهُ عمَّا ذاقَ عندَ مناجاتِهِ ممَّا لا يُقدَرُ على وصفِهِ.. لَمَا أمكن أنْ يأنس إلى شيءٍ مِنَ المخلوقات أبداً ، ولَما انتفعَ  ِبهِ أحدٌ ، فسبحانَهُ مِنْ لطيف ما أوسعَ كرمَهُ وأعظم جلاله !
Hearing and his powers until he perceived by it His eternal speech, then after that He prevented him and returned him to what he was before hearing His speech, and such is the meaning of His speech to the people of Paradise. And it is narrated: that Moses, peace be upon him, upon his arrival from the intimate conversation, used to plug his ears lest he hear the speech of creatures; for it became to him like the most severe of the repulsive sounds of beasts, so that he could not bear hearing it, due to the newness of what he had tasted of pleasures that cannot be encompassed or qualified... when hearing the speech of Him Whom nothing is like, Glorified and Exalted. And were it not that He, glory to Him, distracts him from what he tasted during his intimate conversation, which cannot be described, it would never have been possible for him to find comfort in any created thing at all, nor would anyone have benefited from him. Glory be to Him, how vast is His generosity and how great is His majesty!
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a description and narration illustrating the effect of hearing God's eternal speech on Moses. It begins by summarizing that God granted Moses the ability to hear His eternal speech, then after the event, He returned him to his normal state so he could interact with creation. The author then narrates a report that after his intimate conversation with God, Moses would cover his ears to avoid hearing human speech, because the sounds of creation seemed like the harshest animal noises—so unpleasant that he could not endure them. This was due to the overwhelming pleasure he had experienced when hearing the speech of God, which is beyond human description or limitation. The author emphasizes that had God not temporarily veiled that experience from Moses, he would never have been able to tolerate any created being or be of benefit to anyone. The passage ends with praise of God's vast generosity and majesty.

Key Terms
كلام قديم (eternal speech)
مناجاة (intimate conversation)
لذات (pleasures)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Moses' aversion to created sounds implies contempt for creation; rather, it is a consequence of the incomparable pleasure of divine speech.
Correction: The text explains that his reaction stems from the intensity of the divine experience, not disdain for creation. Without God veiling that experience, he could not function.

Checkpoint
Why did Moses plug his ears after hearing God's speech?
A) Because the sounds of creation were physically painful.
B) Because he preferred silence over all sounds.
C) Because he found created speech unbearably coarse compared to divine speech.
D) Because he was commanded to do so.

Key Takeaway
The sound of God's eternal speech is so sublime that it makes all created sounds unbearable by comparison.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How would your life change if you truly believed that hearing God's speech is more pleasurable than anything in this world? Would you still find yourself longing for worldly conversations and distractions?
?
The source says that had God not veiled the experience, Moses could never bear creation. In what ways has God veiled His overwhelming presence from you so that you can function? Does this realization move you to thank Him for His mercy?
?
If the sound of God's speech made all created sounds repulsive to Moses, what does your attachment to created sounds (music, gossip, praise) reveal about your spiritual state? Are you willing to seek His speech more fully?

Preservation of Moses' Essence Before Divine Glory
Highlights the wonder that Moses' essence was not annihilated upon perceiving divine majesty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extraordinary preservation of Moses' essence during his encounter with divine glory and the necessity of divine support for such an experience.

Core Idea
Only God's own holding power preserved Moses from annihilation before His majesty.

This passage presents one of the most astonishing aspects of Moses' encounter with God: that his very essence did not dissolve into nothingness upon witnessing the divine glory. The author invites us to reflect on the sheer intensity of divine self-disclosure and the indispensable role of God's sustaining power. Let's explore what this reveals about both God's majesty and His mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's astonishment come through.
2
Identify the wonder
The passage describes the non-melting and non-annihilation of Moses' essence. Notice the phrase 'until it becomes pure non-existence' to understand the natural outcome that was prevented.
3
Understand the condition
Ask: Why did Moses' essence not dissolve? The passage answers: because God firmly established and held it back, just as He holds the heavens and earth.
This shows that divine preservation is necessary.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The comparison to holding the heavens and earth emphasizes God's absolute power and that without His direct intervention, creation cannot endure His manifestation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أعجب الأمورِ في هذا : عدم ذوبان الذاتِ مِنْ موسى عليهِ الصلاة والسلام وتلاشيها حتى تصير عدماً محضاً ؛ عندَ اطِّلاعِها مِنْ ذي الجلال على ما اطَّلَعَتْ ، لولا أنَّهُ ثَبَّتَها وأمسكها الذي أمسكَ السماوات والأرض .
And among the most astonishing matters in this: the non-melting of the essence of Moses, peace and blessings be upon him, and its dissolution until it becomes pure non-existence, upon its beholding from the Possessor of Majesty what it beheld, were it not that He had firmly established it and held it back, He who holds the heavens and the earth.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage presents a second wonder: that Moses' very being was not destroyed or annihilated when he perceived the divine majesty. The author says it is astonishing that Moses' essence did not melt away and dissolve into nothingness upon witnessing the grandeur of God. The explanation given is that it was only because God Himself held Moses together and preserved him—just as He sustains the heavens and the earth—that Moses remained intact. The point is to show the overwhelming nature of divine self-disclosure and the necessity of divine support to endure it.

Key Terms
ذات (essence)
ذوبان (melting/dissolution)
تلاشي (annihilation)
ذي الجلال (Possessor of Majesty)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses had a defect or weakness that nearly caused his annihilation.
Correction: The passage highlights the intensity of divine manifestation, not any defect; Moses was preserved solely by God's power.

Checkpoint
What is the astonishing wonder about Moses mentioned in this passage?
A) That Moses did not die upon hearing God's speech.
B) That Moses' essence did not melt or become annihilated despite his perception of divine glory.
C) That Moses was able to see God physically.
D) That Moses was returned to his normal state immediately.

Key Takeaway
Moses' preservation before God's glory is a testament to God's sustaining power alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that God's holding power alone kept Moses from annihilation. When in your life have you felt overwhelmed by circumstances, and did you truly recognize that it was God's hand sustaining you—not your own strength? How does this reshape your trust in Him?
?
If even a prophet like Moses needed God's direct preservation to withstand divine glory, what does that say about your own reliance on God in moments of spiritual encounter or fear? Are you living as though you are self-sufficient?
?
God holds the heavens and the earth, yet the same power was needed to hold Moses' essence. Do you believe God is actively holding every detail of your existence, or do you slip into thinking you are in control? What would change in your daily worship if you truly internalized this?

Defining Divine Oneness as Negation of Division and Peer
Sanusi defines oneness as negation of division and peer, then summarizes as negation of two quantities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core definition of God's oneness as the negation of division and peer, summarized by the negation of continuous and discontinuous quantity.

Core Idea
God's oneness means He cannot be divided and has no rival in divinity.

Sanusi begins by stating the meaning of God being 'one' (wāḥid). After the preliminary classification of unity types, he now defines it as a double negation: God does not accept division, and there is no peer for Him in divinity. He then condenses this into the negation of two kinds of quantity—continuous (length, width, depth) and discontinuous (number). This sets the foundational vocabulary for the rest of the discussion on tawḥīd.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening definition
Read the Arabic phrase stating the meaning of oneness: 'أنَّ المرادَ مِنْ كونه جل وعلا واحداً نفي قبولِهِ الانقسام ، ونفي نظير له في الألوهية'. Notice that it is a negation of two things.
The word 'نفي' appears twice – pay attention to what is denied.
2
Identify the summary
Look for the word 'وحاصله' which means 'its summary'. It follows the definition and reduces it to 'نفي الكمية المتصلة، والكمية المنفصلة'.
These are technical terms: continuous quantity and discontinuous quantity.
3
Reflect on the linkage
Ask yourself: how does negating division and negating a peer correspond to negating the two quantities? This connection will be clarified in the commentary.
Think of continuous quantity as internal division (parts) and discontinuous quantity as external peers (multiple individuals of the same kind).
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to ensure you grasp the 'sum' of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذا فاعلم : أنَّ المرادَ مِنْ كونه جل وعلا واحداً نفي قبولِهِ الانقسام ، ونفي نظير له في الألوهية . جميع وحاصله : نفي الكميَّةِ المتصلة ، والكمية المنفصلة . وفي معنى نفي نظير له تعالى في الألوهِيَّةِ : نفْيُ شريك معَهُ في الممكناتِ ، فلا مُؤثَّرَ في جميعها سواه ، فهو الواحد في ذاتِهِ ؛ أي : غيرُ مُؤلَّفٍ مِنْ جزأين فأكثرَ ، والواحد في صفاتِهِ ، فلا مثلَ لهُ ولا نظير ، والواحد في أفعالِهِ ، فلا شريكَ لهُ فيها ولا ضدَّ ولا وزير وليسَتِ الوَحدةُ الثابتة لذاتِهِ تعالى بمعنى تناهيه في الدقة والصغرِ إلى حد لا ينقسمُ ! وإلا لزمَ أنْ يكونَ جوهراً فرداً ، ولا بمعنى أنَّهُ معنى مِنَ المعاني ؛ لأنَّ المعاني لا تقبلُ الانقسام ، وإلا لزمَ أنْ يكونَ صفةً غير قائم بنفسه ، بل محتاجاً إلى محل يقوم به ، وقد سبق استحاله ذلك في حقَّهِ تعالى
And if you have understood this, then know: that the meaning of His being, exalted and high, one (wāḥid) is the negation of His accepting division, and the negation of any peer (naẓīr) for Him in divinity. And its sum is: the negation of continuous quantity (al-kammiyya al-muttaṣila) and discontinuous quantity (al-kammiyya al-munfaṣila). And in the meaning of the negation of a peer for Him, exalted, in divinity is: the negation of a partner with Him in the possible things, so there is no effecter (mu’aththir) among them all other than Him. So He is the one in His essence (dhātihi): i.e., not composed of two parts or more, and the one in His attributes: so there is no likeness (mithl) nor peer (naẓīr) for Him, and the one in His actions: so there is no partner (sharīk) for Him in them, nor opponent (ḍidd) nor vizier (wazīr). And the unity (al-waḥda) established for His essence, exalted, is not in the sense of His fineness and smallness to the extent that He cannot be divided! For then He would have to be an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), nor in the sense that He is a meaning (maʿnā) among meanings, because meanings do not accept division, otherwise He would have to be an attribute not self-subsisting, but needing a substrate (maḥall) to subsist in, and the impossibility of that with respect to Him, exalted, has preceded.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage defines God's oneness (waḥdāniyya) after the preliminary classification of types of unity. It is a definition passage. The author states that the intended meaning of God being 'one' is twofold: (1) negation of Him accepting division, and (2) negation of any peer (naẓīr) for Him in divinity. He then summarizes this as the negation of two types of quantity: continuous quantity (kammiyya muttaṣila) i.e., length, width, depth, and discontinuous quantity (kammiyya munfaṣila) i.e., number. Next, he elaborates on the meaning of negating a peer: it includes negating a partner (sharīk) in all possible things, so that no other being has independent effect in existence; only God is the true agent. Consequently, God is one in His essence (dhāt)—not composed of parts; one in His attributes—no one shares His attributes; and one in His actions—no partner, opponent, or vizier. The author then clarifies two misconceptions: God's unity is not like the indivisibility of a very small particle, for that would make Him a composed atomic substance; nor is it like the simplicity of an abstract meaning, for such meanings are attributes dependent on a subject, whereas God is self-subsistent. Both are impossible for God, as earlier proofs established.

Key Terms
الوحدانية
الكمية المتصلة
الكمية المنفصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi identify as the 'sum' (ḥāṣil) of the meaning of God's oneness?
A) Negation of a peer in divinity only
B) Negation of continuous and discontinuous quantity
C) Negation of division only
D) Affirmation of simplicity

Key Takeaway
God's oneness is defined as the denial of any internal division (continuous quantity) and any external rival (discontinuous quantity).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat God as divisible, giving parts of your heart to worldly pursuits as if they were separate from His claim on you? How does the negation of division challenge your divided loyalties?
?
If God has no peer, then your ultimate allegiance is due to Him alone. Is there any person, desire, or goal you secretly elevate to a peer of God in your daily choices? What must you change to make His oneness real in your actions?
?
The passage defines oneness as the negation of any rival in divinity. Have you ever acted as if some created thing—status, wealth, or relationship—could rival God's importance in your life? What concrete step will you take today to affirm that He alone is worthy?

Divine Transcendence

The Three Dimensions of God's Oneness: Essence, Attributes, Actions
Sanusi elaborates the negation of a peer into three aspects of oneness: essence, attributes, and actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three aspects of divine oneness—essence, attributes, and actions—and how each aspect negates a specific form of multiplicity or partnership.

Core Idea
God is one in essence, attributes, and actions—no composition, no likeness, no partner.

In the previous lesson, Sanusi gave the general definition of oneness. Now he unpacks the negation of a peer by explaining three specific dimensions: oneness of essence, oneness of attributes, and oneness of actions. This elaboration shows that tawḥīd is not merely abstract but covers every aspect of God's reality. He also emphasizes that only God is the true effecter in all possible things, rejecting any notion of independent secondary causality.

Guided Path
1
Examine the three 'one in' phrases
In the Arabic, find the three phrases: 'فهو الواحد في ذاته', 'والواحد في صفاته', 'والواحد في أفعاله'. Notice that each is followed by an explanation (e.g., 'أي غير مؤلف من جزأين فأكثر').
These three aspects cover all dimensions of God's being.
2
Understand the negation of peer as negation of partner in creation
Sanusi states that negating a peer means negating a partner in 'الممكنات' (possible things) and that no effecter (مؤثر) other than God exists. This denies any independent agent besides God.
This is a key point: all causal power belongs to God alone.
3
Differentiate the three negations
For essence: composition is negated. For attributes: likeness and peer are negated. For actions: partner, opponent, and advisor are negated. Identify the different categories denied.
Each negation addresses a potential form of multiplicity.
4
Connect to the broader argument
Reflect on how this threefold division strengthens the case for tawḥīd. It leaves no room for any share in God's unique nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذا فاعلم : أنَّ المرادَ مِنْ كونه جل وعلا واحداً نفي قبولِهِ الانقسام ، ونفي نظير له في الألوهية . جميع وحاصله : نفي الكميَّةِ المتصلة ، والكمية المنفصلة . وفي معنى نفي نظير له تعالى في الألوهِيَّةِ : نفْيُ شريك معَهُ في الممكناتِ ، فلا مُؤثَّرَ في جميعها سواه ، فهو الواحد في ذاتِهِ ؛ أي : غيرُ مُؤلَّفٍ مِنْ جزأين فأكثرَ ، والواحد في صفاتِهِ ، فلا مثلَ لهُ ولا نظير ، والواحد في أفعالِهِ ، فلا شريكَ لهُ فيها ولا ضدَّ ولا وزير وليسَتِ الوَحدةُ الثابتة لذاتِهِ تعالى بمعنى تناهيه في الدقة والصغرِ إلى حد لا ينقسمُ ! وإلا لزمَ أنْ يكونَ جوهراً فرداً ، ولا بمعنى أنَّهُ معنى مِنَ المعاني ؛ لأنَّ المعاني لا تقبلُ الانقسام ، وإلا لزمَ أنْ يكونَ صفةً غير قائم بنفسه ، بل محتاجاً إلى محل يقوم به ، وقد سبق استحاله ذلك في حقَّهِ تعالى
And if you have understood this, then know: that the meaning of His being, exalted and high, one (wāḥid) is the negation of His accepting division, and the negation of any peer (naẓīr) for Him in divinity. And its sum is: the negation of continuous quantity (al-kammiyya al-muttaṣila) and discontinuous quantity (al-kammiyya al-munfaṣila). And in the meaning of the negation of a peer for Him, exalted, in divinity is: the negation of a partner with Him in the possible things, so there is no effecter (mu’aththir) among them all other than Him. So He is the one in His essence (dhātihi): i.e., not composed of two parts or more, and the one in His attributes: so there is no likeness (mithl) nor peer (naẓīr) for Him, and the one in His actions: so there is no partner (sharīk) for Him in them, nor opponent (ḍidd) nor vizier (wazīr). And the unity (al-waḥda) established for His essence, exalted, is not in the sense of His fineness and smallness to the extent that He cannot be divided! For then He would have to be an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), nor in the sense that He is a meaning (maʿnā) among meanings, because meanings do not accept division, otherwise He would have to be an attribute not self-subsisting, but needing a substrate (maḥall) to subsist in, and the impossibility of that with respect to Him, exalted, has preceded.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage defines God's oneness (waḥdāniyya) after the preliminary classification of types of unity. It is a definition passage. The author states that the intended meaning of God being 'one' is twofold: (1) negation of Him accepting division, and (2) negation of any peer (naẓīr) for Him in divinity. He then summarizes this as the negation of two types of quantity: continuous quantity (kammiyya muttaṣila) i.e., length, width, depth, and discontinuous quantity (kammiyya munfaṣila) i.e., number. Next, he elaborates on the meaning of negating a peer: it includes negating a partner (sharīk) in all possible things, so that no other being has independent effect in existence; only God is the true agent. Consequently, God is one in His essence (dhāt)—not composed of parts; one in His attributes—no one shares His attributes; and one in His actions—no partner, opponent, or vizier. The author then clarifies two misconceptions: God's unity is not like the indivisibility of a very small particle, for that would make Him a composed atomic substance; nor is it like the simplicity of an abstract meaning, for such meanings are attributes dependent on a subject, whereas God is self-subsistent. Both are impossible for God, as earlier proofs established.

الواحد في الأفعال

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does 'oneness of essence' mean?
A) God is extremely small and indivisible
B) God is not composed of two or more parts
C) God has no partner in His actions
D) God's attributes are many

Key Takeaway
God's oneness is expressed in three dimensions: essence (no parts), attributes (no equal), and actions (no partner).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Today, identify one situation where you acted out of fear of people rather than trust in God. What would it look like to surrender that area to Him alone?
?
If God's essence is not composed of parts—if He is utterly simple and perfect—then your self-worth cannot come from being 'partly good' or 'partly faithful.' How has the belief that you are fragmented or incomplete stopped you from turning wholly to God? Will you ask Him to heal your divided heart and make you single-minded in devotion?
?
God has no partner in His actions—not even your closest ally or your greatest enemy can overrule His decree. Recall a recent disappointment or injustice. Whom did you blame? Will you now confess that no human or jinn is a 'opponent' (ضد) of God, and accept that all that happened came from the One who has no vizier? How would that change your response to hardship?
