Two Misconceptions About God's Unity Rejected
Sanusi rejects two false interpretations of divine unity: atomistic indivisibility and abstract meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two common misconceptions about divine unity—that it implies extreme smallness or abstract attribute-like simplicity—and why both are impossible for God.

Core Idea
God's unity is not like an indivisible atom nor like an abstract meaning depending on a substrate.

Having defined God's oneness as negation of division and peer, Sanusi now clarifies what it does NOT mean. Some might think that 'one' means something extremely small that cannot be divided (like an atom), or that it means an abstract quality (like an accident) that inheres in a subject. Sanusi rejects both, showing that they would imply composition or dependence, which are impossible for God. This final part safeguards the correct understanding of tawḥīd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rejected meanings
Read the Arabic: 'وليسَتِ الوَحدةُ الثابتة لذاتِهِ تعالى بمعنى تناهيه في الدقة والصغرِ إلى حد لا ينقسمُ ... ولا بمعنى أنَّهُ معنى مِنَ المعاني'. Two alternative interpretations are explicitly denied.
Pay attention to the 'لا بمعنى' (not in the sense of) constructions.
2
Understand the first misconception: atomism
Sanusi warns that unity does not mean extreme fineness making God indivisible like a 'جوهر فرد' (indivisible atom). That would imply composition (jism) or being a substance composed of atoms.
Recall that earlier proofs established God is not a body.
3
Understand the second misconception: accidental meaning
Unity is not like an abstract meaning (معنى) because meanings do not divide, but they are attributes that need a subject. That would make God an accident, not self-subsistent.
God is qā'im bi-nafsih (self-subsisting), not needing a substrate.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
Sanusi notes that the impossibility of both has been proven earlier. This reinforces the correct understanding of tawḥīd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذا فاعلم : أنَّ المرادَ مِنْ كونه جل وعلا واحداً نفي قبولِهِ الانقسام ، ونفي نظير له في الألوهية . جميع وحاصله : نفي الكميَّةِ المتصلة ، والكمية المنفصلة . وفي معنى نفي نظير له تعالى في الألوهِيَّةِ : نفْيُ شريك معَهُ في الممكناتِ ، فلا مُؤثَّرَ في جميعها سواه ، فهو الواحد في ذاتِهِ ؛ أي : غيرُ مُؤلَّفٍ مِنْ جزأين فأكثرَ ، والواحد في صفاتِهِ ، فلا مثلَ لهُ ولا نظير ، والواحد في أفعالِهِ ، فلا شريكَ لهُ فيها ولا ضدَّ ولا وزير وليسَتِ الوَحدةُ الثابتة لذاتِهِ تعالى بمعنى تناهيه في الدقة والصغرِ إلى حد لا ينقسمُ ! وإلا لزمَ أنْ يكونَ جوهراً فرداً ، ولا بمعنى أنَّهُ معنى مِنَ المعاني ؛ لأنَّ المعاني لا تقبلُ الانقسام ، وإلا لزمَ أنْ يكونَ صفةً غير قائم بنفسه ، بل محتاجاً إلى محل يقوم به ، وقد سبق استحاله ذلك في حقَّهِ تعالى
And if you have understood this, then know: that the meaning of His being, exalted and high, one (wāḥid) is the negation of His accepting division, and the negation of any peer (naẓīr) for Him in divinity. And its sum is: the negation of continuous quantity (al-kammiyya al-muttaṣila) and discontinuous quantity (al-kammiyya al-munfaṣila). And in the meaning of the negation of a peer for Him, exalted, in divinity is: the negation of a partner with Him in the possible things, so there is no effecter (mu’aththir) among them all other than Him. So He is the one in His essence (dhātihi): i.e., not composed of two parts or more, and the one in His attributes: so there is no likeness (mithl) nor peer (naẓīr) for Him, and the one in His actions: so there is no partner (sharīk) for Him in them, nor opponent (ḍidd) nor vizier (wazīr). And the unity (al-waḥda) established for His essence, exalted, is not in the sense of His fineness and smallness to the extent that He cannot be divided! For then He would have to be an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), nor in the sense that He is a meaning (maʿnā) among meanings, because meanings do not accept division, otherwise He would have to be an attribute not self-subsisting, but needing a substrate (maḥall) to subsist in, and the impossibility of that with respect to Him, exalted, has preceded.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage defines God's oneness (waḥdāniyya) after the preliminary classification of types of unity. It is a definition passage. The author states that the intended meaning of God being 'one' is twofold: (1) negation of Him accepting division, and (2) negation of any peer (naẓīr) for Him in divinity. He then summarizes this as the negation of two types of quantity: continuous quantity (kammiyya muttaṣila) i.e., length, width, depth, and discontinuous quantity (kammiyya munfaṣila) i.e., number. Next, he elaborates on the meaning of negating a peer: it includes negating a partner (sharīk) in all possible things, so that no other being has independent effect in existence; only God is the true agent. Consequently, God is one in His essence (dhāt)—not composed of parts; one in His attributes—no one shares His attributes; and one in His actions—no partner, opponent, or vizier. The author then clarifies two misconceptions: God's unity is not like the indivisibility of a very small particle, for that would make Him a composed atomic substance; nor is it like the simplicity of an abstract meaning, for such meanings are attributes dependent on a subject, whereas God is self-subsistent. Both are impossible for God, as earlier proofs established.

المعنى
الصفة
المحل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think God's oneness means He is extremely small and indivisible, like an atom, or that He is an abstract quality like an accident.
Correction: Sanusi rejects both: being an atom implies composition, and being an attribute implies dependence. Both contradict God's necessary existence and self-sufficiency.

Checkpoint

D) Because meanings are multiple

Key Takeaway
God's oneness is not like the indivisibility of an atom nor the simplicity of an attribute; it is a unique negation of any composition or dependence.

The Incapacity of Intellects and the Longing of the Elect
Sanusi describes intellect's incapacity and quotes a Sufi poem on longing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how rational theology (kalam) leads to the recognition of reason's limits and the soul's longing for direct encounter with the divine.

Core Idea
Reason unveils its own limits, and longing begins where intellect ends.

After establishing divine attributes through rational proof, Sanusi now turns to what reason cannot achieve: full perception of the divine essence. This lesson presents a dramatic shift from discursive argument to ecstatic longing, culminating in a poem by the Sufi saint Abu Madyan that uses the metaphor of a caged bird to describe the soul's yearning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the full translation. Notice the shift in tone from analytical prose to poetic ecstasy.
The passage includes editorial notes; focus on the main text and poem.
2
Identify the key transition
Locate where Sanusi moves from describing the intellect's failure to describing the soul's longing. Mark the phrase 'then intellects became incapable' and the description of fluttering behind veils.
This transition is the doctrinal pivot of the lesson.
3
Analyze the poem's metaphor
Read the poem by Abu Madyan. Identify the metaphor of the caged bird and how it illustrates the soul's longing for the divine. Notice the references to 'intoxication' (sukr) and the removal of obligation.
The poem uses concrete imagery to express spiritual realities.
4
Reflect on the integration of kalam and Sufism
Ask yourself: why does Sanusi, a kalam scholar, include a Sufi poem? Discuss how this shows that rational theology can lead to spiritual experience, not away from it.
This lesson bridges two disciplines often seen as separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عَجَزَتِ العقول بعد عن الإدراكِ ، وانقطع تشرُّفُها للخوض فيما خرج عن دائرة التوهمات والتخيلات وقُصارى أمرِها (١) : أنَّها صارَتْ مِنْ أجلِ اللمحة التي لَحَظَتْ ، والرَّمزةِ التي بها غابَتْ عن العوالم كلها وفيها تاهَتْ وبها وَلِهَتْ.. تتطايرُ مِنْ وراءِ حُجُبِ الكبرياء وأردية العزّ ؛ شوقاً إلى ما لا يُكيَّفُ مِنْ جميل اللقاء ، وتتنسَّمُ مِنْ مواهب الزيادةِ لكشف الغطاء ، ما تُروِّحُ بهِ على القلب المحترق الأحشاء ، وربَّما عظُمَ الشوقُ بلطْفِ نسيم المزيدِ ، فشطحَتِ الذواتُ شَطْحاً طارَتْ بِهِ الرُّوحُ عن سجن الجسدِ ، واتصلت بما لا نهاية لزيادة نعيمه على طولِ الأبدِ (١) وللـولـي القطب الـجـامـع أبـي مـديـن رضي الله عنـه فـي هـذا المعنى : (من الطويل) قُلْ لِلَّذِي يَنْهَى عَنِ الْوَجْدِ أَهْلَهُ إِذَا لَمْ تَذُقْ مَعْنَا شَرَابَ الْهَوَى دَعْنَا إِذَا اهْتَزَّتِ الْأَرْوَاحُ شَوْقاً إِلَى اللَّقَا نَعَمْ تَرْقُصُ الْأَشْبَاحُ يَا جَاهِلَ الْمَعْنَى أَمَا تَنْظُرُ الطَّيْرَ الْمُقَفَّصَ يَا فَتَى إِذَا ذَكَرَ الْأَوْطَانَ حَنَّ إِلَى الْمَغْنَى فَفَرَّجَ بِالتَّغْرِيدِ مَا بِفُؤَادِهِ فَتَضْطَرِبُ الْأَعْضَاءُ بِالْحِسُ وَالْمَعْنَى وَيَرْقُصُ فِي الْأَقْفَاصِ شَوْقاً إِلَى اللُّقَا فَيَهْتَزُّ أَرْبَابُ الْعُقُولِ إِذَا غَنَّى كَذَلِكَ أَرْوَاحُ الْمُحِيِّينَ يَا فَتَى تُهَزْهِزُهَا الْأَشْوَاقُ لِلْعَالَمِ الْأَسْنَى أَنَلزَمُهَا بِالصَّبْرِ وَهْيَ مَشُوقَةٌ فَهَلْ يَسْتَطِيعُ الصَّبْرَ مَنْ شَاهَدَ الْمَعْنَى فَيَا حَادِيَ الْعُشَّاقِ قُمْ وَأَحَدُ قَائِماً وَزَمْزِمْ لَنَا بِاسْمِ الْحَبِيبِ وَرَوْحْنَا وَصُنْ سِرَّنَا فِي سُكْرِنَا عَنْ حَسُودِنَا وَإِنْ أَنْكَرَتْ عَيْنَاكَ شَيْئاً فَسَامِحْنَا فَإِنَّا إِذَا طِبْنَا وَطَابَتْ عُقُولُنَا وَخَامَرَنَا خَمْرُ الْغَرَامِ تَهَيَّكْنَا فَلا نَلِ السَّكْرَانَ فِي حَالِ سُكْرِهِ فَقَدْ رُفِعَ التَّكْلِيفُ فِي سُكْرِنَا عَنَّا (١) في هامش (ب) : ( هذا الكلام على طريق أهل التصوف رضي الله تعالى عنهم ) . (٢) ومطلع هذه القصيدة : تضيقُ بنا الدنيا إذا غبتموا عنا وتذهب بالأشواق أرواحنا منَّا (٣) كذا بالعين المهملة في النسخ، ولو كانت بالمعجمة لحسنت أيضاً
Then intellects became incapable of perception, and their honor of delving into what falls outside the sphere of imaginations and representations ceased, and the utmost of their affair (1) is that they became, because of the glance they perceived and the sign by which they disappeared from all worlds and in which they wandered and by which they were captivated, fluttering from behind the veils of majesty and the mantles of glory, longing for the incomparable beauty of the encounter, and breathing in from the gifts of increase to remove the covering, what refreshes the heart burning in its bowels, and perhaps the longing intensifies with the gentle breeze of abundance, so the selves soared with a soaring that carried the spirit out of the prison of the body, and they connected with what has no end of increase of bliss throughout eternity (1). And for the saint, the universal pole, Abu Madyan, may God be pleased with him, on this meaning: (from ṭawīl meter): Say to the one who forbids his people from ecstasy: If you have not tasted with us the wine of love, leave us. When spirits tremble with longing for the encounter, yes, bodies dance, O ignorant of the meaning. Do you not see the caged bird, O youth, when it remembers its homeland, it yearns for the abode? Then it releases what is in its heart by chirping, so limbs tremble by sense and meaning, and it dances in the cage, longing for the encounter, so the people of intellects are moved when it sings. Likewise, the spirits of the lovers, O youth, are shaken by longings for the highest realm. Do I constrain them with patience while they are longing? Can the one who has witnessed the meaning be patient? O driver of lovers, arise and be resolute, and hum for us with the name of the beloved and refresh us, and guard our secret in our intoxication from the envious. And if your eyes deny something, forgive us. Indeed, when we become pure and our intellects become pure, and wine of passion pervades us, we are enraptured. So do not blame the drunk in his state of intoxication, for obligation is removed from us in our intoxication. (1) In the margin of (b): (This speech is according to the way of the Sufis, may God be pleased with them.) (2) The beginning of this poem: 'The world narrows for us when you are absent from us, and with longings our spirits depart from us.' (3) Thus with the undotted 'ayn in the copies, and if it were dotted (ghayn) it would also be good.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage is a descriptive and poetic culmination of the previous rational discourse. After establishing divine attributes by proof, the author turns to the incapacity of intellects to fully grasp the divine essence. The 'utmost of their affair' is that they become lost in the contemplation of a single glance, fluttering behind the veils of divine majesty, longing for an incomparable encounter. The language becomes increasingly ecstatic: the self soars (shaṭaḥa) out of the prison of the body and connects to infinite bliss. Then the author quotes the Sufi saint Abu Madyan's poem, which uses the metaphor of a caged bird yearning for its homeland to illustrate the soul's longing for the divine. The poem argues that those who have experienced the 'meaning' (al-maʿnā) cannot be patient, and in the state of spiritual intoxication (sukr), religious obligations are lifted (a reference to the condition of the entranced). The editorial notes clarify that this section is in the Sufi tradition. This lesson bridges kalam and taṣawwuf, showing that rational proof leads to a stage where reason yields to direct spiritual experience. The author does not reject rational proofs but presents them as a step toward a higher longing.

Correction: The passage argues that intellects become incapable of perception beyond a certain point; their highest achievement is a longing glance, and true understanding requires spiritual longing and encounter.

Proof of Oneness: The Impossibility of a Second God
Sanusi states the necessity of oneness and the first consequence of a second god.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial step of Sanusi's proof: why the existence of a second god would lead to unavoidable inability if they disagree.

Core Idea
If there were two gods, disagreement would paralyze both.

Sanusi begins the proof of oneness by stating that the Maker must be one. He then presents a conditional: if there were a second, then any disagreement between them would result in the inability of both or at least one. This shows that a second god is incompatible with divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Sanusi states the conclusion first, then gives the conditional argument.
The passage is compressed; focus on the logical structure.
2
Understand the conditional structure
Identify the 'if... then...' structure: if a second existed and they disagreed, then inability follows. Why must inability be necessary?
Disagreement between two omnipotent wills would mean one cannot fulfill its will, indicating limitation.
3
Contemplate the implication of disagreement
Consider that any disagreement (even possible) would imply a defect. This shows that a perfect being cannot have a partner.
The proof covers both necessary and possible disagreement, as shown later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقرير دليل الوحدانية ]
ثم نقولُ : يجب لهذا الصانع أن يكون واحداً ؛ إذ لو كانَ مَعَهُ ثانٍ لَلزِمَ عجزُهما أو عجز أحدهما عند الاختلافِ ، وقهرهما أو قهرُ أحدهما عند الاتفاق الواجب ، مع استحالة ما عُلِمَ إمكانُهُ لكلّ واحدٍ باعتبار الانفراد (١) ، ونفي وجوب الوجودِ لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما ؛ للاستغناء بكلِّ منهما عن كلِّ منهما ، فإن لم يجب اتفاقهما بل جازَ اختلافهما .. لزمَ قبولُهما العجز ، وعادَ الأَوَّلُ .
(١) لأنه إذا توجّه أحدهما إلى تسكين زيد مثلاً ، والفرض : أن الآخر يجب عليه موافقة صاحبه .. كان التحريك في جانبه مستحيلاً ، وقد كان ممكناً في حقه قبل أن يتوجه صاحبه إلى التسكين . ( حامدي » ( ص٣٠٨ ) .
Establishment of the proof of oneness. Then we say: It is necessary for this Maker to be one; for if there were with Him a second, then the inability of both or the inability of one of them would be necessary in the case of disagreement, and the overpowering of both or the overpowering of one of them would be necessary in the case of the obligatory agreement, given the impossibility of what was known to be possible for each one considered individually, and the negation of the necessity of existence for each of them; because each would be independent of the other. If their agreement is not necessary but their disagreement is possible, then the acceptance of inability would be necessary for both, and the first [argument] returns. (1) Because if one of them intends to still Zayd, for example, and the assumption is that the other must agree with his companion, then movement on his part would be impossible, even though it was possible in his right before his companion intended to still.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the oneness of God (tawhid). The author argues that if there were two gods, then either they would agree or disagree. If they disagree, the inability of both or at least one is necessary, implying incapability. If they must obligatorily agree, then one or both would be overpowered, negating their independence. Moreover, each would be independent of the other, which negates the necessity of existence for each (since a necessary being cannot be independent of another necessary being). Finally, if their agreement is not necessary but disagreement is possible, then both must accept inability, returning to the first case. The footnote illustrates: if one intends to still Zayd, and the other must agree, then the second cannot cause movement (which was possible before), showing incapability.

Key Terms
واحد
صانع
ثان
عجز
اختلاف

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the logical outcome if two gods disagree?
A) Both become incapable.
B) One overpowers the other.
C) Both remain capable.
D) The universe is destroyed.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of disagreement between two gods proves their inability, contradicting divine perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that no other power can frustrate God's will, would you be as anxious as you are about the opinions and actions of others? Where do you place your trust when you fear human opposition?
?
When your own desires conflict with God's command, do you see that conflict as a proof that you are not a god? How does that realization humble you and drive you to submit?
?
If the possibility of disagreement between two gods proves they cannot exist, what does that say about your own tendency to attribute ultimate control to multiple sources in your life (wealth, health, people)? Would you be willing to consciously surrender those false 'gods' today?

Proof of Oneness: Agreement and Independence
Sanusi completes the proof by analyzing agreement, independence, and the return to inability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why even agreement between two gods would imply overpowering or dependence, negating their necessity.

Core Idea
Even if two gods agree, one must be overpowered or dependent.

Having covered disagreement, Sanusi now examines the case of agreement and the logical consequences of two independent necessary beings. Even if they always agree, one must be subjugated, which contradicts independence. Moreover, if agreement is not necessary, the problem of inability returns. The footnote illustrates the absurdity of forced agreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the complete passage
Read the Arabic and translation for this lesson. Notice it covers three sub-arguments: agreement, independence, and the return to inability.
The footnote gives a concrete example to clarify the agreement scenario.
2
Analyze the agreement argument
If two gods must always agree, then one is effectively overpowered (قهر) because its will is constrained by the other's. This shows dependence, not absolute independence.
Obligatory agreement implies one god's will is subordinate.
3
Understand the independence argument
Each god would be self-sufficient, so neither needs the other. This negates the necessity of existence for each, because a necessary being cannot be independent of another necessary being.
The argument is: if two necessary beings exist, each is independent, but that implies each is not necessary for the other—contradicting necessity.
4
Examine the third scenario
If agreement is not necessary, then disagreement is possible, leading back to inability. Thus, all possibilities are exhausted.
This covers the logical space comprehensively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقرير دليل الوحدانية ]
ثم نقولُ : يجب لهذا الصانع أن يكون واحداً ؛ إذ لو كانَ مَعَهُ ثانٍ لَلزِمَ عجزُهما أو عجز أحدهما عند الاختلافِ ، وقهرهما أو قهرُ أحدهما عند الاتفاق الواجب ، مع استحالة ما عُلِمَ إمكانُهُ لكلّ واحدٍ باعتبار الانفراد (١) ، ونفي وجوب الوجودِ لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما ؛ للاستغناء بكلِّ منهما عن كلِّ منهما ، فإن لم يجب اتفاقهما بل جازَ اختلافهما .. لزمَ قبولُهما العجز ، وعادَ الأَوَّلُ .
(١) لأنه إذا توجّه أحدهما إلى تسكين زيد مثلاً ، والفرض : أن الآخر يجب عليه موافقة صاحبه .. كان التحريك في جانبه مستحيلاً ، وقد كان ممكناً في حقه قبل أن يتوجه صاحبه إلى التسكين . ( حامدي » ( ص٣٠٨ ) .
Establishment of the proof of oneness. Then we say: It is necessary for this Maker to be one; for if there were with Him a second, then the inability of both or the inability of one of them would be necessary in the case of disagreement, and the overpowering of both or the overpowering of one of them would be necessary in the case of the obligatory agreement, given the impossibility of what was known to be possible for each one considered individually, and the negation of the necessity of existence for each of them; because each would be independent of the other. If their agreement is not necessary but their disagreement is possible, then the acceptance of inability would be necessary for both, and the first [argument] returns. (1) Because if one of them intends to still Zayd, for example, and the assumption is that the other must agree with his companion, then movement on his part would be impossible, even though it was possible in his right before his companion intended to still.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the oneness of God (tawhid). The author argues that if there were two gods, then either they would agree or disagree. If they disagree, the inability of both or at least one is necessary, implying incapability. If they must obligatorily agree, then one or both would be overpowered, negating their independence. Moreover, each would be independent of the other, which negates the necessity of existence for each (since a necessary being cannot be independent of another necessary being). Finally, if their agreement is not necessary but disagreement is possible, then both must accept inability, returning to the first case. The footnote illustrates: if one intends to still Zayd, and the other must agree, then the second cannot cause movement (which was possible before), showing incapability.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if two gods always agree, there is no problem.
Correction: Forced agreement implies subordination, which contradicts independence and necessity. The footnote shows that even in agreement, one god's action becomes impossible, indicating inability.

Checkpoint

A) Both remain independent.
B) One or both are overpowered.
C) They are both necessary.
D) They can still disagree.

Key Takeaway
Both agreement and disagreement between two gods demonstrate their incapacity, proving that only one Necessary Being can exist.

The Three Demands of Tawhid and the Dilemma of Two Gods
Sanusi enumerates the three demands of tawhid and begins the proof for the second demand with a logical dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the overall structure of Sanusi's argument for the impossibility of a second god, including the three demands of tawhid and the logical dilemma that forms the core proof.

Core Idea
If two gods existed, their wills would either conflict or agree—both are impossible.

Sanusi begins by outlining the three foundational demands of tawhid, then focuses on the second: proving that no partner can exist in divinity. This lesson presents the opening of that proof, where he sets up a logical dilemma: if there were two gods, their universally related wills would either differ or agree regarding every possible act. Both alternatives are later shown to be impossible, establishing the impossibility of multiple gods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three demands
Read the passage carefully and note the three demands listed by Sanusi. The first demand (unity of essence) was already covered; the second (denial of a partner) is the focus here; the third (dissimilarity to creation) is mentioned for context.
The three demands are: 1) oneness of essence, 2) denial of a peer in divinity and exclusive creation, 3) dissimilarity to all originated things.
2
Understand the logical implication
Sanusi argues that if there were two gods, their wills (and powers) would necessarily relate to every possible thing. When two wills relate to the same act, they must either agree or differ. This is the 'implication' (ملازمة) of his proof.
The necessity of universal relation of God's will and power was established earlier.
3
Recognize the two alternatives
The two alternatives are: they differ (اختلاف) or they agree (اتفاق). Both are claimed to be impossible, and the proof proceeds to demonstrate each.
These two alternatives form the horns of the dilemma.
4
See the overall proof structure
The proof is a classic kalam argument: if there were two gods, it leads to either contradiction (divergence) or subordination (agreement), both of which are impossible. Therefore, two gods cannot exist.
This structure is used in many theological proofs for divine unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أنَّ الكلام في هذا الفصل مُرتّب على ثلاثة مطالب : الأول : إقامة البرهان على وحدة الذاتِ ؛ بمعنى : نفي تركيبها وعدم انقسامها . الثاني : نفي نظير له تعالى أو قسيم في الألوهية ، وفي انفراده تعالى بإيجادِ جميع الكائناتِ ذواتاً كانَتْ أو أفعالاً إسنادِ التأثيرِ لغيرِهِ في شيءٍ مِنَ الممكناتِ : معناه وعدم . الثالثُ : وَحدتُه تعالى ؛ بمعنى : مخالفته لجميع الحوادثِ ، فلا مثل له منها ، كما أنَّهُ لا ضدَّ له فيها . أما المطلب الأوّلُ : فقد سبق الكلامُ عليهِ عندَ ذكر تنزُّهِهِ تعالى عن الجرمية والتركيب ، فانظره هنالِكَ (١) . وأمَّا برهانُ المطلب الثاني : فهو الذي تعرض له هنا ، فنقولُ : الدليل على نفْي شريك لهُ تعالى في أُلوهِيَّتِهِ : أَنَّهُ لو كانَ مَعَهُ إِلهُ آخِرُ لم يخلُ : إِمَّا أنْ يختلفا في الإرادة على حكم التضاد أو يتفقا ، والتالي بقسميه محال ، فالمقدَّم مثله أما الملازمةُ : فدليلُها ما سبقَ مِنْ وجوب عموم تعلقِ إرادة الإله : وقدرته وسائرِ صفاتِهِ المتعلّقة ، فلو كانَ ثَمَّ إلهانِ لَوجبَ تعلُّقُ إرادةِ كلّ واحدٍ منهما وقدرتِهِ بكلّ ممكن ، ومهما تعلّق بالفعل إرادتان لم يخلُ مِنَ الاتفاق عليه أو التباين . وأما بطلان التالي : فببطلان طرفيهِ ؛ وهما الاختلافُ والاتفاق . (١) تقدم ( ص ٢٤٨ ) . اختلفا فوجه بطلان الطرفِ الأَوَّلِ ـ وهو الاختلافُ - أن نقول : لوِ في الفعل ؛ بأنْ يريد أحدهما وجودَ الجوهر ويريد الآخرُ عدمه ، أو يريد أحدهما حركتهُ والآخرُ تسكينَهُ.. لَلزِمَ عجزهما أو عجزُ أحدهما ، معَ زيادة مستحيلاتٍ سنذكرها ؛ وذلك لأنَّ نفوذ إرادتهما معاً مستحيل ؛ لما يؤدّي إليهِ مِن اجتماع النقيضين أو ما في حكمهما فيكون الجوهرُ في الزمانِ الواحدِ موجوداً معدوماً ، أو متحركاً ساكناً ، وذلكَ لا يُعقَلُ، فإذاً لا بدَّ مِنْ تعطّل النفوذ لإحدى الإرادتين أو لكليهما، فإن تعطَّلَنا معاً لزم عجز الإلهين بتعدرِ الفعل مِنْ كلّ واحدٍ منهما (١) ويلزم أيضاً عليه خلو المحل عن النقيضين ، وأيضاً فلا مانع مِنْ نفوذ إرادة كلّ واحدٍ منهما وقدرتِهِ .. إلا نفوذ إرادة الآخرِ وقدرته ، فإذا لم تنفذ الإرادتانِ لزم وجود الفعل بهما وعدم وجودِهِ بهما إِنْ ثبت المانع (٢) ، أو حصول المنعِ مِنْ غيرِ مانعِ إِنْ لم يثبتِ المانع . فهذهِ ثلاثة أوجه مِنَ الاستحالاتِ ، كلُّها تلزم على تقديرِ تعطيل الإرادتين . وأَمَّا إِنْ كانَتْ إرادة أحدهما خاصَّةً هي المتعلقة : فيستحيلُ مِنْ أوجه : أحدها : أَنَّهُ يلزمُ عليهِ عدمُ عموم تعلقِ إرادة الإله وقدرته ، وقد سبق أنَّ ذلك مستحيل (٣) ، وإذا استحال لم يمكن أن يكونَ . (١) في هامش ( ب ) : ( بلغ ) . (٢) يعني : لزم وجود الفعل بسبب تخصيص إرادتيهما له وجوداً أو عدماً ، والمانع هنا : نفوذ إحدى الإرادتين (٣) تقدم ( ص ٣٨٩) .
Know that the discussion in this chapter is arranged on three demands: The first: establishing the proof for the oneness of the Essence, meaning the negation of its composition and its non-division. The second: the denial of a peer or counterpart to Him, Most High, in divinity, and in His exclusivity, Most High, in creating all beings, whether substances or actions, and the non-attribution of effect to anyone else in anything among possible things: its meaning and non- [?]. The third: His oneness, Most High, meaning His dissimilarity to all originated things, so there is no likeness to Him from them, just as there is no opposite to Him in them. As for the first demand: the discussion about it has preceded when mentioning His transcendence, Most High, over corporeality and composition, so look there (1). As for the proof of the second demand: it is that which he treats here, so we say: The evidence for the denial of a partner to Him, Most High, in His divinity: that if there were with Him another god, it would not be free from either: that they differ in will according to the judgment of opposition, or that they agree. And the consequent, with its two divisions, is impossible, so the antecedent is likewise. As for the implication: its evidence is what preceded concerning the necessity of the universal relation of the will of the God and His power and all His related attributes. For if there were two gods, it would be necessary for the will of each one of them and His power to relate to every possible thing. And whenever two wills relate to an act, it is not free from agreement upon it or divergence. And as for the falsity of the consequent: it is by the falsity of its two sides, which are divergence and agreement. (1) Preceded (p. 248). They differed. So the aspect of the falsity of the first side — which is divergence — is that we say: if [they differed] in the act; such that one of them wills the existence of the substance and the other wills its non-existence, or one wills its motion and the other its rest... then their impotence or the impotence of one of them would be necessary, along with additional impossibilities we will mention. And that is because the penetration of both wills together is impossible, due to what it leads to of the conjunction of two contradictories or what is in their ruling, so the substance would be in one time existent and non-existent, or moving and at rest, and that is not conceivable. Therefore, there must be suspension of penetration of one of the wills or both. If both are suspended, then the impotence of the two gods follows due to the act being impossible from each one of them (1), and also upon it follows the emptiness of the locus from the two contradictories. Moreover, there is no impediment to the penetration of the will of each one of them and His power except the penetration of the will of the other and His power. So if the two wills do not penetrate, then the existence of the act by them and the non-existence of it by them would be necessary if the impediment is established (2), or the occurrence of prevention without an impediment if the impediment is not established. These are three aspects of impossibilities, all of which follow on the assumption of the suspension of the two wills. And as for if the will of one of them is specifically the one that relates: then it is impossible from [several] aspects: one of them: that upon it follows the lack of universal relation of the will of the God and His power, and it has preceded that this is impossible (3), and if it is impossible, it cannot be. (1) In margin (b): (بلغ). (2) Meaning: the existence of the act would be necessary due to the specification of their two wills to it as existence or non-existence, and the impediment here is the penetration of one of the two wills. (3) Preceded (p. 389).
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a proof (برهان) for the second of three demands concerning the oneness of Allah. The author first outlines the three demands: (1) proof of the unity of the essence (no composition or division), (2) denial of a partner or peer in divinity and in exclusive creation, and (3) the oneness meaning dissimilarity to all originated things. He notes that the first demand was already covered earlier. He then focuses on the second demand. The proof is a classic kalam argument: if there were two gods, their wills would either differ (disagree) or agree. Both possibilities are shown to be impossible, so the existence of two gods is impossible. The author first demonstrates the impossibility of disagreement: if two gods will opposite things (e.g., one wills existence of a substance, the other wills non-existence), then either both wills are ineffective (leading to impotence of both) or one is ineffective (leading to impotence of one). He then elaborates three specific impossibilities that arise if both wills are suspended: (a) impotence of both gods, (b) the impossible situation of a locus being empty of both contradictories, and (c) the paradoxical occurrence of prevention without an impediment. Next, he addresses the scenario where the wills agree but one will is specifically related (i.e., not universal). This leads to the impossibility that one god's will would not be universally related, which contradicts the necessary attribute of God. The argument is structured as a logical dilemma, thoroughly dismantling any possibility of multiple gods.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that two gods would necessarily conflict physically, like two kings fighting.
Correction: The argument is logical, not physical. It shows that even if two gods cooperated, their wills would have to be independently universal, which is impossible because two independent universal wills would either conflict or one would be subordinate.

Checkpoint
What are the two alternatives considered if there were two gods, according to Sanusi's proof?
A) They would be equal or unequal.
B) They would differ or agree in will.
C) They would create or not create.
D) They would be eternal or temporal.

Key Takeaway
The proof of tawhid begins with a logical dilemma: two gods would have to have either conflicting or agreeing wills, both of which are impossible, so no second god can exist.

The proof shows that agreement between two wills leads to redundancy. In your spiritual life, are you trying to please Allah and also seek the approval of others in matters where they conflict? Where is your ultimate allegiance, and what practical step will you take today to align your will solely with His?

Divine Transcendence
advanced
15 min
The Impossibility of Conflicting or Agreeing Wills in Two Gods
Sanusi presents the detailed proof that two gods with differing wills is impossible (three absurdities) and that agreeing wills also fails.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed arguments Sanusi gives to show why two gods with differing wills leads to impossible consequences, and why the alternative of agreeing wills also fails.

Core Idea
Two gods with conflicting wills leads to impotence and absurdities; agreeing wills contradicts the necessity of universal will.

Sanusi now unpacks both horns of the dilemma. First, he demonstrates in detail why disagreement between two gods is impossible, listing three concrete absurdities that would follow. Then he briefly addresses the other horn—agreement—showing that it would imply one god's will is not universally related, which contradicts the nature of divinity. This completes the proof that no partner can exist.

Guided Path
1
Follow the argument for disagreement
Read the section on disagreement carefully. Sanusi says that if two gods will opposite things (e.g., existence vs. non-existence of a substance), then either both wills are ineffective or one is. He then lists three impossibilities that follow from both wills being ineffective.
The three impossibilities are: a) impotence of both gods, b) emptiness of the locus from both contradictories, c) prevention without an impediment.
2
Identify the three absurdities
List each absurdity Sanusi mentions. The first: if both wills are suspended, both gods are impotent. The second: the locus would be empty of both existence and non-existence, which is impossible. The third: we would have prevention (of the act) without any impediment, which is incoherent.
These are classic theological arguments against multiple gods.
3
Understand the scenario of one will prevailing
Sanusi also considers the case where one will prevails over the other. He argues that this leads to the impossibility that the defeated god's will is not universally related, which contradicts the necessary attribute of God.
This addresses the agreement horn briefly.
4
See the overall conclusion
Both horns—disagreement and agreement—are shown to be impossible. Therefore, the existence of a second god is rationally impossible.
This completes the proof for the second demand of tawhid.

(١) تقدم ( ص ٢٤٨ ) . اختلفا فوجه بطلان الطرفِ الأَوَّلِ ـ وهو الاختلافُ - أن نقول : لوِ في الفعل ؛ بأنْ يريد أحدهما وجودَ الجوهر ويريد الآخرُ عدمه ، أو يريد أحدهما حركتهُ والآخرُ تسكينَهُ.. لَلزِمَ عجزهما أو عجزُ أحدهما ، معَ زيادة مستحيلاتٍ سنذكرها ؛ وذلك لأنَّ نفوذ إرادتهما معاً مستحيل ؛ لما يؤدّي إليهِ مِن اجتماع النقيضين أو ما في حكمهما فيكون الجوهرُ في الزمانِ الواحدِ موجوداً معدوماً ، أو متحركاً ساكناً ، وذلكَ لا يُعقَلُ، فإذاً لا بدَّ مِنْ تعطّل النفوذ لإحدى الإرادتين أو لكليهما، فإن تعطَّلَنا معاً لزم عجز الإلهين بتعدرِ الفعل مِنْ كلّ واحدٍ منهما (١) ويلزم أيضاً عليه خلو المحل عن النقيضين ، وأيضاً فلا مانع مِنْ نفوذ إرادة كلّ واحدٍ منهما وقدرتِهِ .. إلا نفوذ إرادة الآخرِ وقدرته ، فإذا لم تنفذ الإرادتانِ لزم وجود الفعل بهما وعدم وجودِهِ بهما إِنْ ثبت المانع (٢) ، أو حصول المنعِ مِنْ غيرِ مانعِ إِنْ لم يثبتِ المانع . فهذهِ ثلاثة أوجه مِنَ الاستحالاتِ ، كلُّها تلزم على تقديرِ تعطيل الإرادتين . وأَمَّا إِنْ كانَتْ إرادة أحدهما خاصَّةً هي المتعلقة : فيستحيلُ مِنْ أوجه : أحدها : أَنَّهُ يلزمُ عليهِ عدمُ عموم تعلقِ إرادة الإله وقدرته ، وقد سبق أنَّ ذلك مستحيل (٣) ، وإذا استحال لم يمكن أن يكونَ . (١) في هامش ( ب ) : ( بلغ ) . (٢) يعني : لزم وجود الفعل بسبب تخصيص إرادتيهما له وجوداً أو عدماً ، والمانع هنا : نفوذ إحدى الإرادتين (٣) تقدم ( ص ٣٨٩) .
Know that the discussion in this chapter is arranged on three demands: The first: establishing the proof for the oneness of the Essence, meaning the negation of its composition and its non-division. The second: the denial of a peer or counterpart to Him, Most High, in divinity, and in His exclusivity, Most High, in creating all beings, whether substances or actions, and the non-attribution of effect to anyone else in anything among possible things: its meaning and non- [?]. The third: His oneness, Most High, meaning His dissimilarity to all originated things, so there is no likeness to Him from them, just as there is no opposite to Him in them. As for the first demand: the discussion about it has preceded when mentioning His transcendence, Most High, over corporeality and composition, so look there (1). As for the proof of the second demand: it is that which he treats here, so we say: The evidence for the denial of a partner to Him, Most High, in His divinity: that if there were with Him another god, it would not be free from either: that they differ in will according to the judgment of opposition, or that they agree. And the consequent, with its two divisions, is impossible, so the antecedent is likewise. As for the implication: its evidence is what preceded concerning the necessity of the universal relation of the will of the God and His power and all His related attributes. For if there were two gods, it would be necessary for the will of each one of them and His power to relate to every possible thing. And whenever two wills relate to an act, it is not free from agreement upon it or divergence. And as for the falsity of the consequent: it is by the falsity of its two sides, which are divergence and agreement. (1) Preceded (p. 248). They differed. So the aspect of the falsity of the first side — which is divergence — is that we say: if [they differed] in the act; such that one of them wills the existence of the substance and the other wills its non-existence, or one wills its motion and the other its rest... then their impotence or the impotence of one of them would be necessary, along with additional impossibilities we will mention. And that is because the penetration of both wills together is impossible, due to what it leads to of the conjunction of two contradictories or what is in their ruling, so the substance would be in one time existent and non-existent, or moving and at rest, and that is not conceivable. Therefore, there must be suspension of penetration of one of the wills or both. If both are suspended, then the impotence of the two gods follows due to the act being impossible from each one of them (1), and also upon it follows the emptiness of the locus from the two contradictories. Moreover, there is no impediment to the penetration of the will of each one of them and His power except the penetration of the will of the other and His power. So if the two wills do not penetrate, then the existence of the act by them and the non-existence of it by them would be necessary if the impediment is established (2), or the occurrence of prevention without an impediment if the impediment is not established. These are three aspects of impossibilities, all of which follow on the assumption of the suspension of the two wills. And as for if the will of one of them is specifically the one that relates: then it is impossible from [several] aspects: one of them: that upon it follows the lack of universal relation of the will of the God and His power, and it has preceded that this is impossible (3), and if it is impossible, it cannot be. (1) In margin (b): (بلغ). (2) Meaning: the existence of the act would be necessary due to the specification of their two wills to it as existence or non-existence, and the impediment here is the penetration of one of the two wills. (3) Preceded (p. 389).
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a proof (برهان) for the second of three demands concerning the oneness of Allah. The author first outlines the three demands: (1) proof of the unity of the essence (no composition or division), (2) denial of a partner or peer in divinity and in exclusive creation, and (3) the oneness meaning dissimilarity to all originated things. He notes that the first demand was already covered earlier. He then focuses on the second demand. The proof is a classic kalam argument: if there were two gods, their wills would either differ (disagree) or agree. Both possibilities are shown to be impossible, so the existence of two gods is impossible. The author first demonstrates the impossibility of disagreement: if two gods will opposite things (e.g., one wills existence of a substance, the other wills non-existence), then either both wills are ineffective (leading to impotence of both) or one is ineffective (leading to impotence of one). He then elaborates three specific impossibilities that arise if both wills are suspended: (a) impotence of both gods, (b) the impossible situation of a locus being empty of both contradictories, and (c) the paradoxical occurrence of prevention without an impediment. Next, he addresses the scenario where the wills agree but one will is specifically related (i.e., not universal). This leads to the impossibility that one god's will would not be universally related, which contradicts the necessary attribute of God. The argument is structured as a logical dilemma, thoroughly dismantling any possibility of multiple gods.

نفوذ إرادة
جوهر
اجتماع النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only works if two gods actively disagree; if they agreed, they could coexist peacefully.
Correction: Even if they agreed, Sanusi shows that agreement also leads to an impossibility: one god's will would not be universally related, contradicting the necessity of a god having universal will and power.

Checkpoint
What is one of the three impossible consequences if both wills are suspended due to disagreement?
A) Both gods would be all-powerful.
B) The substance would exist and not exist simultaneously.
C) The locus would be empty of both contradictories.
D) The wills would be in harmony.

Key Takeaway
Two gods cannot exist because their wills would either conflict, leading to absurdities like impotence and contradictions, or one would lack universal will—impossible for a deity.

How does this lesson's proof that only God can possess universal and effective will challenge you to reexamine where you place your trust? What is one concrete change you will make today to realign your reliance solely on God?

Impossibility of Disagreement Between Two Gods
Sanusi gives two reasons why disagreement between two gods is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two logical arguments that prove disagreement between two gods is impossible.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot disagree without logical contradiction.

This short but dense passage from Sanusi presents two powerful arguments that rule out the possibility of disagreement between two gods. Using concrete examples like motion and rest, Sanusi shows that any scenario of conflicting wills leads to absurdity. The arguments underscore that a second god would either be powerless or the very nature of possibility would be violated.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow. Then read the translation to understand the literal meaning.
Notice the concise style.
2
Understand the first argument (motion and rest)
Identify how Sanusi uses the example of motion and rest to show that if one God wills motion, the other's will for rest would make rest impossible, inverting reality.
This argument relies on the impossibility of a possible thing becoming impossible due to another's will.
3
Understand the second argument (the preventer)
Examine the argument that if one God's will attaches to the opposite of what the other wills, then the preventer would impose a ruling on something it does not have, which is absurd.
The key is that prevention (man') is a ruling that cannot reside in the agent if the prevented thing is not under its domain.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Sanusi concludes both scenarios are impossible (mustahil). Ask yourself: What does this teach about the nature of divine unity?
These arguments reinforce that tawhid is not just a statement but a logical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسكون مثلاً ؛ لأنَّه إنه لا جزء إلله ، فإذا فرضنا تعلُّق إرادة أحدهما بخصوص الحركة مثلاً .. صار وقوع السكون الممكنِ مِنَ الآخرِ مستحيلاً ، وذلك قلب للحقائق
وأيضاً : كونُ المانع لهُ تعلَّق إرادة الآخرِ بضده .. يلزم منه إيجاب المانع حكم المنع لما لم يقم به ، وذلك كله مستحيل
For example, rest: because it has no part of godhead. If we suppose that the will of one of them is specifically attached to motion, then the occurrence of possible rest from the other becomes impossible, and that is an inversion of realities. Also: the fact that the preventer has the will of the other attached to its opposite necessitates that the preventer imposes the rule of prevention upon that which does not reside in it, and all of that is impossible.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage provides two arguments demonstrating the impossibility of two gods disagreeing. The first argument uses the example of motion and rest: if two gods exist, and one wills motion specifically for some entity, then the other willing rest for the same entity becomes impossible because rest would then be prevented by the first will, contradicting its possibility—this inverts the nature of possibilities. The second argument concerns a 'preventer' (mani'): if one god's will attaches to the opposite of what the other wills, then the preventer (e.g., the god willing motion preventing rest) would be attributed with a ruling (the prevention) that does not properly reside in it, which is absurd. Both cases show that disagreement leads to logical contradictions, hence impossible.

إرادة
حركة
سكون

منع
مستحيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could avoid disagreement by always willing the same thing.
Correction: Sanusi argues that even the possibility of disagreement is impossible because if they were truly independent, their wills would not necessarily align; but the very concept of two independent omnipotent beings leads to logical contradictions.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi argue would happen if one god wills motion and the other wills rest for the same thing?
A) Both would occur simultaneously.
B) Rest would become impossible, contradicting its possibility.
C) The gods would compromise and one would change its will.
D) Neither motion nor rest would occur.

Key Takeaway
Disagreement between two gods is logically impossible because it leads to contradictions such as inverting possibilities and attributing rulings to improper subjects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Sanusi says that a potential disagreement would 'invert realities.' In what areas of your life do you invert reality by acting as if there are competing authorities or as if Allah's will could be overruled? How would your daily decisions change if you fully internalized that only one will ultimately matters?
?
The argument shows that a preventer cannot impose its rule on something outside itself. Are there areas where you try to control outcomes that are outside your domain, acting as if you have a 'preventer's power'? How does recognizing Allah's exclusive sovereignty change your attitude toward those uncontrollable matters?

Impossibility of Possible Agreement Between Two Gods
Sanusi argues that possible agreement between two gods implies possible disagreement, which is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why possible agreement between two gods is impossible because it logically entails possible disagreement, which is already proven impossible.

Core Idea
Possible agreement between two gods would imply possible disagreement, which is impossible.

Sanusi continues his argument against multiple gods by examining a scenario where two gods could potentially agree. He shows that if their agreement is merely possible, then disagreement must also be possible. Since disagreement has already been shown impossible, the scenario collapses. This lesson walks through that logical deduction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let Sanusi's own words set the stage.
Notice the condensed theological style.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument is a conditional proof: if agreement is possible (antecedent), then disagreement is possible (consequent). Identify these two parts.
The passage explicitly states the implication.
3
Understand why the consequent is false
Recall that earlier arguments proved disagreement between two gods is impossible. Since the consequent is false, the antecedent (possible agreement) must also be false.
This is a classic modus tollens argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : ( فإن لم يجب اتفاقهما ، بل جازَ اختلافهما .. لزمَ قبولهما العجز ، وعادَ الأَوَّلُ ( هذا هو النوع الثاني مِنْ نوعي الاتفاق ؛ وهو الاتفاق الجائز . فذَكَرَ في وجهِ بطلانِهِ : أَنَّهُ يلزمُ فيهِ ما يلزمُ في الاختلافِ ؛ مِنْ عجزهما أو عجز أحدهما ؛ يعني : معَ سائر الاستحالاتِ التي قدَّمنا ذكرها هناكَ ، ووجه ذلكَ ظاهرُ ؛ لأنَّهُ كلَّما كانَ الاتفاق جائزاً كانَ الاختلاف جائزاً ؛ لأنَّ جواز أحد المتقابلين يستلزم جواز الآخر ، لكنَّ التالي باطل ؛ لما تقدَّمَ مِنِ استحالة الاختلافِ مِنْ أوجه ، فالمقدَّم ـ وهو كون الاتفاق جائزاً ـ محال
And His statement: 'If their agreement is not necessary, but rather their disagreement is possible, then it would necessitate their incapability, and the first [argument] returns.' This is the second type of agreement: possible agreement. He mentioned in the aspect of its invalidity that it necessitates what was necessitated in disagreement: their incapability or the incapability of one of them—meaning, along with the other impossibilities that we mentioned there. And the justification for that is clear: because whenever agreement is possible, disagreement is possible, because the possibility of one of two correlatives necessitates the possibility of the other. But the consequent is false, due to what preceded of the impossibility of disagreement in multiple respects. So the antecedent—that agreement is possible—is impossible.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage discusses the second type of agreement between two gods: possible agreement (ittifaq ja'iz). Sanusi explains that if their agreement is not necessary but merely possible, then by logical correlation, disagreement would also be possible (since possibility is symmetrical between opposites). However, disagreement has already been proven impossible through multiple arguments (as shown earlier). Since the consequent (disagreement being possible) is false, the antecedent (agreement being possible) must also be false. Therefore, possible agreement is impossible. This argument is straightforward: possible agreement entails possible disagreement, but disagreement is impossible, so possible agreement cannot stand.

اختلاف

مقدّم
تالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if two gods could agree sometimes and disagree at others, this would solve the problem of multiple gods.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even the mere possibility of agreement implies the possibility of disagreement, which is already proven impossible. Thus, possible agreement cannot exist.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi consider possible agreement between two gods impossible?
A) Because agreement must be necessary for deities.
B) Because it implies possible disagreement, which is already proven impossible.
C) Because two gods cannot have free will.
D) Because possible agreement leads to a contradiction in attributes.

Key Takeaway
Possible agreement between two gods is impossible because it would imply possible disagreement, which has already been proven impossible.

Two Gods Agreeing? Composition and Dependency Paradox
Sanusi presents two arguments against two gods necessarily agreeing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessary agreement of two gods leads to absurd consequences of composition and contradictory dependency, reinforcing the impossibility of multiple necessary beings.

Core Idea
Necessary agreement of two gods implies each is composed and events become both needy and independent.

Sanusi presents two powerful arguments against the possibility of two gods necessarily agreeing. The first shows that agreement implies each god possesses only parts of attributes, leading to composition—an impossibility for the divine. The second reveals a deeper contradiction: if events are independent of each god separately, they are simultaneously in need and not in need of them. This passage strengthens the tawhid argument by exposing logical inconsistencies.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first argument: composition from parts
Read how necessary agreement forces each god to have only a part of knowledge, power, and will, implying composition (tarkib). Then note that composition of the deity is impossible, especially from separate parts.
Sanusi emphasizes that even composition from connected parts is impossible, so separate parts are even more absurd.
2
Understand the second argument: contradictory dependency
Examine the consequence that events would be both in need of each god and independent of each god simultaneously—a contradiction. This is derived from the necessary independence of events from each god regarding the other.
This argument is stronger because it does not rely on the inverse of a proof.
3
Compare the two arguments and note the role of 'aks al-dalil
Realize that the first argument might be rebutted as appealing to the inverse of a proof, but Sanusi claims he has established it in a way that avoids this. The second argument avoids this issue entirely.
The footnote explains 'aks al-dalil: absence of the proof does not entail absence of the proven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بكل واحدٍ منهما جزء العلمِ وجزء القدرةِ وجزء الإرادة إلى غير ذلكَ ممَّا لا يقولُ به عاقل ، وإذا كان تركيب الإله مِن جزأين مُتصلين محالاً . . فما بالك بتركيبه من جزأين منفصلين ؟!
ويلزم أيضاً مِنْ وجوب استغناء الحوادث بكل منهما عن الآخر : أن تكون الحوادث محتاجةً لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما ، غنيةً عن كل واحدٍ منهما ! وهو جمع بين متنافيين ، وهذا اللازم أقوى مِنَ الذي قبلَهُ؛ لأنَّ السابق قد يُدَّعَى فِيهِ أَنَّهُ مِنْ بابِ التمسُّكِ بعكس الدليل (۱) ، وإن كنَّا نحنُ قد قرَّرناه على وجهِ لا يَرِدُ عليهِ (٢) ، بخلاف هذا
(۱) إذ الدليل يلزم من وجوده وجود المدلول ، ولا يلزم من عدمه عدم المدلول . (٢) أي : التمسك بعكس الدليل ؛ يلزم من عدمه عدم المدلول
حامدي » ( ص ٣١٤ )
Each one of them would have part of knowledge, part of power, and part of will, and so on to that which no intelligent person would affirm. And if composition of the deity from two connected parts is impossible, then what about its composition from two separate parts?! And it also follows from the necessary independence of events from each of them [the two gods] regarding the other: that events would be in need of each of them and independent of each of them simultaneously! This is a combination of two contradictory things, and this necessary consequence is stronger than the one before it, because the earlier may be claimed to be an instance of appealing to the inverse of the proof (1), although we have established it in a way that it is not subject to that objection (2), unlike this one. (1) i.e., the proof: the existence of the proof entails the existence of the proven, but the non-existence of the proof does not entail the non-existence of the proven. (2) i.e., appealing to the inverse of the proof: it entails the non-existence of the proven from the non-existence of the proof. Hamidi (p. 314)
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments against the possibility of two gods necessarily agreeing (ittifaq wajib). First, if they necessarily agreed, each would have only a part of knowledge, power, and will, implying composition (tarkib). Since composition from two connected parts is already impossible for the deity, composition from separate parts is even more absurd. Second, if events were necessarily independent of each god—meaning each could create events independently of the other—then events would paradoxically be in need of both gods (since both are required for the agreement) and independent of each simultaneously. This contradiction is stronger than the first because the first might be rebutted as merely appealing to the inverse of a proof (arguing from absence of composition to impossibility of agreement), but the author notes he has already established that argument in a way that avoids that objection. The footnote explains the concept of 'aks al-dalil (inverse of the proof).

Key Terms
اتفاق واجب
تركيب
استغناء
إحتياج
جزء
عكس الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessary agreement between two gods is harmonious and avoids conflict.
Correction: The passage shows that such agreement implies composition (each god having only parts) and a contradiction regarding events (needing and not needing both gods simultaneously).

Checkpoint
What does the first argument in this passage conclude about two gods if they necessarily agree?
A) They would each be independent and self-sufficient.
B) They would each be composed of parts like knowledge and power.
C) They would both be incapable of acting.
D) They would be identical in every aspect.

Key Takeaway
The necessary agreement of two gods leads to impossible composition and contradictory dependency, reinforcing the tawhid principle that there can be only one necessary being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even momentarily, felt that someone other than Allah could truly grant you success or avert harm? How does this lesson's argument expose the absurdity of attributing any real power to anyone besides Him? Will you now actively remind yourself of this whenever such thoughts arise?
?
The lesson shows that two gods would lead to events being both needy and independent—a contradiction. Can you see parallels in your own heart where you rely on created things while simultaneously claiming dependence on Allah? What would it take to remove that contradiction from your life?
?
If you truly internalize that only One Being can be necessary and independent, how would that change the way you fear people's opinions or seek their approval? Commit to one specific action today that proves your reliance is on Allah alone.

Impossibility of Contingent Agreement Between Two Gods
Sanusi argues against the possibility of contingent agreement between two gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why contingent agreement between two gods is impossible, because it entails the possibility of disagreement, which leads to incapability.

Core Idea
Contingent agreement is impossible because it opens the door to disagreement.

Sanusi now examines the second type of agreement: contingent agreement. He argues that if two gods could agree contingently, they could also disagree, since agreement and disagreement are opposites. But disagreement has already been proven impossible, so contingent agreement must also be impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the conditional syllogism
Notice that Sanusi uses a conditional: if contingent agreement is possible, then disagreement is possible.
3
See the consequence
Understand that possible disagreement leads to incapability, which is impossible for God.
4
Appreciate the reformulation
Sanusi offers a clearer formulation: 'Whenever their agreement is possible, they must accept incapability.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و
وقوله : ( فإن لم يجب اتفاقهما ، بل جازَ اختلافهما .. لزمَ قبولهما العجز ، وعادَ الأَوَّلُ ( هذا هو النوع الثاني مِنْ نوعي الاتفاق ؛ وهو الاتفاق الجائز .
فذَكَرَ في وجهِ بطلانِهِ : أَنَّهُ يلزمُ فيهِ ما يلزمُ في الاختلافِ ؛ مِنْ عجزهما أو عجز أحدهما ؛ يعني : معَ سائر الاستحالاتِ التي قدَّمنا ذكرها هناكَ ، ووجه ذلكَ ظاهرُ ؛ لأنَّهُ كلَّما كانَ الاتفاق جائزاً كانَ الاختلاف جائزاً ؛ لأنَّ جواز أحد المتقابلين يستلزم جواز الآخر ، لكنَّ التالي باطل ؛ لما تقدَّمَ مِنِ استحالة الاختلافِ مِنْ أوجه ، فالمقدَّم ـ وهو كون الاتفاق جائزاً ـ محال
وبعبارة أخرى : أن نقول : كلَّما جازَ اتفاقهما جازَ اختلافهما ،
وكلما جازَ اختلافُهما لزمَ قبولُهما العجز ؛ لأن الاختلاف ملزوم للعجز ، فالقابل للاختلافِ قابل للعجزِ ؛ ضرورةَ أنَّ القابلَ لملزوم الشيء قابل للازمِهِ ، فينتج إذاً : كلَّما جازَ اتفاقهما لزمَ قَبولهما العجز ، وهذا التقرير أنسب للفظ العقيدة(۱)
1 لو تُصوَّرَ إلهانِ متفقان للزمهما العجز ]
ويلزمُ أيضاً في الاتفاقِ مطلقاً العجزُ ؛ لأنَّ الفعل الواحد يستحيل عليه الانقسام ، فيتمانعان فيه ، فيلزم عجزهما أو عجز أحدهما كما في الاختلافِ ، والعجز على الإله محال ؛ لأنَّهُ يُضادُّ القدرة ; فإنْ كانَ قديماً لزم استحالة عدمِهِ ، فيجب ألا يقدر هذا الإله على شيءٍ دائماً ، وإنْ كانَ حادثاً فضدُّه - وهو القدرة - قديمة

(۱) في هامش ( ب ) : ( بلغ )
(۲) كذا في النسخ ، على معنى : أن ضدَّ العجز هو القدرة ، وهي قديمة ،
وسيطابق في الشرح .
And His statement: 'For if their agreement is not necessary, but rather their disagreement is possible ... then they must accept incapability, and the first [argument] returns.' This is the second type of the two types of agreement: contingent agreement. So he mentioned in the way of its invalidity: that it necessarily entails what is entailed in disagreement: their being incapable or one of them being incapable; meaning: along with the other impossibilities that we previously mentioned there. And the reason for that is apparent, because whenever agreement is possible, disagreement is possible, because the possibility of one of two opposites entails the possibility of the other, but the consequent is false, due to the previously established impossibility of disagreement from many aspects, so the antecedent—that agreement is possible—is impossible. In other words, we say: Whenever their agreement is possible, their disagreement is possible, and whenever their disagreement is possible, they must accept incapability, because disagreement is necessitating of incapability, so what is capable of disagreement is capable of incapability, necessarily because what is capable of the necessitating thing is capable of its necessary consequence. So it follows that whenever their agreement is possible, they must accept incapability. And this formulation is more suitable to the wording of the creed (1). [Marginal note:] If two agreeing gods were imagined, they would both be incapable. And it also follows in agreement absolutely that they are incapable, because one single act cannot be divided, so they would conflict over it, and thus their incapability or the incapability of one of them follows, just like in disagreement. And incapability of the deity is impossible because it contradicts power. For if it were eternal, its non-existence would be impossible, so this deity would never be able to do anything. And if it were temporal, then its opposite—which is power—is eternal, so its non-existence is impossible, so incapability would not exist. Also, it is impossible for the deity to be described by a temporal attribute. (1) Marginal note in copy (b): 'Reached.' (2) As in the copies, meaning: that the opposite of incapability is power, and it is eternal, and it will correspond in the commentary.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments against the possibility of two gods agreeing contingently (ittifaq ja'iz) and any agreement at all. First, if their agreement is possible, then their disagreement is also possible, because two opposites (agreement and disagreement) are such that if one is possible, the other must be possible. However, disagreement has already been proven impossible (as it leads to incapability and other absurdities). Therefore, agreement being possible is itself impossible. The author restates this argument syllogistically: if A then B, but B is false, so A is false. He notes that this formulation aligns better with the creed (al-'aqida). A marginal note adds that if two agreeing gods were imagined, they would be incapable. Second, he argues that any agreement (whether necessary or contingent) necessarily entails incapability because a single act cannot be divided between two agents; they would conflict, leading to both or one being incapable. Incapability is impossible for God because it contradicts power (qudra). If incapability were eternal (qadim), it would be impossible to cease, so God would never be able to do anything. If it were temporal (hadith), then its opposite (power) would be eternal and cannot cease to exist, so incapability could not exist. Furthermore, it is impossible for God to have a temporal attribute. The footnote clarifies that the opposite of incapability is power, which is eternal.

Key Terms
اتفاق جائز
اختلاف
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if two gods agree contingently, they could avoid the problems of disagreement.
Correction: The possibility of agreement necessarily entails the possibility of disagreement, because they are opposites. Thus contingent agreement is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is contingent agreement between two gods impossible?
A) Because it leads to both gods being unable to act at all.
B) Because it entails the possibility of disagreement, which is impossible.
C) Because it would require them to share all attributes.
D) Because agreement is inherently impossible for two gods.

Key Takeaway
Contingent agreement between two gods is impossible because it would imply the possibility of disagreement, which leads to incapability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that the mere possibility of disagreement between two gods would entail their incapability. Are you absolutely convinced that nothing can be even possibly co-eternal with Allah? What lingering doubt in your heart needs to be uprooted by this logical certainty?
?
If someone presented you with a hypothetical scenario of two gods always agreeing, would you now see the logical flaw that even this scenario is impossible? How will you use this argument to strengthen your own conviction when you encounter subtle forms of shirk in your thoughts?
?
The argument here is rigorous and leaves no room for compromise. Does your belief in Allah's oneness rest on such intellectual certainty, or is it merely inherited and unexamined? What step will you take today to make your tawhid more than a tradition?

Any Agreement Between Two Gods Leads to Incapability
Sanusi argues that any agreement between two gods leads to incapability due to the impossibility of dividing a single act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that any agreement between two gods, whether necessary or contingent, leads to incapability because a single act cannot be divided.

Core Idea
Any agreement between two gods makes them incapable because a single act cannot be shared.

Sanusi now presents a second, more general argument: even if two gods were in complete agreement, they would still be incapable because a single act cannot be performed by two agents. This leads to logical absurdities that are impossible for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the argument about the single act.
2
Understand the conflict
Notice that a single act cannot be divided, so two gods would conflict over it, leading to incapability.
3
Consider the implications of incapability
Incapability is impossible for God because it contradicts power, and it would be either eternal or temporal, both impossible.
4
Reflect on the uniqueness of divine power
This argument reinforces that only one God can possess absolute power without conflict.
