Objection and First Answer: The Impossibility of Partitioning Divine Power
Sanusi presents an objection and first answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that any attempt to divide creation among multiple gods is impossible because divine power and will must encompass all possible things, leading to inevitable conflict.

Core Idea
Divine power cannot be limited; partition implies limitation, which is impossible for God.

Sanusi anticipates an objection to the proof of divine unity: could not the world be partitioned among two gods, each controlling a separate domain? In this passage, he states the impossibility and provides a preliminary answer based on the necessity of divine will and power being universal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. The objection proposes dividing the world into two parts, each god controlling one part, so no conflict occurs.
Notice the phrase 'فإن قلت' signaling an objection.
2
Identify the first answer
The answer refers to a prior principle: the impossibility of finitude in God's objects of power and will. Any division would limit God, which is impossible.
This answer is brief but relies on earlier established doctrine.
3
Reflect on the logic
Why does universality of will and power prevent division? Because if God must will all possibles, two gods would both will the same things, leading to conflict.
This is the core of the first argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يستحيل توازعُ العالم على آلهة متعددة. فإن قلت : فلم لا يجوز أن ينقسم العالم بينهما قسمين ، فيكون أحدهما قادراً على أحد القسمين ، والآخر على الآخر ، فلا يلزم التمانع ؟ فالجواب : أنه تقرر قبل استحالة التناهي في مقدورات الإله ومراداته ، فيستحيل هذا الفرض الذي ذكر في السؤال. وأيضاً : فالقسمان إن كانا معاً في الجواهر لزم من تعلق القدرة ببعضها تعلقها بالجميع؛ للتماثل، فيلزم التمانع، وإن كان أحد القسمين الجواهر والآخر الأعراض .. فذلك لا يعقل؛ إذ القدرة على إيجاد الجواهر لا تعقل بدون القدرة على أعراضها، وكذا العكس؛ للتلازم الذي بينهما، ثم ذلك لا يدفع التمانع عندما يريد أحدهما أن يوجد الجوهر، والآخر لا يريد أن يوجد عرضه. هذا السؤال إيراد على الملازمة التي ذكرنا أولاً؛ وهي قولنا في العقيدة: ( إذ لو كان معه ثانٍ للزم عجزهما ... ( إلى آخره. ووجه الإيراد أن يقال : لا تسلم أنه يلزم من وجود الله ثانٍ عجزهما أو عجز أحدهما؛ لأن ذلك إنما يلزم لو كان يجب أن تتعلق إرادة كل واحد منهما وقدرته بمراد الآخر ومقدوره، ولم لا يجوز أن يكون أحدهما قسيماً للآخر؛ بحيث ينقسم العالم بينهما قسمين، كل ينفرد بقسم، فلا تزاحم بينهما ولا تمانع حتى يلزم عجزهما أو عجز أحدهما؟! أجاب في العقيدة بوجهين: الأول : أن القسيم محال؛ لما عرفت من وجوب عموم تعلق إرادة الإله وقدرته، فيجب تعلق إرادة كل منهما وقدرته بكل ممكن، فيلزم التمانع كما سبق. الثاني : أن أحد النوعين الذي تعلقت به إرادة أحدهما وقدرته إن كان مماثلاً للنوع الآخر الذي هو مقدور الإله الثاني ومراده؛ كأن يكون النوعان معاً من الجواهر .. لزم عموم قدرة كل واحد منهما وإرادته للنوعين؛ ضرورة أن القادر على أحد المثلين قادر على مثله، وإن كان مخالفاً له؛ كأن يكون أحدهما جواهر والآخر أعراضاً .. فهو محال من وجهين: أحدهما: أن الجوهر والعرض لما لم يمكن انفكاك أحدهما عن الآخر .. استحال تصور القدرة على أحدهما بدون الآخر. ثانيهما: أن التمانع لا ينتفي بذلك على تقدير تسليمه؛ لأنه من (1) القسيم هنا : المقاسم، لا بمعنى الشيء الذي ينقسم إليه وإلى غيره شيء آخر؛ كنوعي الجنس مثلاً. مفاد « حامدي » ( ص۳۱۸ )
It is impossible for the world to be shared among multiple gods. If you say: Why is it not permissible for the world to be divided between them into two parts, so that one of them is able over one part and the other over the other part, and thus mutual hindrance does not occur? The answer: It has been established before the impossibility of finitude in the objects of power and will of the god, so this hypothesis mentioned in the question is impossible. Also: If the two parts are both in substances, then from the attachment of power to some of them follows its attachment to all of them due to similarity, so mutual hindrance occurs. And if one of the two parts is substances and the other accidents, then that is not conceivable, for power to bring about substances is not conceivable without power over their accidents, and likewise the reverse, due to the inseparability between them. Moreover, that does not remove mutual hindrance when one of them wants to bring about the substance and the other does not want to bring about its accident. This question is an objection to the implication we mentioned first, namely our statement in the Creed: 'For if there were a second with Him, the disability of both would follow...' etc. The point of the objection is to say: It is not granted that from the existence of a second god follows the disability of both or one of them, because that only follows if it were necessary that the will and power of each one attach to the object of will and power of the other. And why is it not permissible for one to be a partitioner (qasīm) of the other, such that the world is divided between them into two parts, each one being unique to a part, so there is no friction between them nor mutual hindrance until the disability of both or one of them follows?! He answered in the Creed with two points: First: That the partitioner (qasīm) is impossible, due to what you have known of the necessity of the universality of the attachment of the god's will and power. So the attachment of the will and power of each of them to every possible thing becomes necessary, so mutual hindrance occurs as before. Second: That one of the two types to which the will and power of one of them attaches—if it is similar to the other type which is the object of power and will of the second god, as if the two types are both substances—the universality of the power and will of each of them over both types follows, necessarily because the one who is able over one of the similars is able over its like. And if it is different from it, as if one of them is substances and the other accidents—then it is impossible from two aspects: One of them: That substance and accident, since neither can be separated from the other, it becomes impossible to conceive of power over one of them without the other. The second: That mutual hindrance is not removed by that, assuming its admission, because... (1) Here 'partition' (qasīm) means 'co-partitioner' not in the sense of something that is divided into it and something else; like two species of a genus, for example. From the benefit of Hamidi (p. 318).
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the proof of divine unity and provides two answers. The objection argues that the world could be divided between two gods, so mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) would not occur. The passage begins by stating the impossibility of sharing the world among multiple gods. Then it voices the objection: why not divide the world into two domains, each god controlling one, avoiding conflict? The first answer refers to a previously established principle: the impossibility of finitude in the objects of divine power and will—since God's power and will must encompass all possible things, any division is impossible. The second answer examines the two cases: if both domains consist of substances, then because substances are similar, power over one implies power over all, leading to conflict. If one domain is substances and the other accidents, this is not conceivable because substances and accidents are inseparable; power cannot be over one without the other. The passage then clarifies that this objection is aimed at the earlier implication (mulāzama) from the creedal statement, saying that the existence of a second god does not necessarily entail disability if their wills and powers are not universally attached. The answer from the Creed has two points: First, the idea of a partitioner (qasīm) is impossible because the divine will and power must be universal to all possibles, so conflict remains. Second, if the types are similar (both substances), each god's power extends to both types due to similarity; if different (substances vs. accidents), it is impossible for two reasons: (a) substances and accidents are inseparable, so power over one requires power over the other; (b) even if admitted, conflict persists because both gods would have to control the same inseparable pair, leading to mutual hindrance. The passage ends with a footnote clarifying the meaning of 'qasīm'.

Key Terms
توازع
تمانع
قسيم
قدرة
إرادة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that dividing the world between gods avoids conflict because each has its own domain.
Correction: Sanusi shows this fails because divine power and will are necessarily universal—they cannot be limited to a part.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the first answer to the objection?
A) The similarity of substances
B) The inseparability of substance and accident
C) The impossibility of finitude in divine power and will
D) The fact that the world is one

Key Takeaway
Divine power and will are necessarily universal; any limitation would contradict God's perfection, making partition among multiple gods impossible.

?
When you rely on something other than God—money, status, relationships—you are implicitly partitioning divine power. Which of these false gods do you need to dethrone right now, and what specific step will you take to acknowledge God's sole sovereignty over that area?

Second Answer: Analysis of Two Possible Divisions
Sanusi analyzes the two possible divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why dividing the world between two gods fails whether the domains are similar (both substances) or different (substances vs accidents), due to the principles of similarity and inseparability.

Core Idea
Substances and accidents are inseparable; true division is impossible.

Sanusi provides a more detailed second answer to the objection. He examines two possibilities for the division: both parts consisting of substances, or one part substances and the other accidents. He shows that in either case, conflict (tamānuʿ) necessarily occurs, demonstrating the impossibility of multiple gods.

Guided Path
1
Examine the case of similar types
If both parts are substances, due to similarity, power over one substance implies power over all substances, leading to conflict.
This is based on the principle of tamāthul (similarity).
2
Examine the case of different types
If one part is substances and the other accidents, this is impossible because substances and accidents are inseparable; power over one requires power over the other, and conflict remains.
This is based on talāzum (inseparability).
3
Understand why conflict persists
Even if division were possible, when one god wants a substance and the other does not want its accident, conflict arises because the accident cannot exist without the substance.
This final point seals the argument.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يستحيل توازعُ العالم على آلهة متعددة. فإن قلت : فلم لا يجوز أن ينقسم العالم بينهما قسمين ، فيكون أحدهما قادراً على أحد القسمين ، والآخر على الآخر ، فلا يلزم التمانع ؟ فالجواب : أنه تقرر قبل استحالة التناهي في مقدورات الإله ومراداته ، فيستحيل هذا الفرض الذي ذكر في السؤال. وأيضاً : فالقسمان إن كانا معاً في الجواهر لزم من تعلق القدرة ببعضها تعلقها بالجميع؛ للتماثل، فيلزم التمانع، وإن كان أحد القسمين الجواهر والآخر الأعراض .. فذلك لا يعقل؛ إذ القدرة على إيجاد الجواهر لا تعقل بدون القدرة على أعراضها، وكذا العكس؛ للتلازم الذي بينهما، ثم ذلك لا يدفع التمانع عندما يريد أحدهما أن يوجد الجوهر، والآخر لا يريد أن يوجد عرضه. هذا السؤال إيراد على الملازمة التي ذكرنا أولاً؛ وهي قولنا في العقيدة: ( إذ لو كان معه ثانٍ للزم عجزهما ... ( إلى آخره. ووجه الإيراد أن يقال : لا تسلم أنه يلزم من وجود الله ثانٍ عجزهما أو عجز أحدهما؛ لأن ذلك إنما يلزم لو كان يجب أن تتعلق إرادة كل واحد منهما وقدرته بمراد الآخر ومقدوره، ولم لا يجوز أن يكون أحدهما قسيماً للآخر؛ بحيث ينقسم العالم بينهما قسمين، كل ينفرد بقسم، فلا تزاحم بينهما ولا تمانع حتى يلزم عجزهما أو عجز أحدهما؟! أجاب في العقيدة بوجهين: الأول : أن القسيم محال؛ لما عرفت من وجوب عموم تعلق إرادة الإله وقدرته، فيجب تعلق إرادة كل منهما وقدرته بكل ممكن، فيلزم التمانع كما سبق. الثاني : أن أحد النوعين الذي تعلقت به إرادة أحدهما وقدرته إن كان مماثلاً للنوع الآخر الذي هو مقدور الإله الثاني ومراده؛ كأن يكون النوعان معاً من الجواهر .. لزم عموم قدرة كل واحد منهما وإرادته للنوعين؛ ضرورة أن القادر على أحد المثلين قادر على مثله، وإن كان مخالفاً له؛ كأن يكون أحدهما جواهر والآخر أعراضاً .. فهو محال من وجهين: أحدهما: أن الجوهر والعرض لما لم يمكن انفكاك أحدهما عن الآخر .. استحال تصور القدرة على أحدهما بدون الآخر. ثانيهما: أن التمانع لا ينتفي بذلك على تقدير تسليمه؛ لأنه من (1) القسيم هنا : المقاسم، لا بمعنى الشيء الذي ينقسم إليه وإلى غيره شيء آخر؛ كنوعي الجنس مثلاً. مفاد « حامدي » ( ص۳۱۸ )
It is impossible for the world to be shared among multiple gods. If you say: Why is it not permissible for the world to be divided between them into two parts, so that one of them is able over one part and the other over the other part, and thus mutual hindrance does not occur? Also: If the two parts are both in substances, then from the attachment of power to some of them follows its attachment to all of them due to similarity, so mutual hindrance occurs. And if one of the two parts is substances and the other accidents, then that is not conceivable, for power to bring about substances is not conceivable without power over their accidents, and likewise the reverse, due to the inseparability between them. Moreover, that does not remove mutual hindrance when one of them wants to bring about the substance and the other does not want to bring about its accident. This question is an objection to the implication we mentioned first, namely our statement in the Creed: 'For if there were a second with Him, the disability of both would follow...' etc. The point of the objection is to say: It is not granted that from the existence of a second god follows the disability of both or one of them, because that only follows if it were necessary that the will and power of each one attach to the object of will and power of the other. He answered in the Creed with two points: First: That the partitioner (qasīm) is impossible, due to what you have known of the necessity of the universality of the attachment of the god's will and power. So the attachment of the will and power of each of them to every possible thing becomes necessary, so mutual hindrance occurs as before. The second: That mutual hindrance is not removed by that, assuming its admission, because... How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah alone has absolute power and any attempt to divide it leads to contradiction, then where in your life have you allowed divided loyalties? What 'other gods' — be they wealth, status, relationships, or desires — have you given a share of your heart's devotion that belongs only to Allah? This lesson shows that such division is not just sinful but logically impossible: can you truly serve two masters? Examine your daily choices and commitments: where do you need to repent and restore exclusive devotion to Allah?
?
Sanusi argues that substances and accidents are inseparable: you cannot have a substance without its accidents. In your own faith, are you trying to separate belief from action? Do you think you can claim to believe in Allah's oneness while your actions show otherwise? The talāzum between substance and accident is like the connection between faith and deeds: true imān must manifest in your life. In what specific areas are you failing to let your belief produce the actions it demands? What will you change starting today to make your faith consistent with your practice?
?
The proof that two gods cannot coexist because of tamānuʿ (mutual hindrance) is intellectually compelling. Yet, the heart often harbors subtle forms of shirk: relying on created means as if they have independent power, fearing people more than Allah, or seeking approval from others over His pleasure. This lesson forces you to ask: In whom or what do you truly place your ultimate trust? When you are anxious, do you turn to Allah first or to other solutions? The logical argument is clear: power must be universal and undivided. Does your heart confirm this? Commit to a specific area where you will actively redirect your trust entirely to Allah.

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Argument on Evil
Ibn al-Tilmisani refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that evil cannot be from Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn al-Tilmisani refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that evil cannot be from Allah by showing that moral categories are relative and based on divine law, and that Allah's names are tawqifiyya.

Core Idea
Good and evil are relative, not intrinsic; Allah creates everything by His power.

The Mu'tazilites argued that if Allah did evil, He would be called evil—a contradiction to His perfection. Ibn al-Tilmisani presents the response of Ahl al-Sunna, showing that actions are attributed to Allah based on their need for a creator, not their moral value. This lesson walks through the key points of that refutation, clarifying how Allah's actions are all good from His perspective.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite Argument
Read the opening lines where the Mu'tazila claim that the doer of good is called good, the doer of evil is called evil, so evil cannot be from Allah. Notice their logical structure.
This sets up the challenge that the Sunni response must answer.
2
Follow the Sunni Responses
Identify each distinct response: (1) actions attributed by origination, (2) good/evil are relative, (3) ethical categories are Shar'i and apply only to humans, (4) Allah's names are tawqifiyya.
These responses dismantle the Mu'tazilite premise step by step.
3
Reflect on the Conclusion
Consider the final point that Allah is called 'Creator of everything' but not specific evil things because names are revealed. This upholds divine perfection while affirming creative power.
The conclusion preserves Allah's transcendence and sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد سلكتِ المعتزلة هذا المسلك؛ حيث قالوا: فاعلُ الخيرِ يُقالُ له: خَيَّرٌ، وفاعلُ الشرِّ يُقالُ لَهُ: شرِّيرٌ، قالوا: فالشرُّ ليسَ مِنْ فعل الله تعالى. قالَ ابنُ التلمساني: (أجابَ المتكلمونَ: بأنَّ الأفعال تُنسَبُ إلى الله تعالى مِنْ حيثُ تجدُّدُها وافتقارها إلى المخصص، وذلكَ لا يختلفُ بكونها خيراً أو شرّاً؛ فإنَّهما أمران إضافيانِ ليسا مِنْ صفاتِ نفس الأفعال، فإنَّ قتل الشخص المعيَّن شيء واحد قد يكون شرّاً بالنسبة إلى أوليائه، وخيراً بالنسبة إلى أعدائه. وإذا حُقِّقَ أنَّ الحسن والقبيح يرجعان إلى الشرع.. فمعنى الحسن: هو المقولُ فيهِ: افعلوه، ومعنى القبيح: هو المقولُ فيهِ: لا تفعلوه. وذلك لا يتحقق إلا بالنسبة إلى العبادِ، فالأفعالُ كلُّها بالنسبة إلى اللهِ تعالى حسنةٌ؛ إذْ معنى الحسن: ما لفاعلهِ أنْ يفعله، وما وردَ الثناء على فاعله، والأفعال كلُّها بالنسبة إلى الله تعالى كذلك؛ لأنَّ لهُ تعالى أَنْ يفعل كل ممكن، وهو المُثْنى عليه بكل كمال. وأما قولُ المعتزلة: فاعلُ الشرِّ شرِّيرٌ .. فليس بلازم؛ فإِنَّ أسماء الله تعالى توقيفيَّةٌ، ولهُ الأسماء الحسنى والصفات العلا، فيُقالُ لَهُ: يا خالق كل شيءٍ، ولا يُقال له: يا خالق القردة والخنازير!)
The Mu'tazila adopted this path; they said: 'The doer of good is called good, and the doer of evil is called evil.' They said: 'Evil is not from the act of Allah Most High.' Ibn al-Tilmisani said: 'The mutakallimun answered: that actions are attributed to Allah Most High from the aspect of their origination and their need for the determiner, and that does not differ whether they are good or evil; for both are relative matters not from the attributes of the actions themselves. For killing a particular person is one thing; it may be evil relative to his allies and good relative to his enemies. And when it is verified that beauty and ugliness revert to the Law... then the meaning of beautiful is that regarding which it is said: do it; and the meaning of ugly is that regarding which it is said: do not do it. And that is realized only with respect to the servants. So all actions are beautiful with respect to Allah Most High, since the meaning of beautiful is that the doer may do it, and praise is given to its doer. All actions with respect to Allah Most High are like that, because He has the right to do every possible thing, and He is praised for every perfection. As for the Mu'tazilites' saying 'the doer of evil is evil,' it is not necessary; for the names of Allah Most High are tawqifiyya, and to Him belong the most beautiful names and the highest attributes. So one says to Him: 'O Creator of everything,' and one does not say: 'O Creator of apes and swine!')
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite argument that evil cannot be attributed to Allah. The Mu'tazilites claimed that the doer of good is called 'good' and the doer of evil is called 'evil', therefore Allah, being good, cannot do evil. Ibn al-Tilmisani presents the response of the mutakallimun (theologians) in several steps. First, they attribute actions to Allah based on their origination and need for a determiner (mukhaṣṣiṣ), not on their moral quality. Good and evil are relative (iḍāfiyān), not intrinsic to actions. For example, killing a person is evil for his relatives but good for his enemies. Second, when analyzed according to the Sharī'a, 'good' (ḥasan) means what the Law commands to do, and 'evil' (qabīḥ) means what it prohibits. These categories apply only to human actions. From Allah's perspective, all actions are ḥasan because He has the right to do anything possible, and He is praised for all perfection. Thus the Mu'tazilite analogy fails. Finally, the argument that calling Allah the doer of evil would require calling Him 'evil' is invalid because Allah's names are tawqīfiyya (based on revealed texts, not human reasoning). He is called 'Creator of everything,' but not specific evil things like apes and swine, even though He creates them.

خيّر
شرّير
أمران إضافيان
الحسن والقبيح
توقيفية
المخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That good and evil are intrinsic properties of actions and that Allah cannot create evil without being called evil.
Correction: Ibn al-Tilmisani shows that good and evil are relative and based on divine command, and Allah's names are determined by revelation, not human reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the response in this passage, why are all actions from Allah considered 'ḥasan'?
A) Because they are always beneficial to all.
B) Because 'ḥasan' means what has the value of being done by its doer and earns praise.
C) Because Allah only does good deeds according to the Shari'a.
D) Because evil is not real.

Key Takeaway
Allah creates all actions, but moral categories are relative and based on Shari'a; His names are from revelation alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter suffering or evil in your own life, do you ever catch yourself silently accusing Allah of being unfair? How can you train your heart to see all events—pleasant and painful—as coming from the Creator of everything, who is praised for every perfection? Commit to a specific dua or reflection you will use the next time you feel shaken by a trial.
?
The Mu'tazilites claimed that doing evil makes one evil. Have you ever judged Allah's actions by human standards—expecting Him to fit your sense of justice? This lesson says His names are from revelation, not your reason. Will you surrender your own definitions of good and evil to what the Law and revelation say, even when they clash with your feelings? Write down one area where you struggle to accept Allah's decree as 'good'.
?
Ibn al-Tilmisani says we do not call Allah 'Creator of apes and swine' even though He creates them. Is there a name or attribute you subconsciously deny to Allah because it seems beneath His majesty? Reflect: do you truly believe that Allah is the Creator of everything, including the things you find repulsive or unjust? Let that sink in until it transforms how you speak about His actions.

Two Verses Refuting Dualism
Sanusi explains two verses refuting dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two key Qur'anic verses refute dualism and establish the necessity of tawhid.

Core Idea
The verses show that dualism leads to corruption or independence, both impossible for God.

In this passage, Imam al-Sanusi explains the logical implications of two verses. The first verse invalidates mutual prevention between gods; the second refutes the dualist claim of two independent agents. Together, they prove that only one God can exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the argument. Then read the translation.
Notice how Sanusi uses concise language.
2
Analyze the first verse
The first verse targets the idea of mutual prevention (tamānuʿ). If there were two gods, they would prevent each other from creating, making the existence of contingent things impossible.
This argument is based on the principle of conflict between equal powers.
3
Analyze the second verse
The second verse refutes dualism (belief in two separate agents for good and evil). It shows that each would be independent and superior to the other, contradicting the nature of the divine who must be supreme.
The dualist claim of two agents with distinct domains is logically inconsistent with the oneness required for divinity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالآية الأولى : كاشفةٌ لوجه الاستدلال على إبطال التمانع في القدرة والعلم وسائر الصفاتِ ؛ لما يفضي إليهِ مِنَ الفسادِ والتمانع المانع من وقوع الممكنات . والآية الثانية : مرشدة إلى إبطالِ قولِ مَنْ يَدَّعي فاعلين يقدرُ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما على غير ما يقدر عليهِ الآخرُ ، كما قالَتِ الثنوية بتمييز فاعل الخير عن فاعل الشرِّ ، فإِنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ منهما يذهب بما خلق ، ويلزم علوٌّ كلّ واحدٍ منهما على الآخرِ ؛ للاستغناء عنه بما يفعله الآخرُ ، فيكون عالياً عليه بذلك ، والإله يعلو ولا يُعلى عليهِ . 6 قيل : ولا يُعرَفُ أحدٌ مِنَ العقلاء يُثْبِتُ فاعلين على النعتِ الأَوَّلِ بل كلُّ مَنْ أثبت فاعلاً غيرَ اللهِ عزَّ وجلَّ فلم يُثبت له عموم تأثير ( انتهى كلام ابن التلمساني ) فأنت ترى كيف مالَ إلى عدم الاكتفاء بالسمع في معرفة الوحدانية بما أوردَهُ مِنَ الحُجَّةِ على ذلكَ ، وإلى قريب منها أشرتُ في العقيدة بقولي : ( لأنَّ ثبوت الصانع لا يتحقق بدونِها ... ) إلى آخره ؛ يعني : أنَّ ثبوت الصانع على سبيل التعيين لفعل مِنَ الأفعال لا يَتحقَّقُ بدون الوحدانية ؛ إذ على تقدير عدمِها لا يُدرى في كلِّ فعلٍ مَنْ فعلَهُ ، ومِنْ جملة ذلكَ : الخارقُ الذي ظهر على يد الرسولِ ؛ فإِنَّهُ لا يُدرى على تقدير عدمِ معرفةِ الوحدانيةِ مَنِ المُرْسِلُ الذي خلق ذلكَ الخارق على يد الرسولِ ليصدَّقَهُ بهِ ، فصارَ ثبوت الصانع المرسل مجهولاً ، فكيف يُعرَفُ منهُ رسولُهُ ؟! وقد عرفت أنَّ الرسولَ لم يُعرَفْ إِلا مِنْ قِبَلِ مرسله المعلوم بخلْقِ أفعال على صفةٍ مخصوصة تدلُّ على ذلك ، فإذا كانَ المرسل مجهولاً إِنَّما يُعرَفُ مِنْ قِبَلِ الرسول . . لزم الدور ضرورةً .
The first verse: it reveals the aspect of inference for invalidating mutual prevention (al-tamānuʿ) in power, knowledge, and all other attributes; because it leads to corruption and mutual prevention that prevents the occurrence of possible things. The second verse: it guides to invalidating the statement of those who claim two agents, each of them able to do what the other cannot, as the Dualists said distinguishing the agent of good from the agent of evil; for each of them takes what he created, and it necessitates that each of them is superior to the other; because he is independent of what the other does, so he becomes superior to him thereby, and the deity is superior and is not surpassed. It was said: And no one among the rational people is known who affirms two agents in the first description; rather, everyone who affirmed an agent other than God, may He be glorified and exalted, did not affirm for him universal influence. (End of the speech of Ibn al-Tilmisani). So you see how he inclined to not being satisfied with revelation (al-samʿ) in knowing divine unity (al-waḥdāniyya) by what he presented of proof for that. And to something close to that I referred in the creed by my saying: 'Because the establishment of the Maker is not realized without it...' etc.; meaning: that the establishment of the Maker as specifically responsible for an action is not realized without divine unity; for if it is absent, it is not known for every action who did it. And among that is: the miracle (al-khāriq) that appeared at the hand of the messenger; for it is not known, on the supposition of not knowing divine unity, who is the sender who created that miracle at the hand of the messenger to give him credence thereby. So the establishment of the Maker-sender becomes unknown. How then can his messenger be known from him?! And you have known that the messenger is not known except from his sender who is known by creating actions of a particular description that indicate that. So if the sender is unknown but is only known from the messenger, then circularity (al-dawr) necessarily follows.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage presents a theological argument for the necessity of divine unity (tawhid) in knowing the sender of a prophet. The author begins by explaining two Qur'anic verses. The first verse reveals the inference to invalidate mutual prevention (tamānuʿ) in God's attributes—if there were two gods, they would prevent each other from creating, making the existence of contingent things impossible. The second verse invalidates dualism (the belief in two agents, one for good and one for evil) by showing that each agent would be independent and superior to the other, contradicting the nature of the divine who is supreme. The author then quotes Ibn al-Tilmisani, who argues that no rational person acknowledges two agents with universal influence; those who affirm a creator other than God do not give that other universal efficacy. The author agrees, noting that Ibn al-Tilmisani relied on rational proof rather than mere revelation for knowing tawhid. The author then expands on this: without knowing tawhid, one cannot determine which agent performed any particular action, including miracles performed by a prophet. Consequently, the sender (God) who created the miracle remains unknown. Since a prophet is known only through his sender, ignorance of the sender leads to circular reasoning (dawr)—the sender would be known only through the messenger, and the messenger only through the sender. This impossibility underscores that tawhid must be known independently before prophethood can be established.

تمانع
فاعلين
ثنوية
وحدانية

Checkpoint
What does the first verse invalidate according to Sanusi?
A) The existence of angels
B) Mutual prevention (tamānuʿ) in God's attributes
C) The need for prophets
D) The concept of free will

Key Takeaway
The two verses demonstrate that belief in more than one God leads to logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely faced a tragedy or deep injustice that made you question God's goodness. Did the thought ever cross your mind that perhaps evil has its own separate source? How does the argument from this lesson—that two independent gods would each be superior to the other—force you to reject that temptation and trust in the oneness of God even when you don't understand His decree?
?
When your desires pull you away from obedience, do you sometimes act as if there are two competing authorities in your life—one calling you to sin and one to righteousness? How does the first verse's argument about mutual prevention expose the impossibility of serving two masters, and what must you do today to surrender fully to the one true God?

The Necessity of Tawhid for Knowing the Sender
Sanusi argues tawhid is prerequisite for knowing the prophet's sender.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of tawhid is necessary to identify the sender of a prophet and avoid circular reasoning.

Core Idea
Without tawhid, the miracle cannot be attributed to a specific sender, leading to circularity.

After refuting dualism, al-Sanusi argues that even miracles cannot prove prophethood unless one already knows tawhid. Otherwise, you don't know who performed the miracle, so you can't know the sender, and you need the sender to know the prophet. This creates a vicious circle.

Guided Path
1
Understand Ibn al-Tilmisani's point
Read the quote from Ibn al-Tilmisani: no rational person affirms two agents with universal influence. Those who affirm a creator other than God do not give that other universal efficacy.
This sets up the rational basis for tawhid, independent of revelation.
2
Apply to the miracle of the prophet
Sanusi then applies this: without knowing tawhid, we cannot attribute any act—including miracles—to a specific agent. The miracle at the prophet's hand could be from any supposed god.
The miracle's agent is ambiguous unless we already know there is only one God.
3
Recognize the circularity
If the sender is unknown, the prophet cannot be known (since the prophet is known through his sender). But if we try to know the sender through the prophet, we get circular reasoning (dawr). This shows tawhid must be known first.
Circularity is a logical impossibility; thus tawhid is a prerequisite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالآية الأولى : كاشفةٌ لوجه الاستدلال على إبطال التمانع في القدرة والعلم وسائر الصفاتِ ؛ لما يفضي إليهِ مِنَ الفسادِ والتمانع المانع من وقوع الممكنات . والآية الثانية : مرشدة إلى إبطالِ قولِ مَنْ يَدَّعي فاعلين يقدرُ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما على غير ما يقدر عليهِ الآخرُ ، كما قالَتِ الثنوية بتمييز فاعل الخير عن فاعل الشرِّ ، فإِنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ منهما يذهب بما خلق ، ويلزم علوٌّ كلّ واحدٍ منهما على الآخرِ ؛ للاستغناء عنه بما يفعله الآخرُ ، فيكون عالياً عليه بذلك ، والإله يعلو ولا يُعلى عليهِ . 6 قيل : ولا يُعرَفُ أحدٌ مِنَ العقلاء يُثْبِتُ فاعلين على النعتِ الأَوَّلِ بل كلُّ مَنْ أثبت فاعلاً غيرَ اللهِ عزَّ وجلَّ فلم يُثبت له عموم تأثير ( انتهى كلام ابن التلمساني ) فأنت ترى كيف مالَ إلى عدم الاكتفاء بالسمع في معرفة الوحدانية بما أوردَهُ مِنَ الحُجَّةِ على ذلكَ ، وإلى قريب منها أشرتُ في العقيدة بقولي : ( لأنَّ ثبوت الصانع لا يتحقق بدونِها ... ) إلى آخره ؛ يعني : أنَّ ثبوت الصانع على سبيل التعيين لفعل مِنَ الأفعال لا يَتحقَّقُ بدون الوحدانية ؛ إذ على تقدير عدمِها لا يُدرى في كلِّ فعلٍ مَنْ فعلَهُ ، ومِنْ جملة ذلكَ : الخارقُ الذي ظهر على يد الرسولِ ؛ فإِنَّهُ لا يُدرى على تقدير عدمِ معرفةِ الوحدانيةِ مَنِ المُرْسِلُ الذي خلق ذلكَ الخارق على يد الرسولِ ليصدَّقَهُ بهِ ، فصارَ ثبوت الصانع المرسل مجهولاً ، فكيف يُعرَفُ منهُ رسولُهُ ؟! وقد عرفت أنَّ الرسولَ لم يُعرَفْ إِلا مِنْ قِبَلِ مرسله المعلوم بخلْقِ أفعال على صفةٍ مخصوصة تدلُّ على ذلك ، فإذا كانَ المرسل مجهولاً إِنَّما يُعرَفُ مِنْ قِبَلِ الرسول . . لزم الدور ضرورةً .

Refutation of the Objection to Multiple Gods
Sanusi refutes an objection by showing logical consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why limiting the number of gods to only actually existing possible things fails to avoid an infinite regress and leads to contradictions.

Core Idea
Limiting gods to actual possible things still leads to an impossible infinite.

The objector tries to salvage the idea of multiple gods by restricting them to only those possible things that will actually exist. Sanusi shows that this restriction causes two fatal problems: it turns non-existent possible things into impossibilities, and even actual existents are endless in succession, leading to an infinite number of co-eternal gods which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the objector's proposal. Understand that they attempt to limit gods to only actual possible things to avoid infinity.
The objector assumes that possible things not decreed to exist are irrelevant.
2
Identify Sanusi's first argument
Notice that Sanusi says limiting gods to actual existents causes an 'inversion of realities': non-existent possible things would become impossible because they lack a potential creator.
This is a logical contradiction: things considered possible would become impossible.
3
Identify Sanusi's second argument
Even actual existents are endless in time (e.g., heaven and hell). Thus there would be infinite gods in sequence, but for eternal gods they must be simultaneous, which is impossible.
The type of infinity (simultaneous vs. successive) is key.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the objection fails decisively: it leads to an impossible simultaneous infinite of co-eternal beings.
This reinforces the proof of God's oneness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : ما المانعُ أنْ يُقالَ بجواز تعددِ الإله بعددِ الممكناتِ ؟ ولا يلزم منه وجود ما لا نهاية لهُ ؛ لأنَّا نقول : المراد بالممكنات ما سبق قضاء الآلهة بأنَّهُ يُوجَدُ ، لا كلُّ ما يفرضُهُ العقلُ مِنَ الممكناتِ وإِنْ كَانَ لا يُوجَدُ أصلاً • قلتُ : يلزمُ مِنْ قصر عددِ الآلهة وقُدَرِهِم وإراداتهم على ما يُوجَدُ مِنَ الممكناتِ دونَ ما لا يُوجَدُ منها . انقلاب الحقائقِ ؛ وهو عَوْدُ الممكنات التي لا تُوجَدُ مستحيلةً ؛ إذ لا يصح الحكم بإمكان وجودِ مع الحكم باستحالة وجودِ صانعِهِ ، على أنَّ ما يُوجَدُ مِنَ الممكناتِ لا نهاية له أيضاً ؛ أعني : باعتبار عدم الانقطاع ، لا أنَّ لجميعها في الوجود الاجتماع (۱) ، ولا شكّ أنَّ هذا النوع مِنْ عدم النهاية ممكن عقلاً ، موجود شرعاً ؛ بدليل نعيم أهل الجنة وعذاب أهل النار فيلزم إذا وُجِدَ لكلِّ واحدٍ منها إلهُ أنْ يدخل في الوجودِ مِنْ عددِ الآلهة ما لا نهاية له ، وعدم النهايةِ اللازمُ في الآلهة هو النوع المستحيلُ منه (۱) ؛ لأنَّه يجب أن يكون بحسب الاجتماع ، لا بحسب عدمِ الانقطاع كما في الممكناتِ المذكورة ؛ لوجوب قدم الإلهِ ، فيستحيل أنْ يتأخَّرَ في هذا الفَرْضِ بعضُ الآلهة على بعض ، وبالله التوفيق
If you say: 'What prevents one from saying that it is permissible for there to be multiple gods according to the number of possible things? And from that does not follow an existence that has no end, because we say: what is meant by possible things is what the decree of the gods has preceded that it will exist, not everything that the mind posits from possible things, even if it does not exist at all.' I say: 'From limiting the number of gods, their powers, and their wills to that which exists from possible things, excluding that which does not exist from them, follows an inversion of realities; and that is the return of possible things that do not exist to being impossible; for it is not correct to judge the possibility of existence together with the judgment of the impossibility of the existence of its maker. Moreover, that which exists from possible things also has no end; I mean: in terms of non-interruption, not that all of them have simultaneous existence. And there is no doubt that this type of non-end is possible rationally, existing legally; by the evidence of the bliss of the people of Paradise and the punishment of the people of Hellfire. So it follows that if there exists for each one of them a god, then there would enter into existence from the number of gods that which has no end, and the necessary non-end in the gods is the type that is impossible from it; because it must be in terms of simultaneity, not in terms of non-interruption as in the mentioned possible things; due to the necessity of the pre-eternity of the god, so it is impossible that in this supposition some gods would be later than others. And with God is success.'
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer within the proof against multiple gods (tamānuʿ). The objector suggests a scenario: suppose there are as many gods as the number of possible things that will actually exist (as determined by divine decree), and no more. This would avoid an infinite number because only actual existents are counted. The objector claims this escapes the earlier reasoning. Sanusi responds with two points. First, limiting the number of gods only to actual existents leads to an 'inversion of realities': possible things that do not exist would become impossible, because they would lack a potential creator, yet they are still considered possible. This is contradictory. Second, even the set of actual existents is infinite in the sense of non-interruption (e.g., successive rewards and punishments are endless in time), though not simultaneous. Thus a god for each would still result in an infinite number of gods. However, this infinity would be simultaneous, which is impossible for beings that are all eternal (pre-eternal). Because an infinite number of co-eternal beings cannot exist together; they would have to be successive, but that would contradict their pre-eternity. Hence the objection fails.

Key Terms
التمانع
الآلهة
الممكنات
عدم النهاية
الاجتماع
عدم الانقطاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that limiting the number of gods to only those possible things that will actually exist avoids the problem of infinite regress.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even this restricted number leads to an infinite simultaneous number of eternal gods, which is logically impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is limiting the number of gods to only existing possible things invalid?
A) Because possible things are infinite in number and cannot be limited.
B) Because it leads to an inversion of realities and an infinite simultaneous regress of gods.
C) Because it implies that some possible things are created by different gods.
D) Because it contradicts the divine decree.

Key Takeaway
The attempt to limit multiple gods to actual possible things fails because it leads to an impossible infinite number of co-eternal beings and contradicts the possibility of non-existent things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage forces you to confront the logical impossibility of multiple gods. In what areas of your life do you still act as if something other than Allah has ultimate control—your career, your reputation, your desires? What will you do today to uproot that hidden shirk?
?
The objector here tried to use intellectual maneuvering to save multiple gods. When have you used clever reasoning to avoid submitting fully to Allah's oneness? Will you let this argument settle into your heart as a decisive proof, or will you rationalize it away?
?
Sanusi shows that even a limited infinite of gods is impossible. How does this deepen your reliance on Allah alone? Commit to one specific action this week that demonstrates your belief that only Allah deserves ultimate trust and worship.

Applying the Proof of Mutual Opposition to Refute the Qadariyya
Sanusi applies the proof of mutual opposition to refute the Qadariyya claim of independent human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof of mutual opposition (dalīl al-tamānuʿ) is used to refute the Qadariyya doctrine of independent human power.

Core Idea
Claiming human power prevails over God's power implies attributing weakness and a partner to Allah.

Sanusi now applies the proof of mutual opposition—previously used to establish God's oneness—to a specific theological error: the Qadariyya claim that human power is independently effective. This lesson shows how the same logical structure that condemns multiple gods also condemns giving human power independent agency alongside God's. As you read, notice how Sanusi draws a direct parallel between the two cases.

Guided Path
1
Recall the proof of mutual opposition
Quickly review the earlier argument: if there were multiple gods, their wills would conflict, necessitating an impossible infinite regress. That proof established God's unique divinity.
Sanusi uses this same proof as the foundation for refuting the Qadariyya.
2
Read the passage closely
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi first states the conclusion, then unpacks the reasoning step by step.
Pay attention to the parallel he draws between multiple gods and the Qadariyya case.
3
Identify the parallel reasoning (wajh)
Sanusi explains that the Qadariyya claim that human power prevents God's power from affecting an act. This is logically identical to one god preventing another's will, which implies incapacity in the one who fails to prevail.
If that is impossible for God, then attributing it to Him is equally impossible.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Sanusi ends with a powerful rhetorical question: if incapacity before an equal god is a defect, how much worse is it before a mere servant? This shows the Qadariyya doctrine is actually a form of shirk.
Consider why this argument would be persuasive to a Muslim audience.

فيلزم إذا وجد لكل واحد منها إله أن يدخل في الوجود من عدد الآلهة ما لا نهاية له، وعدم النهاية اللازم في الآلهة هو النوع المستحيل منه؛ لأنه يجب أن يكون بحسب الاجتماع، لا بحسب عدم الانقطاع كما في الممكنات المذكورة؛ لوجوب قدم الإله، فيستحيل أن يتأخر في هذا الفرض بعض الآلهة على بعض، وبالله التوفيق

فصل في تفرده تعالى بإيجاد جميع الأفعال، وأن أفعال السوى
وبهذا الدليل بعينه - أعني: دليل التمانع - نستدل على أنه جل وعلا هو الموجد لأفعال العباد، ولا تأثير لقدرهم الحادثة فيها، بل هي موجودة مقارنة لها

يعني: أن الدليل على رد مذهب القدرية القائلين بأن القدرة الحادثة للعباد هي المؤثرة في أفعالهم على وفق اختيارهم، ولا تأثير للقدرة القديمة أصلاً في تلك الأفعال الاختيارية، ولا جريان لها على وفق إرادته، جل وتعالى عما يقول الظالمون علواً كبيراً. هو دليل التمانع السابق.

ووجهه: أن اللازم فيه في تعدد الآلهة ثبوت العجز للإله عند عدم نفوذ إرادته، وذلك بعينه لازم في مذهب القدرية؛ فإنهم جعلوا تعلق قدرة العبد وإرادته بالفعل مانعة من تعلق قدرة الله تعالى وإرادته بذلك الفعل، مع القطع بأن ذلك الفعل من جملة الممكنات التي قام البرهان القطعي على وجوب تعلق قدرة الله تعالى وإرادته بوصف العموم بجميعها.

فصار إذاً هذا الفعل قد توجهت نحوه قدرة العبد وقدرة مولانا جل وعلا، وإرادة العبد وإرادة مولانا سبحانه وتعالى؛ لما عرفت من عموم تعلق قدرته تعالى وإرادته، ثم زعمت القدرية مجوس هذه الأمة: أن الذي نفذ وأثر في الفعل - والحالة هذه - إنما هو أضعف القدرتين وأضعف الإرادتين؛ وهما قدرة العبد الفقير الحقير وإرادته! وهل هذا القول الشنيع إلا قول بإثبات الشريك له تعالى، ووسم له بنقيصة العجز وغلبة الغير له؟!

وإذا كان عجز الإله بتقدير نفوذ إرادة إله آخر يماثله قادحاً هيته، وموجباً لنقصه وعدم ذاته.. فكيف بعجزه لنفوذ قدرة عبده وإرادته؟!
So it follows that if there is a god for each one of them, there would come into existence from the number of gods what has no end, and the infinity necessary in the gods is the type that is impossible from it; because it must be according to aggregation, not according to lack of termination as in the previously mentioned contingent things; due to the necessity of the pre-eternity of the god, so it is impossible that in this supposition some gods be later than others, and with Allah is success.

Chapter on His uniqueness, exalted is He, in creating all actions, and that the actions of others [are created by Him]
And by this same proof – I mean: the proof of mutual opposition – we argue that He, majestic and exalted, is the creator of the actions of servants, and there is no effect of their originated power in them, rather they are existent concurrent with it.

Meaning: that the proof for rejecting the doctrine of the Qadariyya, who say that the originated power of servants is the effective one in their actions according to their choice, and there is no effect of the eternal power at all in those voluntary actions, and no running of them according to His will – majestic and exalted is He above what the wrongdoers say, greatly exalted – is the preceding proof of mutual opposition.

And its face: that what is necessary in it regarding the multiplicity of gods is the establishment of incapacity for the god when His will does not prevail, and that same thing is necessary in the doctrine of the Qadariyya; for they made the attachment of the servant's power and will to the act a preventer of the attachment of Allah's power and will to that act, while being certain that that act is among the contingent things for which a decisive proof has been established on the obligation of the attachment of Allah's power and will with the attribute of generality to all of them.

So therefore this act has been directed towards it the power of the servant and the power of our Master, majestic and exalted, and the will of the servant and the will of our Master, glorified and exalted; because of what you have known of the generality of the attachment of His power and will. Then the Qadariyya – the Magians of this nation – claimed that the one which penetrated and had effect in the act – in this situation – is only the weaker of the two powers and the weaker of the two wills; namely the power of the poor, contemptible servant and his will! And is this dreadful statement anything but a statement affirming a partner to Him, exalted, and branding Him with the deficiency of incapacity and the overcoming of another over Him?

And if the incapacity of the God by supposing the prevalence of the will of another god similar to Him is a defect in His essence and necessitating His deficiency and non-existence of His essence... then how about His incapacity due to the prevalence of the power of His servant and his will?
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage is a proof-by-analogy argument. Sanusi first recalls the conclusion of the proof of mutual opposition (dalīl al-tamānuʿ): if there were multiple gods, it would necessitate an infinite regress of a specific impossible kind (aggregation, not temporal succession), which is impossible because each god must be eternal. Then he announces a new chapter: that this same proof demonstrates God's uniqueness in creating all actions and that human actions are created by God, not by human power. He explains that the proof against the Qadariyya (those who claim human power is independently effective) is exactly the same proof of mutual opposition. The reasoning (wajh) is: in the multiple-gods case, the necessary consequence is that each god would be incapable when another's will prevails—this is a defect. In the Qadariyya doctrine, they claim that the human's power and will prevent God's power and will from attaching to the same act, even though that act is among all contingent things to which God's power and will must attach universally. Thus, both God's and the human's power and will face the same act. But then the Qadariyya assert that it is the weaker power (human) that prevails and is effective, which is absurd. This implies that God is weak and has a partner. Sanusi drives the point home with a rhetorical question: if God being unable to prevail against another god would be a defect in His divinity, how much more so being unable to prevail against His servant's power?

Key Terms

القدرية
القدرة الحادثة
العجز
الإرادة
الشريك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qadariyya doctrine is merely a matter of human free will and does not involve shirk.
Correction: Sanusi demonstrates that claiming human power is independently effective inevitably leads to attributing a partner and deficiency to God, which is shirk.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the necessary consequence of the Qadariyya claim that human power is independently effective?
A) That God's power is unlimited but human power is an illusion
B) That God is weak and has a partner, similar to the case of multiple gods
C) That human actions have no moral value
D) That God's will is inapplicable to human actions

Key Takeaway
The proof of mutual opposition not only establishes God's oneness but also refutes any doctrine that grants independent agency to created beings, as it would imply limitation and partnership with God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself believing that your success, health, or achievement came solely from your effort and planning, forgetting that Allah is the ultimate creator of every action? How does this lesson challenge you to repent from that hidden shirk?
?
If the Qadariyya doctrine implies that your will can overpower Allah’s will, then what about the moments you insist on your own desires despite knowing Allah’s command? In what specific area of your life are you most prone to acting as if your will is independent?
?
The proof shows that any claim of independent agency reduces Allah to weakness. When you rely on your own power without seeking Allah’s help, are you not, in that moment, practically denying His absolute control? What step will you take today to consciously affirm that every action you do is only by His permission and power?

The Impossible Consequences of Human Power Creating Acts
Sanusi proves the impossibility of human power creating acts using a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that human power cannot independently create acts, using a dilemma that leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
Human power cannot be truly creative — it would either negate its own nature or oppose God's omnipotence.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument by dilemma, one of the key tools in kalam. Sanusi takes the opponent's position — that human power can create acts — and shows it leads to two impossible conclusions. Before reading, bear in mind that the discussion is not about free will but about the nature of created power and its inability to produce real effects independently.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation carefully. Notice the dense logical structure.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the two branches of the dilemma
The dilemma asks: is the effect (ta'thir) an essential attribute of the power or not? Find these two branches in the text.
The first branch is 'if the first', the second is 'if the second'.
3
Understand why each branch leads to impossibility
For each branch, note the specific absurdity it produces: either the power's essential attribute is negated, or it overcomes God's power, or an infinite regress occurs.
These are all impossible in Sunni theology.
4
Examine the attempted objection and its refutation
Sanusi anticipates a possible counterargument and refutes it. See how he responds to the claim that the effect is only in accordance with God's will.
The refutation relies on the essential nature of the attribute.
5
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Consider the overall conclusion: originated power cannot create. How does this argument reinforce divine transcendence and omnipotence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنَّ القدرة الحادثة على مقتضى هذا القول : إمَّا أنْ يكونَ مِنْ صفةِ نفسها إيجاد الفعل الذي تتعلَّقُ بهِ أو لا ؛ فإنْ كانَ الأوَّلُ لزمَ عندَ تعلُّقها بالفعل إمَّا سلب صفتِها النفسيَّةِ إِنْ لم تُؤثر في الفعل ، وكان الموجدُ له هو الله تعالى ، أو غلبتها لقدرته تعالى إِنْ كانَتْ هي التي أثَرَتْ في الفعل ، وفرضنا أنَّ اللهَ تعالى أرادَ أَنْ يُوجِدَ ذلكَ الفعلَ بقدرته ، وكلا الأمرين محال ولا يدفع محذور ما لزمَ مِنَ العجز والغلبة في الثاني .. قوله : ( إِنَّ تأثيرها إنَّما هو على وَفْقِ إرادتِهِ تعالى ( لأنَّ التأثيرَ إِذا قُدِّرَ أَنَّهُ صفةٌ نفسيَّةُ للقدرة الحادثة.. لم يمكن أن يتوقف ثبوتُهُ لها على شيء أصلاً ، وإنْ كانَ الثاني - وهو أنَّ التأثير ليس صفةً نفسيَّةً للقدرةِ الحادثة - لزمَ أنْ تفتقر إلى معنى يقوم بها ويُوجِبُ لها التأثير ، وننقل حينئذٍ الكلام إلى ذلك المعنى الذي أوجب لها التأثير : هل ذلك أيضاً مِنْ صفة نفسِهِ ، أو لمعنى قامَ بهِ ؟ ويلزم التسلسل ، وقيام المعنى بالمعنى
Because the originated power (al-qudra al- ḥāditha) according to this statement: either it is of its own essential attribute to create the act to which it is related, or not. If the first, then upon its relation to the act it necessarily entails either the negation of its essential attribute if it does not effect the act, while the one who causes it to exist is God the Exalted, or its being overcome by His power if it is that which effected the act, and we suppose that God the Exalted intended to create that act by His power; and both matters are impossible. And the harm entailed from the inability and overcoming in the second [branch] is not repelled by his saying: 'Its effect is only in accordance with His will' because the effect, when it is assumed to be an essential attribute of the originated power, cannot have its subsistence for it depend on anything at all. And if the second—that the effect is not an essential attribute of the originated power—then it necessarily requires that it be in need of a meaning subsisting in it which necessitates the effect; then we transfer the discussion to that meaning which necessitated the effect: whether that also is from its own attribute, or by a meaning subsisting in it? And this leads to infinite regress (tasalsul) and the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage is a proof by dilemma against the claim that human power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) can independently create acts. The author, al-Sanusi, assumes a premise from the opponent and shows it leads to absurdities. He structures the argument as follows: either the effect (ta'thir) is an essential attribute of the power itself, or it is not. If it is essential, then when the power relates to an act, two impossible outcomes follow: either (a) the power does not effect the act, meaning its essential attribute is negated (since it fails to produce its natural effect), while God is the true creator, or (b) the power does effect the act, but then it must overcome God's own creative power, which is impossible because God is all-powerful. Both are impossible. Then he addresses an attempted objection: someone might say that the power's effect is always in accordance with God's will. But al-Sanusi responds that if the effect is an essential attribute, it cannot depend on any external condition—it would necessarily occur whenever the power relates to an act, leading to the conflict mentioned. Next, he considers the second branch: if the effect is not an essential attribute but rather something acquired through another attribute, then an infinite regress arises because that attribute would itself need another attribute to produce its effect, and so on. This regress is impossible. Thus the original position is refuted.

Key Terms
القدرة الحادثة
التأثير
الصفة النفسية
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this argument is about free will or moral responsibility.
Correction: The argument is strictly about the nature of created power: whether it can truly create acts or not. It does not deny human agency in a causal sense but denies that humans can bring about existence independently of Allah.

Checkpoint
What are the two possible outcomes if the effect (ta'thir) is an essential attribute of the originated power?
A) Either the power creates the act or it does not.
B) Either the power's essential attribute is negated (if it does not effect) or it overcomes God's power (if it does effect).
C) Either the power is effective or it is not effective, but both are possible.
D) Either the power acts independently or it depends on God's will.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's dilemma shows that the claim of human power creating acts leads to impossibility, affirming that all creation belongs to Allah alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly believe that your own effort and ability are what truly produce your good deeds, even if you verbally attribute them to Allah? How does this lesson expose that hidden shirk in your heart?
?
If every genuine act of goodness you perform is entirely created by Allah, what does that mean for your sense of pride? Will you now actively train yourself to attribute every success to Him alone, starting today?
?
When you feel weak or incapable, do you despair because you think your own power has failed you? How does this truth about human power being non-creative free you to rely completely on Allah's omnipotence in your daily struggles?

The Sinner's Objection and the Creation of Inclination
Sanusi presents a Mu'tazili-type objection and cuts it short with the creation of inclination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazili principle of 'salvation and what is best' leads to absurd consequences, and how the Ash'ari doctrine of the creation of inclination refutes such objections.

Core Idea
The sinner's objection shows the Mu'tazila are hoist by their own petard.

Sanusi puts a powerful hypothetical in the mouth of a sinner to expose a fatal flaw in the Mu'tazili worldview. If God were obliged to create only what is best for each servant, then creating a soul destined for eternal punishment would be unjust and self-defeating. This lesson walks through the sinner's rhetorical barrage and then points to Sanusi's terse refutation: the creation of inclination.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the sinner's questions. Notice the escalating frustration: from 'why create desire?' to 'why create me at all?'
The translation will help clarify the rhetorical tone.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili assumption
All the sinner's questions rely on the Mu'tazili axiom that God must always do what is best (al-salāh wa'l-aslah). Ask yourself: what would happen if that principle were true?
This is the key to understanding the reductio.
3
Understand the refutation reference
Sanusi ends by pointing to his earlier statement about 'creation of inclination'. This is the Ash'ari counter: God creates both the act and the inclination, so the sinner's 'why' questions are based on a false premise.
The refutation is not detailed here; it is invoked as a known doctrine.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Reflect on why this argument matters for divine transcendence. It shows that human reason cannot demand explanations from God—His wisdom is beyond our grasp.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم خلقت معصية ، فكان للعاصي أنْ يحتج أيضاً على مذهبكم ـ لو صحتِ الحجَّةُ ـ بمثل ما يحتج به على مذهبنا بزعمكم ، فيقول : يا ربّ ؛ لي القدرة وأنت تعلمُ أنّي أعصي بها ؟! ولِمَ خلقْتَ ليَ الشهوة فيها ؟! بل ولِمَ خلقتني أصلاً إذْ علمْتَ أنِّي لستُ ممَّنْ يصلحُ لطاعتِكَ ؟! وإذْ خلقتني فلِمَ لم تُمثني صغيراً قبل أن أبلغ سنَّ التكليف ؟! وإذْ بلَّغتني سنّ التكليف فلِمَ لم تجعلني مجنوناً لا أميز الأرضَ مِنَ السماء ؟! فذلكَ أسهل عليَّ بكثيرٍ مِمَّا عَرَّضَتَني لَهُ مِنَ العذاب الذي لا يُطاقُ ، وإذ جعلتني عاقلاً فلِمَ كلَّفتَني أصلاً ، وقد علمْتَ أنَّ التكليف لا يفيد في شيئاً ، بل هو من أعظم المصائبِ عليَّ ؟! وغير هذا ممَّا ينشأُ عن توهمات فاسدة . وإلى هذا المعنى أشرتُ بقولي : ( وأيضاً : يبطل بمسألة خلْقِ الداعي ... ) إلى آخره
Why did You create disobedience? Then the sinner would also argue against your doctrine—if the argument were valid—just as he argues against our doctrine according to your claim, saying: O Lord, I have power and You know that I disobey with it? And why did You create for me the desire in it? Rather, why did You create me at all when You knew that I am not among those who are fit for Your obedience? And when You created me, why did You not let me die as a child before I reached the age of obligation? And when You caused me to reach the age of obligation, why did You not make me insane, unable to distinguish earth from the sky? That would have been much easier for me than what You exposed me to of unbearable torment. And when You made me rational, why did You obligate me at all, when You knew that obligation profits me nothing, rather it is among the greatest calamities for me? And other things arising from corrupt fantasies. And to this meaning I referred with my saying: 'Also: it is invalidated by the creation of inclination...' to the end.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage presents a hypothetical objection that a sinner could raise against the Mu'tazili position, followed by Sanusi's reference to his own refutation. The passage begins with a series of rhetorical questions put in the mouth of a disobedient person, all based on the Mu'tazili assumption that God must act for the good of His servants. The sinner argues: if God knew I would disobey with the power He gave me, why did He give it? Why did He create the desire? Why create me at all? Why not let me die before obligation? Why not make me insane so I wouldn't be accountable? Why impose obligation if it brings only torment? These are intended to show that the Mu'tazili principle of 'salvation and what is best' leads to absurd consequences when applied to God's actions. Sanusi then states that this is the meaning of his earlier statement that the Mu'tazili position is invalidated by the issue of 'creation of inclination'—a reference to the Ash'ari doctrine that God creates both the action and the inclination, which deflects such objections.

Key Terms
مذهب
الصلاح والأصلح
خلق الداعي
القدرة
التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sinner's questions represent an actual Ash'ari argument against divine justice.
Correction: The passage is a reductio ad absurdum against the Mu'tazila. The Ash'ari position is that God is not obligated to create what is best, so the sinner's questions are based on a false premise.

Checkpoint
What does the sinner's hypothetical objection aim to demonstrate?
A) That God is unjust for punishing sinners.
B) That the Mu'tazili principle of al-salāh wa'l-aslah leads to absurdities.
C) That humans have free will to disobey.
D) That God should not have created the world.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili principle of 'salvation and what is best' is self-defeating when pushed to its logical conclusion, and the Ash'ari doctrine of the creation of inclination provides a sound refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disobey Allah, do you ever catch yourself internally blaming Him for giving you the desire or the circumstance? How does Sanusi's point about the creation of inclination (خلق الداعي) challenge that blame—and what would it mean for you to accept that Allah creates both the test and your choice? Are you willing to take full responsibility before Him tonight in your prayer?
?
The sinner in this passage wishes he had never been created or had been made insane to escape accountability. Have you ever felt such bitterness toward your own existence because of your struggles with sin? If so, what does this lesson teach you about the true cause of your suffering—is it Allah's 'injustice' or your own wrong response to His decree? Will you repent of that ingratitude today?
?
The Mu'tazili principle sounds compassionate (God must do what is best for you), but Sanusi shows it leads to absurdity. In your own life, do you hold a hidden expectation that Allah owes you a 'fair' or 'easy' life because He loves you? How would your relationship with Him change if you truly believed He owes you nothing and that His obligations are only from His own justice—and yet He still shows mercy? Will you thank Him for His mercy even when life is hard?

Ash'ari Doctrine: Created Power Has No Effect
Sanusi states the Ash'ari doctrine of created power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Ash'ari theology, created power (al-qudrah al-ḥādithah) relates to its object without any causal effect, analogous to knowledge, and is limited to its own locus.

Core Idea
Created power is like knowledge: it relates without causing.

Sanusi now turns from the attributes of Allah to the nature of human power. He clarifies the Ash'ari position: our power does not produce anything; it is simply a relation to an action that Allah creates. This directly opposes the Mu'tazilite view of human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari statement
Read the first part of the passage, which states the Ash'ari doctrine of created power. Note the key phrase 'لا تأثير لها' (no effect).
This is a direct denial of causal efficacy.
2
Understand the analogy to knowledge
Sanusi compares the relation of power to its object to the relation of knowledge to its object. How is the relation similar?
Knowledge relates to its object without changing it; power relates without effecting it.
3
Recognize the locus restriction
Notice that created power only relates to an object within its own locus. If the object is outside, there is no relation at all.
This limits power's scope to the immediate context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا الذي ذُكِرَ في أوصافه تعالى إلى هنا هو يجبُ في حقِّهِ تعالى ، وإذا عُلِمَ ما يجبُ عُلِمَ ما يستحيلُ ؛ وهو الواجب (۲) ضدُّ ذلك الواجب . مذهب أهل الحقِّ على ما سبق : أنَّ القدرة الحادثة لا تأثير لها في شيءٍ مِنَ الممكناتِ ، وهي تتعلق بمقدورها تعلقاً مِنْ غيرِ تأثير ، بل نسبته كنسبة العلم الذي يتعلَّق بمعلومِهِ ولا يُؤثر فيهِ (۱) ، إلا أنَّ القدرة الحادثة لا تتعلَّق بمقدور إلا في محلها (٢) ، وما خرجَ عن محلها فلا نسبة بينَهُ وبينَ القدرة لا بتأثير ولا غيره . والمعتزلة قد سبق أن مذهبهم أنَّ العبد مخترع أفعاله ، ووافقوا أنَّ القدرة الحادثة لا تتعلَّق مباشرة إلا بالمقدور الذي في محلها ، غير أنَّهم يرون أنَّ ما في محلّها سبب يُوجَدُ بهِ ما هو خارج عن محلّها ، وزعموا أنَّ السبب والمسبَّبَ مقدورانِ للعبدِ ، إلا أنَّ أحدهما مباشرةً ، والآخر ـ وهو المسبب ـ بواسطة إيقاع السبب ، ولم يذكروا تولداً في محلّ القدرة الحادثة إلا العلم النظريَّ ؛ فإِنَّ النظر عندَهم يُولِّدُهُ في محل القدرة عليهِ (۳).
This which has been mentioned concerning His attributes, up to here, is what is obligatory (wājib) with respect to Him, and when what is obligatory is known, what is impossible (mustaḥīl) is known; and it [i.e., the impossible] is the opposite of that which is obligatory. The doctrine of the people of truth (Ahl al-Ḥaqq), as previously stated, is that the originated power has no effect upon any of the possible things, and it is related to its object of power with a relation without effect; rather, its relation is like the relation of knowledge which relates to its object and does not affect it, except that the originated power is not related to an object of power except in its locus, and what leaves its locus there is no relation between it and the power, neither by effect nor otherwise. And the Mu'tazila, as previously stated, their doctrine is that the servant is the inventor of his actions, and they agreed that the originated power is not directly related except to the object of power which is in its locus; however, they hold that what is in its locus is a cause (sabab) through which what is outside its locus comes into existence, and they claimed that the cause and the caused are both within the servant's power, except that one is direct and the other—namely the caused—is by means of effecting the cause. And they did not mention generation (tawallud) in the locus of the originated power except in the case of theoretical knowledge (al-ʿilm al-naẓarī), for in their view speculation (al-naẓar) generates it in the locus of the power over it.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement that first summarizes a principle about Allah's attributes: that what is mentioned as obligatory (wājib) for Him implies its opposite, the impossible (mustaḥīl). Then it presents the Ash'ari position (madhhab ahl al-ḥaqq) on the created power (al-qudrah al-ḥādithah). The key claim is that this power has no causal effect (ta'thīr) on any possible thing; its relation (taʿalluq) to its object of power is merely a relation without producing any effect, analogous to the relation of knowledge to its object. However, unlike knowledge, created power only relates to an object of power that is in its locus (maḥall); if the object is outside the locus, there is no relation at all—not even by way of effect. Sanusi then contrasts this with the Mu'tazilite view, stating that they agree that power directly relates only to an object in its locus, but they add that this object in the locus serves as a cause (sabab) that brings about something outside the locus, and both the cause and the caused are under the servant's power—one directly, the other by means of causing the cause. Finally, Sanusi notes that the only case where the Mu'tazila admit generation (tawallud) within the locus of power is theoretical knowledge (al-ʿilm al-naẓarī), because they hold that speculation (al-naẓar) generates knowledge in the locus of the power over speculation (i.e., the intellect). This clarifies that the Ash'ari denial of generation is absolute, while the Mu'tazila allow it only in a special case.

Key Terms
القدرة الحادثة (created power)
التعلق (relation/attachment)
التأثير (effect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That created power has some effective role in producing actions, i.e., that human power causes the action to occur.
Correction: The Ash'ari position is that created power does not cause or effect anything; it merely accompanies the action which is created by Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position as presented by Sanusi, how does created power relate to its object of power?
A) With a relation that causes the object to exist.
B) With a relation that produces the object indirectly.
C) With a relation without any effect.
D) With no relation at all, even if the object is in its locus.

Key Takeaway
Created power is a mere relation in its locus, without any causal effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision or move your hand, do you truly feel that your power is merely a relation without any effect? How would your sense of responsibility change if you internalized that all effective power belongs to Allah alone?
?
If created power only relates within its locus and has no effect, then what is the real source of the changes you witness in the world? Does this lesson reveal a hidden reliance on your own ability that you need to repent from?
?
The text says your power is like knowledge—it relates but does not cause. When you 'know' something, you don't create it. When you 'will' something, do you act as if you create the result? What specific situation today can you practice attributing all effect to Allah?

Mu'tazilite View: Power, Causation, and the Exception of Tawallud
Sanusi contrasts the Mu'tazilite view on causality and tawallud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position that created power can indirectly cause effects through a causal chain, with the sole exception of theoretical knowledge generated by speculation.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila allow generation (tawallud) only for theoretical knowledge.

After presenting the Ash'ari view, Sanusi contrasts the Mu'tazilite doctrine. They agree on the direct relation of power to its locus, but add that what is in the locus can be a cause for something else. However, they limit the concept of 'generation' (tawallud) to one special case: theoretical knowledge arising from speculation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite position
Read the second part of the passage, which describes the Mu'tazilite view on causality and tawallud.
Notice the agreement on direct relation, then the addition of causation.
2
Understand the cause-and-caused model
The Mu'tazila hold that the direct act in the locus is a cause (sabab) that brings about another effect outside the locus. Both cause and caused are under the servant's power.
This is a form of indirect agency.
3
Identify the exception of tawallud
Sanusi notes that the only case where Mu'tazila admit generation (tawallud) within the locus of power is theoretical knowledge (al-ʿilm al-naẓarī) – it is generated by speculation (al-naẓar).
All other effects are considered caused, not generated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا الذي ذُكِرَ في أوصافه تعالى إلى هنا هو يجبُ في حقِّهِ تعالى ، وإذا عُلِمَ ما يجبُ عُلِمَ ما يستحيلُ ؛ وهو الواجب (۲) ضدُّ ذلك الواجب . مذهب أهل الحقِّ على ما سبق : أنَّ القدرة الحادثة لا تأثير لها في شيءٍ مِنَ الممكناتِ ، وهي تتعلق بمقدورها تعلقاً مِنْ غيرِ تأثير ، بل نسبته كنسبة العلم الذي يتعلَّق بمعلومِهِ ولا يُؤثر فيهِ (۱) ، إلا أنَّ القدرة الحادثة لا تتعلَّق بمقدور إلا في محلها (٢) ، وما خرجَ عن محلها فلا نسبة بينَهُ وبينَ القدرة لا بتأثير ولا غيره . والمعتزلة قد سبق أن مذهبهم أنَّ العبد مخترع أفعاله ، ووافقوا أنَّ القدرة الحادثة لا تتعلَّق مباشرة إلا بالمقدور الذي في محلها ، غير أنَّهم يرون أنَّ ما في محلّها سبب يُوجَدُ بهِ ما هو خارج عن محلّها ، وزعموا أنَّ السبب والمسبَّبَ مقدورانِ للعبدِ ، إلا أنَّ أحدهما مباشرةً ، والآخر ـ وهو المسبب ـ بواسطة إيقاع السبب ، ولم يذكروا تولداً في محلّ القدرة الحادثة إلا العلم النظريَّ ؛ فإِنَّ النظر عندَهم يُولِّدُهُ في محل القدرة عليهِ (۳).
This which has been mentioned concerning His attributes, up to here, is what is obligatory (wājib) with respect to Him, and when what is obligatory is known, what is impossible (mustaḥīl) is known; and it [i.e., the impossible] is the opposite of that which is obligatory. The doctrine of the people of truth (Ahl al-Ḥaqq), as previously stated, is that the originated power has no effect upon any of the possible things, and it is related to its object of power with a relation without effect; rather, its relation is like the relation of knowledge which relates to its object and does not affect it, except that the originated power is not related to an object of power except in its locus, and what leaves its locus there is no relation between it and the power, neither by effect nor otherwise. And the Mu'tazila, as previously stated, their doctrine is that the servant is the inventor of his actions, and they agreed that the originated power is not directly related except to the object of power which is in its locus; however, they hold that what is in its locus is a cause (sabab) through which what is outside its locus comes into existence, and they claimed that the cause and the caused are both within the servant's power, except that one is direct and the other—namely the caused—is by means of effecting the cause. And they did not mention generation (tawallud) in the locus of the originated power except in the case of theoretical knowledge (al-ʿilm al-naẓarī), for in their view speculation (al-naẓar) generates it in the locus of the power over it.

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement that first summarizes a principle about Allah's attributes: that what is mentioned as obligatory (wājib) for Him implies its opposite, the impossible (mustaḥīl). Then it presents the Ash'ari position (madhhab ahl al-ḥaqq) on the created power (al-qudrah al-ḥādithah).
