This clarifies that the Ash'ari denial of generation is absolute, while the Mu'tazila allow it only in a special case.

Key Terms
السبب (cause)
المسبب (caused)
النظر (speculation)
العلم النظري (theoretical knowledge)
التولد (generation/tawallud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila believe all effects of human actions are generated (tawallud) directly from the power.
Correction: The Mu'tazila actually limit tawallud to theoretical knowledge; other effects are considered caused (muhdath) by means of a cause (sabab) but not generated.

Checkpoint
What is the only case in which the Mu'tazila accept generation (tawallud) within the locus of created power?
A) All physical actions.
B) Ethical obligations.
C) Theoretical knowledge.
D) No case at all.

Key Takeaway
The only case of tawallud accepted by Mu'tazila is theoretical knowledge from speculation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the Mu'tazila taught that your direct power is limited to what is within your immediate locus. When you find yourself worrying about outcomes beyond your control—like how others will respond or what will happen tomorrow—are you acting as if you have power over effects that only Allah controls? What would it mean for you today to truly surrender those indirect results to Him?
?
The only case of tawallud the Mu'tazila accepted is theoretical knowledge generated from sincere speculation. When was the last time you engaged in deep, disciplined reflection (naẓar) on the signs of Allah in order to truly know Him? Is your certainty about Allah built on such generated knowledge, or on assumptions you have never examined? Will you commit to spending focused time this week in careful reflection on one of Allah's attributes?
?
If the Mu'tazila are correct that only your direct actions are within your power, then how responsible do you feel for the unintended consequences of your actions? Do you sometimes excuse yourself by saying 'I didn't mean for that to happen'? How does this passage challenge you to take responsibility for the effects you set in motion, while also acknowledging that Allah alone creates them?

The Mu'tazilite Argument from 'Vision Perceives Him Not'
Sanusi presents the Mu'tazilite argument from Qur'an 6:103.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilites used Qur'an 6:103 to deny the vision of God and their two distinct purposes for doing so.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites weaponized a verse against vision by defining vision as perception.

The Mu'tazilites frequently invoked the Qur'anic verse 'Vision perceives Him not' to oppose the Ash'ari doctrine that believers will see God in the Hereafter. Sanusi, quoting Ibn al-Tilimsani, explains that they used this verse in two ways: to counter the proof texts for vision, and to assert the absolute impossibility of seeing God. In this lesson, we will examine the logic of their argument, which hinges on equating vision with perception and claiming the verse's generality.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite syllogism
Focus on the first purpose: they argue that vision is perception of the sight, and since the verse denies perception, it denies vision. Identify the minor and major premises.
The minor premise is that vision equals perception; the major is that perception is universally negated.
2
Understand the second purpose
The second purpose treats the verse as self-praise, so negating perception is a perfection. Affirming it would be a deficiency, impossible for God.
This argument aims to show that vision itself is impossible, not just denied.
3
Reflect on the strength of the argument
Consider why the Mu'tazilites thought the verse was decisive. They relied on the definite plural 'al-absar' indicating absolute generality, extending to all times and individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله تعالى : ﴿ لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ [الأنعام : ١٠٣] فقد قالَ ابنُ التلمساني : ( هذه الآيةُ يتمسَّكُ بها المعتزلة تارةً على نفي وقوعِ الرؤيةِ مُعارَضةً لما تمسَّكْنا بهِ مِنَ الآيِ ، وتارة يتمسكون بها في امتناع الرؤية الذي هو نفس مذهبهم
وتوجيهها على القصدِ الأول : أنَّ الرؤية إدراك البصر ، ولا شيء من إدراك البصر يتعلَّقُ بهِ تعالى ؛ ينتج : لا شيء مِنَ الرؤية يتعلَّقُ بهِ جل وعزَّ ، ودليل الصغرى : أنَّ الرؤية هي الإدراك ؛ لأنه لا يصح ثبوت الرؤيةِ مع نفي الإدراكِ ، ودليلُ الكبرى : عموم نفي الإدراك في الآية عن كل بصر ؛ لأنَّ الجمعَ المحلى بالألف واللام يقتضي الاستغراق ، ويلزمُ مِنْ عمومِهِ في الأبصار عمومهُ في الأزمانِ ، فيلزمُ ألا يراه كافر ولا مؤمن في الدنيا ولا في الآخرة .
فلأنه تعالى
وأمَّا توجيهها على القصدِ الثاني - وهو امتناع الرؤية - : ذكرها في مَعرِضِ التمدُّحِ بها ، فيكون نفي الإدراك بالنسبة إليه كمالاً
فثبوتُهُ في حقِّهِ نقص ، والنقص على الله تعالى محال ((۱)
والجواب عن الآية من وجوه :
أحدها : أنَّا لا نُسلّمُ أنَّ الإدراك بمعنى الرؤية ، بل هو أخص ؛ وهو في الحادثِ : عبارةٌ عن إبصارِ الشيء مع إبصارِ جوانبه وأطرافِهِ ، وهذا في حقٌّ الله تعالى محال ، فيتعيَّنُ حمله على مجازِهِ ؛ وهو لا يُرى رؤية إحاطة ، كما أخبر عن نفسِهِ أَنَّهُ لا يُعلَمُ علمَ إحاطة بقولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ بِهِ عِلْمًا ﴾ [طه : ۱۱۰] ، ونفي الإبصار الخاص لا يُوجِبُ نفي أصل الإبصارِ ؛ وهو الذي ندَّعيه ، وبهذا تعرفُ أَنَّ النصوص الدالة على الرؤية يجب تقييدها بنفي الإحاطة ؛ للتوفيق بينَ النصوص .
سلَّمنا أنَّ الإدراك بمعنى الرؤية ، لكن لا نُسلّم العموم في الأزمان ، بل المراد نفي الرؤية في الدنيا ؛ للجمع بين هذا وبين ما اقتضى الرؤية في الآخرة ، أو ندعي التخصيص في الأفرادِ ، وأنَّ المؤمنين خارجونَ مِنْ هذا العموم للأدلة الواردة فيهم .
أو نقولُ : إنَّ لفظ ( الأبصار ( جمع مُحلّى بالألف واللام يفيد في الثبوتِ العموم ، فسلبه يفيد سلب العموم ؛ لأنَّ النفْيَ تابع لما أشعرَ بِهِ اللفظ المثبتُ ، وذلك لا يفيد عموم السلبِ ؛ لأنَّ سَلْبَ العمومِ لا ينافي ثبوت الحكم لبعض الأفراد ، فيتحقَّقُ بنفي الحكم عن فردِ مِنَ الأفراد ، بخلافِ عموم السلبِ ؛ فإنَّه يكذبُ بثبوتِ الحكم لفردِ مِنَ الأفرادِ ، ولهذا كذَّبَ الله تعالى اليهود حيثُ قالوا : ﴿ إِذْ قَالُوا مَا أَنزَلَ اللهُ عَلَى بَشَرٍ مِّن شَيْءٍ بقوله : ﴿قُلْ مَنْ أَنزَلَ الْكِتَبَ ﴾ [الأنعام : ٩١] ، حيثُ ادعوا عموم السلب ، والدلالة للمعتزلة بالآية تتوقف على تحقق الثاني دونَ الأَوَّلِ ؛ فإِنَّ الأشعريَّةَ لا تدَّعي أَنَّهُ يراه كلُّ أحدٍ ، وإِنَّما يراه المؤمنون دونَ الكافرين ، ونقيضُ الموجبة الكلية التي سلبتها الآية هي السالبة الجزئية التي دلّتْ عليها الآيةُ ، لا السالبة الكلية التي
As for His statement, the Exalted: 'Vision perceives Him not' [al-An'am 6:103], Ibn al-Tilimsani said: 'The Mu'tazilites use this verse sometimes to deny the occurrence of vision, in opposition to the verses we have used, and sometimes they use it to assert the impossibility of vision, which is their actual doctrine.' And its interpretation according to the first purpose: that vision is the perception of the sight, and nothing of the perception of the sight pertains to Him, the Exalted; the result: nothing of vision pertains to Him, the Mighty and Majestic. The proof of the minor premise: that vision is perception, because the affirmation of vision with the negation of perception is not valid. The proof of the major premise: the generality of the negation of perception in the verse concerning every sight, because the definite plural with alif and lam necessitates universality, and from its generality concerning sights follows its generality concerning times, and thus it follows that no disbeliever nor believer sees Him in this world nor in the Hereafter. But because He, the Exalted, ... And as for its interpretation according to the second purpose—which is the impossibility of vision—He mentioned it in the context of self-praise, so the negation of perception relative to Him is perfection, and its affirmation in His case is deficiency, and deficiency concerning God, the Exalted, is impossible. The answer to the verse is from several aspects: First: we do not concede that perception means vision; rather it is more specific. And it, regarding originated beings, is an expression of seeing a thing together with seeing its sides and extremities. This concerning God, the Exalted, is impossible, so it must be interpreted metaphorically: that He is not seen with an encompassing vision, just as He informed about Himself that He is not known with encompassing knowledge in His statement, the Exalted: 'And they encompass Him not with knowledge' [Ta-Ha 20:110]. And the negation of specific vision does not entail the negation of the essence of vision, which is what we claim. And by this you know that the texts indicating vision must be restricted by the negation of encompassing, for reconciliation between the texts. We concede that perception means vision, but we do not concede the generality concerning times; rather the intended meaning is the negation of vision in this world, for reconciliation between this and what entails vision in the Hereafter. Or we claim specification concerning individuals, and that the believers are excluded from this generality due to the evidences transmitted concerning them. Or we say: the word 'al-absar' (sights) is a definite plural with alif and lam, which in affirmative statements indicates generality. Its negation indicates negation of generality, because the negation follows what the affirmative word indicated. And this does not entail universal negation, because the negation of generality does not contradict the affirmation of the ruling for some individuals; it is realized by negating the ruling for one individual, unlike universal negation, which is contradicted by the affirmation of the ruling for one individual. For this reason God, the Exalted, refuted the Jews when they said: 'God has not sent down anything to a human being' with His statement: 'Say: Who sent down the Book?' [al-An'am 6:91], when they claimed universal negation. And the implication for the Mu'tazilites from the verse depends on the realization of the second without the first; because the Ash'arites do not claim that everyone sees Him; rather, the believers see Him, not the disbelievers. And the contradictory of the universal affirmative which the verse negated is the particular negative which the verse indicates, not the universal negative which ...
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite argument from the Qur'anic verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Vision perceives Him not). Sanusi begins by quoting Ibn al-Tilimsani, who notes that the Mu'tazilites use this verse in two ways: first, to oppose the verses that affirm the vision of God (ru'yat Allah), and second, to prove the impossibility of vision as their own doctrine. Sanusi then explains the Mu'tazilite syllogism for the first purpose: they define vision as 'idrak al-basar' (perception of the sight), then argue that no perception of the sight pertains to God, so no vision pertains to God. The minor premise ('vision is perception') is supported by the claim that it is impossible to affirm vision while denying perception. The major premise ('no perception pertains to God') is supported by the verse's generality: the definite plural 'الأبصار' indicates universality, and this universality extends to all times, implying that no one sees God ever. For the second purpose, the Mu'tazilites argue that the verse is self-praise, so negating perception is a perfection for God, and affirming it would be a deficiency, which is impossible for God. After presenting the Mu'tazilite arguments, Sanusi gives the Ash'ari answers. The first answer is that 'idrak' is more specific than 'ru'ya'; it means seeing something along with its sides and extremities, which is impossible for God. Therefore, the negation must be interpreted metaphorically as the negation of encompassing vision, not vision itself. This is analogous to the verse 'ولا يحيطون به علما' (they encompass Him not with knowledge). Negating specific vision does not negate the essence of vision, which the Ash'arites affirm. Thus, texts affirming vision must be qualified by the negation of encompassing to reconcile them. The second answer concedes that idrak means vision but restricts the negation to this world only, reconciling with verses affirming vision in the Hereafter, or specifies that believers are excluded from the generality. The third answer is a logical point: the definite plural in an affirmative statement indicates generality, but its negation indicates negation of generality (salb al-‘umum), not universal negation (umum al-salb). Negation of generality is true if the ruling is negated for at least one individual, while universal negation is false if the ruling holds for any individual. Sanusi supports this with the example of the Jews who claimed universal negation that God sent down nothing to any human, and God refuted them by pointing to the Torah. The Mu'tazilite argument requires universal negation (that no one sees God), but the Ash'arites only claim that some see Him (believers in the afterlife), so the verse does not contradict them. The passage ends with the incomplete statement that the contradictory of the universal affirmative negated by the verse is the particular negative, not the universal negative.

الجمع المحلى بالألف واللام

Misconception: Some think the verse absolutely denies all vision of God, but the Mu'tazilite argument relies on a specific definition of vision as perception and the general scope of the negation.
Correction: The Ash'arites will later show that perception is not identical to vision, and the negation is not universal.

The Mu'tazilites argue from the verse that vision is perception, and since perception is universally negated, vision is denied for all in all times.

Ash'ari Refutations of the Mu'tazilite Argument
Sanusi presents the three Ash'ari responses to the Mu'tazilite use of Qur'an 6:103.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three key Ash'ari responses to the Mu'tazilite use of Qur'an 6:103, including the distinction between perception and vision, the restriction of negation to this world, and the logical difference between negation of generality and universal negation.

Core Idea
The verse denies encompassing vision, not all vision; negation is not universal.

Having presented the Mu'tazilite argument, Sanusi now delivers the Ash'ari counterarguments. He offers three distinct answers, each undermining a key assumption of the Mu'tazilite syllogism. The first answer distinguishes 'idrak' from 'ru'ya,' the second restricts the negation to this world or to non-believers, and the third provides a sophisticated logical analysis of the scope of negation in Arabic. This lesson unpacks these responses, showing how the Ash'arites reconcile the verse with texts affirming vision.

Guided Path
1
Study the first answer
Read the first answer carefully: 'idrak' is more specific than 'ru'ya', meaning encompassing vision. Negating that does not negate vision itself.
Sanusi draws an analogy with the verse about encompassing knowledge.
2
Analyze the second answer
The second answer concedes that 'idrak' means vision but restricts the negation to this world or excludes believers.
This is a concession for the sake of argument, not the primary position.
3
Understand the third answer
The third answer is a logical argument about 'salb al-umum' vs 'umum al-salb'. The definite plural in negation indicates negation of generality, not universal negation.
The example of the Jews in the Qur'an illustrates the difference.

أما قوله تعالى : ﴿ لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ [الأنعام : ١٠٣] فقد قالَ ابنُ التلمساني : ( هذه الآيةُ يتمسَّكُ بها المعتزلة تارةً على نفي وقوعِ الرؤيةِ مُعارَضةً لما تمسَّكْنا بهِ مِنَ الآيِ ، وتارة يتمسكون بها في امتناع الرؤية الذي هو نفس مذهبهم
وتوجيهها على القصدِ الأول : أنَّ الرؤية إدراك البصر ، ولا شيء من إدراك البصر يتعلَّقُ بهِ تعالى ؛ ينتج : لا شيء مِنَ الرؤية يتعلَّقُ بهِ جل وعزَّ ، ودليل الصغرى : أنَّ الرؤية هي الإدراك ؛ لأنه لا يصح ثبوت الرؤيةِ مع نفي الإدراكِ ، ودليلُ الكبرى : عموم نفي الإدراك في الآية عن كل بصر ؛ لأنَّ الجمعَ المحلى بالألف واللام يقتضي الاستغراق ، ويلزمُ مِنْ عمومِهِ في الأبصار عمومهُ في الأزمانِ ، فيلزمُ ألا يراه كافر ولا مؤمن في الدنيا ولا في الآخرة .
فلأنه تعالى
وأمَّا توجيهها على القصدِ الثاني - وهو امتناع الرؤية - : ذكرها في مَعرِضِ التمدُّحِ بها ، فيكون نفي الإدراك بالنسبة إليه كمالاً
فثبوتُهُ في حقِّهِ نقص ، والنقص على الله تعالى محال ((۱)
والجواب عن الآية من وجوه :
أحدها : أنَّا لا نُسلّمُ أنَّ الإدراك بمعنى الرؤية ، بل هو أخص ؛ وهو في الحادثِ : عبارةٌ عن إبصارِ الشيء مع إبصارِ جوانبه وأطرافِهِ ، وهذا في حقٌّ الله تعالى محال ، فيتعيَّنُ حمله على مجازِهِ ؛ وهو لا يُرى رؤية إحاطة ، كما أخبر عن نفسِهِ أَنَّهُ لا يُعلَمُ علمَ إحاطة بقولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ بِهِ عِلْمًا ﴾ [طه : ۱۱۰] ، ونفي الإبصار الخاص لا يُوجِبُ نفي أصل الإبصارِ ؛ وهو الذي ندَّعيه ، وبهذا تعرفُ أَنَّ النصوص الدالة على الرؤية يجب تقييدها بنفي الإحاطة ؛ للتوفيق بينَ النصوص .
سلَّمنا أنَّ الإدراك بمعنى الرؤية ، لكن لا نُسلّم العموم في الأزمان ، بل المراد نفي الرؤية في الدنيا ؛ للجمع بين هذا وبين ما اقتضى الرؤية في الآخرة ، أو ندعي التخصيص في الأفرادِ ، وأنَّ المؤمنين خارجونَ مِنْ هذا العموم للأدلة الواردة فيهم .
أو نقولُ : إنَّ لفظ ( الأبصار ( جمع مُحلّى بالألف واللام يفيد في الثبوتِ العموم ، فسلبه يفيد سلب العموم ؛ لأنَّ النفْيَ تابع لما أشعرَ بِهِ اللفظ المثبتُ ، وذلك لا يفيد عموم السلبِ ؛ لأنَّ سَلْبَ العمومِ لا ينافي ثبوت الحكم لبعض الأفراد ، فيتحقَّقُ بنفي الحكم عن فردِ مِنَ الأفراد ، بخلافِ عموم السلبِ ؛ فإنَّه يكذبُ بثبوتِ الحكم لفردِ مِنَ الأفرادِ ، ولهذا كذَّبَ الله تعالى اليهود حيثُ قالوا : ﴿ إِذْ قَالُوا مَا أَنزَلَ اللهُ عَلَى بَشَرٍ مِّن شَيْءٍ بقوله : ﴿قُلْ مَنْ أَنزَلَ الْكِتَبَ ﴾ [الأنعام : ٩١] ، حيثُ ادعوا عموم السلب ، والدلالة للمعتزلة بالآية تتوقف على تحقق الثاني دونَ الأَوَّلِ ؛ فإِنَّ الأشعريَّةَ لا تدَّعي أَنَّهُ يراه كلُّ أحدٍ ، وإِنَّما يراه المؤمنون دونَ الكافرين ، ونقيضُ الموجبة الكلية التي سلبتها الآية هي السالبة الجزئية التي دلّتْ عليها الآيةُ ، لا السالبة الكلية التي
As for His statement, the Exalted: 'Vision perceives Him not' [al-An'am 6:103], Ibn al-Tilimsani said: 'The Mu'tazilites use this verse sometimes to deny the occurrence of vision, in opposition to the verses we have used, and sometimes they use it to assert the impossibility of vision, which is their actual doctrine.' And its interpretation according to the first purpose: that vision is the perception of the sight, and nothing of the perception of the sight pertains to Him, the Exalted; the result: nothing of vision pertains to Him, the Mighty and Majestic. The proof of the minor premise: that vision is perception, because the affirmation of vision with the negation of perception is not valid. The proof of the major premise: the generality of the negation of perception in the verse concerning every sight, because the definite plural with alif and lam necessitates universality, and from its generality concerning sights follows its generality concerning times, and thus it follows that no disbeliever nor believer sees Him in this world nor in the Hereafter. But because He, the Exalted, ... And as for its interpretation according to the second purpose—which is the impossibility of vision—He mentioned it in the context of self-praise, so the negation of perception relative to Him is perfection, and its affirmation in His case is deficiency, and deficiency concerning God, the Exalted, is impossible. The answer to the verse is from several aspects: First: we do not concede that perception means vision; rather it is more specific. And it, regarding originated beings, is an expression of seeing a thing together with seeing its sides and extremities. This concerning God, the Exalted, is impossible, so it must be interpreted metaphorically: that He is not seen with an encompassing vision, just as He informed about Himself that He is not known with encompassing knowledge in His statement, the Exalted: 'And they encompass Him not with knowledge' [Ta-Ha 20:110]. And the negation of specific vision does not entail the negation of the essence of vision, which is what we claim. And by this you know that the texts indicating vision must be restricted by the negation of encompassing, for reconciliation between the texts. We concede that perception means vision, but we do not concede the generality concerning times; rather the intended meaning is the negation of vision in this world, for reconciliation between this and what entails vision in the Hereafter. Or we claim specification concerning individuals, and that the believers are excluded from this generality due to the evidences transmitted concerning them. Or we say: the word 'al-absar' (sights) is a definite plural with alif and lam, which in affirmative statements indicates generality. Its negation indicates negation of generality, because the negation follows what the affirmative word indicated. And this does not entail universal negation, because the negation of generality does not contradict the affirmation of the ruling for some individuals; it is realized by negating the ruling for one individual, unlike universal negation, which is contradicted by the affirmation of the ruling for one individual. For this reason God, the Exalted, refuted the Jews when they said: 'God has not sent down anything to a human being' with His statement: 'Say: Who sent down the Book?' [al-An'am 6:91], when they claimed universal negation. And the implication for the Mu'tazilites from the verse depends on the realization of the second without the first; because the Ash'arites do not claim that everyone sees Him; rather, the believers see Him, not the disbelievers. And the contradictory of the universal affirmative which the verse negated is the particular negative which the verse indicates, not the universal negative which ...
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite argument from the Qur'anic verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Vision perceives Him not). Sanusi begins by quoting Ibn al-Tilimsani, who notes that the Mu'tazilites use this verse in two ways: first, to oppose the verses that affirm the vision of God (ru'yat Allah), and second, to prove the impossibility of vision as their own doctrine. Sanusi then explains the Mu'tazilite syllogism for the first purpose: they define vision as 'idrak al-basar' (perception of the sight), then argue that no perception of the sight pertains to God, so no vision pertains to God. The minor premise ('vision is perception') is supported by the claim that it is impossible to affirm vision while denying perception. The major premise ('no perception pertains to God') is supported by the verse's generality: the definite plural 'الأبصار' indicates universality, and this universality extends to all times, implying that no one sees God ever. For the second purpose, the Mu'tazilites argue that the verse is self-praise, so negating perception is a perfection for God, and affirming it would be a deficiency, which is impossible for God. After presenting the Mu'tazilite arguments, Sanusi gives the Ash'ari answers. The first answer is that 'idrak' is more specific than 'ru'ya'; it means seeing something along with its sides and extremities, which is impossible for God. Therefore, the negation must be interpreted metaphorically as the negation of encompassing vision, not vision itself. This is analogous to the verse 'ولا يحيطون به علما' (they encompass Him not with knowledge). Negating specific vision does not negate the essence of vision, which the Ash'arites affirm. Thus, texts affirming vision must be qualified by the negation of encompassing to reconcile them. The second answer concedes that idrak means vision but restricts the negation to this world only, reconciling with verses affirming vision in the Hereafter, or specifies that believers are excluded from the generality. The third answer is a logical point: the definite plural in an affirmative statement indicates generality, but its negation indicates negation of generality (salb al-‘umum), not universal negation (umum al-salb). Negation of generality is true if the ruling is negated for at least one individual, while universal negation is false if the ruling holds for any individual. Sanusi supports this with the example of the Jews who claimed universal negation that God sent down nothing to any human, and God refuted them by pointing to the Torah. The Mu'tazilite argument requires universal negation (that no one sees God), but the Ash'arites only claim that some see Him (believers in the afterlife), so the verse does not contradict them. The passage ends with the incomplete statement that the contradictory of the universal affirmative negated by the verse is the particular negative, not the universal negative.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'arite refutation hinges on three points: the distinction between perception and vision, the temporal or personal restriction of the negation, and the logical nuance of negation in Arabic.

Refuting the Mu'tazilite 'Lan Tarani' Argument
Sanusi presents the Mu'tazilite argument and gives the first answer using linguistic analysis and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's first refutation of the Mu'tazilite proof from 'lan tarani' and how it actually proves the possibility of seeing Allah.

Core Idea
Lan tarani actually proves that seeing Allah is possible, not impossible.

The Mu'tazilites used the verse 'Lan tarani' (You will never see Me) to argue that Allah cannot be seen. Sanusi provides a clever linguistic and logical response: the choice of words itself implies that seeing is possible; otherwise, Allah would have said something else. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite proof
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi presents the Mu'tazilite argument. Notice how they use 'lan' to claim eternal negation of seeing Allah.
The Mu'tazilites rely on 'lan' indicating perpetuity.
2
Understand Sanusi's reply
Read Sanusi's answer. He argues that if seeing Allah were impossible, the verse would have used a different expression. The fact that it says 'lan tarani' shows that seeing is possible but prevented in that instance.
3
Analyze the analogy
Study the analogy of the stone and food. Notice how 'you will not eat it' implies the food is edible but prevented, whereas if it were inedible, the reply would be 'this is not eaten'.
This analogy clarifies the linguistic point.
4
Connect to the broader principle
Reflect on how this refutation ties into the rational judgment of possibility (ja'iz). Sanusi shows that the Mu'tazilite proof backfires and actually supports the Sunni position.
This is a key moment in the debate over ru'ya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وممَّا تمسَّكَتْ بهِ المعتزلةُ : قوله تعالى : لَن تراني ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٤٣]، و ( لن ) تفيدُ التأبيد ؛ بدليل قوله تعالى : ﴿ قُل لَّن تَتَّبِعُونَا * [الفتح : ١٥] ، والمراد هنا : التأبيد ، والمجاز والنقلُ على خلافِ الأصل ، فوجبَ أنْ يُقالَ : إِنَّ موسى عليه السلام لن يرى الله البتةَ ، وكلٌّ مَنْ قالَ : إنَّ موسى لن يرى الله البتة . . قالَ : إِنَّ غيرَهُ لا يراه والجواب : أنَّ هذا يدلُّ على كونِهِ تعالى جائزَ الرؤيةِ ؛ لأنَّه لو كان ممتنع الرؤية لقال : لا تصح رؤيتي ، أو لن تمكن ، أو لا أرى ، ونحو هذا ، ألا ترى أَنَّ مَنْ كانَ في كُمِّهِ حجرٌ ، فظَنَّهُ بعضُهم طعاماً ، فقال : أعطني هذا لاكله .. كانَ الجواب الصحيحُ له : إِنَّ هذا لا يُؤكَلُ ، أمَّا إذا كانَ طعاماً يصحُ أَكْلُهُ فحينئذٍ يصح أن يقول المجيبُ : إنَّكَ لن تأكله ، وهذا واضح .
Among what the Mu'tazilites clung to is His statement, Exalted: 'You will never see Me' [al-A'raf 7:143], and 'lan' indicates eternity, as evidence by His statement: 'Say: you will never follow us' [al-Fath 48:15], and the intended meaning here is eternity, and metaphor and transference are contrary to the original rule. So it must be said that Moses, peace be upon him, will never see God at all, and whoever says that Moses will never see God at all says that others will not see Him. The answer: that this actually indicates that He, Exalted, is possible of seeing, because if He were impossible of seeing, He would have said: 'My seeing is not valid', or 'it is not possible', or 'I am not seen', and the like. Do you not see that if someone has a stone in his sleeve, and some people think it is food, and he says 'give me this to eat it', the correct answer to him is: 'This is not eaten.' However, if it is food that can be eaten, then it is correct for the respondent to say: 'You will not eat it.' And this is clear.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite argument from the verse 'lan tarani' (You will never see Me) and the first answer. The Mu'tazilites claim that 'lan' indicates eternal negation, so Moses will never see God, and by extension no one else will. The answer is that this statement actually implies that seeing God is possible, because if it were impossible, the reply would have been something like 'it is not possible to see Me'. The analogy of the stone and food illustrates: if something is impossible, you say it cannot be eaten; if it is possible but prevented, you say 'you will not eat it'. Similarly, 'lan tarani' implies the possibility of seeing God but that Moses is prevented from it in that moment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Lan tarani' proves that Allah cannot be seen at all, eternally.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the verse actually indicates that seeing Allah is possible, but it is prevented in this life for Moses.

Checkpoint

B) It is possible.
C) It is necessary.
D) It is a metaphor.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite proof from 'lan tarani' is refuted by Sanusi's insight that the expression itself shows the possibility of seeing Allah.

Proof for the Possibility of Seeing God
Sanusi proves the possibility of seeing God by showing that visibility requires existence alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that the possibility of seeing God is grounded in the fact that being seen requires only existence, not physical attributes.

Core Idea
The possibility of seeing God follows logically from existence being the condition for visibility.

Sanusi presents a rigorous rational argument for the possibility of seeing God (ruʾya). This lesson walks through each step of his proof, demonstrating that the attribute enabling vision is existence itself. Understanding this proof clarifies a central Ashʿarī position against those who deny the possibility of beholding God.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the complete argument in one go.
Notice the repeated use of 'لا جائز' (it is not possible).
2
Identify the dichotomy
The proof begins by ruling out the possibility that the attribute of being seen is negative. Understand why.
3
Follow the positive branch
Once established as positive, the argument subdivides the positive into restricted or not restricted by existence, then into attribute or qualified entity. Follow each branch to see which leads to absurdity.
4
Confirm the conclusion
The only consistent branch is that being seen is restricted to existence. Apply this to God: He is existent, so He can be seen.
This is a proof of possibility (imkān al-ruʾya), not actuality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا جائزَ أن يكون أمراً عدميّاً ، وإلا لصح رؤية المعدوم ، وامتنع رؤية الموجود ، ولأن العدمَ لا يصلحُ أن يكون علة للأمر الثبوتي ، تعيَّنَ أنْ يكونَ أمراً ثبوتيّاً ، والأمرُ الثبوتي لا يخلو : إمَّا أن يتقيَّد بالوجودِ أو لا ؛ فإن لم يُقيَّد بالوجودِ امتنع رؤية الموجودِ ، وإنْ تقيَّد بالوجودِ فلا يخلو : أنْ يتقيَّد بكونه صفةً أو موصوفاً . لا جائزَ أنْ يتقيَّد بأحدهما ، وإلا لما رئي الآخرُ ، فتعيَّنَ أَنَّهُ إِنَّما صح رؤيته لكونه موجوداً ، والبارى تعالى موجود ، فصح أن يُرى .
It is not possible that it (the attribute of being seen) be a non‑existent thing; otherwise, the vision of the non‑existent would be valid, and the vision of the existent would be impossible. And because non‑existence is not fit to be a cause for a positive thing, it is determined that it must be a positive thing. And the positive thing is not free: either it is restricted by existence or not; if it is not restricted by existence, vision of the existent would be impossible; and if it is restricted by existence, it is not free: either it is restricted by being an attribute or by being a qualified thing. It is not possible that it be restricted by one of them, otherwise the other would not be seen. So it is determined that its being seen is only valid because it is existent, and the Creator (al‑Bāriʾ) – exalted is He – is existent, so it is valid that He be seen.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage presents a logical proof for the possibility of seeing God (ruʾyat Allāh). It classifies as a proof (istidlāl) based on the nature of the attribute of being seen. The argument proceeds in steps: (1) The attribute due to which something is seen (i.e., the relation of vision) cannot be a negative (ʿadamī) attribute, because if it were, then non‑existent things would be seen and existent things would not—clearly false. Moreover, a negative attribute cannot be the cause (ʿilla) of a positive judgment (al‑amr al‑thubūtī) such as being seen. (2) Therefore, it must be a positive (thubūtī) attribute. (3) A positive attribute is either restricted to existence or not. If not restricted, then existents would not be seen—absurd. So it must be restricted to existence. (4) When restricted to existence, it is either restricted to being an attribute (ṣifa) or to being a qualified entity (mawṣūf). But it cannot be restricted to one alone, for then the other would not be seen. Hence, it is restricted to existence itself, i.e., the mere fact of being existent. (5) Since God (al‑Bāriʾ) is existent, He must be capable of being seen. The proof is a standard Ashʿarī argument for the possibility of ruʾya, showing that the underlying attribute (the thing due to which something is seen) is simply existence, not a specific mode like being physical.

Key Terms
رؤية (ruʾya: vision)
أمر عدمي (ʿadamī: negative attribute)
أمر ثبوتي (thubūtī: positive attribute)
موجود (mawjūd: existent)
معدوم (maʿdūm: non‑existent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision requires physical contact or a body.
Correction: This proof shows that the necessary condition for visibility is simply existence, not physicality.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why cannot the attribute of being seen be a negative (ʿadamī) attribute?
A) Because a negative attribute cannot be a cause for a positive judgment.
B) Because negative attributes do not exist.
C) Because the Qurʾan states that God sees.
D) Because the Prophet saw God.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of seeing God is rationally established because being seen requires only existence, and God is maximally existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof says that existence alone qualifies something to be seen. If you truly believed that God's existence is as obvious as the existence of this screen, would you live differently? What would change?
?
The author argues that the attribute enabling vision is neither attribute nor qualified entity, but existence itself. Does that realization strip away any subconscious expectation you have of what seeing God must be like? What are you still holding onto?
?
This is an intellectual proof, but it points to a reality: God could be seen if He wills. Does that thought make your heart yearn for the Hereafter, or does it remain a cold fact? Pray about this and write down what you truly desire.

Al-Juwayni's Critique of the Ru'ya Proof
Al-Juwayni critiques the proof by analogous reasoning leading to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Juwayni's critique of the ru'ya proof using the shared cause of createdness and why it leads to an absurd conclusion.

Core Idea
If existence causes createdness, then God would be created—an absurdity that refutes the proof.

Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, a master of kalam, presents a powerful objection to the proof for seeing Allah. He uses the example of substance and accident both being created, and argues that the common property of createdness must have a shared cause. This leads to a dilemma that forces the original argument into an absurd conclusion. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the literal meaning.
Notice the concise style of al-Juwayni.
2
Identify the logical structure
The passage presents an analogy: substance and accident are both created, so the common property of createdness requires a shared cause. Recognize this as an objection (naqḍ) by parallel reasoning.
3
Trace the dilemma
The shared cause is narrowed to either temporal origination (ḥudūth) or existence (wujūd). Ḥudūth is rejected for the same reason given in the original proof. Therefore existence must be the cause.
This step is crucial for understanding the inference.
4
Realize the absurd conclusion
If existence is the cause of createdness, then since God exists, He would be valid to be created. This is false, so the original reasoning must be flawed.
The conclusion is an absurdity (muḥāl) that refutes the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمامُ في المعالم : وهذا عندي ضعيفٌ ؛ لأنَّهُ يُقالُ : الجوهر والعرضُ مخلوقانِ ، فصحَّةُ المخلوقية فيهما حكم مشترك بينهما، فلا بدَّ لهُ مِنْ علَّةٍ مشتركة ، والمشترك إمَّا الحدوث أو الوجود، والحدوث باطل ؛ لما ذكرتموه ، تعيَّن الوجود ، فوجبَ كونه تعالى يصح أن يكون مخلوقاً ! وكما أنَّ هذا باطل فكذلك ما ذكرتموه .
Al‑Imām said in al‑Maʿālim: “This, in my view, is weak; because it is said: substance (jawhar) and accident (ʿaraḍ) are created (makhluqān), so the validity of createdness (makhluqiyya) in both of them is a shared judgment between them. It must have a shared cause. The shared cause is either temporal origination (ḥudūth) or existence (wujūd). Temporal origination is invalid for the reason you mentioned, so existence is determined. Then it necessarily follows that the Exalted is valid to be created! And just as this is false, so is what you mentioned.”
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al‑Haramayn al‑Juwaynī’s critique of the previous proof for ruʾya. It is an objection (naqḍ) that attempts to refute the argument by analogy. The Imam argues: (1) Substance and accident are both created (makhluqūn). The quality of being created (makhluqiyya) is a common property predicated of both; therefore it must have a common cause. (2) The common cause can only be either ḥudūth (temporal origination) or wujūd (existence). Ḥudūth is invalid for the same reason given in the original proof (presumably because ḥudūth is a relative notion and not a positive attribute shared perfectly). (3) Therefore, existence must be the cause of createdness. (4) If so, then God—since He exists—would have to be created! (5) That conclusion is false, so the original line of reasoning is invalid. The critique uses the same logical structure (positive attribute shared by two things implying a common cause) to generate an absurd conclusion, thereby refuting the proof.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar: substance)
عرض (ʿaraḍ: accident)
مخلوقية (makhluqiyya: createdness)
حدوث (ḥudūth: temporal origination)
علة مشتركة (ʿilla mushtaraka: shared cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Juwayni's critique is valid and permanently refutes the proof.
Correction: This passage only presents the critique; the author will later strengthen the original proof and answer this objection. The critique is a dialectical step, not a final refutation.

Checkpoint
What absurd conclusion does al-Juwayni derive from the original proof's method?
A) That God is a substance.
B) That God must be created.
C) That God cannot be seen.
D) That God's existence is accidental.

Key Takeaway
Al-Juwayni's critique uses the same logical method of the original proof to show it leads to an absurdity, thereby questioning its validity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you unconsciously apply human standards to Allah, thinking that what is true for creation must also be true for the Creator? When you say 'Allah is like...' in your heart, are you committing the same error that Al-Juwayni exposed?
?
Al-Juwayni showed that a flawed premise can lead to a blasphemous conclusion. When it comes to your own beliefs, how certain are you that your theological premises are sound? Are you willing to accept that your reasoning might be flawed, even if it feels logical?
?
This argument guards against attributing createdness to Allah. What other attributes or limitations do you inadvertently assign to Allah because you haven't examined the logical consequences of your beliefs? Will you commit to examining those beliefs more carefully, starting today?

The Lams Argument and Multiple Perceptions of God
Sanusi discusses the second critique based on touch and the possibility of all five senses perceiving God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the touch-based critique of the vision argument is considered strong, and how scholars differ on whether all five senses can perceive God.

Core Idea
Touch's contact is customary, not rational, so it doesn't preclude perceiving God without physical means.

This lesson examines the second major objection to the proof for seeing God in the afterlife. The objection is based on the sense of touch. Some scholars attempted to distinguish touch from sight by appealing to physical effects, but the author argues that this distinction is weak because touch's contact is merely customary—not a rational necessity. This opens the door for all five senses to potentially perceive God without physical conditions, a view held by leading Ashʿarī theologians.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the full English translation. Notice the author's critical tone and the names of scholars mentioned.
The passage contains a quote from Ibn al-Tilmisānī, so pay attention to the shift in voice.
2
Identify the key move
The author rejects the distinction between touch and sight based on 'affecting and being affected'. Identify the reason given: the contact in touch is customary, not rationally necessary.
3
Understand the implications
Reflect on what this means for the possibility of perceiving God by touch. Note that Imām al-Ḥaramayn and al-Ashʿarī accepted all five senses, while others like ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd and al-Qalānisī did not.
This shows the range of scholarly opinion within the Ashʿarī school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أيضاً قوي ، وجواب الأستاذ عنه بالفرق بينَ اللمس والرؤية ؛ لوجود التأثير والتأثرِ في الأَوَّلِ بخلاف الثاني . . ضعيف ؛ فإنَّ الاتصال الثابتَ معَ اللمس عادي لا عقلي ، فلم لا يجوز أن يتعلَّق هذا الإدراك بهِ تعالى مِنْ غير اتصال ولا تكييف ؟! وإمام الحرمين قد التزم هذا ، وصحح تعلُّق الإدراكاتِ الخمسةِ بهِ تعالى مِنْ غيرِ أنْ تقارنها الأسباب المتصلة بها عادةً ، ونسب هذا أيضاً للشيخ الأشعري ، خلاف ما ذهَبَ إِليهِ عبدُ اللهِ بنُ سعيد والقلانسي مِنْ منع تعلَّقِ باقي الإدراكاتِ بهِ تعالى. وقد اقتصر الإمامُ في المعالم على هذين النقضينِ ، قالَ ابنُ التلمساني : وقد أوردَ عليها في الأربعين وغيره أسئلة عديدة ، وأكَّد ورودَها بقولِهِ : وأنا غير قادر على الجواب عنها ، فمَنْ قَدَرَ على الجواب عنها أمكنه أنْ يتمسَّكَ بهذه الطريقة ، وقد تصدى جماعةٌ مِنَ الفضلاء للجواب عنها ، وكان شيخُنا تقي الدين يقولُ : إِنَّ بعضَها لا يمكنُ الجواب عنه بما يشفي الغليلَ . قالَ ابنُ التَّلِمْسانيّ : ونحنُ نشيرُ إليها على وجه الاختصار ، ونُنبِّه على القويّ منها والضعيف ، وبالله التوفيق
And the second (argument) is also strong, and the master’s response to it by distinguishing between touch and sight – due to the existence of affecting and being affected in the first unlike the second – is weak; because the contact established with touch is customary (ʿādī), not rational (ʿaqlī). So why is it not permissible that this perception be related to Him – exalted is He – without contact and without modality (takyīf)?! And Imām al‑Ḥaramayn has committed to this, and he affirmed the relation of all five perceptions to Him – exalted is He – without the usually accompanying connected causes, and he attributed this also to al‑Shaykh al‑Ashʿarī, contrary to what ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd and al‑Qalānisī held of denying the relation of the remaining perceptions to Him – exalted is He. And al‑Imām, in al‑Maʿālim, confined himself to these two refutations. Ibn al‑Tilmisānī said: “He (al‑Rāzī) presented in al‑Arbaʿīn and other works numerous objections, and he emphasized their occurrence by saying: ‘I am unable to answer them, so whoever can answer them can adhere to this method.’ And a group of virtuous men undertook to answer them, and our teacher Taqī al‑Dīn used to say: ‘Some of them cannot be answered in a way that satisfies the yearning.’” Ibn al‑Tilmisānī said: “And we will allude to them concisely, and we will point out the strong and the weak among them, and with Allah is success.”
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the critical evaluation of the two refutations (naqḍ) presented by al‑Juwaynī against the proof for ruʾya. The author first addresses the second refutation concerning touch (lams). He acknowledges that the second argument is also strong (qawī), but he rejects the response given by the philosopher (al‑ustādh) – likely al‑Rāzī or another – who tried to distinguish touch from sight by appealing to the fact that touch involves physical effect (taʾthīr) and being affected (taʾaththur). The author counters that the contact associated with touch is merely customary (ʿādī), not rationally necessary. Therefore, it is possible for the perception of touch to relate to God without contact or modality (takyīf). The author then notes that Imām al‑Ḥaramayn himself accepted that all five senses can perceive God without the usual physical conditions, and he attributed this view to al‑Ashʿarī. This contradicts the position of ʿAbd Allāh ibn Saʿīd and al‑Qalānisī who denied that the other senses (besides sight) could perceive God. The passage concludes with a note from Ibn al‑Tilmisānī that al‑Rāzī raised many objections against this line of reasoning, and that he himself would briefly discuss them, indicating the ongoing scholarly debate on this topic.

Key Terms
لمس (lams: touch)
رؤية (ruʾya: vision)
تأثير (taʾthīr: affecting)
تأثر (taʾaththur: being affected)
إدراك (idrāk: perception)
عادي (ʿādī: customary)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that physical touch necessarily implies physical contact, so perceiving God by touch would require physical contact.
Correction: The argument shows that touch perception can transcend physical norms because the contact associated with touch is merely customary (ʿādī), not rationally necessary. Therefore, it is possible for touch to perceive God without contact or modality.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the distinction between touch and sight based on 'affecting and being affected' to be weak?
A) Because touch does not involve any effect.
B) Because the contact in touch is customary, not rationally necessary.
C) Because sight also involves affecting.
D) Because al-Ashʿarī rejected touch perception of God.

Key Takeaway
The perception of God by touch is not ruled out by customary physical associations; rather, it is possible without contact or modality, and this position is supported by leading Ashʿarī scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God is distant because you cannot perceive Him with your physical senses? How does this lesson challenge you to believe that He can be perceived beyond your usual experiences? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that all your senses could perceive God without physical limits?
?
Al‑Rāzī admitted he could not answer every objection. Are there faith questions you have that you find difficult to answer? How does encountering humility in great scholars free you to trust Allah's wisdom even when you lack complete answers? Will you commit to seeking knowledge while resting in divine sovereignty?
?
If God could be perceived by touch without physical contact, how would that reshape your awareness of His nearness right now? Are you living as if God is only known through certain channels? What practical step can you take this week to cultivate a sense of God's presence beyond the customary?

Refutation of Mental Aspects Argument
Sanusi refutes the opponent's reliance on mental aspects without causal explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of the opponent who affirms mental aspects but denies causal explanation, and the resulting inconsistency.

Core Idea
Affirming mental aspects without causal explanation leads to self-contradiction.

In this passage, Sanusi exposes a flaw in the opponent's position. The opponent accepts mental aspects (al-wujuh wa al-i'tibarat al-'aqliyya) but refuses to accept causal explanation (al-ta'lil). Yet when arguing about the common categories between substance and accident, the opponent relies on the very rules of mental causes that he rejects. Sanusi then quotes the opponent's own reasoning to highlight the inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
The passage uses technical terms; note the key terms listed below.
2
Identify the two opposing claims
Notice that the opponent affirms mental aspects (الوجوه والاعتبارات العقلية) but denies causal explanation (التعليل). Locate where Sanusi states this.
The opponent's position is quoted in the latter part: stating that huduth cannot be a cause.
3
Understand the inconsistency
Ask yourself: how does the opponent's argument about huduth and ru'yah depend on the rules of mental causes? Sanusi shows that the opponent's reasoning implicitly adopts causal logic, which contradicts his denial of ta'lil.
This is the core refutation: you cannot reject causation while using causal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنَّهُ قائل بالوجوه والاعتبارات العقلية، فقد تُصُوِّرَ العموم والخصوص . ويُرَدُّ عليهِ : بأنَّهُ وإِنْ قالَ بالاعتبارات العقلية فإنَّه لم يقل بالتعليل ، ومعتمدُكم فيما تطلبونَ مِنْ أقسام المشترك بين الجوهر والعرض المرئيين .. مبني على التزام أحكام العلل العقليَّةِ ، وقلتُم : إنَّ الحدوث لا يكونُ علَّةً؛ لأنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ إِلا بِشِرْكَةٍ مِنَ العدمِ والعدمُ السابق لا يجامعُ الوجودَ ، والعلة يجب مقارنتها للمعلول ، وصحة الرؤية أمر ثبوتي ، والأمرُ العدمي لا يكون علَّةً للأمر الثبوتي ولا جزءاً منها
Indeed, he is one who affirms mental aspects and considerations, so generality and particularity are conceived. And it is refuted by saying: although he affirms mental considerations, he does not affirm causal explanation (ta'lil), and your reliance on what you seek from the categories of that which is common between the visible substance and accident is based on adopting the rules of mental causes (al-`ilal al-`aqliyyah). And you said: temporal origination (al-huduth) cannot be a cause because it is only understood as involving a partnership with non-existence, and prior non-existence does not coexist with existence, and the cause must be concomitant with its effect, and the soundness of vision (ru'yah) is a positive (thubuti) matter, and a non-existential matter (amr `adami) cannot be a cause for a positive matter, nor even a part of it.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. The author addresses an opponent who claims that God has 'mental aspects and considerations' (al-wujuh wa al-i`tibarat al-`aqliyyah) and thereby conceives of generality and particularity. The author refutes this by pointing out that even if the opponent acknowledges these mental aspects, he does not accept causal explanation (al-ta`lil). Yet the opponent's argument in distinguishing the common between substance and accident (al-jawhar wa al-`arad) is based on the rules of mental causes. The author then quotes the opponent's own position: that temporal origination (al-huduth) cannot be a cause because it involves a mixture of existence and non-existence, and non-existence cannot be a cause for a positive attribute like the soundness of vision. This sets up the inconsistency: the opponent rejects causal explanation but relies on causal logic anyway.

Key Terms
الوجوه والاعتبارات العقلية
التعليل
العلل العقلية
الحدوث
العدم
الرؤية
الثبوتي
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that affirming mental aspects automatically entails affirming causal explanation.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the opponent affirms mental aspects but explicitly denies causal explanation; they are distinct concepts.

Checkpoint
What is the core inconsistency the author highlights in the opponent's position?
A) The opponent denies mental aspects yet uses them.
B) The opponent accepts causal explanation but denies mental aspects.
C) The opponent does not accept causal explanation yet relies on causal reasoning.
D) The opponent affirms both mental aspects and causal explanation.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the opponent by showing that denying causal explanation while relying on causal reasoning is self-contradictory.

The Precondition vs Cause Objection
Sanusi objects that the opponent's argument proves only a precondition, not a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between a validator (musahhih) and a cause (`illa), and why the opponent's argument for vision requiring a cause is insufficient.

Core Idea
A precondition isn't the same as a cause—the opponent confuses them.

This passage presents a clever logical objection from Sanusi. He concedes causal reasoning but challenges the conclusion. The opponent argues that if vision's validity needed no validator, it would apply universally. Sanusi points out that this only proves a validator is needed—which could be a mere condition like life is for knowledge. This subtle distinction is key in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice how Sanusi structures his objection: concession, question, opponent's response, analysis, example.
The passage is dense but logical; take your time.
2
Identify the opponent's claim and Sanusi's objection
The opponent claims vision's validity is a caused judgment. Sanusi asks why. The opponent's reply is that without a validator it would apply universally. Sanusi objects that this only proves a validator, not specifically a cause.
Spot the key terms: علّة (cause) vs مصحح (validator).
3
Understand the example of life as condition vs cause
Sanusi gives the example: life is a condition (شرط) for knowledge, power, and will to subsist, but it is not their cause. This illustrates that a validator can be a condition, not a cause. The opponent's argument fails to prove causation.
This is 'strong' (قوي) according to Sanusi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : سلَّمنا صحة التعليل، لكن لمَ قلتُم : إِنَّ صحَّةَ الرؤيةِ مِنَ الأحكام المعلَّلة ؟ وقولكم في جوابِهِ : إِنَّهُ لو لم يتوقف على مُصحح لعم حكمه المعدوم والموجود .. لا ينتج إلا أنَّه يتوقف على مُصحح ، وهو أعم مِنَ العلَّةِ؛ إذ قد يكون شرطاً؛ فإنَّ الحياة شرط للقيام العلم والقدرة والإرادة بالمحل، وليستْ علةَ لها، وهو قوي
The fourth: We grant the soundness of causal explanation, but why do you say that the soundness of vision is among the caused judgments? And your statement in answer to it: if it were not dependent on a validator (musahhih), its ruling would apply universally to the non-existent and existent... it only yields that it depends on a validator, which is more general than the cause (`illa), for it may be a condition (shart), for life is a condition for the subsistence of knowledge, power, and will in the substrate, yet it is not a cause for them, and this is strong.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is an objection ('Fourth'). The author begins by conceding the possibility of causal explanation (ta`lil) for argument's sake. He then questions why the opponent insists that the validity of vision (sihhat al-ru'yah) is a caused judgment (hukm mu`allal). The author cites the opponent's own reply: if the validity were not dependent on a validator (musahhih), it would apply to both non-existent and existent things universally. The author points out that this argument only proves dependence on a validator, not specifically a cause. A validator can be a condition (shart), which is more general than a cause. He gives the example: life is a condition for the existence of knowledge, power, and will in a subject, but it is not the cause of those attributes. This is a strong point (wa huwa qawiyy). Thus, the opponent has not proven that the validity of vision requires a cause—it may only require a precondition.

Key Terms
المصحح
الشرط
العلة
التعليل
الحكم المعلل
الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a validator (musahhih) must always be a cause (`illa).
Correction: The passage shows that a validator can be just a condition (shart), as life is for knowledge, power, and will. The opponent's argument does not prove causation.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why does the opponent's argument not prove that validity of vision requires a cause?
A) Because validity is not a caused judgment.
B) Because a validator may be just a condition, not a cause.
C) Because the opponent does not accept causal explanation.
D) Because life is not a condition for vision.

Key Takeaway
The objection shows that the opponent's argument only establishes a need for a validator, which may be a condition, not necessarily a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever confused a condition for a cause in your relationship with Allah? For instance, do you sometimes assume your worship causes His mercy, when it might only be a condition? How does this distinction challenge your understanding of divine grace?
?
The example of life as a condition for knowledge reminds us that many gifts from Allah are conditions, not causes. In what area of your life have you attributed an effect solely to a cause, ignoring the conditions Allah provides? How does this insight affect your humility and gratitude?
?
Sanusi’s objection shows that a seemingly strong argument can prove less than it claims. When you hold a belief about Allah’s attributes, do you ever critically examine whether the reasoning truly supports the conclusion? What step can you take this week to verify one of your assumptions about God’s nature?

The Fifth Objection on Vision as Common Judgment
Sanusi argues against the objection by asserting uniformity of the judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the validity of vision is considered a common judgment despite differences in the visibility of substances and accidents.

Core Idea
Validity of vision is a single judgment unaffected by its objects.

Sanusi presents a fifth objection that questions whether the validity of seeing a substance and an accident is truly the same. The objector argues that since visibility differs with the object, the judgment itself must differ. Sanusi then clarifies that the judgment, in its essence, remains identical.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the passage once to get the gist. Notice the objector grants the opponent's causal explanation but denies that the validity of vision is a common judgment.
The objector uses the example of substance and blackness.
2
Understand the objector's reasoning
The objector says the validity of a substance being visible differs from that of blackness; if they were equal, one could substitute for the other. The addition (idafa) of the judgment to different objects creates difference.
3
Examine Sanusi's answer
Sanusi responds that the validity of vision, considered purely as validity, does not differ based on what it is added to, just as the essence of knowledge does not differ with different objects.
This maintains that the judgment is common in its essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : سلَّمنا صحة تعليلِهِ، لكن لا نُسلّمُ أَنَّ صحة الرؤيةِ حكم مشترك؛ فإنَّ صحة كون الجوهر مرئيّاً مخالف لصحة كونِ السوادِ مرئيّاً، ولو تساوتا لقامَتْ إحداهما مَقامَ الأخرى، وللإضافة أثر في المخالفة. وجوابه : أنَّ صحة الرؤية بما هي صحة رؤيةٍ .. لا تختلفُ بما تُضاف إليه، كما لا تختلفُ حقيقة العلم باختلافِ مُتعلقاتِهِ
The fifth: We grant the soundness of his causal explanation, but we do not concede that the validity of vision is a common judgment (hukm mushtarak); for the validity of a substance being visible is different from the validity of blackness being visible, and if they were equal, one would stand in the place of the other, and addition (al-idafa) has an effect in the difference. And its answer: that the validity of vision insofar as it is validity of vision does not differ by that to which it is added, just as the reality of knowledge does not differ by the difference of its objects.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is an objection ('Fifth'). The author again grants the opponent's causal explanation for argument. He then denies that the validity of vision is a common judgment (hukm mushtarak) shared by substance and accident. He argues that the validity of a substance being visible differs from the validity of blackness (a color) being visible; if they were identical, one could substitute for the other. The addition (al-idafa) of the judgment to different objects creates difference. The author then gives the answer to this objection: the validity of vision, considered purely as validity of vision, does not differ based on what it is attributed to, just as the essence of knowledge (haqiqat al-`ilm) does not differ despite different objects of knowledge. Thus, the judgment is common in its essence.

Key Terms
الحكم المشترك
الإضافة
صحة الرؤية
الجوهر
العرض
العلم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that different objects of visibility entail different judgments of validity, so the judgment is not common.
Correction: The author clarifies that the judgment qua judgment is the same; the difference lies only in the objects to which it is attributed.

Checkpoint
What is the opponent's claim that the fifth objection denies?
A) That the validity of vision is a caused judgment.
B) That the validity of vision is a common judgment.
C) That substances and accidents are equally visible.
D) That addition has an effect on difference.

Key Takeaway
The validity of vision is a common judgment unaffected by the particular object to which it is attributed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The objector granted the causal explanation but denied the common judgment. In your daily life, do you sometimes accept theological truths intellectually but fail to let them unify your heart? What would change if you truly saw all of Allah's actions as coming from one source?

Distinguishing Intellectual Attributes from General Ones
Sanusi defends that equal rulings cannot have different causes, refuting a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why intellectual attributes (like knowing and powerful) cannot be caused by different realities, and why the example of color-ness does not apply to them.

Core Idea
Intellectual attributes are defined by their correlates, so different causes would change their very meaning.

Sanusi addresses a clever objection: if a common attribute like 'color-ness' can be caused by various specific colors, why can't intellectual attributes be caused differently? His response reveals a key difference between real-world properties and the rational judgments that define Allah's attributes. Let's see why the analogy fails.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The opponent concedes that a common attribute may exist but claims that equal rulings can still have different causes. He offers the example of 'color-ness' (al-lawniyyah) which is common to all colors yet caused by each specific color.
This is a counterexample aimed at breaking the rule that different causes cannot produce identical rulings.
2
Identify the key distinction
Sanusi distinguishes intellectual attributes (al-ahkam al-`aqliyyah) like 'being knowing' (al-`alimiyyah) from sensory generalities like color-ness. Unlike color-ness, intellectual attributes are not defined by themselves but by their correlates (knowledge and power).
This distinction is the heart of the answer.
3
Apply the distinction
If we explained 'being knowing' by a reality contrary to knowledge, its very intelligibility would be inverted. This is impossible. In contrast, color-ness is simply the general property of having a color, and its specific causes do not change its meaning.
The opponent's example actually reinforces Sanusi's point: the specific (al-akhass) cannot be a cause for the general (al-a`amm) in the case of intellectual attributes.
4
Realize the conclusion
Sanusi concedes that specific colors cause color-ness, but he argues that this is a case where the specific is a cause for the general, which is precisely what is denied for intellectual attributes. Thus the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : سلَّمنا أنَّه مشترك، ولكن لا نُسلّم امتناع تعليل الأحكام المتساوية بعلل مختلفةٍ؛ فإنَّ اللونية مشترك، ووجودها معلَّل بخصوصيَّاتِ الألوانِ . وجوابُهُ : أنَّ الأحكام العقليَّةَ كالعالميَّةِ والقادرية لا تتميز باعتبارِ ذاتِها، وإنَّما تتميّز باعتبارِ مُوجِباتِها مِنَ العلم والقدرة ، فلو علَّلْنا العالميَّة بحقيقة تخالف العلم .. لزمَ قَلْبُ معقولها، وذلك محال وأمَّا لزومُ اللونية لخصوصيَّاتِ الألوان فمُسلَّم، والممنوع كونُ الأخص علة للأعم
The sixth: We concede that it is common, but we do not concede the impossibility of explaining equal rulings by different causes; for 'color-ness' (al-lawniyyah) is common, and its existence is caused by specific colors. And its answer: that the intellectual rulings (al-ahkam al-`aqliyyah) like being knowing (al-`alimiyyah) and being powerful (al-qadiriyyah) are not distinguished by their own essence, but are distinguished by their necessary correlates from knowledge and power. So if we were to cause being knowing by a reality contrary to knowledge, it would invert its intelligibility, and that is impossible. As for the necessary connection of color-ness to specific colors, that is conceded, and what is prevented is the specific being a cause for the general.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is an objection ('Sixth'). The author's opponent concedes that the validity of vision is a common judgment but objects to the claim that equal rulings cannot have different causes. The opponent gives an example: 'color-ness' (al-lawniyyah) is a common attribute shared by all colors, yet its existence in each color is caused by the specific color (e.g., whiteness, blackness). Therefore, one common attribute can have multiple specific causes. The author's answer distinguishes between intellectual attributes (al-ahkam al-`aqliyyah) like being knowing (`alimiyyah) and being powerful (qadiriyyah). These attributes are not distinct in their essence; they are distinguished only by their correlates (muqibat): knowledge and power. If we were to explain the attribute of being knowing by something contrary to knowledge, the very intelligibility of the attribute would be inverted—this is impossible. As for the example of color-ness, the author concedes that specific colors cause color-ness, but he argues that this is a case where the specific (al-akhass) is a cause for the general (al-a`amm), which is the very thing that is denied. Thus, the opponent's example actually reinforces the author's position.

العالمية
القادرية
الأخص
الأعم
العلة
اللونية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a general attribute like color-ness can have specific causes, then intellectual attributes could too.
Correction: The author shows they are different because the identity of intellectual attributes depends entirely on their correlates, making different causes impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the analogy of color-ness for intellectual attributes?
A) Because color-ness is not an intellectual attribute.
B) Because intellectual attributes are distinguished by their correlates, making different causes impossible.
C) Because specific colors are not causes of color-ness.
D) Because the opponent misapplied the term 'al-akhass'.

Key Takeaway
Intellectual attributes are uniquely tied to their correlates, making different causes impossible, unlike general properties like color-ness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This passage says changing the cause of 'being knowing' would invert its meaning. In your own life, are there times you treat Allah's knowledge as if it were like human knowledge—subject to change or limitation? How can you actively guard your heart against such a reduction?
?

Refuting the Need for Direction in Vision
Sanusi rejects the claim that vision requires direction and facing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the claim that seeing Allah requires direction and facing, by exposing the flawed physical theory behind it.

Core Idea
Vision does not require direction; the objection is based on a false theory of sight.

Some argue that seeing Allah would imply He is in a direction, which is impossible for the transcendent Creator. Sanusi meets this objection on its own ground: he shows that the theory of vision they assume—that light rays shoot from the eye to the object—is scientifically unsound. Once that premise falls, the whole objection collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi quickly identifies the basis of the objection and then refutes it with a single powerful counterexample.
The passage is very condensed; take time to parse each phrase.
2
Identify the objection and its basis
The objection is that vision requires direction (al-jiha) and facing (al-muqabala). Sanusi traces this to the emission theory of vision. Can you see why this theory is assumed?
The innovators (al-mubtadiʿa) rely on this theory to deny the beatific vision.
3
Understand Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi argues that if the emission theory were true, a person would only see something the size of their pupil, which is obviously false. This shows the theory is invalid, and so the objection based on it is invalid too.
4
Reflect on the theological consequence
Ask yourself: if the objection fails, does that prove that Allah can be seen? Not yet—but it removes one barrier. The positive proof for vision comes elsewhere.
This lesson clears a major objection, paving the way for affirming the beatific vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و معتمدُ مَنْ أَحالَها مِنَ المبتدعة : أنَّها تستدعي الجهة والمقابلة ، وهو باطل ؛ لأنَّ ذلكَ مُفرَّع على انبعاث الأشعة ، فتتصل بالمرئي ، وذلكَ لو صح لوجب ألا يرى الإنسانُ إِلا قدْرَ حدقتِهِ ، وهو باطل على الضرورة .
And the basis of those who deem it impossible among the innovators is that it necessitates direction and facing, which is false; because that is branched from the emission of rays, which then connect with the seen object, and if that were correct, it would necessitate that a man sees only the size of his pupil, which is false by necessity.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that seeing God would require direction (al-jiha) and facing (al-muqabala), which are impossible for God. The author (Sanusi) identifies this as the basis of the innovators (al-mubtadiʿa) who deny the vision of God. He declares the objection false because it is based on the theory that vision occurs by the emission of rays from the eye that connect with the object seen. He argues that if this theory were correct, then a person would only see an object equal to the size of his pupil, which is obviously false by immediate necessity (ḍarūra). Thus, the objection collapses because its premise (the emission theory) is unsound. This refutes the claim that vision requires direction and facing.

المقابلة
انبعاث الأشعة
المرئي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that seeing anything, including Allah, necessarily requires spatial direction and facing.
Correction: Sanusi shows that this belief is based on a false scientific model of vision. Rejecting the model removes the need to assert direction for vision.

Checkpoint

A) Because God is not in a direction
B) Because the emission theory of vision is false
C) Because vision is a mental act
