Refuting the Rays Argument Against Seeing Allah
Sanusi refutes the rays theory of vision used to deny seeing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that seeing Allah requires direction is based on a flawed theory of vision known as the rays theory.

Core Idea
The rays theory of vision is self-defeating and cannot be used to deny the possibility of seeing Allah.

Sanusi begins by addressing a key objection raised by those who deny the possibility of seeing Allah. They claim that vision requires the object to be facing the observer and in a direction, which they argue is impossible for Allah. Sanusi shows that this objection rests on a specific physical theory of vision—the emission of rays from the eye—and that this theory is fundamentally flawed.

Guided Path
1
Read the innovators' argument
Read the opening lines where the innovators claim that seeing Allah requires direction and facing. Understand their reasoning.
This argument is from the Mu'tazila and others who deny ru'ya.
2
Examine the rays theory
The passage details the rays theory: luminous parts separate from the eye and attach to the seen object. Note the conditions they require (facing, moderate distance).
They call the tip of the ray at the object 'base of ray' and the part at the eye 'emitted ray'.
3
Find Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi points out that if vision depended on rays connecting to the object, we would only see objects the size of our pupil. This is obviously false from common experience.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand the conclusion
The innovators conclude that Allah cannot be seen because He is not a body and has no direction. Sanusi will later provide an alternative view.
This sets up the Ash'ari theory in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ رؤيته سبحانه لا تستدعي الجهة والمقابلة ]
و معتمدُ مَنْ أَحالَها مِنَ المبتدعة : أنَّها تستدعي الجهة والمقابلة ، وهو باطل ؛ لأنَّ ذلكَ مُفرَّع على انبعاث الأشعة ، فتتصل بالمرئي ، وذلكَ لو صح لوجب ألا يرى الإنسانُ إِلا قدْرَ حدقتِهِ ، وهو باطل على الضرورة .
الأشعة عندهم : أجزاء مضيئة تنفصلُ مِنَ العين وتتشبث بالمرئي فيرى ؛ بشرط أنْ يكونَ في مقابلة الرائي ، وبشرط انتفاء القربِ والبعدِ المفرطينِ ، وإِنَّما تقعُ الرؤيةُ عندَهم بالطَّرْفِ بِطَرَفِ تلكَ الأشعةِ المتصل بالمرئي، ويُسمُّونَهُ : قاعدة الشعاع ، ويُسمُّونَ المتصل منها بالناظرِ (۱) : منبعثَ الشعاع ، وقالوا : إنَّ قاعدة الشعاع إذا لاقت جسماً صقيلاً لا تضرُّس فيه كالمرآة . . لم تتشبَّتْ بهِ ، بل تنعكس إلى الرائي فيرى نفْسَهُ
قالوا : وإِنَّما لم يرَ داخلَ الجفن للقرب المفرط ، فلهذا قالوا : لا يصحُ أن يُرى جلَّ وعزَّ ؛ لاستحالةِ اتصال الأشعةِ بهِ ؛ لأنَّها إِنَّما تتصل بالأجسام ، ولاستدعائها جهةً تنبعث إليها ، والله جل وعلا ليسَ بجرم ولا في جهة .
وأهل الحقِّ رضيَ الله تعالى عنهم يقولون : الإدراك معنى يخلقه الله تعالى في المدرِكِ ؛ فإِنْ خُلِقَ في جزءٍ مِنَ العينِ سُمِّيَ إبصاراً ، وفي جزءٍ مِنَ القلبِ سُمِّيَ عِلْماً ، وفي جزء مِنَ الأُذُنِ سُمِّيَ سمعاً ، وفي اللسانِ سُمِّيَ ذوقاً ، وفي كلِّ الجسدِ سُمِّيَ حِسّاً ، واختصاص خلْقِهِ بهذهِ المَحال إنَّما هو بحكم العادة ، وكذا اختصاص بعضها ؛ بأنْ يكونَ المُدرَكُ في جهةٍ وغير قريب جداً ولا بعيد جداً . . إنَّما هو بحكمِ العادة ، ويجوز أن تنخرقَ العادةُ ، فيتعلَّق بما هو قريب جداً أو بعيد جداً ، بل وبما ليس في جهةٍ كما جرت العادة بذلك في العلم(٢)
[His vision, exalted be He, does not require direction and facing]
And the basis of those among the innovators who deemed it impossible is that it requires direction and facing, and this is false; because that is predicated on the emission of rays, which then connect with the seen object. And that, if it were correct, would necessarily mean that a person sees only the size of his pupil, which is false by necessity.
Rays according to them are luminous parts that separate from the eye and cling to the seen object, so he sees, on condition that it is facing the seer, and on condition of the absence of excessive proximity and distance. And vision according to them occurs only by the tip of those rays connected to the seen object, and they call it the 'base of the ray', and they call the part connected to the beholder (1): 'the emitted ray'. And they said: When the base of the ray meets a smooth body without indentations, like a mirror... it does not cling to it, but rather reflects back to the seer and he sees himself.
They said: And he does not see the inside of the eyelid because of excessive proximity. For this reason they said: It is not valid that He, mighty and majestic, be seen, because it is impossible for rays to connect to Him; since they only connect to bodies, and because they require a direction to which they are emitted, and Allah, mighty and majestic, is neither a body nor in a direction.
And the people of truth, may Allah be pleased with them, say: Perception is a meaning that Allah, exalted, creates in the perceiver; so if it is created in a part of the eye, it is called sight; in a part of the heart, it is called knowledge; in a part of the ear, it is called hearing; in the tongue, it is called taste; in all of the body, it is called touch. And the specification of its creation in these loci is only by the rule of habit, and likewise the specification of some of them, such that the perceived object is in a direction and not very near nor very far, is only by the rule of habit. And it is permissible for the habit to be broken, so that it (perception) attaches to what is very near or very far, nay, even to what is not in a direction, just as the habit has occurred in the case of knowledge (2).
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection raised by some innovators (mubtadi'a) who argue that seeing Allah (ru'ya) is impossible because it would require direction (jiha) and facing (muqābala). The author first states their argument and then refutes it by showing that it is based on a flawed theory of vision: the emission of rays (ash'ā') from the eye. He points out that if vision depended on rays connecting to the object, a person would only see an object the size of their pupil, which is obviously false. He then elaborates on the rays theory: rays are luminous particles that separate from the eye and attach to the seen object, requiring the object to be facing the seer and at a moderate distance. The tip of the rays that contacts the object is called the base of the ray (qā'idat al-shu'ā'), and the part connected to the eye is the emitted ray. When the base meets a smooth surface like a mirror, it reflects back, allowing the seer to see themselves. They also claim that one does not see the inside of the eyelid due to excessive proximity. Based on this, they conclude that Allah cannot be seen because rays cannot connect to Him—He is not a body and has no direction. In response, the Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth, i.e., the Ash'aris) provide an alternative account of perception: perception (idrāk) is a meaning (ma'nā) that Allah creates directly in the perceiver's organ. When created in the eye, it is called sight; in the heart, knowledge; in the ear, hearing; in the tongue, taste; in the whole body, touch. The specific assignment of these perceptions to particular organs is merely by the rule of habit ('āda). Likewise, the condition that the perceived object be in a direction and at a moderate distance is also merely habitual. Since habit can be broken (inkhirāq al-'āda), Allah can create perception of something very near, very far, or even without direction, just as He creates knowledge of things without the knower being in a direction relative to them. Thus, the innovators' argument fails because it mistakenly assumes vision depends on physical rays, whereas in reality perception is directly created by Allah and can occur without such conditions.

Key Terms
رؤية
جهة
مقابلة
أشعة
قاعدة الشعاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rays theory proves that vision requires direction and facing, so Allah cannot be seen because He has no direction.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the rays theory is false because it leads to absurd consequences (seeing only the size of the pupil). Moreover, the Ash'ari theory of perception does not depend on rays.

Checkpoint
According to the innovators' rays theory, what condition is necessary for vision to occur?
A) The object must be in a direction and facing the seer.
B) The object must be very large.
C) The eye must be illuminated.
D) The seer must be at a specific distance.

Key Takeaway
The rays theory of vision is unsound and cannot be used to deny the possibility of seeing Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth from the Quran or Sunnah because your limited reasoning told you it was impossible? Where in your life are you still doing that, and what would it cost you to submit fully to revelation?
?
The innovators built their argument on a flawed human theory. What theories or assumptions about Allah have you unconsciously adopted from your culture, education, or personal bias? Are you willing to dismantle them even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
If the rays theory is so obviously false that a child can see its absurdity, why do intelligent people still cling to it? What does this teach you about the arrogance of human intellect when it opposes divine truth? Will you humble yourself today?

The Ash'ari Theory of Perception: Vision Without Direction
Sanusi presents the Ash'ari theory that perception is a meaning created by Allah, subject to habit which can be broken.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari view that perception is a meaning directly created by Allah, which can occur without direction or normal conditions.

Core Idea
Perception is a direct creation of Allah, independent of rays and habitual conditions.

Having refuted the rays theory, Sanusi now presents the positive Ash'ari account of perception. He explains that perception is a meaning (ma'nā) that Allah creates in the perceiver. The specific localization of perception (in eye, ear, etc.) is merely a matter of habit, and habit can be broken. Therefore, vision of Allah can occur without direction or facing.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari definition of perception
The passage defines perception as a meaning that Allah creates in the perceiver. Note the different names it takes depending on the organ.
Sight is perception in the eye, knowledge in the heart, etc.
2
Understand the role of habit
The conditions like being in a direction and moderate distance are only due to habit, not necessity. Allah can break these habits.
This is the principle of inkhirāq al-'āda.
3
Apply to seeing Allah
Since habit can be broken, Allah can create perception of things without direction, such as Himself. This is analogous to how knowledge of something does not require direction.
The passage mentions knowledge as an example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ رؤيته سبحانه لا تستدعي الجهة والمقابلة ]
و معتمدُ مَنْ أَحالَها مِنَ المبتدعة : أنَّها تستدعي الجهة والمقابلة ، وهو باطل ؛ لأنَّ ذلكَ مُفرَّع على انبعاث الأشعة ، فتتصل بالمرئي ، وذلكَ لو صح لوجب ألا يرى الإنسانُ إِلا قدْرَ حدقتِهِ ، وهو باطل على الضرورة .
الأشعة عندهم : أجزاء مضيئة تنفصلُ مِنَ العين وتتشبث بالمرئي فيرى ؛ بشرط أنْ يكونَ في مقابلة الرائي ، وبشرط انتفاء القربِ والبعدِ المفرطينِ ، وإِنَّما تقعُ الرؤيةُ عندَهم بالطَّرْفِ بِطَرَفِ تلكَ الأشعةِ المتصل بالمرئي، ويُسمُّونَهُ : قاعدة الشعاع ، ويُسمُّونَ المتصل منها بالناظرِ (۱) : منبعثَ الشعاع ، وقالوا : إنَّ قاعدة الشعاع إذا لاقت جسماً صقيلاً لا تضرُّس فيه كالمرآة . . لم تتشبَّتْ بهِ ، بل تنعكس إلى الرائي فيرى نفْسَهُ
قالوا : وإِنَّما لم يرَ داخلَ الجفن للقرب المفرط ، فلهذا قالوا : لا يصحُ أن يُرى جلَّ وعزَّ ؛ لاستحالةِ اتصال الأشعةِ بهِ ؛ لأنَّها إِنَّما تتصل بالأجسام ، ولاستدعائها جهةً تنبعث إليها ، والله جل وعلا ليسَ بجرم ولا في جهة .
وأهل الحقِّ رضيَ الله تعالى عنهم يقولون : الإدراك معنى يخلقه الله تعالى في المدرِكِ ؛ فإِنْ خُلِقَ في جزءٍ مِنَ العينِ سُمِّيَ إبصاراً ، وفي جزءٍ مِنَ القلبِ سُمِّيَ عِلْماً ، وفي جزء مِنَ الأُذُنِ سُمِّيَ سمعاً ، وفي اللسانِ سُمِّيَ ذوقاً ، وفي كلِّ الجسدِ سُمِّيَ حِسّاً ، واختصاص خلْقِهِ بهذهِ المَحال إنَّما هو بحكم العادة ، وكذا اختصاص بعضها ؛ بأنْ يكونَ المُدرَكُ في جهةٍ وغير قريب جداً ولا بعيد جداً . . إنَّما هو بحكمِ العادة ، ويجوز أن تنخرقَ العادةُ ، فيتعلَّق بما هو قريب جداً أو بعيد جداً ، بل وبما ليس في جهةٍ كما جرت العادة بذلك في العلم(٢)
[His vision, exalted be He, does not require direction and facing]
And the basis of those among the innovators who deemed it impossible is that it requires direction and facing, and this is false; because that is predicated on the emission of rays, which then connect with the seen object. And that, if it were correct, would necessarily mean that a person sees only the size of his pupil, which is false by necessity.
Rays according to them are luminous parts that separate from the eye and cling to the seen object, so he sees, on condition that it is facing the seer, and on condition of the absence of excessive proximity and distance. And vision according to them occurs only by the tip of those rays connected to the seen object, and they call it the 'base of the ray', and they call the part connected to the beholder (1): 'the emitted ray'. And they said: When the base of the ray meets a smooth body without indentations, like a mirror... it does not cling to it, but rather reflects back to the seer and he sees himself.
They said: And he does not see the inside of the eyelid because of excessive proximity. For this reason they said: It is not valid that He, mighty and majestic, be seen, because it is impossible for rays to connect to Him; since they only connect to bodies, and because they require a direction to which they are emitted, and Allah, mighty and majestic, is neither a body nor in a direction.
And the people of truth, may Allah be pleased with them, say: Perception is a meaning that Allah, exalted, creates in the perceiver; so if it is created in a part of the eye, it is called sight; in a part of the heart, it is called knowledge; in a part of the ear, it is called hearing; in the tongue, it is called taste; in all of the body, it is called touch. And the specification of its creation in these loci is only by the rule of habit, and likewise the specification of some of them, such that the perceived object is in a direction and not very near nor very far, is only by the rule of habit. And it is permissible for the habit to be broken, so that it (perception) attaches to what is very near or very far, nay, even to what is not in a direction, just as the habit has occurred in the case of knowledge (2).
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection raised by some innovators (mubtadi'a) who argue that seeing Allah (ru'ya) is impossible because it would require direction (jiha) and facing (muqābala). The author first states their argument and then refutes it by showing that it is based on a flawed theory of vision: the emission of rays (ash'ā') from the eye. He points out that if vision depended on rays connecting to the object, a person would only see an object the size of their pupil, which is obviously false. He then elaborates on the rays theory: rays are luminous particles that separate from the eye and attach to the seen object, requiring the object to be facing the seer and at a moderate distance. The tip of the rays that contacts the object is called the base of the ray (qā'idat al-shu'ā'), and the part connected to the eye is the emitted ray. When the base meets a smooth surface like a mirror, it reflects back, allowing the seer to see themselves. They also claim that one does not see the inside of the eyelid due to excessive proximity. Based on this, they conclude that Allah cannot be seen because rays cannot connect to Him—He is not a body and has no direction. In response, the Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth, i.e., the Ash'aris) provide an alternative account of perception: perception (idrāk) is a meaning (ma'nā) that Allah creates directly in the perceiver's organ. When created in the eye, it is called sight; in the heart, knowledge; in the ear, hearing; in the tongue, taste; in the whole body, touch. The specific assignment of these perceptions to particular organs is merely by the rule of habit ('āda). Likewise, the condition that the perceived object be in a direction and at a moderate distance is also merely habitual. Since habit can be broken (inkhirāq al-'āda), Allah can create perception of something very near, very far, or even without direction, just as He creates knowledge of things without the knower being in a direction relative to them. Thus, the innovators' argument fails because it mistakenly assumes vision depends on physical rays, whereas in reality perception is directly created by Allah and can occur without such conditions.

Key Terms
إدراك
معنى
عادة
انخراق العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Ash'ari view implies that seeing Allah requires a direction, but the passage clarifies that direction is merely a habit and can be broken.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the requirement of direction is only by habit, and habit can be broken. Therefore, vision of Allah can occur without direction.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view, why is seeing Allah possible despite Him having no direction?
A) Because Allah is a body and can be seen.
B) Because perception is a meaning created by Allah, and habit can be broken.
C) Because the rays theory is correct for bodies but not for Allah.
D) Because the Qur'an explicitly states that Allah will be seen.

Key Takeaway
Allah can create perception of Himself without direction, as habit is not a necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your sight is nothing but a meaning Allah creates each moment, independent of your eye's physical processes, how does that transform your gratitude for being able to see? Will you thank Allah specifically for creating this meaning in you every time you open your eyes today?
?
The Ash'aris teach that habit can be broken—Allah could create perception of Himself without any direction. Do you truly live as if the unseen realities of faith are more certain than the visible world? What one habit in your daily life reveals that you trust your senses more than Allah's promise?
?
You habitually assume that what you perceive is caused by physical mechanisms. But this lesson says that assumption is only habit. Are you willing to let go of that habit and accept that Allah's power over perception is absolute? What would change in your relationship with the Quran if you believed that Allah creates your understanding directly, not through your intellect alone?

God's Acts Do Not Imply Change or Perfection in Him
Sanusi argues that God does not gain perfection from His acts nor suffer loss from not acting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's acts do not imply any change or acquisition of perfection in Him, and how His perfection is intrinsic and eternal.

Core Idea
God's perfection is intrinsic, not dependent on His actions.

Sanusi addresses a critical theological concern: if God acts, does that mean He gains something? If He refrains, does He lose? In this passage, he argues that God's perfection is not affected by His acts or their absence. Instead, acts serve as signs for us, not as sources of perfection for Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage closely
Read the Arabic and English translation to understand the argument's flow.
Notice the logical connectors used.
2
Trace the logical steps
Identify the premise about temporal things and the conclusion about God's perfection.
3
Reflect on the misconception
Consider why someone might think acts change God, and how Sanusi refutes it.
The answer lies in God's intrinsic perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحدوثِ لما سواه (١) ، وإنْ كانَ حادثاً لزمَ أيضاً اتصافُ ذاتِهِ تعالى بالحوادثِ ، وقد سبق استحالتها عليهِ (٢) ؛ فهو سبحانَهُ لا يتجدَّدُ لهُ بفعلٍ مِنَ الأفعالِ كمال ، ولا بتركِهِ نقص ، بل هو الكامل بذاتِهِ وصفاتِهِ في أزلِهِ وفيما لا يزالُ ، وإنَّما الأفعال دلتنا نحنُ على معرفة وجودِهِ وصفاتِهِ على حسَبِ ما هو مُقرَّرٌ ، وإلى هذا المعنى أشرتُ بقولي : ( والأفعالُ كلُّها ؛ خيرها وشرها . . . ) إلى آخره.
The temporality (al-ḥudūth) of everything other than Him (1). And if He [i.e., something else] were temporal, that would also necessitate that the essence of the Exalted be qualified by temporal events (al-ḥawādith), and the impossibility of that upon Him has already preceded (2). So He, glorified, does not acquire through any act of the acts a perfection, nor through its abandonment a deficiency. Rather, He is perfect by His essence and attributes in His pre-eternity and in what never ceases. Rather, the acts have indicated to us knowledge of His existence and attributes according to what is established. And to this meaning I have alluded by my saying: 'And all the acts, their good and evil...' etc.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage is a theological proof of God's transcendence above temporal change and acquisition from deeds. The author begins by stating that everything other than God is temporal (ḥādith). If something that is not God were to be temporal, then that would necessitate describing God's essence with temporal events, which has already been proven impossible. So God, exalted, does not acquire perfection from any act nor deficiency from abandoning it. Rather, He is perfect by His own essence and attributes in His pre-eternity and everlastingly. Acts only serve as signs for us to know His existence and attributes. The author then references his own earlier statement that acts, both good and evil, are all from God, but that does not imply change in God. The key point is that God's perfection is intrinsic and not dependent on His actions.

Key Terms
الحدوث
الحوادث
الكمال
النقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's actions add to His perfection or that refraining from actions diminishes Him.
Correction: Sanusi states that God is perfect by His essence and attributes eternally, so acts do not change His state.

Checkpoint

A) Because He does not perform acts
B) Because His perfection is intrinsic and not dependent on acts

D) Because acts are created by humans

Key Takeaway
God's perfection is intrinsic and eternal; acts are signs for us, not sources of change in Him.

The Benefit of Reward and Punishment: God’s Transcendence and Our Growth
Sanusi explains the purpose of reward and punishment relative to God and creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that reward and punishment do not add perfection to God or cause deficiency, and that their benefit lies in strengthening our knowledge of His attributes and free will.

Core Idea
Reward and punishment are for our benefit, not God's.

Sanusi addresses a subtle but crucial point: the rewards of Paradise and the punishments of Hell are immense, yet they do not affect God’s own perfection in any way. This lesson explores why that is, and then turns to how these realities actually benefit us by deepening our awareness of His majesty, choice, and sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two-part structure: first, what reward and punishment do NOT do to God; second, what they DO for us.
The passage uses a conditional 'if you look at...' to introduce the reflection.
2
Identify the two key claims
The first claim is that reward/punishment cause no renewal of perfection or deficiency in God. The second is that they uniformly indicate His existence, attributes, majesty, and beauty, increasing our knowledge of His free choice.
3
Understand why God's perfection is unaffected
Sanusi explains that God has always possessed all perfection befitting Him from pre-eternity; no external event can add to or subtract from that. This guards against thinking God's actions are compelled.
The phrase 'لا يُوجِبُ له سبحانَهُ تجدُّدَ كمال' is key.
4
Reflect on the human benefit
Ask yourself: How do reward and punishment increase our knowledge? They reveal God's choice and dominion, showing He is not compelled. That awareness deepens our appreciation of His majesty.
Sanusi says they 'only increased us in strength of knowledge' – not that they add information, but strengthen certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مراده بكلا النوعين : الثواب والعقاب ؛ أي : إذا نظرت إلى الثواب وما احتَوَت عليهِ الجنَّةُ مِنْ دقائقِ النعيم الخارقة للعوائد التي لم تخطر قط على بال ، وإلى مثلِها مِنْ دقائقِ العذابِ وما احتَوَت عليهِ جهَنَّمُ مِنْ أنواع العذابِ التي لا تُكيَّفُ .. كلُّ ذلكَ لا يُوجِبُ له سبحانَهُ تجدُّدَ كمال لذاتِهِ ولا لصفةٍ مِنْ صفاتِهِ ؛ حتى يجب ذلك عليه ، بل كلُّ كمال يليقُ بهِ فلم يزل متّصفاً بهِ في الأزلِ ولا يزال (۱) ، ولا يُوجِبُ لهُ فعله أو تركه نقصاً حتى يستحيل عليه .
وأما فائدتها بالنسبة إلينا : فهي مستويةٌ في دلالتها على وجودِهِ تعالى ووجود صفاته العليَّةِ وسَعَةِ جلالِهِ وعظيم جماله ، بل لم يزدنا وقوع النوعين وخلْقُهُ تعالى الأضداد إلا قوَّةَ علم بعظيم اختيارِهِ وسَعَةِ مُلْكِهِ ، وأنَّهُ ليسَ مجبوراً على فعْلِ مِنَ الأفعالِ
His intent by 'both types' is: reward and punishment; that is, when you look at reward and what Paradise contains of subtleties of bliss that break habits and that never occurred to the mind at all, and at similar subtleties of punishment and what Hell contains of types of punishment that cannot be qualified... all that does not necessitate for Him, glory be to Him, any renewal of perfection for His essence nor for any of His attributes, such that it would be obligatory upon Him. Rather, every perfection befitting Him has not ceased to be an attribute of Him in pre-eternity and will not cease (1), and neither His doing nor leaving necessitates any deficiency such that it would be impossible for Him.
As for its benefit relative to us: it is uniform in its indication of His existence, Most High, and the existence of His exalted attributes, and the vastness of His majesty and the greatness of His beauty. Rather, the occurrence of both types and His, Most High, creation of opposites only increased us in strength of knowledge of His tremendous choice and the vastness of His dominion, and that He is not compelled in any of the actions.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage explains the benefit of reward and punishment. First, the author clarifies that the two types referred to are reward (thawāb) and punishment (ʿiqāb). Upon examining the exquisite bliss of Paradise and the indescribable torments of Hell, none of this necessitates any new perfection for God's essence or attributes—He has always possessed all perfection from eternity. His actions or refraining do not cause deficiency. Second, the benefit for us humans is that both reward and punishment equally indicate God's existence, attributes, majesty, and beauty. They only increase our knowledge of His free choice and sovereignty, proving He is not compelled in any action.

Key Terms
ثواب
عقاب
كمال
اختيار
مجبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reward and punishment imply a change in God's essence or that He is compelled to reward or punish.
Correction: The passage clarifies that reward and punishment do not necessitate any renewal of perfection or deficiency for God, and they are not obligations upon Him. Rather, they increase our knowledge of His free choice.

Checkpoint

A) They add new perfection to Him.
B) They cause no renewal of perfection or deficiency.
C) They obligate Him to reward the obedient.
D) They prove He is compelled to act.

Key Takeaway
Reward and punishment reveal God's unending perfection and our growing knowledge of His majesty and free choice.

Two Proofs That God Acts Without Purpose
Sanusi argues that God has no purpose in His actions, with two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot act for a purpose, based on two rational proofs from the Sunni creed that safeguard His absolute freedom and transcendence.

Core Idea
God's actions are free, not driven by purposes that would compel Him.

This passage from Sanusi's creed presents two powerful arguments that God's actions are not motivated by purposes (aghrāḍ). The author first clarifies the meaning of purpose and then demonstrates that attributing purpose to God would either imply necessity and compulsion or lead to infinite regress. These arguments safeguard God's absolute freedom and transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from a preliminary argument to two formal proofs.
The passage is dense; do not worry about understanding every detail at first.
2
Understand the initial classification of purpose
The author begins by classifying any possible purpose as either a benefit returning to God or to His act. Reflect on why both are impossible.
This sets the stage for the proofs.
3
Examine the first proof: purpose makes action obligatory
The first proof argues that if an act had a purpose, the act would be obligatory and God would be compelled, contradicting His free choice. Trace the definition of purpose and the entailment.
The Quranic verse 28:68 supports this.
4
Examine the second proof: purpose leads to infinite regress
The second proof argues that if the purpose is eternal, the act would be eternal; if originated, it would require another purpose, leading to infinite regress. Both are impossible.
This proof relies on the impossibility of an infinite chain of originated things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فالغرضُ إِمَّا مصلحةٌ تعود إليه ، أو إلى فعله ، والأوَّلُ محالٌ ؛ لاستلزام اتصاف ذاته العليَّةِ بالحوادث والثاني محالٌ ؛ لعدم وجوبِ مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح ولأنَّه قادر على إيصال تلك المصلحة إلى العبد مثلاً بغير واسطة ، ولأنَّهُ يلزمُ فيهِ تعليلُ الشيء بنفسِهِ ، أو التسلسل لنقل الكلام إلى تلك المصلحة نفسها يعني : أَنَّكَ إذا عرفت استواء الأفعالِ بالنسبة إليهِ تعالى ، وأنه مختار في جميعها لا يجب عليهِ منها شيء.. لزمَ ألا يكونَ لهُ تعالى غرض في شيء منها ؛ أي: لا علَّةَ لشيءٍ مِنَ الأفعالِ مشتملة على حكمة تبعثه على إيجادِ ذلك الفعل أو إعدامِهِ ، بل هو جلَّ وعلا مختار في كلا الأمرين ، واستدل في العقيدة على هذا المطلب بأوجه : الأول : أنَّهُ لو كانَ لهُ غرضُ في فعلِ مِنَ الأفعالِ لكانَ ذلكَ الفعَلُ واجباً عليه ، لا يتأتّى له تركُهُ ، وبيانُ الملازمة : أنَّ معنى الغرضِ : أن يشتمل الفعل على حكمة تبعثه عقلاً على إيجادِهِ ؛ بحيثُ يلزم نقصُهُ لو لم يفعل ، هذا معنى الغرض ، فيكون موجباً للفعل ، وإلا لم يكن غرضاً له علة فيهِ فقولي : ( وإلا لم يكن علَّةٌ لهُ ) بيان للملازمة ، وأما قولي : ( فيكونُ مقهوراً ) فهو بيان للثانية ؛ وهي قولنا : لكنَّه لا يكونُ الفعلُ واجباً عليه ؛ لما يلزمُ عليهِ مِنْ قهرِهِ وعدم اختياره ؛ إذ المختار هو الذي يتأتّى منه الفعلُ والتركُ ، والفَرْضُ أَنَّ هذا الفعلَ فيهِ غرضُ لا يتأتّى معَهُ تركُهُ ، وقد علمت فيما سبق وجوبَ كونه جل وعلا مختاراً ، فبطل إذا أنْ يكونَ في فعلٍ مِنْ أفعالِهِ غرضُ يحمله على ، قال تعالى : ﴿ وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيَختارُ ﴾ [القصص : ٦٨] . الثاني : أنَّ الغرضَ : إما أن يكون قديماً فيجب قدم الفعل ، وإلا كانَ البارى جلَّ وعلا ناقصاً ؛ لفواتِ غرضِهِ ، أو حادثاً فيحتاج هذا الغرضُ إلى غرض حادثٍ ؛ إذْ هو مِنْ جملة الأفعال الحادثة ، ويلزمُ التسلسل وحوادث لا أوَّلَ لها ، وقدمُ الفعلِ باطل ؛ لما عرفْتَ مِنْ
And also: The purpose is either a benefit that returns to Him, or to His act. The first is impossible because it entails attributing originated things to His exalted essence. The second is impossible because taking care of what is beneficial and most advantageous is not obligatory upon Him, and because He is able to convey that benefit to the servant, for example, without an intermediary, and because it would necessitate either explaining the thing by itself, or an infinite regress if the discussion is transferred to that benefit itself. That is, if you know that actions are equal in relation to Him, exalted, and that He is free in all of them, none of them being obligatory upon Him, then it follows that He has no purpose in any of them; that is, no cause that contains a wisdom that impels Him to bring that act into existence or to annihilate it. Rather, He, glorious and exalted, is free in both matters. And he [the author] adduced in the Creed for this claim several proofs: The first: that if He had a purpose in any act, that act would be obligatory upon Him, such that its abandonment would be impossible for Him. The explanation of the entailment is that the meaning of purpose is that the act contains a wisdom that impels Him rationally to bring it into existence, such that His deficiency would necessarily follow if He did not do it. This is the meaning of purpose, so it makes the act necessary, otherwise it would not be a purpose that is a cause in it. So my saying: 'otherwise it would not be a cause for it' is an explanation of the entailment. And as for my saying: 'then He would be compelled', it is an explanation of the second [premise]; that is our saying: but the act would not be obligatory upon Him because what follows from it is His being compelled and lacking choice; for the free agent is the one from whom both the act and its abandonment are possible, and the assumption is that this act contains a purpose such that its abandonment is not possible with it. And you have already known the necessity of His being free, exalted. So it is invalid that in any of His acts there be a purpose that impels Him to do it. He said: 'And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses' [Quran 28:68]. The second: that the purpose is either eternal, then the act would necessarily be eternal, otherwise the Creator would be deficient due to the loss of His purpose; or it is originated, then this purpose would need an originated purpose, since it is among originated acts, and this would entail an infinite regress and originated things without beginning. And the act's being eternal is invalid because of what you know about
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage is a composite argument proving that God does not act for a purpose (gharad). It begins with a preliminary argument: if God had a purpose, it would be either a benefit returning to Himself (impossible, as it would entail His essence being subject to originated things) or a benefit returning to His act (impossible, because God is not obliged to choose the most beneficial for creatures; He can benefit them directly without an act; and it would either be a tautology or lead to infinite regress if we ask about the purpose of that benefit). The author then explains that once we know all actions are equally possible for God and He is free (mukhtar) with no obligation, it follows that He cannot have a purpose that drives Him. He then presents two formal proofs from his creed (al-'Aqida): the first proof argues that if an act had a purpose, that act would be obligatory (wajib) on God, meaning He could not omit it, because the very definition of a purpose is that it contains a wisdom that rationally impels Him, such that omitting it would be a deficiency. But this would imply God is compelled (maqhur) and not free, contradicting His essential freedom (ikhtiyar) and the Quranic verse (28:68). The second proof argues that the purpose must be either eternal (qadim) or originated (hadith). If eternal, the act would be eternal as well, which is false. If originated, then that originated purpose would itself need another originated purpose, leading to an infinite regress of originated things (tasalsul), which is impossible. Hence, God has no purpose in any of His acts.

Key Terms
غرض
حكمة
علة
اختيار
موجَب
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'purpose' (gharad) and 'wisdom' (hikma) are the same, meaning God acts for a purpose.
Correction: The text distinguishes them: purpose (gharad) is a cause that impels, while wisdom (hikma) is the resulting benefit from the act; God's actions have wisdom but not purpose.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, if God had a purpose in an act, what would follow?
A) The act would be voluntary
B) The act would be obligatory and He would be compelled
C) The act would be impossible
D) The purpose would be eternal

Key Takeaway
God's freedom requires that He not be compelled by any purpose; His actions are purely voluntary and without any external or internal cause that necessitates them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray or worship, do you ever catch yourself acting as if God 'needs' your worship or that it fulfills a purpose in Him? How does this lesson challenge that hidden assumption?
?
If God’s actions are not driven by any need or purpose, then why do you sometimes feel that He owes you an explanation for the trials you face? What would change in your heart if you truly believed He is free and not compelled?
?
Consider a time when you wanted God to act according to your idea of what is beneficial. In light of this lesson, are you willing to surrender your sense of what God 'should' do and trust His freedom, even when it seems purposeless to you?

The Impossibility of Purpose in Allah's Actions
Sanusi presents the third proof against attributing purpose to Allah's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that refutes attributing any purpose to Allah's actions, based on the impossibility of benefit returning to Allah or to creation without leading to contradiction or infinite regress.

Core Idea
Attributing purpose to Allah leads to logical dead ends: either temporal accidents or infinite regress.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a crisp logical proof that Allah's actions cannot be driven by any purpose or benefit, whether for Himself or for creation. By carefully analyzing each possibility, he shows that both lead to irrational conclusions, thereby affirming divine transcendence. This argument is a cornerstone of Ash'ari theology, emphasizing that God's actions are free from external motives.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the division of purposes
Read the passage and identify the two categories of purpose Sanusi presents: a benefit returning to Allah Himself or a benefit returning to His creation.
Notice that he begins with 'الثالثُ : الغرضُ' indicating this is the third point in a series.
2
Refutation of purpose benefiting Allah
Understand why a purpose benefiting Allah Himself is impossible: it would necessitate describing His essence with temporal occurrences (hawadith) and imply that He is deficient without His acts.
This aligns with the principle that Allah is free from temporal accidents and is perfect in His essence.
3
Refutation of purpose benefiting creation
Learn why a purpose benefiting creation is also impossible: Allah is not obligated to observe what is beneficial for creation, and He can directly grant benefit or remove harm without any mediating act.
The argument also highlights that a servant's purpose is tied to pleasure/pain, but Allah has no such need.
4
Follow the regress argument
Trace Sanusi's additional argument: if we insist the benefit itself is the purpose, asking 'why this benefit?' leads either to circular reasoning (the benefit being its own purpose) or to an infinite regress of purposes.
This shows the futility of trying to ground a divine purpose in any created benefit.
5
Conclude the argument
Synthesize the logical conclusion: since both categories are false and any alternative leads to regress, Allah's actions are entirely free from purpose or motive.
This establishes the Ash'ari position of divine transcendence beyond anthropomorphic motivations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثُ : الغرضُ : إِمَّا مصلحة في الفعل تعود إليه تعالى ، أو مصلحة تعود إلى خلقه والأوَّلُ باطل ؛ لأنَّهُ يُوجِبُ اتِّصاف ذاته بالحوادثِ ، وقد مرَّ بطلانه ، ويُوجِبُ أيضاً أن يكون ناقصاً في ذاتِهِ ، وإنما تكمل بأفعاله . والثاني باطل ؛ لما عرفتَ مِنْ عدم وجوب مراعاة الصلاح والأصلح عليهِ تعالى ، ولأنَّ غرض العبد إنَّما هو حصول لذة له ، أو دفع ألم ، والله سبحانَهُ قادر على إيصال ذلك له بغيرِ واسطة فعلٍ . وأيضاً : ننقلُ الكلامَ إلى هذه المصلحة فنقول : ما الموجِبُ لخلقها ووجودها بواسطة الفعل ؟ فإن قيل : لذاتِ كونها مصلحة .. لزم تعليلُ الشيء بنفسِهِ ؛ لأنَّها صارَتْ غرض نفسها وإن قيلَ : لغرض زائدِ عليها .. نُقِلَ الكلامُ إلى ذلكَ الغرضِ ، ولزم التسلسل .
The third: Purpose: either a benefit in the act returning to Him, exalted, or a benefit returning to His creation. The first is false because it necessitates the description of His essence with temporal occurrences, and its falsehood has passed, and it also necessitates that He be deficient in His essence, and that He is only completed by His acts. The second is false because you have known from the non-obligation of observing what is beneficial and most beneficial upon Him, exalted, and because the purpose of the servant is only the attainment of pleasure for him, or repulsion of pain, and Allah, glorified is He, is able to convey that to him without the mediation of an act. Also: we transfer the discourse to this benefit and say: what necessitates its creation and existence by means of the act? If it is said: because it is a benefit, then this necessitates explaining a thing by itself, because it became its own purpose. And if it is said: for an additional purpose, then the discourse is transferred to that purpose, and infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 531

Commentary
This passage is a proof against attributing purpose (gharad) to Allah's actions. The author (Sanusi) presents the third point in a series of arguments. He divides possible purposes into two categories: either a benefit that returns to Allah Himself, or a benefit that returns to His creation. The first is false because it would imply that Allah's essence is subject to temporal occurrences (hawadith), which is impossible, and would also imply that He is deficient and only completed by His actions. The second is false for two reasons: (1) It is not obligatory upon Allah to consider what is beneficial or most beneficial for creation, as He is free from obligation; (2) The purpose of a servant is to attain pleasure or avoid pain, but Allah is able to grant these directly without any mediating act. Furthermore, if one insists that the benefit of the act is the purpose, then asking 'why this benefit?' leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul) or circular reasoning. Thus, both possibilities are rationally untenable, proving that Allah's actions are free from external purposes.

Key Terms
الغرض
المصلحة
التسلسل
الحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that attributing purpose to God is harmless and even necessary to explain why He acts.
Correction: Sanusi's argument shows that any purpose leads to logical contradictions (temporal accidents in God, deficiency, or infinite regress). Thus, God's actions are beyond such anthropomorphic motives.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider a purpose that returns to Allah Himself to be false?
A) Because it would imply Allah is in need of His creation
B) Because it would obligate Allah to do what is beneficial for Himself
C) Because it would necessitate describing His essence with temporal occurrences and imply deficiency
D) Because it would make His actions dependent on external factors

Key Takeaway
Allah's actions are absolutely free from any external purpose or motive, as any attempt to posit such leads to rational impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's actions are not for any purpose, what does that reveal about your expectations when you ask Him for something? Do you assume He must grant your request because it seems beneficial to you? How does this passage challenge that assumption, and how might it change your dua?
?
Is there a situation in your life where you believe Allah's wisdom must align with your sense of what is good? How does the rejection of purpose in divine actions free you from expecting God to act according to your standards? What would it look like to trust His actions without needing to understand a reason?
?
The passage argues that Allah can grant benefit without any act. How does this truth affect your reliance on means like money, health, or relationships? Do you ever trust these means more than the One who doesn't need them? What would change if you truly internalized that Allah's granting of benefit is not tied to any act or purpose?

Avoiding Implication of Motive in Divine Acts
Sanusi criticizes the term 'ba'ith' and interprets Quranic verses to avoid implying purpose for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why and how expressions implying a motive for God's actions must be reinterpreted to preserve divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Never attribute motives to God; reinterpret any apparent purpose language.

Sanusi addresses a critical methodological principle: Allah's actions are not motivated by external purposes. He critiques Ibn al-Hajib's use of the term 'ba'ith' (motive) and shows how to interpret Quranic verses that seem to imply a purpose. This lesson clarifies the proper way to speak about divine acts without compromising tanzih.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem with 'motive'
Read the passage to see why 'ba'ith' is problematic when applied to Allah. Note that it implies Allah acts for a reason external to Himself.
Ibn al-Hajib used it in legal theory, but Sanusi says it's misleading even if interpreted correctly.
2
Examine the Quranic example
Look at verse 51:56: 'I created jinn and mankind only to worship Me.' Sanusi explains that this must be interpreted to avoid implying God has a purpose.
He offers two interpretations: the lām of eventual outcome or subsidiary metaphor.
3
Apply the principle to other texts
Sanusi warns that any Quranic or prophetic expression that seems to attribute a motive to God must be reinterpreted.
This is a key principle of the Ash'ari approach to divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرعي ورعيه تفضّل (۱) ، لا بالحكم العقلي وإيجابه الأحكام ، ولهذا اعتُرِضَ على ابن الحاجب قوله في ( أصوله » في باب القياس عندما تعرض لذكر شروط العلَّةِ : ( ومنها : أن تكون بمعنى الباعثِ )(۲) ، وتُؤوَّلُ بأنَّ مرادَهُ الباعثةُ للمُكلَّف على الامتثال ، لا الباعثة له تعالى على الحكم ، والحقُّ أنَّها معَ ذلكَ عبارةٌ موهمةٌ ، فيجب تجنبها وكذا ما يُوجَدُ في الكتاب أو السنَّةِ مِنْ أفعال الله تعالى موهماً للتعليل بالأغراض ؛ كقوله تعالى : ﴿ وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونِ ﴾ [الذاريات : ٥٦] ، فإِنَّهُ يجب تأويلُهُ ، فَتُجعَلُ اللام في قوله تعالى : لِيَعْبُدُونِ ( لامَ الصيرورة ، مثلُها في قوله تعالى : ﴿ فَالْتَقَطَهُ آلُ فِرْعَوْنَ لِيَكُونَ لَهُمْ عَدُوًّا وَحَزَنًا ﴾ [القصص : ؟] ، أو هي مِنَ الاستعارة التبعية على ما تقرَّرَ في فنّ البيانِ
The legal [judgment] and its observation are a favor [from God], not by the rational judgment and its necessitation of rulings. For this reason, objection was raised against Ibn al-Hajib regarding his statement in his 'Principles' in the chapter on analogy when he mentioned the conditions of the cause: 'Among them: that it be in the sense of the motive [ba'ith]'. It is interpreted that what he meant is the motive for the obligated to comply, not the motive for Him, exalted, for the ruling. The truth is that, despite that, it is a misleading expression, so it must be avoided. Likewise, whatever is found in the Book or the Sunna of God's actions that misleadingly implies explanation by purposes, such as His saying, 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me' [51:56]; for it must be interpreted. So the lām in His saying 'to worship Me' is made the lām of eventual outcome, like it in His saying, 'Then the family of Pharaoh picked him up to be for them an enemy and a sorrow' [28:?], or it is from subsidiary metaphor according to what is established in the craft of rhetoric.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage discusses the danger of using language that implies God acts for motives. The author begins by noting that legal rulings and their observance are a favor from God, not necessitated by rational judgment. He then mentions the objection to Ibn al-Hajib's use of the term 'motive' in his work on legal theory. Ibn al-Hajib's statement was interpreted to mean the motive for the human to obey, not a motive in God. However, the author insists the term is still misleading and should be avoided. The same applies to Quranic verses that might seem to attribute purposes to God, such as 'I created jinn and humans only to worship Me.' Such verses must be interpreted so that the lām (indicating purpose) is understood as a lām of eventual outcome (like in the story of Moses being picked up by Pharaoh's family) or as a subsidiary metaphor. This preserves divine transcendence by avoiding any implication that God acts for external motives.

Key Terms
باعث
موهم
لام الصيرورة
استعارة تبعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'motive' when applied to God does not imply a change in His essence; it only describes the outward relation.
Correction: Even if 'motive' is interpreted as the human's motive, the expression is still misleading because it suggests God acts for a reason. Sanusi insists on avoiding such language entirely.

Checkpoint

A) By reinterpreting the lām as a lām of eventual outcome
B) By ignoring such verses as metaphorical
C) By applying a literal reading and affirming purpose for God
D) By denying the authenticity of such verses

Key Takeaway
Any expression that suggests a motive behind God's actions must be reinterpreted to protect divine transcendence.

Refuting the Foolishness Argument
Sanusi refutes a Mu'tazilite argument about purpose in divine actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that divine acts without purpose would be foolish, by redefining foolishness in human terms and denying its application to God.

Core Idea
Foolishness requires ignorance; God's perfect knowledge makes it impossible.

The Mu'tazilites argued that if God acts without purpose, His actions would be foolish, contradicting His wisdom. Sanusi responds by clarifying the definition of foolishness, showing it depends on ignorance and short-sightedness—qualities incompatible with divine transcendence. This passage reveals a key method of refutation: exposing anthropomorphic assumptions in theological objections.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite objection
Read the first part of the passage where the Mu'tazilite argument is presented: no purpose implies foolishness, which is impossible for a wise God.
Note the logical structure: if no purpose, then foolish; but God is wise, so purpose must exist.
2
Examine Sanusi's redefinition of foolishness
Focus on Sanusi's definition of foolishness (safah): it involves ignorance of consequences or preference for immediate pleasure, leading to harm.
This definition is key to denying the implication.
3
See how the redefinition denies the implication
Sanusi argues that since God has perfect knowledge and is free from ignorance and base desires, the definition of foolishness cannot apply to Him. Thus, the logical implication (no purpose → foolish) is denied.
This is a classic example of man' al-luzum (denial of entailment).
4
Reflect on divine transcendence
Consider how this refutation upholds divine transcendence (tanzih) by removing anthropomorphic concepts from discussions about God's actions and rulings.
The Mu'tazilite argument assumed a human-like framework for divine action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفي الفائدة نفي للعبث والسفه ]
قالوا : إذا لم يكن غرض فالفعل سفه
قلنا : السفه عرفاً : ما فُعِلَ معَ الجهل بالعواقب ، أو ترجيح اللذة الحاضرة ، حتى يفعل السفيه ما فيه ضرره أو حتفه وهو لا يشعرُ ، وأين هذا مِنْ فعل المتعالي عن تجدُّدِ كمال أو نقصان ، الذي لا يعزب عن علمه شيء على الإطلاق في سر وإعلان ؟!
هذهِ شبهةٌ مِنْ جهةِ المعتزلة القائلين بثبوتِ الأغراض الموجبة للأفعال والأحكام، وتقريرها أن قالوا : لو كان الفعلُ أو الحكم وقعا بغيرِ غرض لَلزِمَ السفه أو العبتُ ممَّنْ صدرا منه ، لكنَّهُ تعالى حكيم يستحيل عليه العبتُ والسفه ، فيستحيلُ إذا أنْ يفعل أو يحكم لا لغرض . والجواب : منع الملازمة ؛ وذلكَ أنَّ السفة في العُرفِ : عبارةٌ عن الجهل بالمصالح ، وخفَّةِ العقل ، حتى إنَّ السفية ليفعل ما يضر به أو يهلكه حالاً أو مالاً وهو لا يشعرُ ، أو يشعرُ للكنَّهُ لجهلِهِ وخِفَّةِ عقلِهِ يُرجحُ المرجوحَ مِنْ قضاءِ لَذَّةٍ حاليَّة لا بقاء لها مثلاً على عقوبات عظيمة دائمة
[Heading:] Denial of benefit is denial of futility and foolishness.
They said: If there is no purpose, then the act is foolish.
We said: Foolishness, conventionally, is what is done with ignorance of consequences, or preferring immediate pleasure, so that the foolish person does what harms him or causes his death without realizing. And where is this compared to the act of the One exalted above acquiring perfection or deficiency, from whose knowledge nothing whatsoever escapes in secret or in public?!
This is a doubt from the direction of the Mu'tazilites who affirm the existence of purposes necessitating actions and rulings. Its presentation is that they said: If the action or ruling occurred without a purpose, then foolishness or futility would necessarily follow from the one from whom they issued. But He, exalted, is wise; futility and foolishness are impossible for Him. Therefore, it is impossible that He acts or judges without a purpose. The answer: denial of the implication. That is because foolishness, conventionally, is an expression of ignorance of benefits and lightness of intellect, such that the foolish person does what harms him or destroys him immediately or financially without realizing, or realizes but due to his ignorance and lightness of intellect he prefers the lesser over great, lasting punishments for example in exchange for a temporary pleasure that has no permanence.
Source pages p. 532

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazilite objection. The heading states the claim: 'Denial of benefit is denial of futility and foolishness.' The Mu'tazilites argue that if God's actions have no purpose, they would be foolish, but God is wise, so He must act for a purpose. The author presents the objection: 'If there is no purpose, the act is foolish.' The answer begins by defining foolishness (safah) conventionally as acting with ignorance of consequences or preferring immediate pleasure, leading to harm. This does not apply to God, whose knowledge is complete and whose act is not based on ignorance or fleeting pleasures. Therefore, the implication (that no purpose equals foolishness) is denied. The author emphasizes that foolishness, as defined, involves ignorance or lack of understanding, which is impossible for God. Thus, the Mu'tazilite argument fails because it relies on a false equivalence between human and divine action.

Key Terms
غرض
سفه
عبث
ملازمة
مصالح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Foolishness is a trait that could be attributed to God if His actions lack purpose.
Correction: Foolishness is defined as ignorance or short-sightedness, which are impossible for God due to His perfect knowledge and transcendence.

Checkpoint
What is the key point in the author's refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that God's actions without purpose would be foolish?
A) He denies that God performs actions without purpose
B) He redefines foolishness as ignorance, which cannot apply to God
C) He argues that foolishness is a human concept inapplicable to God
D) He asserts that God's actions do have purposes but are hidden

Key Takeaway
Foolishness is a human condition rooted in ignorance; attributing it to God is only possible if one anthropomorphizes the Divine, which the definition refutes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial you cannot understand, do you ever doubt God's wisdom because you cannot see a purpose? How does this lesson's definition of foolishness expose the flaw in that doubt? Will you now commit to trusting God's perfect knowledge even when His ways are hidden from you?
?
Consider a time you insisted on a certain outcome, believing it was for your benefit, yet it didn't happen. Did you feel God was being arbitrary or foolish? Reflect on how this passage redefines the very meaning of foolishness—as ignorance and short-sightedness. Are you willing to let go of your need to see a clear 'purpose' in everything, and instead rest in the certainty that God's knowledge is flawless and His actions are beyond reproach?

Refuting Purpose in Divine Actions and Defining Divine Wisdom
Sanusi defines al-'abath, refutes necessity of purpose for God, and defines divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine wisdom cannot mean acting for a purpose, and how Sanusi defines it instead as God's knowledge and power.

Core Idea
God's wisdom is His knowledge and power, not acting for a purpose.

This passage tackles a crucial point of divergence between Sunni Islam and the Mu'tazilites: does God act for a purpose? Sanusi clarifies that while human actions may require purpose to be meaningful, God is absolutely self-sufficient. He defines divine wisdom not as acting for a goal, but as the perfection of knowledge and power. Let's explore his argument.

Guided Path
1
Define 'abath (futility)
Read how Sanusi defines 'abath conventionally: doing something heedlessly without intention. Understand that this involves deficiency.
This sets up the discussion about purpose.
2
Separate 'abath from purpose
Notice that Sanusi argues there is no necessary link between 'abath and the negation of purpose. God has no purpose yet His actions are not futile.
3
Learn the definition of divine wisdom
Sanusi defines wisdom (hikmah) as God's knowledge of things and His power to perfect them. This is the core positive definition.
Contrast with the Mu'tazilite view.
4
Refute the Mu'tazilite claim
The Mu'tazilites equate wisdom with acting for a purpose. Sanusi rejects this: wisdom in God is an attribute, not an action for a goal.
5
Understand terminology avoidance
We avoid terms like 'abath and safah for God not because they deny purpose, but because they imply deficiency. This is a linguistic and theological point.
Sanusi emphasizes the conventional meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العبتُ فيُطلَقُ في العرفِ : على فعل الشيء معَ الدُّهول وعدمِ القصدِ وهذا كلُّهُ لا لزوم بينَهُ وبينَ نفْي الغرضِ ؛ لأنَّا نقولُ : إِنَّهُ تعالى لا غرضَ له في الفعلِ ، معَ أنَّ أفعالَهُ كلَّها جاريةٌ على وَفْقِ علمِهِ وإرادتِهِ ، لا يلحقه ضررٌ مِنْ قِبَلِها ، ولا يتجدَّدُ له كمال بفعلها ، بل هو الغني في ذاتِهِ وصفاتِهِ أزلاً وفيما لا يزالُ ثم الحكمة المنسوبة إليهِ تعالى : عبارة عن علمه بالأشياء وقدرتِهِ على إحكامها وإتقانها ، فهي تقتضي العلم والقدرة ، وهما واجبانِ لهُ تعالى ، لا فعل الشيء لغرض كما زعمت المعتزلة وإذا فهمت هذا في [?] جاريةٌ على وَفْقِ علمِهِ ، لا يتطرَّقُ لَهُ مِنْ قِبَلِها نقص كيفما وجهها على أفعاله فافهم مثله في أحكامه ؛ فإنَّها أيضاً على عبيده وإن فسَّرَ المعتزلة السَّفَة والعبث بنفي الغرض الملازمة ، ومنعنا الاستثنائية سلمنا وقصارى الأمر : أنَّا نمنع على هذا التقرير إطلاق هذين اللفظين بالنسبة إليه تعالى ؛ لإيهامهما معنى يستحيلُ في حقه ؛ وهو ما ذكرنا أنَّهما يدلان عليه عرفاً ، لا لما ذكروهُ مِنْ دلالتهما على نفي الغرض
As for al-'abath (futility), it is conventionally defined as doing something with heedlessness and absence of intention. All of this has no necessary connection to the negation of purpose; because we say: He, exalted, has no purpose in action, while all His actions proceed in accordance with His knowledge and will. No harm befalls Him from them, nor does He acquire perfection by performing them; rather He is Self-sufficient in His essence and attributes eternally and forever. Then wisdom (al-hikmah) attributed to Him, exalted, is an expression of His knowledge of things and His power to perfect and solidify them. So it necessitates knowledge and power, and both are obligatory for Him, exalted—not performing an action for a purpose as the Mu'tazilites claimed. And if you understand this in [?] proceeding according to His knowledge, no deficiency accrues to Him from them no matter how He directs them in His actions, then understand the same regarding His judgments; for they also apply to His servants. And if the Mu'tazilites interpret al-safah (foolishness) and al-'abath as the negation of purpose with the necessary connection, and we deny the exception, we concede. The upshot is: we deny, on this account, the application of these two terms to Him, exalted, because they suggest a meaning impossible with respect to Him, which is what we mentioned that they conventionally indicate, not because they indicate the negation of purpose as they claimed.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that God must act for a purpose and defines divine wisdom. It begins by defining 'abath (futility) as doing something heedlessly without intention. Sanusi argues that even if we accept this definition, there is no necessary link between it and the negation of purpose, because God has no purpose yet His actions are perfectly aligned with His knowledge and will, and He suffers no harm or gain. Then he defines divine wisdom: it is God's knowledge of things and His power to perfect them, not acting for a purpose. The Mu'tazilites' interpretation of safah and 'abath as mere negation of purpose is rejected because these terms conventionally imply defect, which cannot be attributed to God. Thus, we avoid using such terms for God not because their meaning is false, but because they suggest a deficiency impossible for Him.

Key Terms
العبت
السفه
الغرض
الحكمة
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine wisdom means God acts for a purpose, like humans do to achieve benefit or avoid harm.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that wisdom for God is His attribute of knowledge and power, not an purpose-driven action. God's actions are wise because they align with His perfect knowledge, not because they aim at an external goal.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of divine wisdom (al-hikmah) according to Sanusi in this passage?
A) Acting for a purpose to achieve benefit or avoid harm
B) God's knowledge of things and His power to perfect them
C) Avoiding futile actions with no purpose
D) Creating the world for the benefit of creatures

Key Takeaway
Sanusi establishes that divine wisdom is God's perfect knowledge and power, not acting for a purpose, and that we avoid terms suggesting deficiency for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi insists that God's actions are not for a purpose—they proceed from His perfect knowledge and will. Where in your life have you assumed that God's actions must have a purpose you can see? Can you release the need to find a reason for every trial and instead trust that His knowledge and power make it wise, even without a purpose you understand?
?
The terms 'abath and safah are avoided for God because they imply deficiency. Examine your own speech and thoughts: have you ever, even subtly, described God's decree as pointless or unfair? What does that reveal about your heart's estimation of His wisdom? Will you commit today to guard your tongue and mind from any word that suggests imperfection in His actions?
?
Sanusi defines wisdom as knowledge and power, not purpose. If you truly internalize that God's wisdom is independent of achieving any external goal, how would that change your reaction when you cannot see the 'point' of your suffering? Would you be able to say, 'It is from His perfect knowledge and power, and that is enough'? Write one concrete step you will take to embody this trust.

Jihad Obligation and Allah's Absolute Will
Sanusi discusses the ruling of jihad depending on numbers and affirms Allah's absolute will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi reconciles the contextual obligation of jihad with the absolute will of Allah, affirming that Allah is not bound by considerations of benefit.

Core Idea
Allah's will is absolute; He is not questioned about His actions.

Sanusi presents a nuanced discussion about the obligation of jihad, showing that its ruling depended on the number of Muslims. However, he immediately qualifies this by reminding us that such considerations are secondary; the core belief is that Allah does whatever He wills without being subject to question or obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the flow from historical ruling to theological principle.
Pay attention to the phrase 'هذا إذا تنزلنا' which signals a conditional concession.
2
Understand the two rulings on jihad
Identify that Sanusi says jihad was first an individual duty when Muslims were few, then became obligatory when they were many. This is based on the Quranic verse.
The verse is part of the source.
3
Recognize the conditional statement
Sanusi says this interpretation holds only if we accept the Mu'tazili principle that Allah considers benefit (صلاح). Then he contrasts it with the correct Ash'ari belief.
This is a key theological pivot.
4
Grasp the affirmation of divine will
The correct belief is that Allah does what He wills and is not questioned. This is supported by Quran 21:23.
Memorize the verse.
5
Reflect on the contrast
Consider the difference between a theology that imposes obligations on Allah (Mu'tazila) and one that affirms His absolute sovereignty (Ash'ari).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أول الإسلام لقلتِهم ، وإيجابُهُ عليهم عندَ كثرتهم ؛ إذ قال الله تعالى : وَقَاتِلُوا الْمُشْرِكِينَ ﴾ [التوبة : ٣٦] ، هذا إذا تنزَّلْنا إلى القولِ باعتبار الصلاح والأصلح ، وإلا فمُعتقَدنا أنَّ الله تعالى يفعل ما يشاء ، ويحكم ما يريدُ ، لَا يُسْأَلُ عَمَّا يَفْعَلُ وَهُمْ يُسْأَلُونَ ﴾ [الأنبياء : ٢٣]
The beginning of Islam (fighting) is due to their small number, and its obligation upon them at their large number; for Allah Most High said: 'And fight the polytheists' [At-Tawbah: 36]. This is if we descend to the doctrine of considering benefit and the most beneficial; otherwise, our belief is that Allah Most High does what He wills and judges what He desires. 'He is not questioned about what He does, but they are questioned' [Al-Anbiya': 23].
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage addresses a seemingly secondary point in a larger polemic: the timing of the obligation of jihad (fighting) against polytheists. Sanusi explains that the command to fight initially applied when Muslims were few, but became obligatory when they became numerous, as per the Quranic verse (9:36). He then qualifies this by saying that this interpretation assumes the principle that Allah considers what is beneficial and most beneficial for His creation (a Mu'tazili principle). But as an Ash'ari, Sanusi states the correct belief: Allah does whatever He wills and judges whatever He desires, and He is not questioned about His actions, while they (the creation) are questioned. This is a strong affirmation of divine sovereignty and a rejection of any notion that Allah must act for a purpose or benefit.

Key Terms
جهاد
صلاح
أصلح
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jihad is always obligatory regardless of circumstances.
Correction: Sanusi explains that the obligation of jihad depended on the numbers of Muslims, and ultimately, Allah's will is absolute and not bound by any principle of benefit.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the correct Ash'ari belief about Allah's actions?
A) Allah acts only for the benefit of creation
B) Allah does what He wills and is not questioned
C) Allah must always choose the most beneficial
D) Allah's actions are subject to human reason

Key Takeaway
Sanusi affirms Allah's absolute will and independence from any requirement to act for benefit, while acknowledging the contextual nature of jihad obligations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems unfair or painful, do you find yourself questioning why Allah allowed it? How does the truth that 'He is not questioned about what He does' confront your tendency to demand explanations? What would it mean for you to truly submit to His absolute will in that moment?
?
Is there an area of your life where you secretly believe Allah owes you something—health, success, or comfort—because you have been 'good'? How does Sanusi's rejection of the doctrine of 'benefit' challenge that assumption, and what would it look like to release that expectation today?
?
If Allah is not bound by any obligation to act for your benefit, does that frighten you or free you? Reflect on a specific decision or hardship you are currently facing. Can you say from your heart, 'He is not questioned about what He does,' and trust Him without needing to understand the reason?

When Ta'wil Becomes Obligatory
Sanusi lays down the rule for ta'wil when the apparent meaning is impossible and provides the rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ta'wil is necessary when the apparent meaning of a revealed text is impossible according to reason, and how the single-interpretation case is handled with an example from the Quran.

Core Idea
Reason and revelation cannot conflict; when they seem to, ta'wil harmonizes them.

In this lesson, we examine the fundamental rule that governs how scholars approach verses whose literal meaning would imply something impossible about Allah. The author explains why turning away from the apparent meaning is not optional but obligatory, and he provides a concrete example from the Quran to illustrate the process.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the rule and rationale
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Let the author's argument flow naturally before you interpret.
Notice the strong consensus language: 'ittifaqan'.
2
Identify the consensus
Find the statement that all scholars agree we must turn away from the impossible apparent meaning. Underline it.
This consensus is the starting point for all ta'wil.
3
Understand the rational argument
The author gives a proof: revelation cannot command what is impossible, and reason is the basis for accepting revelation. Trace this logical chain.
If we denied reason, we would destroy the foundation of transmission.
4
Analyze the example of ma'iyya
The verse 'He is with you' cannot mean physical companionship. See how the author derives the single valid interpretation: knowledge and care.
This illustrates the case where only one interpretation remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان ضوابط التأويل ، ومتى يُصار إليه ]
أما ما استحالَ ظاهرُهُ ؛ نحو : ﴿ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى ﴾ فإنَّا نصرفُهُ عن ظاهره اتفاقاً ، ثم إنْ كانَ له تأويل واحد تعيَّنَ الحمل عليهِ ، وإلا وجبَ التفويضُ معَ التنزيهِ ، وهو مذهب الأقدمينَ ، خلافاً لإمام الحرمين .
لمَّا ذكرَ أنَّ ما يُجوِّزُهُ العقل إذا أخبر الشرعُ بوقوعِهِ يجبُ أنْ يُؤْمَنَ بهِ على ظاهره ، ولا يجوز تأويلُه ؛ والتعرُّضُ لتأويله بدعة . . ذكرَ أَنَّ
عن
ما أخبر الشرعُ بهِ ، وكانَ ظاهرُهُ مستحيلاً عندَ العقل فإنَّا نصرفُهُ ظاهره المستحيل ؛ لأنَّا نعلم قطعاً أنَّ الشرع لا يخبر بوقوع ما لا يمكنُ وقوعه ، ولو كذبنا العقل في هذا ، وعملنا بظاهرِ النقل المستحيل . . لأدَّى ذلك إلى انهدام النقلِ أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ العقل أصل لثبوتِ النبوَّاتِ التي يتفرّعُ عنها صحة النقل ، فيلزم إذاً مِنْ تكذيب العقل تكذيب النقل .
ثم بعد صرْفِ اللفظ عن ظاهره المستحيل :
إنْ لم يكن له بعد ذلكَ إلا تأويل واحد صحيح .. تعيَّنَ الحمل عليه ؛ لعدم وجودِ غيره ؛ وذلكَ مثل قوله : ( وَهُوَ مَعَكُمْ أَيْنَ مَا كُنتُمْ ) [الحديد : ٤] فَإِنَّهُ يستحيلُ حملُهُ على ظاهرِهِ مِنَ المصاحبة بالذاتِ ، ولم يبقَ بعد ذلك إلا حمله على المعيَّة بالعلم والرعاية ونظيره :
إِلَّا هُوَ رَابِعُهُمْ ... [المجادلة : ٧] الآيةَ ، ونحو ذلك مما هو كثير
وإِنْ كانَ لهُ بعد ذلك تأويلات ، كلُّ واحدٍ منها مستقيم ؛ فهل يُعيَّنُ واحدٌ منها ليندفعَ اللَّبْسُ عن العوام ، وهو مذهب إمام الحرمين عنِ التعيين ويُفوَّضُ الأمرُ فيهِ إلى اللهِ تعالى دفعاً للتحكُّم ، وهو مذهب الأقدمينَ ؟
Statement of the rules of ta'wil (interpretation), and when it should be applied. As for that whose apparent meaning is impossible, like {He established Himself upon the Throne} (20:5), we turn it away from its apparent meaning by consensus. Then if it has one valid interpretation, it must be taken upon that; otherwise, tafwid (delegation) together with tanzih (transcendence) is obligatory, and that is the madhhab of the early scholars, contrary to Imam al-Haramayn. When he mentioned that what reason deems possible, if revelation informs of its occurrence, must be believed in its apparent meaning, and its ta'wil is not permissible, and attempting its ta'wil is an innovation... he mentioned that what revelation informs of, and its apparent meaning is impossible according to reason, then we turn it away from its impossible apparent meaning, because we know with certainty that revelation does not inform of the occurrence of what cannot occur. If we were to deny reason in this, and act upon the apparent meaning of the impossible transmitted text, that would lead to the demolition of the transmitted text as well, because reason is the basis for establishing prophethood from which the soundness of transmission derives; so from discrediting reason necessarily follows discrediting transmission. Then after turning the word away from its impossible apparent meaning: if it has no valid interpretation other than one, then it must be taken upon that, because there is no other; and that is like His saying: {And He is with you wherever you are} (57:4) — it is impossible to take it upon its apparent meaning of essential companionship, and there remains after that only taking it upon companionship by knowledge and care, and its like: {Except He is the fourth of them} (58:7), and similar things that are many. And if after that it has interpretations, each of which is valid, then is one specified to remove confusion from the common people – which is the madhhab of Imam al-Haramayn – or is the matter delegated to Allah Almighty to avoid arbitrary judgment – which is the madhhab of the early scholars?
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage explains the rules of ta'wil (interpretation) when the apparent meaning of a revealed text is impossible according to reason. The author begins by stating that when the apparent meaning is impossible (like 'establishing Himself on the Throne' which suggests physical location), all scholars agree that we must turn away from that apparent meaning. Then, after turning away, if there is only one valid interpretation, it must be adopted; otherwise, tafwid (delegating the meaning to Allah) along with tanzih (affirming transcendence) is obligatory. This is the position of the early scholars, contrary to Imam al-Haramayn. The author then provides the logical reason for this: we know with certainty that revelation cannot report something impossible, and if we were to reject reason in this, we would also destroy transmission because reason is the foundation for confirming prophethood. After giving the general principle, he gives examples: verses about God's 'withness' (ma'iyya) cannot mean literal spatial companionship, so they must be interpreted as knowledge and care. Then he raises the case of multiple valid interpretations: should one be specified to remove confusion (Imam al-Haramayn's view) or should the matter be delegated to Allah (the early scholars' view)? The passage ends with this question, leaving the two positions presented.

Key Terms
التأويل (ta'wil)
التفويض (tafwid)
التنزيه (tanzih)
المستحيل (impossible)
ظاهر (apparent meaning)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think ta'wil is always permissible or always forbidden.
Correction: The passage shows ta'wil is obligatory only when the apparent meaning is impossible; otherwise, the literal meaning is taken.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if we deny reason and adhere to the impossible apparent meaning of a revealed text?
A) Revelation would still be valid.
B) The transmission would be destroyed because reason is its foundation.
C) Reason and revelation would be reconciled.
D) The impossible meaning would become possible.

Key Takeaway
When the apparent meaning of a revealed text is impossible, it must be interpreted validly; reason is the foundation for revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'He established Himself upon the Throne,' do you feel a tension between your reason and the words? How does Sanusi’s rule compel you to trust that both reason and revelation are from Allah and cannot conflict?
?
If you were to abandon reason in interpreting Allah’s attributes, what would you lose? Can you honestly say you believe reason is a gift from Allah to be used in understanding His Book, or do you fear it might lead you astray?
?
Sanusi warns that denying reason destroys the very basis of revelation. Is there an area of your faith where you are reluctant to use your intellect, perhaps out of fear or habit? What step will you take this week to reconcile your mind and your heart in light of this lesson?
